Response to the Objection on Time's Perpetuity or Finitude
Author responds to an objection by citing Plato and Aristotle's views on time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author addresses the objection that time must be either perpetual or finite by referencing the views of Plato and Aristotle, and why this does not undermine his own position.

Core Idea
The dichotomy of time is neutralized by showing it does not affect the author's earlier argument.

In this brief passage, the author tackles an opponent's objection that time must be either perpetual or finite. Rather than choosing one side, he invokes the opinions of two great philosophers—Plato and Aristotle—to demonstrate that regardless of which view one adopts, the earlier answer remains valid. This shows a strategic approach to handling objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation together. Notice the structure: objection, then reference to two doctrines, then conclusion.
2
Identify the two positions
Plato says time is perpetual in essence but changes in relations; Aristotle says it is finite in essence. Understand the nuance.
3
Understand the author's strategy
The author does not argue which view is correct; instead, he says that on either view, the answer has already been given. This is a way of side-stepping the objection.
4
Reflect on the earlier answer
Though not repeated here, consider that the author likely argued that time is neither perpetually existing nor finite in a way that affects divine attributes.
Think about how this connects to the broader discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما قوله خامساً : الزمان إما أن يكون دائماً أو منقضياً ، قلنا : أما مذهب أفلاطون فهو أنه دائم في ذاته وجوهره ويتبدل بحسب نسبه . وأما مذهب أرسطاطاليس فهو أنه منقض في ذاته . وعلى التقديرين فالجواب قد سلف .
And as for his fifth statement: 'Time is either perpetual or finite.' We said: As for the doctrine of Plato, it is that it is perpetual in its essence and substance, and changes according to its relations. As for the doctrine of Aristotle, it is that it is finite in its essence. On both suppositions, the answer has already preceded.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage presents another objection: the opponent presents a dichotomy—time is either perpetual (da'im) or finite (munqadi). The author responds by noting that even if we accept one of these positions, the answer to the objection has already been given earlier. He references the views of Plato and Aristotle: Plato held that time is eternal in its essence but changes in its relations; Aristotle held that time is finite in its essence. In both cases, the author claims that the answer has already been provided, likely referring to earlier arguments that time is neither perpetual nor finite in the way the opponent assumes, or that the dichotomy is false. The exact earlier answer is not repeated here.

Key Terms
دائماً
منقضياً
ذات
جوهر
نسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author accepts that time must be either perpetual or finite.
Correction: The author does not accept the dichotomy as a genuine problem; he shows that even if one accepts one of the two views, his earlier argument remains unaffected.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the doctrines of Plato and Aristotle regarding time?
A) Both agree that time is perpetual.
B) Plato says time is perpetual in essence; Aristotle says it is finite in essence.
C) Both say time is finite.
D) Plato says time is finite; Aristotle says it is perpetual.

Key Takeaway
The author's response to the objection on time's perpetuity or finitude is to show that the earlier argument stands regardless of which philosophical view one adopts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter an apparent dilemma (like time being either perpetual or finite), do you immediately feel compelled to resolve it, or do you trust that the truth is not dependent on that dichotomy? How does this passage challenge your need to have all answers before you can be certain?
?
The author cites Plato and Aristotle without endorsing either. Is your faith in God's creation of time anchored in a specific theory, or is it stronger than any philosophical position? Are you willing to say, as the author does, that your belief stands regardless of the outcome of academic debates?
?
This passage shows that the author's earlier argument is not affected by the dispute over time. Reflect on an area where you have doubts about your faith because of a perceived contradiction. Could it be that your faith is actually independent of that contradiction? What would it mean for you to say, 'The answer has already preceded'?

Second Argument: The Eternity of Duration and Time
A philosophical argument for the eternity of duration and time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that duration and time must be eternal, including the trilemma and the reductio ad absurdum used to refute the idea of a beginning and end.

Core Idea
Duration and time cannot have a beginning or end without contradiction.

This lesson presents a sophisticated philosophical argument for the eternity of duration and time. The argument proceeds by considering all logical possibilities and showing that only one is coherent. As you read, notice how the author uses the concepts of priority and posteriority to derive the necessary eternal nature of time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the trilemma
Read the Arabic passage and translation. The argument begins by listing three possibilities for duration and time: having no beginning and no end, having both beginning and end, or having one without the other. Identify each option.
The first option is the desired conclusion; the other two are to be eliminated.
2
Follow the refutation of the second option
The author assumes duration has a beginning and an end. Notice how this leads to two 'non-existences': one preceding duration (without beginning) and one following it (without end). Understand how these non-existences share the property of being non-existence but differ in priority and posteriority.
Key insight: priority and posteriority are distinct from non-existence itself.
3
Understand how priority and posteriority imply eternity
The argument claims that priority and posteriority are the essence of duration and time. Since the pre-duration non-existence has priority without beginning and the post-duration non-existence has posteriority without end, duration and time must themselves be without beginning and end. Recognize the contradiction: assuming a beginning leads to no beginning, so the assumption is impossible.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: assuming the opposite leads to a logical contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : المدة والزمان. إما أن يقال : لا أول له ، ولا آخر له أو يقال : له أول وآخر. أو يقال : حصل له أحدهما دون الثاني . والأول هو المطلوب وأما الثاني : فنقول : على هذا التقدير تكون المدة مسبوقة بعدم لا أول ، وستصير ملحوقة بعدم لا آخر له . بعدم فهذان العدمان قد اشتركا في كون كل واحد منهما عدماً ، وامتاز أحدهما عن الآخر بكون أحدهما متقدماً على هذا الوجود ويكون الآخر متأخراً على هذا الوجود ، وما به المشاركة غير ما به الممايزة . فهذا التقدم والتأخر اللذان بهما امتاز أحد العدمين على الثاني ، لا بد وأن يكونا مغايران للعدم ، الذي هو القدر المشترك بين العدمين . ولا معنى للمدة والزمان ، إلا الأمر الذي به يمتاز أحد المثلين عن الآخر في معنى التقدم والتأخر ، ومفهوم القبلية والبعدية . ثم من المعلوم : أن أحد العدمين ، موصوف بالقبلية والتقدم وصفاً لا أول له . والعدم الثاني موصوف بالبعدية والتأخر ، وصفاً لا آخر له فهذا يقتضي القطع بكون المدة والزمان حاصلين حصولاً لا أول له ، ولا آخر له . فيثبت أن إثبات الأولية والآخرية للمدة ، يوجب نفي الأولية والآخرية عنها . وذلك محال . فيثبت : أن إثبات الأول والآخر للمدة : محال
The second argument: Duration and time. Either it is said: It has no beginning and no end, or it is said: It has a beginning and an end, or it is said: It has one of them without the other. The first is what is sought. As for the second, we say: On this supposition, the duration would be preceded by a non-existence that has no beginning, and it will be followed by a non-existence that has no end. By non-existence, these two non-existences share in that each one of them is a non-existence, and one of them is distinguished from the other by one being prior to this existence and the other being posterior to this existence. That by which they participate is other than that by which they differ. This priority and posteriority by which one of the two non-existences is distinguished from the second must be different from the non-existence that is the common factor between the two non-existences. There is no meaning to duration and time except the matter by which one of the two similars is distinguished from the other in the sense of priority and posteriority, and the concept of beforeness and afterness. Then it is known that one of the two non-existences is described by beforeness and priority as a description that has no beginning, and the second non-existence is described by afterness and posteriority as a description that has no end. This necessitates the decisive judgment that duration and time are obtained in an obtaining that has no beginning and no end. Thus it is established that establishing beginning and end for duration necessitates negating beginning and end from it. And that is impossible. So it is established that establishing beginning and end for duration is impossible.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument (hujja) for the eternity of duration and time. The author presents a trilemma: either duration/time has no beginning and no end (the desired conclusion), or it has both a beginning and an end, or it has one without the other. He then refutes the second option (having a beginning and end). He argues that if duration had a beginning, it would be preceded by a non-existence that itself has no beginning (since before the beginning of duration, there is an endless non-existence). Similarly, if it had an end, it would be followed by an endless non-existence. These two non-existences share the property of being non-existence but differ in priority and posteriority relative to the existence of duration. The priority of the first non-existence and the posteriority of the second are attributes distinct from their non-existence. These attributes (priority and posteriority) are the very meaning of duration and time. Therefore, duration and time must themselves exist as the measure of these relations. But these relations (priority of the pre-duration non-existence and posteriority of the post-duration non-existence) are themselves endless (the pre-duration non-existence has no beginning, the post-duration non-existence has no end). Hence, duration and time must be without beginning and without end. Thus, positing a beginning and end for duration leads to a contradiction (establishing beginning and end negates them), so it is impossible. Therefore, duration must be eternal. This is a typical philosopher's argument for the eternity of time. The author may be presenting this argument either as an opponent's argument or as a stepping stone to refute it. Given the context, it appears to be part of a series of arguments the author is responding to or analyzing. Without further context, we treat it as a self-contained argument.

Key Terms
المدة
الزمان
العدم
التقدم
التأخر
القبلية
البعدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is circular because it assumes the eternity of time to prove it.
Correction: The argument does not assume eternity; it assumes a beginning and then derives a contradiction, showing that the assumption is false. This is a reductio ad absurdum, not a circular argument.

Checkpoint
What is the first possibility in the trilemma about duration and time?
A) It has a beginning and an end.
B) It has neither beginning nor end.
C) It has a beginning but no end.
D) It has an end but no beginning.

Key Takeaway
Duration and time are necessarily eternal because any assumption of a beginning or end leads to a contradiction involving endless non-existences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If time itself cannot have a beginning or end, what does that mean for your attachment to moments that are slipping away? Will you anchor your hope in the Eternal rather than the fleeting?
?
The argument uses the logical impossibility of a beginning to time to force an eternal perspective. Is your daily consciousness shaped by the eternal, or are you so absorbed in the temporal that you live as if this world is all there is?
?
The passage reduces the assumption of a beginning to absurdity. Where in your life have you assumed a 'beginning' to your own story that excludes the greater, eternal context of your existence? How can you realign your narrative with the truth that only Allah is without beginning?

The Eternity of Time from the Distinction of Non-Existences
Argument for the pre-eternity of time from the distinction of two non-existences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the distinction between two non-existences implies that time has no beginning and no end.

Core Idea
Time must be eternal because it distinguishes non-existences that lack beginning and end.

In this lesson, we explore a profound argument for the eternity of time. The author considers two non-existences: the non-existence before a thing exists and after it perishes. These share the property of being non-existence but differ in priority and posteriority. This difference cannot come from non-existence itself, so it must come from time, which therefore must be beginningless and endless.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two non-existences
Grasp that before something exists there is a state of non-existence, and after it ceases there is another non-existence.
These are sometimes called the two non-existences (al-ʿadamān).
2
Identify common and distinguishing factors
Both non-existences share the attribute of being non-existence, but they differ in that one is before and one is after.
This difference is priority (taqaddum) and posteriority (taʾakhkhur).
3
Recognize the necessity of time
The distinction of before/after must come from something beyond non-existence, which is time (al-muddah wa al-zamān).
Time is the measure of priority and posteriority.
4
See the implications for eternity
Since the first non-existence has no beginning and the second no end, time must also have no beginning and no end.
This is because time is what distinguishes them.
5
Conclude impossibility of time having beginning or end
If time had a beginning and end, that would contradict the nature of the non-existences it distinguishes.
Thus asserting a first and last for time is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على هذا الوجود ويكون الآخر متأخراً على هذا الوجود ، وما به المشاركة غير ما به الممايزة . فهذا التقدم والتأخر اللذان بهما امتاز أحد العدمين على الثاني ، لا بد وأن يكونا مغايران للعدم ، الذي هو القدر المشترك بين العدمين . ولا معنى للمدة والزمان ، إلا الأمر الذي به يمتاز أحد المثلين عن الآخر في معنى التقدم والتأخر ، ومفهوم القبلية والبعدية . ثم من المعلوم : أن أحد العدمين ، موصوف بالقبلية والتقدم وصفاً لا أول له . والعدم الثاني موصوف بالبعدية والتأخر ، وصفاً لا آخر له . فهذا يقتضي القطع بكون المدة والزمان حاصلين حصولاً لا أول له ، ولا آخر له . فيثبت أن إثبات الأولية والآخرية للمدة ، يوجب نفي الأولية والآخرية عنها . وذلك محال . فيثبت : أن إثبات الأول والآخر للمدة : محال
Upon this existence, and the other is posterior to this existence, and that by which they share is other than that by which they differ. So this priority and posteriority by which one of the two non-existences is distinguished over the other must necessarily be different from non-existence, which is the common factor between the two non-existences. And there is no meaning of duration and time except the thing by which one of the two similar things is distinguished from the other in the sense of priority and posteriority, and the concept of before and after. Then it is known that one of the two non-existences is described as 'before' and priority, a description that has no beginning, and the second non-existence is described as 'after' and posteriority, a description that has no end. So this necessitates the certainty that duration and time are obtained in an obtaining that has no beginning and no end. Thus it is established that asserting beginning and end for duration entails negating beginning and end from it, and that is impossible. So it is established that asserting a first and a last for duration is impossible.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is a self-contained argument for the pre-eternity (azaliyya) of time and duration (al-muddah wa al-zaman). The author begins by noting that when we have two non-existences (al-'adamayn)—the non-existence before a thing comes to be and the non-existence after it perishes—they share the attribute of being non-existence, but they differ in priority (taqaddum) and posteriority (ta'akhkhur). This distinction cannot be inherent in non-existence itself, since non-existence is a single common notion. Therefore, the distinction must come from something else, which the author identifies as duration or time (al-muddah wa al-zaman). Time is precisely that which allows us to say one non-existence is 'before' and the other 'after'. The author then observes that the first non-existence is described as having no beginning (lā awwala lahu) and the second as having no end (lā ākhira lahu). Consequently, the time that distinguishes them must also have no beginning and no end. If one were to claim that time has a beginning and an end, that would require negating its beginning and end, which is contradictory. Hence, asserting a beginning and end for time is impossible; time must be eternal and everlasting.

Key Terms
عدم (ʿadam)
وجود (wujūd)
مدة (muddah)
زمان (zamān)
تقدم (taqaddum)
تأخر (taʾakhkhur)
قبلية (qabliyyah)
بعدية (baʿdiyyah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-existence itself has priority and posteriority.
Correction: Priority and posteriority are positive attributes that must come from something other than non-existence, namely time.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, why must time have no beginning and no end?
A) Because the two non-existences are identical in every respect.
B) Because the priority of one non-existence has no beginning and the posteriority of the other has no end, so the time distinguishing them must share those features.
C) Because time is an illusion with no real existence.
D) Because the world is eternal and requires an eternal container.

Key Takeaway
Time must be eternal because it is the basis for distinguishing two non-existences that are beginningless and endless.

The Third Argument: Time Must Be Pre-Eternal
Third proof that time is pre-eternal based on distinction of times of existence and non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the concept of origination (ḥudūth) logically requires the pre-eternity of time.

Core Idea
Origination cannot be conceived without a temporal distinction.

Sanusi presents his third argument for the pre-eternity of time, a dialectical proof that forces a crucial choice. The argument shows that if the world were originated, then either the time of non-existence is distinct from the time of existence, or it is not. Only the first option is coherent, and it entails that time exists before the world—hence, time is pre-eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if the world were originated, then...'
2
Identify the two divisions
The argument presents a dichotomy: either the time of non-existence is distinct from the time of existence, or it is not. Find where each division is stated and note the reasoning attached to each.
3
Understand why the second division is false
Ask yourself why the author rejects the idea that there is no distinction. The passage says reason cannot judge something as non-existent at one time and existent at another without temporal specification. Work through that reasoning slowly.
This step connects directly to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : لو كان العالم حادثاً ، لكان إما أن يكون وقت العدم متميزاً عن وقت الوجود، أو لا يحصل هذا الامتياز . فإن كان الأول ، فقد حصل قبل حدوث العالم أوقات مختلفة ، حتى تميز وقت العدم عن وقت الوجود . وهذا يقتضي قدم المدة والزمان • وأما القسم الثاني فباطل لأنا ما لم تخصص العدم بوقت ، والوجود بوقت آخر ، فإنه لا يمكننا أن نعقل الحكم بأنه معدوم تارة ، وموجود أخرى . وكيف لا نقول ذلك وصريح العقل يشهد بأن الحادث إنما حصل وجوده بعد عدمه ؟ وهذا الترتيب لا يحصل إلا إذا كان وقت العدم ، متميزاً عن وقت الوجود .
The third argument: If the world were originated (ḥādith), then either the time of non-existence is distinct from the time of existence, or this distinction does not occur. If the first, then before the occurrence of the world there have occurred different times, such that the time of non-existence is distinct from the time of existence. And this necessitates the pre-eternity of duration and time. As for the second division, it is false, because unless we specify non-existence with a time and existence with another time, we cannot rationally judge that it is non-existent at one time and existent at another. How can we not say that, while sound reason witnesses that the originated thing only came to be after its non-existence? And this order does not occur unless the time of non-existence is distinct from the time of existence.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument (al-ḥujjah al-thālithah) for the pre-eternity of time. The author sets up a conditional: if the world were originated (ḥādith), then either the time of its non-existence is distinct from the time of its existence, or it is not. If it is distinct, then there must have been times before the world's existence to allow that distinction, which implies that time existed before the world—i.e., time is pre-eternal. The second option (no distinction) is false because sound reason requires that when we say something was non-existent and then existent, we must be able to assign different times to these states. Without a temporal distinction, we could not conceive of the transition from non-existence to existence. Therefore, the only coherent possibility is that time is pre-eternal.

Key Terms
حادث (ḥādith)
وقت العدم (waqt al-ʿadam)
وقت الوجود (waqt al-wujūd)
قدم (qidam)
مدة (muddah)
زمان (zamān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The world could come to be without any pre-existing time.
Correction: The passage argues that origination demands a temporal distinction between non-existence and existence, which presupposes time before the world.

Checkpoint
What is the false division in the third argument?
A) That the time of non-existence is distinct from the time of existence.
B) That the world is eternal and not originated.
C) That there is no distinction between the time of non-existence and the time of existence.
D) That time is created with the world.

Key Takeaway
The origination of the world necessarily implies that time is pre-eternal, because the transition from non-existence to existence requires distinct times.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that time itself is pre-eternal—it is not self-originating but a created framework. Do you live your daily moments as if time is your own possession, or do you recognize it as a divine loan from the Eternal? What one change can you make tomorrow to treat time with the reverence it deserves?
?
The rational judgment that you came into being after non-existence requires a real temporal distinction. Have you ever truly thanked the One who appointed the specific time of your existence? How will you this week show gratitude for the time you have been given?

The First Branch: Specified Creation Leads to Infinite Regress
First division of the fourth argument: specified creation leads to infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why specifying a time for the creation of time leads to an infinite regress, and how that regress ultimately forces the conclusion that time is eternal.

Core Idea
If time is created at a specific time, that time itself requires creation — leading to an infinite chain.

Sanusi presents the first branch of his fourth argument. He assumes that if time is originated, God must have intended it. If He intended it at a particular moment, that moment itself becomes a problem. Let's follow the reasoning and see why it collapses into either impossibility or the desired conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice the conditional structure and the two subcases for the regress.
This is a single complex argument; let it sink in.
2
Identify the two cases of intention
The author divides divine intention into two cases: with specification and without. Focus on the first case here.
The first case is examined in this lesson.
3
Understand the regress argument
If God specifies a time for creation, that time would itself need creation, leading to an infinite regress. Analyze the two possibilities for this regress.
The regress is either all-at-once or sequential.
4
See the outcomes
The regress all-at-once is impossible. The sequential regress with no beginning is exactly the claim that time is eternal.
Thus the first case leads to either impossibility or the desired conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إن المدة لو كانت حادثة ، لما حصل حدوثها إلا بإحداث الفاعل . وعلى هذا التقدير، فنقول : إنه تعالى إما أن يقصد إحداث تلك المدة ، بشرط أن يخصص ذلك الإحداث بوقت معين ، أولاً بهذا الشرط . والأول محال . لأن الكلام في هذا الوقت، كالكلام في الأول ويلزم التسلسل ثم ذلك التسلسل إن وقع دفعة واحدة ، فهو محال . وإن وقع على أن يكون كل جزء منه مسبوقاً بغيره، لا إلى أول . فهو المطلوب . وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : إنه تعالى قصد إحداث الوقت والزمان لا بشرط أن يوقع ذلك في زمان معين، بل قصد إحداث الوقت والزمان من غير هذا الشرط فعلى هذا التقدير وجب أن لا يتأخر وقوع الأثر عن ذلك القصد فلما كان القصد إلى تكوين المدة والزمان : أزلياً. وجب كون المدة والزمان : أزلياً . وهو المطلوب .
The fourth argument: If duration were originated, its origination would only occur by the agent's bringing about. On this assumption, we say: He, exalted, either intends to bring about that duration with the condition that He specifies that bringing about at a particular time, or without this condition. The first is impossible, because the discussion about this time is like the discussion about the first, and an infinite regress (tasalsul) follows. Then that regress, if it occurs all at once, is impossible; and if it occurs such that each part is preceded by another without a beginning, then that is the desired conclusion. As for the second division, namely that He, exalted, intended to bring about time and duration without the condition of placing that in a particular time—rather He intended to bring about time and duration without this condition—then on this estimation, it follows that the occurrence of the effect is not delayed from that intention. Since the intention to compose duration and time was eternal (azaliyyan), duration and time must be eternal. And that is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument (al-ḥujjah al-rābiʿah). It considers the hypothesis that time (al-muddah wa al-zamān) is itself originated. If so, it must be brought into existence by an agent (God). The author divides the divine intention into two cases: (1) God intends to create time but specifies a particular time for that creation. This leads to an infinite regress because that specified time would itself require a preceding time, and so on. If the regress occurs all at once (i.e., an infinite series of times created simultaneously), that is impossible. If it occurs sequentially with no beginning (i.e., an infinite regress), then that is exactly the claim that time is eternal (since an infinite series of times implies eternity). So the first case leads to either impossibility or the desired conclusion. (2) God intends to create time without specifying a particular time—i.e., unconditionally. Since God's intention is eternal, and the effect (time) cannot be delayed from an unconditional eternal intention, time must be eternal. Thus, in both cases, the hypothesis that time is originated leads to the conclusion that time is eternal, which is a contradiction, so time cannot be originated; it must be pre-eternal.

Key Terms
مدة (muddah)
حادثة (ḥādithah)
إحداث (iḥdāth)
تسلسل (tasalsul)
قصد (qaṣd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if time is created at a specific time, that time itself is uncreated or exempt from the argument.
Correction: The argument shows that the specified time would itself need to be created, leading to an infinite regress. There is no exemption.

Checkpoint
According to the first branch, what is the consequence if the infinite regress occurs sequentially with no beginning?
A) It is impossible.
B) It shows time is eternal.
C) It proves the regress all at once.
D) It contradicts the hypothesis.

Key Takeaway
Specifying a time for creation leads to an infinite regress, which ultimately forces the conclusion that time is eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every moment you experience is created by God at that very moment, and not a single instant exists independently of His will, how would you live today differently? What would you do right now if you truly believed that?
?
The argument shows that time cannot be created at a specific time without infinite regress. Does your daily life reflect a belief that time is subject to God, or do you act as if time is an independent force that controls you? Be honest.
?
If the infinite regress of times is impossible all at once, then the only alternative is that time is eternal. How does this challenge your personal understanding of God's relationship with time? Will you begin to see your past, present, and future as directly held by Him every moment?

The Second Branch: Unconditional Creation Implies Eternity
Second division of the fourth argument: unconditional intention leads to eternal time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why if God intends to create time unconditionally, the eternality of His intention entails the eternality of time.

Core Idea
An unconditional eternal intention requires an immediate eternal effect.

Sanusi now examines the second branch of the argument. If God intends to create time without specifying any particular moment, then His intention is eternal and unconditional. Since an unconditional intention cannot be delayed, the effect—time—must be eternal. This leads to a direct contradiction of the hypothesis that time is created.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage for the second branch. Note the conditional structure and the key terms.
This is a short but powerful argument.
2
Understand the unconditional intention
God intends to create time without any condition of when. This means the intention is not tied to any specific time.
3
Connect intention to effect
An unconditional intention must be followed immediately by its effect. Since God's intention is eternal, time must be eternal.
This is the core logical step.

Source Text

The fourth argument: If duration were originated, its origination would only occur by the agent's bringing about. On this assumption, we say: He, exalted, either intends to bring about that duration with the condition that He specifies that bringing about at a particular time, or without this condition. The first is impossible, because the discussion about this time is like the discussion about the first, and an infinite regress (tasalsul) follows. The Twelfth Proof: Time as a Necessary Framework
Sanusi presents the twelfth proof that time cannot be originated because origination presupposes time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the twelfth proof for the beginninglessness of time: that conceiving of temporal origination logically presupposes the existence of time, making it impossible for time itself to be originated.

Core Idea
Time is the framework that makes origination conceivable; it cannot itself be originated.

Sanusi concludes his series of twelve proofs with a powerful conceptual argument. He shows that the very idea of something 'happening' or 'being eternal' requires a temporal framework. This proof demonstrates that time must be a necessary precondition for talking about origination, and therefore time itself cannot be a temporally originated entity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to get the flow of the proof.
Notice Sanusi's emphasis on 'conceiving' – this is a conceptual argument.
2
Identify the key concepts: huduth and dawam
Sanusi defines how we conceive of temporal origination and perpetuity. Both require the assumption of time (waqt/zaman). Trace this reasoning.
3
Follow the reductio ad absurdum
Sanusi argues that if time were originated, we would need to assume a prior time to conceive its origination – a contradiction. Understand why this makes time's non-existence impossible.
This is the heart of the proof.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The proof establishes that time must be beginningless. Consider why this conclusion is necessary for the broader theological argument.
This is the twelfth proof supporting the eternity of time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية عشر: إنا لا نعقل حدوث شيء، ولا دوام شيء، إلا مع فرض الوقت والزمان. فإنا البتة لا نعقل من الحادث إلا أنه الذي حدث في وقت بعد أن كان معدوماً في الوقت السابق عليه. ولا نعقل البتة من الدائم القديم إلا أنه الذي يفرض وجوده حاصلاً في وقت إلا وقد كان موجوداً قبله. فإذا كنا لا نعقل معنى الحدوث ومعنى البقاء إلا مع فرض الأوقات، إما بهذه العبارة المذكورة أو بعبارات أخرى تفيد أيضاً معاني الأوقات، فحينئذ ظهر أنه لا يمكننا أن نعقل معنى الحدوث ومعنى الدوام إلا مع تقدير وجود الأوقات. وإذا ثبت هذا، ظهر أن القول بحدوث المدة والوقت محال. إلا أنا إذا حكمنا بحدوثهما، وثبت أن الحدوث لا يمكن تعقله إلا مع فرض الوقت، لزم أنا متى فرضنا عدم الوقت، فإنه يلزم من فرض عدمه فرض وجوده. وذلك يقتضي أن يكون فرض عدمه محالاً. فهذه الوجوه الاثني عشر في هذا الباب مذكورة لإثبات هذا المطلوب. ومن أراد الزيادة عليها فلينظر فيما كتبناه في تحقيق الكلام في المدة والزمان. والله أعلم بالصواب.
The twelfth proof: We do not conceive the temporal origination (huduth) of anything, nor the perpetuity (dawam) of anything, except with the assumption of time (al-waqt) and duration (al-zaman). For we absolutely do not conceive of the originated thing except that it occurred at a time after having been non-existent in the time preceding it. And we absolutely do not conceive of the eternal perpetual thing except that its existence is assumed to obtain at a time, and it had existed before it. Since we do not conceive the meaning of origination and the meaning of perpetuity except with the assumption of times—either by this stated expression or by other expressions that also convey the meanings of times—then it becomes clear that we cannot conceive the meaning of origination and the meaning of perpetuity except with the supposition of the existence of times. If this is established, it becomes clear that the statement that duration and time are originated is impossible. Except that if we judge that they are originated, and it is established that origination cannot be conceived except with the assumption of time, then it follows that whenever we assume the non-existence of time, the assumption of its non-existence necessitates the assumption of its existence. And that necessitates that assuming its non-existence is impossible. So these twelve proofs in this chapter are mentioned to prove this desired conclusion. And whoever desires additional proofs, let him look at what we have written in the verification of the discussion on duration and time. And God knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage presents the twelfth and final proof for the eternity of time. The proof is conceptual: it argues that all human understanding of temporal origination (huduth) and perpetuity (dawam) necessarily presupposes the existence of time (waqt/zaman). Every originated thing is conceived as occurring at some time after being non-existent at an earlier time; every eternal thing is conceived as existing at all times. Since we cannot think of origination and perpetuity without times, time must be a necessary framework for these concepts. If we then claim that time itself is originated, we run into a contradiction: to conceive of time's origination, we would need to assume a time before time, leading to an infinite regress. Therefore, assuming time's non-existence forces us to assume its existence, making its non-existence impossible. The passage concludes by noting that these twelve proofs together establish the desired conclusion, and refers readers to a fuller treatment elsewhere. The proof is a classic kalam argument for the necessity of a beginningless duration.

Key Terms
حدوث
دوام
وقت
زمان
المدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument is circular: using the concept of time to prove time's eternity.
Correction: It is a transcendental argument: it shows that time is a necessary condition for the very concept of origination, so time cannot be another originated thing without contradiction.

Checkpoint
Why does the twelfth proof conclude that time cannot be originated?
A) Because if time were originated, it would need to be conceived in a prior time.
B) Because the Quran explicitly says time is eternal.
C) Because originated things cannot be conceived at all.
D) Because time is identical to God's essence.

Key Takeaway
The concept of temporal origination necessarily requires a temporal framework, so time cannot itself be a temporary entity; it must be beginningless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If time is beginningless and necessary, then every moment of your life is situated within a framework that did not begin with you. How does that change the way you view the urgency of your repentance and good deeds?
?
This proof shows that change itself presupposes an unchanging temporal backdrop. Your life is full of changes—birth, growth, death. In light of this, are you living as if your moments are just random, or are you aware that they are grounded in a necessary order from Allah?
?
If time cannot be eliminated even in thought, then your existence is never independent of that framework. How would your daily choices shift if you truly internalized that you are always 'in time' before the Eternal?

The Reality of Dimensions and the Opponent's Claim
Author establishes reality of space and dimensions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's argument that dimensions are real and do not admit non-existence, and why the opponent uses this to claim bodies are necessary.

Core Idea
Dimensions are real and permanent, but that does not make bodies necessary.

The author begins by establishing that space (ḥayyiz) is an independently existing reality, not dependent on our assumptions. He then argues that dimensions—length, breadth, depth—are similarly real and cannot cease to exist. The opponent seizes on this to argue that if dimensions are eternal, then bodies composed of them must be necessary. But the author is about to show that this reasoning is flawed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on space and dimensions
Read the Arabic passage once to get the gist. Identify the claim that space is a real entity and that dimensions are permanent.
Focus on the phrases 'حيز أمر موجود' and 'لا تقبل العدم'.
2
Understand the opponent's conclusion
Notice that after establishing the reality of dimensions, the opponent concludes that bodies are necessary. This is the claim to be refuted.
The sentence 'لزوم القول بكون الأجسام واجبة الوجود لذواتها' is the opponent's statement.
3
Prepare for the refutation
The author does not accept this conclusion. In the next lesson, he will examine the nature of dimension to show its contingency.
This lesson sets the stage for the motion argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن السؤال الثاني : إن الجسم إذا كان حاصلا في الحيز ، قبل فرض الفارضين ، واعتبار المعتبرين ، وبعد ذلك . وجب الحكم بأن الحيز أمر موجود في نفسه ، وأنه سواء وجد الفرض أو لم يوجد ، فهو حاصل في نفس الأمر . وتمام الكلام في هذا المعنى سيأتي في مسألة الخلاء . فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن هذه الأبعاد الممتدة طولاً وعرضاً ، وعمقاً : أمور لا تقبل العدم ، ولا تبدل وجودها بالعدم . وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : لزوم القول بكون الأجسام واجبة الوجود لذواتها . لأن تلك الأبعاد لما كانت أموراً موجودة في أنفسها ، متحققة في ذواتها ، فهي إما أن تكون قابلة للحركة أو لا تكون قابلة للحركة ، والثاني باطل . فتعين الأول. وإنما قلنا : إن الثاني باطل لأن طبيعة البعد ، قابلة للحركة إذ لو لم تكن قابلة للحركة لما كان الجسم قابلاً للحركة . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إما أن تبقى ذاته في قول الحركة ، أو لا بد من شيء آخر ، فإن كان الأول فهو قابل للحركة على الإطلاق ، فكل بعد فهو قابل للحركة . وإن كان الثاني ، فذلك الشيء إما أن يكون حالا في البعد أو محلاً له . أو لا حالا فيه ولا محلاً له . فإن كان الأول ، فنقول : اتصاف البعد بذلك الحال ممكن ، وبتقدير حصول هذا المعنى يكون قابلا للحركة ، والموقوف على الممكن ممكن ؛ فهذا البعد قابل للحركة . وإن كان الثاني ، فهذا يقتضي كون البعد حالا في محل ، وذلك محال . لأن ذلك المحل، إما أن يكون مختصاً بالحيّز والجهة ، وإما أن لا يكون . فإن كان الأول ، فهو أيضاً بعد ، فيكون محل البعد : بعداً . وهو محال ، وإن كان الثاني فهو باطل من وجهين : الأول: إن حلول ما يكون مختصاً بالحيز والجهة، فيما لا اختصاص له بالحيز والجهة : محال
And the answer to the second question: When a body is located in space (al-ḥayyiz), before any assumption by assumers or consideration by considerers, and after that, it must be judged that space is a thing existing in itself, and that whether assumption exists or not, it is realized in actual fact. The complete discussion of this meaning will come later in the issue of void (al-khalā'). So it is established by what we have mentioned that these extended dimensions (al-ab‘ād) in length, breadth, and depth are things that do not admit non-existence, and do not exchange their existence for non-existence. And when this is established, we say: This necessitates the claim that bodies are necessary of existence (wājibat al-wujūd) in themselves. Because those dimensions, since they are things existing in themselves, realized in their essences, are either capable of motion or not capable of motion, and the second is false. So the first is determined. And we only said that the second is false because the nature of dimension (ṭabī‘at al-bu‘d) is capable of motion; for if it were not capable of motion, then the body would not be capable of motion. And when this is established, we say: Either its essence persists in the state of motion, or there must be something else. If the first, then it is capable of motion absolutely, so every dimension is capable of motion. And if the second, then that thing is either a quality (ḥāl) in the dimension or a locus (maḥall) for it, or neither a quality nor a locus. If the first, we say: The description of the dimension by that quality is possible (mumkin), and on the assumption that this meaning occurs, it becomes capable of motion, and that which depends on the possible is possible; so this dimension is capable of motion. And if the second, this necessitates that the dimension is a quality in a locus, and that is impossible. Because that locus either is specific to space and direction, or not. If the first, then it too is a dimension, so the locus of the dimension is a dimension, which is impossible. And if the second, it is false from two aspects: The first: That the indwelling of that which is specific to space and direction in that which has no specificity to space and direction is impossible.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is an answer to a second objection, likely from an opponent who argues that if dimensions are eternal and real, then bodies would be necessary. The author begins by asserting that space (al-ḥayyiz) is a real entity independent of our assumptions. He then establishes that dimensions (length, breadth, depth) are real and do not admit non-existence. The opponent's claim is that this forces bodies to be necessary. The author then examines whether dimensions are capable of motion. He argues that the nature of dimension is capable of motion; otherwise bodies could not move. He then considers two possibilities: either dimension itself is capable of motion, or it requires an external factor. If it requires an external factor, that factor must be a quality in the dimension or a locus for the dimension. If it is a quality, the dimension can become capable of motion. If it is a locus, then the dimension would be a quality in some other thing, which leads to an impossibility: either the locus is also a dimension (infinite regress) or it is not spatial, which is impossible because a spatial thing cannot inhere in a non-spatial thing. The conclusion, though incomplete in this passage, is that dimensions are capable of motion, implying they are contingent, not necessary, thus refuting the opponent's claim that bodies are necessary.

Key Terms
حيز
بعد
عدم
وجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does the opponent conclude from the reality of dimensions?
A) Bodies are contingent.
B) Bodies are necessary.
C) Space is non-existent.
D) Dimensions are indivisible.

Key Takeaway
Dimensions are real and permanent, but this does not automatically make bodies necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the space around you, do you take it for granted? This lesson shows that space is real independent of your awareness. How does this reality of the physical world point to a Creator who sustains it? What does it mean for your daily life that the dimensions you move through are not illusions but real signs of Allah's power?
?
The opponent claims that if dimensions are permanent, then bodies must be necessary in themselves. But the author will show that dimensions are contingent. Reflect: Are there areas in your life where you mistakenly believe something is permanent or necessary (like your habits, status, or possessions) when in reality they are contingent and subject to change? How does this lesson challenge you to see everything as contingent upon Allah?
?
The author states that space exists whether any person assumes it or not. This objectivity of creation should lead to humility. Does your relationship with the physical world reflect that it is a sign from Allah, or do you treat it as self-sufficient? What one change can you make today to see the world around you as a dependent creation?

8 min
The Contingency of Dimensions through Motion
Author argues dimensions are capable of motion, hence contingent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author proves that dimensions are capable of motion, thereby showing they are contingent and not necessary, refuting the opponent's claim that bodies are necessary.

Core Idea
The capability of motion in dimensions proves their contingency.

Having established that dimensions are real and permanent, the author now confronts the opponent's claim that bodies are necessary. He does so by examining the nature of dimension and showing that it is capable of motion. Through a careful analysis of possibilities—whether motion is intrinsic or requires an external factor—the author demonstrates that dimensions are contingent, not necessary. This refutes the argument that bodies must be necessary.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
The author starts with the established premise that dimensions are real and existing in themselves.
This builds on the previous lesson.
2
Analyze the dichotomy
Dimensions are either capable of motion or not. The author argues that not capable is false because the nature of dimension allows motion.
Consider the implication: if dimensions were not capable of motion, bodies could not move.
3
Examine intrinsic vs. extrinsic motion
If dimensions are capable of motion, either motion is intrinsic (the dimension itself moves) or requires an external factor.
The author explores both sub-options.
4
Evaluate the external factor cases
If an external factor is needed, it must be either a quality (ḥāl) in the dimension or a locus (maḥall) for it. The author shows that the quality case is possible, but the locus case leads to impossibility (infinite regress or non-spatial inherence).
This subtle argument eliminates the need for an external locus.
5
Conclude contingency
Since dimensions are capable of motion (either intrinsically or via a possible quality), they are contingent, not necessary. Thus bodies are also contingent.
The opponent's claim is refuted.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن السؤال الثاني : إن الجسم إذا كان حاصلا في الحيز ، قبل فرض الفارضين ، واعتبار المعتبرين ، وبعد ذلك . وجب الحكم بأن الحيز أمر موجود في نفسه ، وأنه سواء وجد الفرض أو لم يوجد ، فهو حاصل في نفس الأمر . وتمام الكلام في هذا المعنى سيأتي في مسألة الخلاء . فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن هذه الأبعاد الممتدة طولاً وعرضاً ، وعمقاً : أمور لا تقبل العدم ، ولا تبدل وجودها بالعدم . وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : لزوم القول بكون الأجسام واجبة الوجود لذواتها . لأن تلك الأبعاد لما كانت أموراً موجودة في أنفسها ، متحققة في ذواتها ، فهي إما أن تكون قابلة للحركة أو لا تكون قابلة للحركة ، والثاني باطل . فتعين الأول. وإنما قلنا : إن الثاني باطل لأن طبيعة البعد ، قابلة للحركة إذ لو لم تكن قابلة للحركة لما كان الجسم قابلاً للحركة . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إما أن تبقى ذاته في قول الحركة ، أو لا بد من شيء آخر ، فإن كان الأول فهو قابل للحركة على الإطلاق ، فكل بعد فهو قابل للحركة . وإن كان الثاني ، فذلك الشيء إما أن يكون حالا في البعد أو محلاً له . أو لا حالا فيه ولا محلاً له . فإن كان الأول ، فنقول : اتصاف البعد بذلك الحال ممكن ، وبتقدير حصول هذا المعنى يكون قابلا للحركة ، والموقوف على الممكن ممكن ؛ فهذا البعد قابل للحركة . وإن كان الثاني ، فهذا يقتضي كون البعد حالا في محل ، وذلك محال . لأن ذلك المحل، إما أن يكون مختصاً بالحيّز والجهة ، وإما أن لا يكون . فإن كان الأول ، فهو أيضاً بعد ، فيكون محل البعد : بعداً . وهو محال ، وإن كان الثاني فهو باطل من وجهين : الأول: إن حلول ما يكون مختصاً بالحيز والجهة، فيما لا اختصاص له بالحيز والجهة : محال
And the answer to the second question: When a body is located in space (al-ḥayyiz), before any assumption by assumers or consideration by considerers, and after that, it must be judged that space is a thing existing in itself, and that whether assumption exists or not, it is realized in actual fact. The complete discussion of this meaning will come later in the issue of void (al-khalā'). So it is established by what we have mentioned that these extended dimensions (al-ab‘ād) in length, breadth, and depth are things that do not admit non-existence, and do not exchange their existence for non-existence. And when this is established, we say: This necessitates the claim that bodies are necessary of existence (wājibat al-wujūd) in themselves. Because those dimensions, since they are things existing in themselves, realized in their essences, are either capable of motion or not capable of motion, and the second is false. So the first is determined. And we only said that the second is false because the nature of dimension (ṭabī‘at al-bu‘d) is capable of motion; for if it were not capable of motion, then the body would not be capable of motion. And when this is established, we say: Either its essence persists in the state of motion, or there must be something else. If the first, then it is capable of motion absolutely, so every dimension is capable of motion. And if the second, then that thing is either a quality (ḥāl) in the dimension or a locus (maḥall) for it, or neither a quality nor a locus. If the first, we say: The description of the dimension by that quality is possible (mumkin), and on the assumption that this meaning occurs, it becomes capable of motion, and that which depends on the possible is possible; so this dimension is capable of motion. And if the second, this necessitates that the dimension is a quality in a locus, and that is impossible. Because that locus either is specific to space and direction, or not. If the first, then it too is a dimension, so the locus of the dimension is a dimension, which is impossible. And if the second, it is false from two aspects: The first: That the indwelling of that which is specific to space and direction in that which has no specificity to space and direction is impossible.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is an answer to a second objection, likely from an opponent who argues that if dimensions are eternal and real, then bodies would be necessary. The author begins by asserting that space (al-ḥayyiz) is a real entity independent of our assumptions. He then establishes that dimensions (length, breadth, depth) are real and do not admit non-existence. The opponent's claim is that this forces bodies to be necessary. The author then examines whether dimensions are capable of motion. He argues that the nature of dimension is capable of motion; otherwise bodies could not move. He then considers two possibilities: either dimension itself is capable of motion, or it requires an external factor. If it requires an external factor, that factor must be a quality in the dimension or a locus for the dimension. If it is a quality, the dimension can become capable of motion. If it is a locus, then the dimension would be a quality in some other thing, which leads to an impossibility: either the locus is also a dimension (infinite regress) or it is not spatial, which is impossible because a spatial thing cannot inhere in a non-spatial thing. The conclusion, though incomplete in this passage, is that dimensions are capable of motion, implying they are contingent, not necessary, thus refuting the opponent's claim that bodies are necessary.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If dimensions are eternal and real, then bodies must be necessary.
Correction: The author shows that dimensions are capable of motion, which is a property of contingent beings, so dimensions (and thus bodies) are contingent.

Key Takeaway
Dimensions are capable of motion, proving they are contingent, not necessary, which refutes the opponent's claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If dimensions—the very fabric of your physical existence—are contingent and in constant potential for motion, what does that say about your own claim to independence? Are you not, like them, utterly dependent on the One who sustains you at every moment? How will you remind yourself today of this dependency?
?
The argument shows that motion cannot be eliminated from the nature of dimensions. What 'motions' in your heart—your desires, fears, ambitions—reveal that your soul is not necessary but contingent, constantly in need of its Creator? Will you let this awareness drive you to seek Him?
?
You rely on the stability of the physical world—your body, your home, the earth beneath you. But this lesson proves that even the dimensions that underlie that stability are capable of change and non-existence. What would it mean for your trust in Allah if you truly internalized that nothing in creation is necessary? Are you prepared to place your absolute trust only in the Necessary Being?

The Initial Claim: Dimensions as Necessary Existents
Sanusi presents the starting premise of the sophism: dimensions are necessary existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a sophistical argument begins by asserting that dimensions are necessary existents and then uses this to claim that bodies must also be necessary.

Core Idea
Dimensions are claimed to be necessary and eternal, setting the foundation for the eternity of bodies.

This lesson opens the examination of a sophisticated argument for the eternity of bodies. The argument starts with a bold premise: dimensions—length, width, depth—cannot cease to exist. From this, it infers that bodies are necessary existents. We will read the opening passage and see how the initial premises are set up.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once. Notice the claim that dimensions do not accept non-existence.
2
Trace the logical move
Identify how the author moves from the necessity of dimensions to the necessity of bodies.
The key is 'If they are capable of motion, then they are bodies; if not, the second is false.'
3
Reflect on the controversy
Consider why this claim would be controversial in Islamic theology. Dimensions are usually considered accidents, not necessary.
This is a sophism, not the author's own view.

يمتنع أن يكون شرطاً لقبول الحركة . وإن كان الثالث ، وهو أن يكون شرط كون البعد قابلا للحركة ، شيء لا يكون حالا فيه ولا محلاً له . فهذا أيضاً باطل ، لأن ذلك الشيء يجب أن لا يكون جسماً ولا جسمانياً ، والثاني : أنه لا أول لوجود الأجسام ، وإلا فقبل ذلك الأول ، لابد وأن تتميز الجوانب بعضها عن البعض . وإذا حصل هذا الامتياز ، كانت الأبعاد موجودة وإذا كانت الأبعاد موجودة، فقد وجد الجسم قبل وجود الجسم . هذا خلف . فثبت : أن هذا الكلام يدل على أن الأجسام الموجودة في هذا الوقت : غير متناهية . وثبت أيضاً : أنه لا أول لوجودها ، ولا آخر لوجودها . فهذا هو الشبهة المذكورة في قدم الأجسام المستنبطة من البحث عن ماهية المكان والله أعلم .
Existent in itself, and whether the assumption exists or not, it is realized in the very thing. And the complete discussion on this meaning will come later in the issue of void. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that these dimensions extended in length, width, and depth are things that do not accept non-existence, nor do they exchange their existence for non-existence. And when this is established, we say: the necessity of saying that bodies are necessary of existence through themselves. Because those dimensions, since they were existent things in themselves, verified in their essences, they are either capable of motion or not capable of motion. And the second is false. So the first is determined. And we only said: that the second is false because the nature of dimension is capable of motion, for if it were not capable of motion, then body would not be capable of motion. And when this is established we say: either its essence remains in the state of motion, or there must be something else. If it is the first, then it is capable of motion absolutely, so every dimension is capable of motion. And if it is the second, then that thing is either inherent in the dimension or a locus for it, or neither inherent in it nor a locus for it. If it is the first, we say: the description of the dimension by that state is possible, and with the supposition of the occurrence of this meaning, it becomes capable of motion, and that which depends on the possible is possible; so this dimension is capable of motion. And if it is the second, this necessitates that the dimension is inherent in a locus, and that is impossible. Because that locus is either specific to space and direction, or not. If it is the first, it is also a dimension, so the locus of the dimension would be a dimension, and that is impossible. And if it is the second, it is false in two respects: First: The inherence of what is specific to space and direction in that which has no specialization to space and direction is impossible. Second: That which is not specific to space and direction, motion upon it is impossible. And what prevents the acceptance of motion is impossible to be a condition for accepting motion. And if it is the third, which is that the condition for the dimension's capability of motion is something that is neither inherent in it nor a locus for it. This is also false, because that thing must not be a body nor corporeal, and then the first investigation returns to it. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that the dimension is necessary of existence through itself, and when the matter is like that, that dimension is capable of motion, and every dimension capable of motion is a body. It results that the body is necessary of existence through itself. And this yields two things: One: that there is no limit to bodies, because if they had a limit, it would be necessary that in the external world there occur a differentiation of one side from the other, so the dimension would be existent. So that which we assumed as a limit for bodies would not be a limit for them. This is a contradiction. And the second: that there is no beginning for the existence of bodies, otherwise before that beginning, the sides must be distinguished from each other. And when this differentiation occurs, the dimensions are existent, and when the dimensions are existent, then body has existed before the existence of body. This is a contradiction. So it is established that this discourse indicates that the bodies existing at this time are infinite. And it is also established that there is no beginning for their existence, nor an end for their existence. So this is the sophism mentioned concerning the eternity of bodies, derived from the investigation of the essence of place. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage presents a sophistical argument (shubha) for the eternity of bodies, likely to be refuted later. The argument proceeds in several steps: it begins by asserting that dimensions (ab‘ād) are necessary existent because they cannot admit non-existence. Then it claims that since dimensions are necessary, bodies must also be necessary. The core reasoning is that dimensions are either capable of motion or not; the latter is impossible because bodies move, so dimensions must be capable of motion. It then analyzes what makes a dimension capable of motion: either its own essence suffices, or something else conditions it. If the essence suffices, all dimensions are capable of motion. If something else conditions it, that condition must be either inherent in the dimension, a locus for it, or neither. Each possibility is examined and rejected except the first (inherence), which is declared possible. The argument concludes that dimensions are necessary existent, capable of motion, and therefore bodies are necessary existent. Two corollaries follow: (1) bodies are infinite in extent, because if they had a limit, a dimension would exist beyond the limit to differentiate sides, making the limit not actually a limit; (2) bodies have no beginning, because before a first body, dimensions would have to exist to differentiate sides, implying a body before the first. The passage ends by identifying this as the sophism on the eternity of bodies derived from the investigation of the essence of place.

The Core Proof: Dimensions are Capable of Motion
Sanusi provides a detailed analysis of the conditions for motion, concluding that dimensions are inherently capable of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed reasoning that dimensions must be capable of motion, and the analysis of what makes them capable.

Core Idea
The argument's core is that motion requires dimensions to be inherently capable of it.

In this lesson, we dive into the heart of the sophism: the proof that dimensions are capable of motion. The author explores various possibilities for what conditions motion, systematically eliminating alternatives to conclude that dimensions themselves must be capable. This leads to the conclusion that bodies are necessary existents.

Guided Path
1
Follow the dichotomies
The argument presents a series of dichotomies (either this or that). Track each one and see which branch is accepted.
The first dichotomy: either the essence remains in motion or something else is needed.
2
Examine the elimination process
Notice how each alternative (except the first) is shown to be impossible or leads to absurdity.
3
Understand the conclusion
After eliminating alternatives, the argument concludes that dimensions are necessary and capable of motion, hence bodies are necessary.
The conclusion is that every dimension capable of motion is a body, so bodies are necessary.

وحينئذ يعود البحث الأول فيه . فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن البعد واجب الوجود لذاته، ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ، كان ذلك البعد قابلاً للحركة وكل بعد قابل للحركة فهو جسم . ينتج أن الجسم واجب الوجود لذاته . وهذا يفيد أمرين : أحدهما : أنه لا نهاية للأجسام لأنه لو حصلت لها نهاية ، لوجب أن يحصل في الخارج امتياز أحد الجانبين عن الآخر فيكون البعد موجوداً . فذلك الذي فرضناه نهاية للأجسام . لم يكن نهاية لها . هذا خلف . والثاني : أنه لا أول لوجود الأجسام ، وإلا فقبل ذلك الأول ، لابد وأن تتميز الجوانب بعضها عن البعض . وإذا حصل هذا الامتياز ، كانت الأبعاد موجودة وإذا كانت الأبعاد موجودة، فقد وجد الجسم قبل وجود الجسم . هذا خلف . فثبت : أن هذا الكلام يدل على أن الأجسام الموجودة في هذا الوقت : غير متناهية . وثبت أيضاً : أنه لا أول لوجودها ، ولا آخر لوجودها . فهذا هو الشبهة المذكورة في قدم الأجسام المستنبطة من البحث عن ماهية المكان والله أعلم .
Existent in itself, and whether the assumption exists or not, it is realized in the very thing. And the complete discussion on this meaning will come later in the issue of void. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that these dimensions extended in length, width, and depth are things that do not accept non-existence, nor do they exchange their existence for non-existence. And when this is established, we say: the necessity of saying that bodies are necessary of existence through themselves. Because those dimensions, since they were existent things in themselves, verified in their essences, they are either capable of motion or not capable of motion. And the second is false. So the first is determined. And we only said: that the second is false because the nature of dimension is capable of motion, for if it were not capable of motion, then body would not be capable of motion. And when this is established we say: either its essence remains in the state of motion, or there must be something else. If it is the first, then it is capable of motion absolutely, so every dimension is capable of motion. And if it is the second, then that thing is either inherent in the dimension or a locus for it, or neither inherent in it nor a locus for it. If it is the first, we say: the description of the dimension by that state is possible, and with the supposition of the occurrence of this meaning, it becomes capable of motion, and that which depends on the possible is possible; so this dimension is capable of motion. And if it is the second, this necessitates that the dimension is inherent in a locus, and that is impossible. Because that locus is either specific to space and direction, or not. If it is the first, it is also a dimension, so the locus of the dimension would be a dimension, and that is impossible. And if it is the second, it is false in two respects: First: The inherence of what is specific to space and direction in that which has no specialization to space and direction is impossible. Second: That which is not specific to space and direction, motion upon it is impossible. And what prevents the acceptance of motion is impossible to be a condition for accepting motion. And if it is the third, which is that the condition for the dimension's capability of motion is something that is neither inherent in it nor a locus for it. This is also false, because that thing must not be a body nor corporeal, and then the first investigation returns to it. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that the dimension is necessary of existence through itself, and when the matter is like that, that dimension is capable of motion, and every dimension capable of motion is a body. It results that the body is necessary of existence through itself. And this yields two things: One: that there is no limit to bodies, because if they had a limit, it would be necessary that in the external world there occur a differentiation of one side from the other, so the dimension would be existent. So that which we assumed as a limit for bodies would not be a limit for them. This is a contradiction. And the second: that there is no beginning for the existence of bodies, otherwise before that beginning, the sides must be distinguished from each other. And when this differentiation occurs, the dimensions are existent, and when the dimensions are existent, then body has existed before the existence of body. This is a contradiction. So it is established that this discourse indicates that the bodies existing at this time are infinite. And it is also established that there is no beginning for their existence, nor an end for their existence. So this is the sophism mentioned concerning the eternity of bodies, derived from the investigation of the essence of place. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage presents a sophistical argument (shubha) for the eternity of bodies, likely to be refuted later. The argument proceeds in several steps: it begins by asserting that dimensions (ab‘ād) are necessary existent because they cannot admit non-existence. Then it claims that since dimensions are necessary, bodies must also be necessary. The core reasoning is that dimensions are either capable of motion or not; the latter is impossible because bodies move, so dimensions must be capable of motion. It then analyzes what makes a dimension capable of motion: either its own essence suffices, or something else conditions it. If the essence suffices, all dimensions are capable of motion. If something else conditions it, that condition must be either inherent in the dimension, a locus for it, or neither. Each possibility is examined and rejected except the first (inherence), which is declared possible. The argument concludes that dimensions are necessary existent, capable of motion, and therefore bodies are necessary existent. Two corollaries follow: (1) bodies are infinite in extent, because if they had a limit, a dimension would exist beyond the limit to differentiate sides, making the limit not actually a limit; (2) bodies have no beginning, because before a first body, dimensions would have to exist to differentiate sides, implying a body before the first. The passage ends by identifying this as the sophism on the eternity of bodies derived from the investigation of the essence of place.

Checkpoint
What does the argument conclude about dimensions after analyzing the conditions for motion?
A) Dimensions are contingent and require an external cause for motion.
B) Dimensions are necessary existents and capable of motion.
C) Dimensions are immaterial and cannot move.
D) Dimensions are only conceivable, not real.

Key Takeaway
Through a series of dichotomies, the argument concludes that dimensions are necessary existents and inherently capable of motion, leading to the necessity of bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If this argument were sound, it would mean that bodies are eternal and independent of God. Do you feel a discomfort in your soul as you read it? Where does that discomfort come from—your rational understanding or your faith in the Qur'an? What would you hold onto if you could not find a flaw in the reasoning?
?
The proof reduces everything to logical dichotomies. When you think about your own existence, do you reduce it to categories, or do you feel your utter dependence on Allah with certainty beyond logic? Is your trust in Allah merely intellectual, or does it survive even when your mind is challenged?
?
Imagine you truly believed this argument—that the physical world is necessary and not created. What would change about your daily life, your prayer, your hope in Allah's mercy? Are there areas in your life where you functionally act as if the world is self-sufficient, as if you don't need Allah every moment?

The Corollaries: Infinity and Eternity of Bodies
Sanusi presents the two corollaries of the argument and identifies the whole as a sophism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two corollaries drawn from the argument—that bodies are infinite in extent and eternal in time—and that the entire argument is presented as a sophism to be refuted.

Core Idea
The sophism concludes that bodies are infinite in space and eternal, then identifies itself as a shubha.

The argument reaches its climax by deducing that bodies have no limit, no beginning, and no end. It then explicitly labels itself as a sophism (shubha) derived from the investigation of place. This lesson covers these final claims and the important note that it is not the author's own position.

Guided Path
1
Read the corollaries
Identify the two corollaries: (1) bodies are infinite in extent, (2) bodies have no beginning or end.
2
Understand the reasoning
Each corollary is supported by a similar reasoning: if there were a limit or beginning, dimensions would exist beyond it, leading to a contradiction.
3
Note the sophism label
The author explicitly calls this a 'shubha' (sophism) derived from the investigation of place, indicating it is not a valid proof.
This is crucial for understanding the author's position.

موجود في نفسه ، وأنه سواء وجد الفرض أو لم يوجد ، فهو حاصل في نفس الأمر . وتمام الكلام في هذا المعنى سيأتي في مسألة الخلاء . فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن هذه الأبعاد الممتدة طولاً وعرضاً ، وعمقاً : أمور لا تقبل العدم ، ولا تبدل وجودها بالعدم . وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : لزوم القول بكون الأجسام واجبة الوجود لذواتها . لأن تلك الأبعاد لما كانت أموراً موجودة في أنفسها ، متحققة في ذواتها ، فهي إما أن تكون قابلة Causality and Created Things
intermediate
