Establishing that Dimension is Necessary of Existence
Razi eliminates alternatives to prove dimension is necessary of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophical argument eliminates alternatives to prove that dimension (buʿd) is necessary of existence and thus body is also necessary.

Core Idea
The argument claims dimension must be necessary because nothing else explains its receptivity to motion.

This passage presents the core of a philosophical doubt used to argue for the pre-eternity of bodies. The argument begins by considering what allows a dimension to be receptive to motion. Through a process of elimination, it concludes that dimension itself must be necessary of existence. Pay close attention to the logical steps as they set the stage for the radical conclusions that follow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three alternatives
The passage considers three possibilities for what makes a dimension receptive to motion. Read carefully to find the first two and how they are rejected.
The third alternative is also rejected, leading to the conclusion.
2
Understand the rejection of the first alternative
The first alternative is that the dimension is specific to place and direction. The argument says that motion is impossible for such a thing, so it cannot be a condition for motion. Make sure you follow this reasoning.
3
Rejection of the second and third alternatives
The second alternative is that something prevents motion; the argument says such a thing cannot be a condition for motion. The third alternative is a condition that is neither a state nor a locus; this leads to an infinite regress. See how these rejections force the conclusion.
The conclusion is that dimension must be necessary of existence.
4
Grasp the final conclusion
After eliminating all alternatives, the argument concludes that dimension is necessary of existence, receptive to motion, and thus body is necessary.
This sets up the implications that follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : إن ما لا يكون مختصاً بالحيز والجهة ، كانت الحركة عليه ممتنعة . وما كان مانعاً من قبول الحركة ، يمتنع أن يكون شرطاً لقبول الحركة . وإن كان الثالث ، وهو أن يكون شرط كون البعد قابلا للحركة ، شيء لا يكون حالاً فيه ولا محلاً له . فهذا أيضاً باطل ، لأن ذلك الشيء يجب أن لا يكون جسماً ولا جسمانياً ، وحينئذ يعود البحث الأول فيه . فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن البعد واجب الوجود لذاته، ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ، كان ذلك البعد قابلاً للحركة وكل بعد قابل للحركة فهو جسم . ينتج أن الجسم واجب الوجود لذاته . وهذا يفيد أمرين : أحدهما : أنه لا نهاية للأجسام لأنه لو حصلت لها نهاية ، لوجب أن يحصل في الخارج امتياز أحد الجانبين عن الآخر فيكون البعد موجوداً . فذلك الذي فرضناه نهاية للأجسام لم يكن نهاية لها . هذا خلف . والثاني : أنه لا أول لوجود الأجسام ، وإلا فقبل ذلك الأول ، لابد وأن تتميز الجوانب بعضها عن البعض . وإذا حصل هذا الامتياز ، كانت الأبعاد موجودة وإذا كانت الأبعاد موجودة، فقد وجد الجسم قبل وجود الجسم . هذا خلف . فثبت : أن هذا الكلام يدل على أن الأجسام الموجودة في هذا الوقت : غير متناهية . وثبت أيضاً : أنه لا أول لوجودها ، ولا آخر لوجودها . فهذا هو الشبهة المذكورة في قدم الأجسام المستنبطة من البحث عن ماهية المكان .
The second [point]: That which is not specific to place (ḥayyiz) and direction (jiha), motion upon it is impossible. And that which prevents the acceptance of motion, it is impossible for it to be a condition for the acceptance of motion. And if it is the third, that the condition for the dimension being receptive to motion is something that is neither a state (ḥāl) in it nor a locus (maḥall) for it. This too is false, because that thing must not be a body (jism) nor a bodily thing (jismānī), and then the first investigation returns upon it. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that the dimension (buʿd) is necessary of existence (wājib al-wujūd) by itself. And whenever the matter is thus, that dimension is receptive to motion. And every dimension receptive to motion is a body. It follows that the body is necessary of existence by itself. And this yields two things: One of them: that there is no end (nihāya) for bodies, because if they had an end, it would be necessary that in the external [world] there occur a distinction (imtiyāz) of one side from the other, and then the dimension would exist. So that which we assumed to be the end of bodies would not be an end for them. This is a contradiction (khalaf). And the second: that there is no beginning (awwal) for the existence of bodies. Otherwise, before that beginning, it is necessary that the sides be distinguished from each other. And when this distinction occurs, the dimensions exist. And when the dimensions exist, then the body has existed before the existence of the body. This is a contradiction. So it is established: that this discourse indicates that the bodies existing at this time are infinite (ghayr mutanāhiya). And it is also established: that there is no beginning for their existence, nor an end for their existence. So this is the doubt (shubha) mentioned concerning the pre-eternity (qidam) of bodies, derived from the investigation into the essence of place (makān).
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage presents a philosophical argument (shubha) for the pre-eternity of bodies, which is a doubt that the author likely intends to refute later. The argument is derived from the nature of space (makān) and proceeds as follows: First, it considers two alternative possibilities for what allows a dimension to be receptive to motion, and refutes them. The second possibility is that something not specific to place or direction cannot have motion; but that which prevents motion cannot be a condition for motion. The third possibility is that the condition is something that is neither a state in the dimension nor a locus for it, which is also impossible because then it would be neither a body nor a bodily thing, leading to an infinite regress. Having eliminated these, it is established that dimension (buʿd) is necessary of existence (wājib al-wujūd) by itself. Since it is necessary, it must be receptive to motion; and every dimension receptive to motion is a body (jism). Therefore, body is also necessary of existence. From this, two conclusions follow: First, bodies have no end (i.e., they are infinite in number), because if they had an end, that end would require a distinction of sides in the external world, implying the existence of a dimension beyond the end, contradicting the assumption of an end. Second, bodies have no beginning (i.e., they are eternal a parte ante), because before any supposed beginning there would have to be a distinction of sides, implying the existence of dimensions and thus bodies before that beginning, which is a contradiction. Hence, the argument concludes that bodies are infinite and without beginning or end—this is the doubt concerning the pre-eternity of bodies.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The reader might mistake this argument for the author's own position.
Correction: The argument is presented as a doubt (shubha) to be refuted, not as a definitive doctrine.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion reached after eliminating the three alternatives?
A) Dimension is necessary of existence.
B) Motion is impossible.
C) Body is not necessary.
D) None of the above.

Key Takeaway
The argument claims that dimension must be necessary of existence because only then can it be receptive to motion, leading to the necessity of body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that Razi eliminates external conditions for dimension's receptivity, concluding that dimension must be necessary by itself. Where in your life do you look for external circumstances to justify your spiritual growth, rather than recognizing that your capacity to change is inherent in your very creation by Allah? What would it take for you to act on the belief that you are already equipped for transformation?
?
The argument claims that whatever prevents motion cannot be a condition for motion. Is there a habit, belief, or attachment in your heart that is preventing you from moving closer to Allah? Why do you allow that condition to remain? What step will you take today to remove that barrier?
?
Razi warns against the infinite regress that arises when the condition for receptivity is sought in something external and non-corporeal. Reflect on your reliance on secondary causes: do you ever fall into the illusion that some good can come from anywhere other than Allah? How can you shift your trust to the only Necessary Being, who is the true source of all receptivity and motion in your life?

Deriving Infinity and Eternity of Bodies
Razi derives infinity and eternity of bodies from the necessity of body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument infers that bodies have no beginning and no end, thus claiming they are pre-eternal and infinite.

Core Idea
The argument concludes that bodies must be infinite in number and eternal in time.

Once the argument established that body is necessary of existence, it draws two radical implications: that bodies have no end (infinite in extension) and no beginning (eternal a parte ante). The reasoning relies on the idea that any boundary or starting point would require a dimension beyond it, leading to contradiction. This forms the doubt for the pre-eternity of bodies.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first implication: no end
The argument says that if bodies had an end, there would need to be a distinction of sides beyond that end, implying a dimension exists, contradicting the end. Follow the logic of the contradiction (khalaf).
2
Understand the second implication: no beginning
Similarly, if bodies had a beginning, before that beginning there would be a distinction of sides, implying dimensions and thus bodies exist before that beginning, leading to a contradiction.
3
Recognize the overall claim
The argument concludes that bodies are infinite and eternal, and this is the doubt about the pre-eternity of bodies derived from the nature of space.
Remember, this is a doubt to be refuted, not the author's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : إن ما لا يكون مختصاً بالحيز والجهة ، كانت الحركة عليه ممتنعة . وما كان مانعاً من قبول الحركة ، يمتنع أن يكون شرطاً لقبول الحركة . وإن كان الثالث ، وهو أن يكون شرط كون البعد قابلا للحركة ، شيء لا يكون حالاً فيه ولا محلاً له . فهذا أيضاً باطل ، لأن ذلك الشيء يجب أن لا يكون جسماً ولا جسمانياً ، وحينئذ يعود البحث الأول فيه . فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن البعد واجب الوجود لذاته، ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ، كان ذلك البعد قابلاً للحركة وكل بعد قابل للحركة فهو جسم . ينتج أن الجسم واجب الوجود لذاته . وهذا يفيد أمرين : أحدهما : أنه لا نهاية للأجسام لأنه لو حصلت لها نهاية ، لوجب أن يحصل في الخارج امتياز أحد الجانبين عن الآخر فيكون البعد موجوداً . فذلك الذي فرضناه نهاية للأجسام لم يكن نهاية لها . هذا خلف . والثاني : أنه لا أول لوجود الأجسام ، وإلا فقبل ذلك الأول ، لابد وأن تتميز الجوانب بعضها عن البعض . وإذا حصل هذا الامتياز ، كانت الأبعاد موجودة وإذا كانت الأبعاد موجودة، فقد وجد الجسم قبل وجود الجسم . هذا خلف . فثبت : أن هذا الكلام يدل على أن الأجسام الموجودة في هذا الوقت : غير متناهية . وثبت أيضاً : أنه لا أول لوجودها ، ولا آخر لوجودها . فهذا هو الشبهة المذكورة في قدم الأجسام المستنبطة من البحث عن ماهية المكان .
The second [point]: That which is not specific to place (ḥayyiz) and direction (jiha), motion upon it is impossible. And that which prevents the acceptance of motion, it is impossible for it to be a condition for the acceptance of motion. And if it is the third, that the condition for the dimension being receptive to motion is something that is neither a state (ḥāl) in it nor a locus (maḥall) for it. This too is false, because that thing must not be a body (jism) nor a bodily thing (jismānī), and then the first investigation returns upon it. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that the dimension (buʿd) is necessary of existence (wājib al-wujūd) by itself. And whenever the matter is thus, that dimension is receptive to motion. And every dimension receptive to motion is a body. It follows that the body is necessary of existence by itself. And this yields two things: One of them: that there is no end (nihāya) for bodies, because if they had an end, it would be necessary that in the external [world] there occur a distinction (imtiyāz) of one side from the other, and then the dimension would exist. So that which we assumed to be the end of bodies would not be an end for them. This is a contradiction (khalaf). And the second: that there is no beginning (awwal) for the existence of bodies. Otherwise, before that beginning, it is necessary that the sides be distinguished from each other. And when this distinction occurs, the dimensions exist. And when the dimensions exist, then the body has existed before the existence of the body. This is a contradiction. So it is established: that this discourse indicates that the bodies existing at this time are infinite (ghayr mutanāhiya). And it is also established: that there is no beginning for their existence, nor an end for their existence. So this is the doubt (shubha) mentioned concerning the pre-eternity (qidam) of bodies, derived from the investigation into the essence of place (makān).
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage presents a philosophical argument (shubha) for the pre-eternity of bodies, which is a doubt that the author likely intends to refute later. The argument is derived from the nature of space (makān) and proceeds as follows: First, it considers two alternative possibilities for what allows a dimension to be receptive to motion, and refutes them. The second possibility is that something not specific to place or direction cannot have motion; but that which prevents motion cannot be a condition for motion. The third possibility is that the condition is something that is neither a state in the dimension nor a locus for it, which is also impossible because then it would be neither a body nor a bodily thing, leading to an infinite regress. Having eliminated these, it is established that dimension (buʿd) is necessary of existence (wājib al-wujūd) by itself. Since it is necessary, it must be receptive to motion; and every dimension receptive to motion is a body (jism). Therefore, body is also necessary of existence. From this, two conclusions follow: First, bodies have no end (i.e., they are infinite in number), because if they had an end, that end would require a distinction of sides in the external world, implying the existence of a dimension beyond the end, contradicting the assumption of an end. Second, bodies have no beginning (i.e., they are eternal a parte ante), because before any supposed beginning there would have to be a distinction of sides, implying the existence of dimensions and thus bodies before that beginning, which is a contradiction. Hence, the argument concludes that bodies are infinite and without beginning or end—this is the doubt concerning the pre-eternity of bodies.

The argument forces you to confront the impossibility of a true boundary. In your spiritual life, have you placed an 'end' on Allah's mercy or a 'beginning' on your forgiveness, as if your sin could outrun His grace? What would it look like to remove that limit today?
?
This passage labels itself a 'shubha'—a doubt that seems convincing but is ultimately false. The First Proof for the Eternity of the World
First proof for the world's eternity: argument from causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof for the world's eternity, which argues that the world cannot cease to exist because any such cessation would require an impossible cause.

Core Idea
The world cannot cease without a cause.

This lesson introduces the first of several proofs for the world's eternity. The author begins by establishing that any change from existence to non-existence requires a cause. He then examines possible causes and shows that none can apply, implying the world must be eternal. We will work through the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: first the claim that the world must be eternal (abadi), then the proof that it must also be eternal without beginning (azali).
The passage ends mid-sentence; the argument continues on the next page.
2
Identify the main argument structure
The proof is a reductio ad absurdum. It supposes the world ceases, then asks whether that cessation has a cause. If uncaused, it's irrational. If caused, the cause is examined. Follow the logical divisions.
The divisions are: cause or no cause; if cause, then capable agent (qadir) or necessitating cause (mujib); if necessitating cause, then existent or non-existent.
3
Understand the three cases of necessitating cause
Three specific ways the world could cease are listed: (1) a free agent destroys it, (2) its opposite occurs, (3) its condition is removed. The argument implies that each case is impossible, but the text cuts off. Reflect on why each might be impossible.
The commentary explains the reasoning behind each case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان أنه يجب أن يكون العالم أبدياً، ثم بيان أنه لما يجب كونه أبدياً، وجب كونه أزلياً. أما بيان أنه يجب أن يكون العالم أبدياً . فقد احتجوا عليه من وجوه : الحجة الأولى : وهي دليل مستنبط من الأصول الكلامية . قالوا : إن هذا العالم ، لو عدم بعد أن كان موجوداً ، لكان عدمه بعد وجوده . إما أن يكون السبب وموجب ، أو لا [ لسبب، (۱) ] الموجب . والثاني باطل . لأن تبدل الوجود بالعدم من غير سبب ولا موجب بوجه من الوجوه على خلاف العقل . وأما الأول . وهو أن العالم يصير معدوماً لسبب . فنقول : ذلك (٢) السبب ، إما أن يكون قادراً ، أو موجباً . وذلك الموجب إما أن يكون أمراً موجوداً ، أو أمراً معدوماً ، فهذه أقسام : أحدها : أن يعدم العالم . لأن القادر المختار أعدمه . وثانيها : أن يعدم العالم . لطريان ضده . وثالثها : أن يعدم العالم لزوال شرطه
A statement that it is necessary that the world be eternal (abadi), then a statement that since it must be eternal, it must also be eternal without beginning (azali). As for the statement that it is necessary that the world be eternal, they have adduced proofs for it from several aspects: The first proof: It is a proof derived from the principles of kalam. They said: This world, if it were to cease to exist after having existed, its non-existence after its existence would either be due to a cause and a necessitator (mu'aththir), or not due to a cause and a necessitator. The second is invalid, because the change from existence to non-existence without any cause or necessitator in any way is contrary to reason. As for the first – that the world becomes non-existent due to a cause – we say: That cause is either a capable agent (qadir) or a necessitating cause (mujib). And that necessitating cause is either something existent or something non-existent. These are the divisions: First: that the world ceases to exist because the free agent (al-qadir al-mukhtar) makes it non-existent. Second: that the world ceases to exist due to the occurrence of its opposite. Third: that the world ceases to exist due to the removal of its condition.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for the eternity (abadi) of the world, and then argues that since it must be eternal a parte post, it must also be eternal a parte ante (azali). The proof is derived from the principles of kalam. The argument begins by supposing the world were to cease after existing. Its non-existence would either be caused or uncaused. The uncaused option is impossible because any change without a cause is contrary to reason. So the non-existence must be caused. The cause is then divided: it could be a capable free agent or a necessitating cause. The necessitating cause is further divided into something existent or non-existent. Then three specific cases are listed: (1) the world ceases because a free agent destroys it, (2) because its opposite occurs, (3) because its condition is removed. The text cuts off before discussing each case, but the structure shows a systematic refutation of possible causes for the world's non-existence, implying that none can apply, therefore the world must remain existent eternally.

Correction: The proof first establishes that the world will never cease to exist (endless eternity), and then infers that since it will never cease, it must have always existed (beginningless). The text states this two-step approach explicitly.

If the world cannot cease without a cause, then your very existence at this moment depends on a cause that sustains you. The World's Necessary Perpetual Existence
Al-Razi enumerates and refutes possible causes for the world's annihilation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's kalam argument that the world cannot cease to exist because any supposed cause of annihilation leads to a logical impossibility.

Core Idea
The world's annihilation is impossible because every possible cause is rationally untenable.

Al-Razi here constructs a rigorous argument to establish the world's necessary perpetual existence. He begins by asserting that if the world were to become non-existent after having existed, that change would require a cause. He then divides all possible causes into categories and demonstrates that each category is false. This lesson walks through that argument step by step, showing why the world's annihilation is rationally impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Pay attention to the logical flow: the main claim, the causal principle, the division of causes, and the refutation of the first cause.
Notice that the passage is dense with technical terms like 'قادر' (capable agent) and 'موجب' (necessitating cause).
2
Understand the causal principle
The argument relies on the principle that any change from existence to non-existence must have a cause. Identify where Al-Razi states this and why he rejects the possibility of uncaused annihilation.
This is a foundational principle in kalam: every event (including cessation) requires a cause.
3
Examine the three possible causes
Al-Razi splits the cause of annihilation into three categories: direct divine action, occurrence of an opposite, and removal of a condition. List these and note that he declares all three false.
The refutation of the first category (direct divine action) is the most detailed part of the passage.
4
Analyze the refutation of direct divine annihilation
Carefully study the three arguments against the idea that God directly annihilates the world. Each argument targets a different aspect: power's effect, the role of capability, and the timing of annihilation.
The third argument involves a paradox about annihilating something in a future moment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان أنه يجب أن يكون العالم أبدياً، ثم بيان أنه لما يجب كونه أبدياً، وجب كونه أنليا
أما بيان أنه يجب أن يكون العالم أبدياً. فقد احتجوا عليه من وجوه : الحجة الأولى : وهي دليل مستنبط من الأصول الكلامية . قالوا : إن هذا العالم ، لو عدم بعد أن كان موجوداً ، لكان عدمه بعد وجوده إما أن يكون لسبب موجب، أو لا لسبب موجب. والثاني باطل . لأن تبدل الوجود بالعدم من غير سبب ولا موجب بوجه من الوجوه على خلاف العقل .
وأما الأول . وهو أن العالم يصير معدوماً لسبب . فنقول : ذلك السبب، إما أن يكون قادراً، أو موجباً. وذلك الموجب إما أن يكون أمراً موجوداً، أو أمراً معدوماً، فهذه أقسام :
أحدها : أن يعدم العالم . لأن القادر المختار أعدمه .
وثانيها : أن يعدم العالم . لطريان ضده
وثالثها: أن يعدم العالم لزوال شرطه
وهذه الأقسام الثلاثة بأسرها باطلة ، فالقول : بعدم العالم : باطل. أما القسم الأول : وهو أن يعدم العالم ، لأجل أن الفاعل المختار يعدمه. فنقول : هذا باطل ويدل عليه وجوه :
الأول : إن القدرة صفة مؤثرة، فلا بد لها من أثر . والعدم نفي محض ، وسلب صرف ، فيمتنع جعله أثراً للقدرة . فيثبت : أنه يمتنع وقوع ذلك العدم بقدرة القادر المختار .
والثاني : إن المقتضى لعدم العالم. إما مجرد كونه قادراً ، وإما أمر يصدر عن كونه قادراً . والأول باطل . لأن القادرية كانت حاصلة في الأزل ، فلو كان مجرد القادرية مانعاً من وجود العالم لكان من الواجب أن لا يوجد العالم البتة . وإن كان الموجب لعدم العالم أثراً يصدر عن القدرة ، فحينئذ المؤثر القريب لعدم العالم : هو ذلك الأثر الصادر عن تلك القدرة . فهذا رجوع إلى القسم الثاني، وهو أن العالم إنما يفنى لحدوث ضد له يوجب عدمه. فإن قالوا : فهذا الكلام بعينه قائم في كيفية تأثير القدرة في الوجود . فنقول : هذا إنما يلزم عن من أثبت القدرة . بمعنى كونها صالحة للطرفين. أما عند من يقول : مجموع القدرة مع الداعي علة موجبة . فهذا غير لازم
الوجه الثالث : وهو أنه تعالى لو أعدم العالم ، فإما أن يعدمه حال كونه موجوداً ، أو في الزمان الثاني من وجوده . والأول يقتضي كونه معدوماً حال كونه موجوداً، وهو محال. والثاني باطل لأن إعدام الزمان الثاني مشروط بحصول الزمان الثاني، الذي هو ممتنع الحصول في الزمان الأول والموقوف على المحال : محال . فوجب أن يكون كونه معدوماً في الحال للشيء في الزمان المستقبل : أمراً محالاً ممتنعاً . وهذا الوجه قد مر ذكره في باب القادر في جانب الإيجاد.
Statement that it is necessary for the world to be eternal, then statement that since it is necessary for it to be eternal, it is necessary for it to be [eternal?]. As for the statement that it is necessary for the world to be eternal, they have argued for it in several ways: The first proof: It is a proof derived from the principles of kalam. They said: If this world were to become non-existent after having existed, its non-existence after its existence would either be due to a necessitating cause or not due to a necessitating cause. The second is false, because change from existence to non-existence without any cause or necessitating factor in any way is contrary to reason. As for the first — that the world becomes non-existent due to a cause — we say: That cause is either a capable agent or a necessitating factor. That necessitating factor is either an existent thing or a non-existent thing. These are the divisions: One: That the world becomes non-existent because the free capable agent annihilates it. Two: That the world becomes non-existent due to the occurrence of its opposite. Three: That the world becomes non-existent due to the removal of its condition. All three of these divisions are entirely false, so the statement of the non-existence of the world is false. As for the first division — that the world becomes non-existent because the free agent annihilates it — we say: This is false, and it is indicated by several arguments: The first: Power (qudra) is an effective attribute, so it must have an effect. Non-existence is pure negation and absolute privation, so it is impossible to make it an effect of power. Thus it is established that it is impossible for that non-existence to occur by the power of the free capable agent. The second: The determinant for the non-existence of the world is either His mere being capable, or something issuing from His being capable. The first is false, because capability (qadiriyya) existed in eternity; if mere capability were a preventer of the existence of the world, it would have been necessary that the world never exist. If the necessitator of the world's non-existence is an effect issuing from power, then the proximate cause of the world's non-existence is that effect issuing from that power. This is a return to the second division, namely that the world perishes only due to the occurrence of an opposite that necessitates its non-existence. If they say: This very argument applies to how power affects existence. We say: This follows only from the one who affirms power in the sense that it is fit for both alternatives. As for the one who says: The combination of power with the motive is a necessitating cause, this does not follow. The third argument: That if He, exalted, annihilates the world, He either annihilates it while it is existent, or in the second moment of its existence. The first entails it being non-existent while it is existent, which is impossible. The second is false because the annihilation of the second moment is conditional upon the occurrence of the second moment, which is impossible of occurrence in the first moment, and what is contingent upon the impossible is impossible. Thus it is necessary that a thing's being non-existent now in the future is an impossible and absurd matter. This argument has already been mentioned in the chapter on the capable agent regarding the aspect of bringing into existence.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of the world's eternal existence (al-azal? actually al-abad? The text says أبدياً, meaning perpetual, without end). The author presents a kalam argument to show that the world cannot cease to exist, hence it must be eternal. The argument proceeds as follows: First, the claim is stated: the world must be eternal. Then the first proof is given: If the world were to become non-existent after existing, its non-existence would either have a necessitating cause (سبب موجب) or not. The second option is rejected as irrational, because a change from existence to non-existence without any cause is impossible according to reason. Therefore, if annihilation occurs, it must have a cause. The cause is then divided: it is either a capable agent (قادر) or a necessitating factor (موجب). The necessitating factor is further divided into something existent or something non-existent. This yields three possibilities for the cause of annihilation: (1) The free capable agent annihilates it directly, (2) Annihilation occurs due to the occurrence of its opposite, (3) Annihilation occurs due to the removal of its condition. The author then declares all three possibilities false. He then refutes the first possibility in detail with three arguments. First argument: Power (قدرة) is an effective attribute that must produce an effect; non-existence is pure negation and cannot be an effect. Therefore, non-existence cannot be directly caused by power. Second argument: The cause of annihilation cannot be mere capability (قادرية) because that would have prevented existence altogether in eternity; if it is an effect of power, then that effect is itself something existent, which reduces to the second category (i.e., a cause that is an existent thing, namely an opposite). The author anticipates an objection: that this reasoning also applies to the power's effect in bringing about existence. He responds that this objection only holds for those who believe power is equally suited for both existence and non-existence; but for those who hold that power combined with a motive becomes a necessitating cause, the objection does not apply. Third argument: Annihilation cannot occur because if God annihilates the world, it would have to be either at the moment of its existence (contradiction) or in a future moment, which requires that future moment to exist now, an impossibility. Therefore, the notion of a thing being non-existent now in the future is impossible. The author notes that this argument has already been mentioned in the chapter on the capable agent regarding creation. The overall conclusion is that the world's annihilation is impossible, so it must be eternal.

Key Terms

موجب

أثر
فناء

داعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's absolute power allows Him to directly annihilate the world at any time.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that non-existence is pure negation and cannot be an effect of power; power produces existence, not non-existence. Therefore direct divine annihilation is logically impossible.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Razi's argument, why is it impossible for God to directly annihilate the world?
A) Because God's power is limited and cannot cause non-existence.
B) Because non-existence is a pure negation and cannot be an effect of power.
C) Because the world is too great to be annihilated by any cause.
D) Because annihilation would require a future moment that does not exist.

Key Takeaway
The world's annihilation is impossible because every possible cause—direct divine action, occurrence of an opposite, or removal of a condition—is logically untenable, so the world must be eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the world will never end, then your actions have eternal consequences. What sin have you committed thinking it would be forgotten or undone by a finite world? How will you repent today?
?
The argument proves that God cannot directly annihilate the world because non-existence is not an effect. Does this make you feel that God is limited? Or does it reveal that His creation is inherently stable? How does this truth change your trust in God's preservation of you personally?
?
You live as if this world will end. But the proof says it is impossible. Are you building your life on temporary or permanent foundations? What is the first concrete change you must make starting this week?

The First Proof: Non-existence Cannot Be an Effect of Power
First proof: non-existence cannot be an effect of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why non-existence cannot be an effect of divine power according to the first proof.

Core Idea
Non-existence is pure negation; power requires a positive effect.

The author begins his refutation of the claim that God can directly annihilate the world by His power. He first asserts that all three divisions are false, then focuses on the first division and presents his first proof. This proof hinges on the nature of power as an effective attribute and the reality of non-existence as pure privation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the author's style.
This is a dense argument; take your time.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on 'power' (al-qudra), 'effect' (al-athar), and 'non-existence' (al-ʿadam). Understand their definitions.
3
Understand the reasoning
See why non-existence, being pure negation, cannot be an effect of an effective attribute.
Consider what it means for an attribute to be 'muʾaththira' (effective).
4
Connect to the conclusion
The proof establishes that it is impossible for non-existence to occur by God's power directly.
This is a foundational premise for subsequent arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذه الأقسام الثلاثة بأسرها باطلة ، فالقول : بعدم العالم : باطل. أما القسم الأول: وهو أن يصدم العالم، لأجل أن الفاعل المختار يعدمه. فنقول : هذا باطل ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إن القدرة صفة مؤثرة، فلا بد لها من أثر . والعدم نفي محض ، وسلب صرف ، فيمتنع جعله أثراً للقدرة . فيثبت : أنه يمتنع وقوع ذلك العدم بقدرة القادر المختار . والثاني : إن المقتضى لعدم العالم. إما مجرد كونه قادراً ، وإما أمر يصدر عن كونه قادراً . والأول باطل . لأن القادرية كانت حاصلة في الأزل ، فلو كان مجرد القادرية مانعاً من وجود العالم . لكان من الواجب أن لا يوجد العالم البتة . وإن كان الموجب لعدم العالم أثراً يصدر عن القدرة ، فحينئذ المؤثر القريب لعدم العالم : هو ذلك الأثر الصادر عن تلك القدرة . فهذا رجوع إلى القسم الثاني، وهو أن العالم إنما يفنى لحدوث ضد له يوجب عدمه. فإن قالوا : فهذا الكلام بعينه قائم في كيفية تأثير القدرة في الوجود . فنقول : هذا إنما يلزم عن من أثبت القدرة . بمعنى كونها صالحة للطرفين. أما عند من يقول : مجموع القدرة مع الداعي علة موجبة . فهذا غير لازم . الوجه الثالث : وهو أنه تعالى لو أعدم العالم ، فإما أن يعدمه حال كونه موجوداً ، أو في الزمان الثاني من وجوده . والأول يقتضي كونه معدوماً حال كونه موجوداً، وهو محال. والثاني باطل لأن إعدام الزمان الثاني مشروط بحصول الزمان الثاني، الذي هو ممتنع الحصول في الزمان الأول والموقوف على المحال : محال . فوجب أن يكون كونه معدوماً في الحال للشيء في الزمان المستقبل : أمراً محالاً ممتنعاً . وهذا الوجه قد مر ذكره في باب القادر في جانب الإيجاد.
And these three divisions entirely are false. So the statement: 'the non-existence of the world' is false. As for the first division: that the agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār) annihilates the world because He chooses to annihilate it. We say: this is false, and it is indicated by aspects: The first: that power (al-qudra) is an effective attribute (ṣifa muʾaththira), so it must have an effect (athar). And non-existence (al-ʿadam) is pure negation (nafī maḥḍ) and sheer privation (salab ṣirf), so it is impossible to make it an effect of power. Thus it is established that it is impossible for that non-existence to occur by the power of the powerful agent (al-qādir al-mukhtār). The second: that the determinant (al-muqtaḍā) for the non-existence of the world is either mere capacity (kawnuhu qādiran) or a matter proceeding from his capacity. The first is false, because capacity existed in eternity (al-azal), so if mere capacity prevented the existence of the world, it would be necessary that the world never exist. And if the cause of the world's non-existence is an effect proceeding from power, then the proximate efficient cause (al-muʾaththir al-qarīb) of the world's non-existence is that effect proceeding from that power. So this reverts to the second division, which is that the world perishes only due to the occurrence of an opposite (ḍidd) necessitating its non-existence. If they say: this same argument stands regarding how power affects existence. We say: this only follows from one who affirms power in the sense that it is suitable (ṣāliḥa) for both possibilities. As for one who says: the combination of power with the motive (al-dāʿī) is a necessitating cause (ʿilla mūjiba), this does not follow. The third aspect: that if He, exalted, were to annihilate the world, He would either annihilate it while it exists, or in the second moment of its existence. The first requires it to be non-existent while it exists, which is impossible. The second is false because the annihilation in the second moment is conditioned upon the occurrence of the second moment, which is impossible to occur in the first moment, and what is dependent on the impossible is impossible. So it is necessary that its being non-existent in the present for a thing in the future is an impossible and impermissible matter. And this aspect has already been mentioned in the chapter on the powerful (al-qādir) regarding the aspect of bringing into existence.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage refutes the first division of those who claim that God can annihilate the world directly by His power. The author begins by stating that all three divisions are false, then focuses on the first: that the free agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār) directly causes the world's non-existence. He provides three proofs (wujūh).

First proof: Power (al-qudra) is an effective attribute—it must produce an effect. Non-existence (al-ʿadam) is pure negation and sheer privation; it has no positive reality, so it cannot be an effect of power. Therefore, it is impossible for the powerful agent to directly cause non-existence.

Second proof: The cause of the world's non-existence is either mere capacity (mujarrad al-qādiriyya) or something proceeding from that capacity. The first is false because capacity existed eternally in God; if it alone prevented the world from existing, the world would never have existed at all. The second option reduces to the second division (annihilation via an opposite), because the proximate cause would be an effect of power. The author then addresses a possible objection: that the same reasoning applies to the effect of power in creation. He responds that this objection only holds for those who define power as equally capable of opposites; but for those who hold that power together with a motive necessitates an effect, the objection does not apply.

Third proof: If God were to annihilate the world, He would do so either while it exists (contradiction—it cannot be both existent and non-existent) or in a second moment of its existence. But the second moment is entirely separate; its existence in the first moment is impossible, and what is conditioned on the impossible is itself impossible. Thus, direct annihilation in the future is impossible.

Key Terms
القدرة
العدم
الأثر
الصفة المؤثرة
السلب الصرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's power can directly produce non-existence as an effect.
Correction: Non-existence is pure negation, not a positive entity, so it cannot be an effect of an effective attribute like power.

Checkpoint
Why does the first proof reject non-existence as an effect of divine power?
A) Because non-existence is an attribute of God.
B) Because non-existence is pure negation and cannot be an effect of an effective attribute.
C) Because power only affects material things.
D) Because the world exists eternally.

Key Takeaway
Non-existence, being pure negation, cannot be an effect of divine power because power is an effective attribute requiring a positive effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's power cannot directly produce non-existence, then what is really happening when you lose something you love? Are you willing to see loss not as God 'unmaking' something, but as a transformation or removal of His sustaining act? Does that change how you process grief?
?
Think about something you have done today—a prayer, a kindness, or even a sin. If your actions are real, positive effects that God's power upholds, how does that affect your sense of accountability? Are you living as if your deeds are mere nothingness, or as if they persist forever before God?
?
You will one day die and your body will cease to function. But non-existence is not an effect of God's power. What does that mean for your soul? Do you truly believe you are going to an everlasting existence, and are you preparing for it as if it is more real than this fleeting life?

The Second Proof: The Dilemma of the Cause of Non-Existence
Second proof: dilemma of cause of non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof, which argues that the cause of the world's non-existence cannot be mere capacity or an effect of power without leading to another division.

Core Idea
If mere capacity caused non-existence, the world would never exist; if an effect caused it, it reverts to annihilation by an opposite.

The second proof presents a dilemma: the cause of the world's non-existence is either God's mere capacity or something proceeding from that capacity. The author argues that the first option is false because capacity existed eternally, and the second option reduces to the second division of annihilation by an opposite. He then addresses an objection related to the nature of power.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the structure: two options and a rebuttal.
2
Analyze the two options
Understand why mere capacity is false (eternal capacity would have prevented existence) and why the second option leads to the second division.
3
Examine the objection and response
The objection claims the same reasoning applies to creation. See how the author defends his position by distinguishing between different conceptions of power.
This is a subtle point about the nature of power.

?

?

Do you truly believe that God's decisions about you are based on perfect wisdom, or do you sometimes think He is indifferent? The Third Proof: The Impossibility of Annihilating the World Now or Later
Third proof: temporal impossibility of direct annihilation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof, which argues that direct annihilation is impossible because it would require either annihilating while existing or in a future moment that cannot be reached.

Core Idea
Annihilating the world now while it exists is contradictory; annihilating in a future moment is impossible because that moment never arrives.

The third proof takes a different approach, focusing on the temporal aspect of annihilation. The author argues that if God were to annihilate the world directly, He would have to do so either while it exists, which is a contradiction, or in a future moment, which is impossible because the future moment's occurrence is conditional on the present, and what depends on the impossible is impossible. This argument was also used earlier regarding creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the third proof passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two alternatives: annihilation in the present or in the second moment.
2
Understand why present annihilation is impossible
It would require the world to be both existent and non-existent at the same time, a contradiction.
This is straightforward logic.
3
Understand why future annihilation is impossible
Annihilation in the second moment requires that the second moment occur, but it cannot occur while the first moment exists. What depends on the impossible is itself impossible.
This is a subtle argument about temporal dependence.

Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage refutes the first division of those who claim that God can annihilate the world directly by His power. The author begins by stating that all three divisions are false, then focuses on the first: that the free agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār) directly causes the world's non-existence. He provides three proofs (wujūh).

First proof: Power (al-qudra) is an effective attribute—it must produce an effect. Non-existence (al-ʿadam) is pure negation and sheer privation; it has no positive reality, so it cannot be an effect of power. Therefore, it is impossible for the powerful agent to directly cause non-existence.

Second proof: The cause of the world's non-existence is either mere capacity (mujarrad al-qādiriyya) or something proceeding from that capacity. The first is false because capacity existed eternally in God; if it alone prevented the world from existing, the world would never have existed at all. The second option reduces to the second division (annihilation via an opposite), because the proximate cause would be an effect of power. The author then addresses a possible objection: that the same reasoning applies to the effect of power in creation. He responds that this objection only holds for those who define power as equally capable of opposites; but for those who hold that power together with a motive necessitates an effect, the objection does not apply.

Third proof: If God were to annihilate the world, He would do so either while it exists (contradiction—it cannot be both existent and non-existent) or in a second moment of its existence. But the second moment is entirely separate; its existence in the first moment is impossible, and what is conditioned on the impossible is itself impossible. Thus, direct annihilation in the future is impossible.

Key Terms
العدم
الزمان الثاني
الموقوف على المحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God can annihilate the world in a future moment without any contradiction.
Correction: The future moment cannot occur simultaneously with the present, so annihilation in the second moment depends on an impossible condition, making it impossible.

Checkpoint
Why is it impossible for God to annihilate the world in a future moment according to the third proof?
A) Because the future moment does not exist at all.
B) Because annihilation in the second moment requires the second moment to occur, which is impossible while the first moment exists.
C) Because God cannot act in the future.
D) Because the world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Direct annihilation is impossible whether attempted in the present or the future, as either involves a contradiction or an impossible dependency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If direct annihilation is impossible, even for God, what does that mean for your attempts to 'wipe away' your sins without effort? Are you expecting a magical removal, or are you willing to engage in the slow process of replacement through good deeds?
?
Consider something in your life you desperately wish would vanish—a trial, a weakness, a memory. This proof shows that God does not work by sudden removal. How does that change your dua and your expectations of how He will help you?
?
The argument says future annihilation is impossible because the future moment never arrives from the present. In your spiritual life, how often do you postpone repentance or change, expecting a future moment to come when you can simply 'erase' your bad habits? What must you do now instead?

Refuting Annihilation via a Created Opposite
Three proofs refuting annihilation via a created opposite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three logical arguments against the claim that God annihilates the world by creating an opposite, and why each argument shows impossibility.

Core Idea
Creating an opposite cannot cause annihilation without circularity or contradiction.

The idea that God could annihilate the world by creating an incompatible opposite seems plausible at first. However, a deeper look reveals three independent logical flaws. This passage presents them as clear proofs: the symmetry of opposition, the vicious circle in causation, and a temporal dilemma. Each proof shows why this mechanism cannot work.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Focus on the overall structure: the author announces the refutation and gives three numbered proofs.
Notice that each proof begins with 'الأول', 'الثاني', 'الثالث'.
2
Analyze the first proof: symmetry of opposition
The first proof argues that opposition is shared between two things. There is no reason to say the prior ceases because the later arrives rather than the later being repelled by the prior. Understand why this symmetry breaks the causal claim.
This proof undermines the idea that one opposite has causal priority over the other.
3
Analyze the second proof: circularity (dawr)
The second proof shows that the arrival of the later opposite depends on the prior's passing. If the prior's passing is caused by the later, then we have a circle: the cause depends on the effect. Grasp why this is logically impossible.
This proof uses the concept of precondition (mashrūṭ) and causation (ta'līl).
4
Analyze the third proof: temporal dilemma
The third proof presents a dilemma: the opposite either occurs during the world's existence or after. Both options lead to impossibility: coexistence of opposites or the later causing the prior. Trace each horn of the dilemma.
This proof relies on the principle that a cause cannot come after its effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أنه تعالى يعدم بواسطة خلق الضد المنافي فنقول هذا أيضاً باطل من وجوه . الأول : وهو أن المضادة أمر مشترك فيه بين الطرفين ، فلم يكن زوال المتقدم ، لأجل طريان المتأخر ، أولى من اندفاع المتأخر لأجل قيام المتقدم . الثاني : إن طريان الضد الطارىء ، مشروط بزوال السابق ، فلو كان زوال السابق معللا بطريان الطارىء ، وقع الدور . وهو محال . الثالث : وهو أن هذا الضد، إما أن يحصل حال وجود العالم ، أو بعده ، والأول باطل ، لأنه يقتضي : الجمع بين الضدين . وهو محال . والثاني يقتضي : أن هذا الضد ، إنما حصل بعد عدم الضد السابق . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، امتنع أن يكون ذلك العدم معللاً بهذا الضد ، لأن المتأخر لا يكون علة للمتقدم .
And as for the second division: that He, exalted, annihilates by means of creating the opposite (al-ḍidd al-munāfī). We say: this too is false from [several] aspects. The first: that opposition (al-muḍādda) is a matter shared between the two sides, so the passing away of the prior is not more likely to be due to the advent of the later than the repulsion of the later due to the persistence of the prior. The second: that the advent of the subsequent opposite is conditioned (mashrūṭ) upon the passing away of the prior. So if the passing away of the prior were caused (muʿallalan) by the advent of the later, circularity (al-dawr) would occur, and it is impossible. The third: that this opposite either occurs during the existence of the world, or after it. The first is false because it necessitates combining two opposites, which is impossible. The second necessitates that this opposite only occurs after the non-existence of the prior opposite. And if this is the case, it is impossible for that non-existence to be caused by this opposite, because the later cannot be a cause of the prior.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage refutes the second division, which claims that God annihilates the world by creating an opposite that is incompatible with it. The author provides three proofs (wujūh).

First proof: Opposition (al-muḍādda) is a relation shared between two things. There is no reason that the prior thing should cease due to the later rather than the later being repelled by the prior. The relationship is symmetrical, so neither direction has priority.

Second proof: The occurrence of the later opposite is conditioned upon the prior opposite having already passed away. If we say that the passing away of the prior is caused by the later opposite, then we have a circular causation: the later causes the prior's passing, but the later's advent depends on that passing. This is a vicious circle (al-dawr) and impossible.

Third proof: The opposite either occurs while the world still exists or after the world has ceased to exist. If it occurs while the world exists, then two opposites would coexist, which is impossible. If it occurs after the world has ceased, then the opposite is later than the non-existence of the world; hence, the later opposite cannot be the cause of the prior non-existence, because the cause must precede or be simultaneous with the effect.

Key Terms
الضد
المضادة
الدور
زوال
طريان
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that creating an opposite can annihilate the world without logical flaw.
Correction: The passage shows three logical impossibilities: symmetrical causation, circular reasoning, and temporal inversion.

Checkpoint
In the second proof against annihilation via an opposite, what logical problem is identified?
A) Contradiction in divine attributes.
B) Causal circularity (dawr).
C) Infinite regress.
D) Violation of non-contradiction.

Key Takeaway
Annihilation by creating an opposite is logically impossible due to symmetry, circularity, or temporal paradox.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The first proof says that opposition is shared equally, so there is no reason to think the prior must give way to the later. In your life, when you face a new situation that seems to oppose your current state (e.g., a difficult trial that challenges your comfort), do you automatically assume the old must go? How does this lesson challenge you to consider whether the new might be repelled instead, and what would that mean for your patience and trust in God's wisdom?
?
The second proof shows that circularity is impossible. Have you ever held a belief about God that was circular—for example, thinking that your good deeds cause God's mercy, but that mercy is needed to do good deeds? How does this lesson push you to examine your understanding of cause and effect in your relationship with your Creator?
?
The third proof presents a temporal dilemma: if the opposite comes after, it cannot be the cause of the prior. When you experience a new emotion or circumstance that follows a change in your life, do you instinctively assume it caused the change? How does this lesson correct that assumption and teach you to rely more on God’s ordainment rather than attributing causality to later events?

Refutation of Annihilation by Removing a Condition
Refutes the claim of annihilation by removing a condition with two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rational proofs that refute the claim that God annihilates the world by removing a condition.

Core Idea
Removing a condition requires a condition itself—leading to impossibility or circularity.

Sanusi now addresses the third division of annihilation: the idea that God destroys the universe by removing a condition necessary for its existence. He presents two arguments that demonstrate the logical impossibility of this scenario. This lesson examines these proofs, which rely on the concepts of essential possibility and the relationship between substances and accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's rhythm and structure come through before interpreting.
Notice the two distinct proofs introduced by 'al-wajh al-awwal' and 'al-wajh al-thānī'.
2
Understand the first proof's dilemma
The first proof presents a dilemma: the condition either remains or does not. If it remains, the problem of the first division recurs; if it does not, that would require a change from essential possibility to impossibility.
This argument assumes that all accidents are enduring, a key premise in kalam.
3
Analyze the second proof's circularity
The second proof examines whether the condition is an accident inhering in the world or a separate existent. If an accident, circular dependency arises because an accident depends on a substance, and the substance would then depend on the accident.
Circularity (al-dawr) is impossible in rational reasoning.
4
Connect to theological principles
These proofs show that God's power does not operate through logical contradictions. They affirm that God can annihilate directly, not via removing a condition that is itself contingent.
This refines our understanding of divine agency and the necessity of proper causal frameworks.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أنه تعالى يعدم العالم بواسطة إعدام الشرط . فنقول : هذا باطل لوجهين : الأول : إن ذلك الشرط، إما أن يكون باقياً ، أو غير باق . فإن كان عاد التقسيم الأول في كيفية عدمه، وإن كان غير باق ، فهذا محال . لأن كل ما دخل في الوجود كان قابلاً للبقاء ، وإلا فيلزم أن ينتقل من الإمكان الذاتي إلى الامتناع الذاتي وهو محال . وهذا الكلام إنما يتقرر على قول من يقول : الأعراض كلها باقية . والوجه الثاني : وهو أن ذلك الشرط، إما أن يكون عرضاً قائماً به ، أو موجوداً مبايناً عنه . والأول باطل ، لأن العرض محتاج في وجوده إلى الجوهر فلو فرضنا كون الجوهر محتاجاً إلى شيء من الأعراض ، لزم الدور ، وهو محال .
And as for the third division: that He, exalted, annihilates the world by means of removing the condition (iʿdām al-sharṭ). We say: this is false from two aspects. The first: that this condition either remains (bāqin) or does not remain. If it remains, then the first division (regarding its annihilation) recurs. If it does not remain, then that is impossible, because everything that enters into existence was capable of remaining (qābilan lil-baqāʾ); otherwise, it would necessitate a transition from essential possibility (al-imkān al-dhātī) to essential impossibility (al-imtināʿ al-dhātī), and that is impossible. And this statement is established only according to the one who says that all accidents (al-aʿrāḍ) are enduring (bāqiya). The second aspect: that this condition is either an accident (ʿaraḍ) inhering in it (i.e., the world), or an existent separate from it. The first is false, because an accident depends in its existence upon the substance (al-jawhar). So if we were to suppose that the substance depends upon something from among the accidents, circularity (al-dawr) would occur, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage refutes the third division, which claims that God annihilates the world by removing a condition necessary for its existence. The author presents two proofs (wajhān).

First proof: The condition in question either remains in existence or does not. If it remains, then the same problem as in the first division arises—how could it be removed? If it does not remain (i.e., it ceases to exist), then that cessation must itself be caused. But this is impossible because everything that comes into existence is by nature capable of remaining; otherwise, it would change from being possible to being impossible in itself, which is a contradiction. The author notes that this argument depends on the view that all accidents are enduring (bāqiya), which some theologians hold.

Second proof: The condition is either an accident inhering in the world itself or an existent separate from it. The first option is false because an accident depends on a substance (jawhar) for its existence. If the world (a substance) depended for its existence on an accident, then there would be a circular dependency: the accident depends on the substance, and the substance depends on the accident. This is impossible. Therefore, the condition cannot be an accident. The second option (a separate existent) is not explicitly refuted here, but the argument implies it would also face difficulties.

Key Terms
الشرط
البقاء
الإمكان الذاتي
الامتناع الذاتي
العرض
الجوهر
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God can annihilate the world by simply removing a condition, similar to how a light can be turned off by removing electricity.
Correction: This is false because the condition itself either must remain (leading to infinite regress) or cease to exist (which would require a change from essential possibility to impossibility). Additionally, if the condition is an accident, circularity arises between substance and accident.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof against the third division, why is it impossible for the condition not to remain?
A) Because the condition is eternal.
B) Because everything that exists is capable of remaining, otherwise it would change from possible to impossible in itself.
C) Because God cannot remove conditions.
D) Because the condition is necessary for God's existence.

Key Takeaway
God's annihilation of the world cannot be explained by removing a condition, as that leads to either essential impossibility or circular dependency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own dependencies—on a job, a relationship, or your health—do you ever assume that simply removing one condition would solve everything? How does this passage challenge that assumption by revealing the deeper impossibility of such a simple removal?
?
If you believe that God's actions are effortless and simple, how does this passage force you to think more deeply about the intricate logical structures that underpin even the most fundamental acts of creation and annihilation?
?
The passage states that everything that comes into existence is capable of remaining. Reflect on something in your life that you assume will end—why do you assume that? Does this passage change how you see the permanence or impermanence of created things, and how does that affect your attachment to them?

First Objection: Shared Opposition
Sanusi presents the first objection: symmetry of opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the shared nature of opposition between two contraries prevents assigning causal priority to either one in a refutation of annihilation by a contrary.

Core Idea
Opposition is mutual, so neither contrary has causal priority.

The first argument against the claim that God annihilates by creating a contrary is rooted in the symmetry of opposition. Sanusi highlights that the relation of opposition is shared between the two parties, meaning neither can be singled out as the cause of the other's removal. This logical observation undermines any attempt to attribute non-existence to the arrival of a new contrary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage for the first objection once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi states the objection concisely.
2
Understand shared opposition
Reflect on the concept of opposition (مضادة). Since both contraries are opposites, the relation applies equally to both; neither has an intrinsic advantage.
This is a key logical insight.
3
Apply to the refutation
Consider why this makes the claim 'annihilation occurs by creating a contrary' problematic. If opposition is shared, the prior is no more caused by the latter than the latter is repelled by the prior.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أنه تعالى يعدم بواسطة خلق الضد المنافي فنقول هذا أيضاً باطل من وجوه . الأول : وهو أن المضادة أمر مشترك فيه بين الطرفين ، فلم يكن زوال المتقدم ، لأجل طريان المتأخر ، أولى من اندفاع المتأخر لأجل قيام المتقدم الثاني : إن طريان الضد الطارىء ، مشروط بزوال السابق ، فلو كان زوال السابق معللا بطريان الطارىء ، وقع الدور . وهو محال . الثالث : وهو أن هذا الضد، إما أن يحصل حال وجود العالم ، أو بعده ، والأول باطل ، لأنه يقتضي : الجمع بين الضدين . وهو محال . والثاني يقتضي : أن هذا الضد ، إنما حصل بعد عدم الضد السابق . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، امتنع أن يكون ذلك العدم معللاً بهذا الضد ، لأن المتأخر لا يكون علة للمتقدم .
As for the second division: that He, exalted, annihilates by means of creating a contrary opposite. We say: This is also false on several counts. First: that opposition is a matter shared between the two sides; so the removal of the prior is not more due to the arrival of the subsequent than the repulsion of the subsequent is due to the persistence of the prior. Second: that the arrival of the contingent contrary is conditional upon the removal of the prior; so if the removal of the prior were caused by the arrival of the contrary, circularity would occur, and that is impossible. Third: that this contrary either occurs at the time of the world's existence or after it. The first is false because it necessitates the conjunction of two opposites, which is impossible. The second necessitates that this contrary only occurred after the non-existence of the prior contrary; and if this is the case, it is impossible for that non-existence to be caused by this contrary, because the subsequent cannot be a cause of the prior.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that God annihilates the world by creating a contrary (ضد) that is incompatible with it. The author presents three objections in sequence. First, opposition is a relation shared by both parties; thus the removal of the prior entity is not more attributable to the arrival of the later than the repulsion of the later is attributable to the persistence of the prior—so no one cause can be privileged. Second, the arrival of the new contrary itself depends on the prior thing's removal; if the prior's removal were caused by the arrival of the contrary, circularity (دور) would result, which is impossible. Third, if the contrary occurs while the world exists, it would require two opposites to coexist, which is impossible; if it occurs after the world ceases to exist, then the world's non-existence would have already occurred before the contrary, so the non-existence cannot be caused by a subsequent contrary, since the later cannot cause the earlier.

Key Terms
مضادة
ضد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One contrary naturally overcomes and replaces the other, so the new contrary causes the prior's non-existence.
Correction: Sanusi shows that opposition is symmetric; neither side has a built-in causal advantage.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the first objection against annihilation by a contrary?
A) The contrary must be eternal.
B) Opposition is shared between the two sides, so neither has priority.
C) The prior contrary is always stronger.
D) The arrival of a contrary requires a cause.

Key Takeaway
The symmetry of opposition means no contrary can be singled out as the cause of the other's non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you lose something you love, do you automatically blame the new circumstance that took its place? This passage says the opposite is equally plausible—the old state could have repelled the new one. How does this change the way you view loss, and how could you turn that into deeper trust in Allah's control?

?
Think of a recent conflict between two desires in your heart—one you had, one that came later. Which one did you consider the 'cause' of the struggle? This lesson shows that opposition is mutual; neither desire is the rightful victor. Are you willing to submit both to Allah's judgment, without assuming one is more valid?

Second Objection: Circularity
Sanusi presents the second objection: circular causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second objection identifies a circular causality in the claim that annihilation occurs by creating a contrary.

Core Idea
The arrival of a new contrary depends on the prior's removal, causing circularity.

The second argument exposes a logical flaw: the arrival of a new contrary is conditional upon the prior thing's removal. If that removal were itself caused by the arrival, a vicious circle (دور) would arise. Sanusi uses this to show the impossibility of the causal claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic for the second objection and its translation. Notice the conditional relationship mentioned.
2
Identify the conditional
Sanusi states that the arrival of the new contrary is conditional (مشروط) on the prior's removal. Understand what that means: the new contrary can only exist after the prior is gone.
3
Trace the circularity
If the prior's removal is then said to be caused by the new contrary's arrival, we have a loop: the prior removal is needed for the arrival, and the arrival causes the removal. This circularity is impossible.
Circularity is a logical impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أنه تعالى يعدم بواسطة خلق الضد المنافي فنقول هذا أيضاً باطل من وجوه . الأول : وهو أن المضادة أمر مشترك فيه بين الطرفين ، فلم يكن زوال المتقدم ، لأجل طريان المتأخر ، أولى من اندفاع المتأخر لأجل قيام المتقدم الثاني : إن طريان الضد الطارىء ، مشروط بزوال السابق ، فلو كان زوال السابق معللا بطريان الطارىء ، وقع الدور . وهو محال . الثالث : وهو أن هذا الضد، إما أن يحصل حال وجود العالم ، أو بعده ، والأول باطل ، لأنه يقتضي : الجمع بين الضدين . وهو محال . والثاني يقتضي : أن هذا الضد ، إنما حصل بعد عدم الضد السابق . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، امتنع أن يكون ذلك العدم معللاً بهذا الضد ، لأن المتأخر لا يكون علة للمتقدم .
As for the second division: that He, exalted, annihilates by means of creating a contrary opposite. We say: This is also false on several counts. First: that opposition is a matter shared between the two sides; so the removal of the prior is not more due to the arrival of the subsequent than the repulsion of the subsequent is due to the persistence of the prior. Second: that the arrival of the contingent contrary is conditional upon the removal of the prior; so if the removal of the prior were caused by the arrival of the contrary, circularity would occur, and that is impossible. Third: that this contrary either occurs at the time of the world's existence or after it. The first is false because it necessitates the conjunction of two opposites, which is impossible. The second necessitates that this contrary only occurred after the non-existence of the prior contrary; and if this is the case, it is impossible for that non-existence to be caused by this contrary, because the subsequent cannot be a cause of the prior.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that God annihilates the world by creating a contrary (ضد) that is incompatible with it. The author presents three objections in sequence. First, opposition is a relation shared by both parties; thus the removal of the prior entity is not more attributable to the arrival of the later than the repulsion of the later is attributable to the persistence of the prior—so no one cause can be privileged. Second, the arrival of the new contrary itself depends on the prior thing's removal; if the prior's removal were caused by the arrival of the contrary, circularity (دور) would result, which is impossible. Third, if the contrary occurs while the world exists, it would require two opposites to coexist, which is impossible; if it occurs after the world ceases to exist, then the world's non-existence would have already occurred before the contrary, so the non-existence cannot be caused by a subsequent contrary, since the later cannot cause the earlier.

Key Terms
دور
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The new contrary simply appears and forces out the prior, so it causes the prior's non-existence.
Correction: The prior removal must happen first; otherwise the new contrary cannot appear. So the arrival cannot be the cause of the removal without circularity.

Checkpoint
What logical problem does the second objection identify?
A) The new contrary may be eternal.
B) The new contrary cannot coexist with the prior.
C) The claim leads to circular causality.
D) The prior contrary is always stronger.

Key Takeaway
Causal circularity arises when the prior removal is both a condition for and a result of the new contrary's arrival.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever justified a belief by something that itself depends on that same belief? How does Sanusi's argument expose the emptiness in that reasoning, and what will you do to break that cycle today?
?
Think of an area in your spiritual life where you expect change—like a new habit replacing a bad one—without truly letting go of the old. What condition are you ignoring, and what step will you take to meet it?
?
Sanusi shows that circular reasoning is impossible. Where in your own thinking have you gone in circles, avoiding the hard truth that a precondition must be fulfilled? What is one thing you will stop pretending about this week?
