Third Objection: Timing of the Contrary
Sanusi presents the third objection: a dilemma of timing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third objection uses the timing of the contrary to show that annihilation by a contrary is impossible, whether the contrary occurs during or after the world's existence.

Core Idea
The contrary either coexists impossibly or comes too late to be a cause.

The third objection considers two possible times for the new contrary to occur: while the world still exists, or after it has ceased. Both options lead to impossibility. If it occurs during the world's existence, two opposites would have to coexist—absurd. If after, then the world's non-existence already happened before the contrary, so the contrary cannot be its cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic for the third objection and its translation. Identify the two cases discussed.
2
Analyze the first case
The contrary occurring during the world's existence would require two opposites to exist together, which is impossible.
Coexistence of opposites is a logical contradiction.
3
Analyze the second case
If the contrary occurs after the world is gone, then the world's non-existence precedes the contrary. The later cannot cause the earlier.
Cause must precede effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أنه تعالى يعدم بواسطة خلق الضد المنافي فنقول هذا أيضاً باطل من وجوه . الأول : وهو أن المضادة أمر مشترك فيه بين الطرفين ، فلم يكن زوال المتقدم ، لأجل طريان المتأخر ، أولى من اندفاع المتأخر لأجل قيام المتقدم الثاني : إن طريان الضد الطارىء ، مشروط بزوال السابق ، فلو كان زوال السابق معللا بطريان الطارىء ، وقع الدور . وهو محال . الثالث : وهو أن هذا الضد، إما أن يحصل حال وجود العالم ، أو بعده ، والأول باطل ، لأنه يقتضي : الجمع بين الضدين . وهو محال . والثاني يقتضي : أن هذا الضد ، إنما حصل بعد عدم الضد السابق . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، امتنع أن يكون ذلك العدم معللاً بهذا الضد ، لأن المتأخر لا يكون علة للمتقدم .
As for the second division: that He, exalted, annihilates by means of creating a contrary opposite. We say: This is also false on several counts. First: that opposition is a matter shared between the two sides; so the removal of the prior is not more due to the arrival of the subsequent than the repulsion of the subsequent is due to the persistence of the prior. Second: that the arrival of the contingent contrary is conditional upon the removal of the prior; so if the removal of the prior were caused by the arrival of the contrary, circularity would occur, and that is impossible. Third: that this contrary either occurs at the time of the world's existence or after it. The first is false because it necessitates the conjunction of two opposites, which is impossible. The second necessitates that this contrary only occurred after the non-existence of the prior contrary; and if this is the case, it is impossible for that non-existence to be caused by this contrary, because the subsequent cannot be a cause of the prior.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that God annihilates the world by creating a contrary (ضد) that is incompatible with it. The author presents three objections in sequence. First, opposition is a relation shared by both parties; thus the removal of the prior entity is not more attributable to the arrival of the later than the repulsion of the later is attributable to the persistence of the prior—so no one cause can be privileged. Second, the arrival of the new contrary itself depends on the prior thing's removal; if the prior's removal were caused by the arrival of the contrary, circularity (دور) would result, which is impossible. Third, if the contrary occurs while the world exists, it would require two opposites to coexist, which is impossible; if it occurs after the world ceases to exist, then the world's non-existence would have already occurred before the contrary, so the non-existence cannot be caused by a subsequent contrary, since the later cannot cause the earlier.

Key Terms
ضد
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The contrary could gradually overcome the world, so it causes annihilation while the world is fading.
Correction: The two cannot coexist at all; overlapping phases still involve coexistence. The only alternative is the contrary appearing after complete non-existence, which eliminates causality.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the third objection?
A) The contrary must be eternal.
B) Both timing options for the contrary are impossible.
C) The contrary can coexist if it is weaker.
D) The prior contrary is immune to harm.

Key Takeaway
The timing dilemma proves that a contrary cannot cause non-existence, as it either forces impossible coexistence or comes too late to be a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief or habit you hold that you know, deep down, involves a logical contradiction — something you say is true but its implications clash with reality. How does the clarity of this passage's dilemma expose the impossibility of that contradiction? Will you continue to hold it, or will you let the truth dismantle it?
?
Have you ever tried to blame something that happened later for a problem that existed before it? For example, excusing a current failure by pointing to a later event that you claim 'caused' it. How does the principle that 'the subsequent cannot be a cause of the prior' force you to stop shifting responsibility and face the true order of cause and effect in your life?
?
This argument is cold logic, yet it touches the deepest question of why anything exists at all. If God's creative act is not subject to such temporal causal chains (since He is outside time), what does that mean for how you trust His power over your life? Are you still trying to fit God into your own cause-and-effect framework, expecting Him to follow the rules He put in place for creation?

Refutation of Annihilation by Removing a Condition
Author refutes annihilation by removal of a condition and concludes all divisions invalid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why removing a condition cannot cause the world's annihilation and why all proposed modes of annihilation are invalid.

Core Idea
Removing a condition leads to circularity or impossibility.

This passage refutes the third proposed mode of annihilation: that God annihilates the world by removing a condition. The author presents two powerful objections showing this proposal is incoherent, ultimately concluding that none of the modes for annihilation are valid. Understanding these objections sharpens our grasp of causality and divine action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let the flow of the argument sink in before analyzing.
Notice the two 'الوجهين' (two counts) structure.
2
Identify the two objections
The first objection focuses on whether the condition persists or not. The second examines whether the condition is an accident or a separate entity.
Each branch leads to a logical problem.
3
Understand the circularity argument
If the condition is an accident, the substance would depend on the accident, but the accident depends on the substance—circularity. This is a key insight.
Circularity (دور) is a standard refutation tool in kalam.
4
See how all three divisions are ruled out
The passage concludes by recalling that the first two divisions (destruction by the Powerful, arrival of a contrary) were already refuted, and now the third is also false, so annihilation is impossible.
This ties back to the broader argument against the world's perishability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أنه تعالى يعدم العالم بواسطة إعدام الشرط . فنقول : هذا باطل لوجهين : الأول : إن ذلك الشرط، إما أن يكون باقياً ، أو غير باق . فإن كان عاد التقسيم الأول في كيفية عدمه، وإن كان غير باق ، فهذا محال . لأن كل ما دخل في الوجود كان قابلاً للبقاء ، وإلا فيلزم أن ينتقل من الإمكان الذاتي إلى الامتناع الذاتي وهو محال . وهذا الكلام إنما يتقرر على قول من يقول : الأعراض كلها باقية . والوجه الثاني : وهو أن ذلك الشرط، إما أن يكون عرضاً قائماً به ، أو موجوداً مبايناً عنه . والأول باطل ، لأن العرض محتاج في وجوده إلى الجوهر فلو فرضنا كون الجوهر محتاجاً إلى شيء من الأعراض ، لزم الدور ، وهو محال . وأما إن كان شيئاً مبايناً عن الجوهر ، فذلك الشيء إن كان باقياً ، عاد التقسيم في كيفية عدمه، وإن كان غير باق ، كان ذلك باطلا . لما بينا أن كل ما كان قابلاً للوجود ، كان قابلاً للبقاء ، فيثبت بهذه البيانات : أن أجسام العالم لو عدمت ، لكان عدمها، إما أن يكون بإعدام القادر ، أو بطريان الضد ، أو بزوال الشرط . وثبت أن الأقسام الثلاثة باطلة ، فكان القول بفناء العالم بعد وجوده باطلاً والله أعلم .
As for the third division: that He, exalted, annihilates the world by means of removing a condition. We say: This is false on two counts. First: that condition either remains or does not remain. If it remains, the first division regarding its mode of non-existence applies; if it does not remain, this is impossible, because everything that enters into existence is capable of persistence, otherwise it would necessitate a transition from essential possibility to essential impossibility, which is impossible. This statement is only established according to the one who says: all accidents persist. Second: that condition is either an accident subsisting in it, or an entity separate from it. The first is false, because an accident is in need of a substance for its existence; if we supposed the substance to be in need of something from the accidents, circularity would occur, which is impossible. And if it is something separate from the substance, then that thing if it persists, the division regarding its mode of non-existence recurs; and if it does not persist, that is false, because we have shown that everything capable of existence is capable of persistence. So by these proofs it is established that if the bodies of the world were annihilated, their non-existence would be either by the destruction of the Powerful, or by the arrival of a contrary, or by the removal of a condition. And it is established that all three divisions are false, so the statement that the world perishes after its existence is false. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage refutes the third division: annihilation by removal of a condition (شرط). The author gives two objections. First, the condition either persists or does not. If it persists, the problem reverts to the earlier division about how it itself ceases; if it does not persist, that is impossible because anything that comes into existence is capable of persistence—otherwise it would change from possible to impossible, which is absurd. This argument assumes the view that all accidents are persistent. Second, the condition is either an accident inhering in the substance or a separate entity. If an accident, circularity arises because the substance then depends on an accident. If separate, it either persists or not; if not, it is impossible as before. Thus all three proposed modes of annihilation are false, concluding that the world cannot perish after existence.

Key Terms
شرط (condition)
عرض (accident)
جوهر (substance)
دور (circularity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that removing a condition could cause annihilation without further problems.
Correction: The condition itself must be accounted for; if it is an accident it leads to circularity, and if it is separate it either persists or impossibly ceases.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if the condition for the world's existence is itself an accident?
A) It is impossible because accidents cannot exist.
B) It leads to circularity because the substance would depend on an accident.
C) It is possible if the accident is eternal.
D) It leads to an infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
Removing a condition cannot cause annihilation because the condition itself either leads to circularity or entails that it must persist, making all proposed modes of annihilation invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage argues that everything that comes into existence is capable of persistence. Do you truly live with the conviction that your existence is sustained by God's power, not by fragile conditions? How would your daily anxieties change if you deeply internalized this?
?
The author shows that circular dependency between substance and accident is impossible. In your relationship with God, are there any 'conditions' you have placed that create a circular reliance—like only feeling close to God when you are sinless? What would it take to break that cycle?
?
If a condition you thought essential for your faith (e.g., a particular feeling, community, or knowledge) were removed, would your faith remain? What does this lesson challenge you to anchor your faith in instead?

Second Proof: Possibility of Non-Existence Cannot Subsist in the Body
Author argues possibility of non-existence requires a substrate not the body itself

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that the possibility of a body's non-existence cannot subsist in the body itself, and why that possibility must have a distinct substrate.

Core Idea
Possibility of non-existence is a real attribute that cannot inhere in the very existence it would negate.

In this passage, the author advances a subtle proof against the possibility of bodies ceasing to exist. He argues that the possibility of non-existence, if it existed, would be an attribute requiring a substrate. But that substrate cannot be the body's own existence, for the reason he sets out. This proof underscores the necessity of a distinct substrate for modal attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical flow: assumption, attribute, substrate options, contradiction.
The passage is a single continuous argument; pay attention to each conditional.
2
Identify the key moves
Identify the premise (possibility exists before non-existence), the claim that possibility is an attribute needing a substrate, and the two possible substrates. Then focus on why the first option fails.
The author uses a reductio ad absurdum: assuming the substrate is the body leads to contradiction.
3
Reflect on the contradiction
Understand why if the possibility inhered in the body's existence, the body would have to exist at the very moment non-existence occurs, which is impossible.
This contradiction proves that the possibility cannot be in the body itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية على أن عدم الأجسام بعد وجودها : محال . هي أن نقول : لو صح العدم على ذوات الأجسام، لكانت صحة عدمها ، حاصلة قبل حصول عدمها . وتلك الصحة صفة موجودة ، ولا بد لها من محل . فنقول : محلها إما أن يكون هو ذات الجسم ، أو شيء آخر ، والأول محال . لان محل صحة الشيء ، هو الذي يمكن اتصافه بذلك الشيء ، فلو كان محل إمكان عدم الجسم ، هو وجود ذلك الجسم ، لزم أن يبقى وجوده حاصلا حال طريان عدمه، وذلك محال . لأن وجود الشيء يمتنع أن يبقى حال عدمه .
The second proof that the non-existence of bodies after their existence is impossible: it is that we say: If non-existence were possible for the essences of bodies, the possibility of their non-existence would exist before their non-existence occurs. And that possibility is an existent attribute, and it must have a substrate. We say: its substrate is either the essence of the body itself or something else. The first is impossible. Because the substrate of a thing's possibility is that which can be described by that thing. So if the substrate of the possibility of the body's non-existence were the existence of that body, it would necessitate that its existence remains at the time of the arrival of its non-existence, and that is impossible. Because it is impossible for a thing's existence to remain during its non-existence.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof against the possibility of bodies ceasing to exist. It argues that the possibility of non-existence must be an attribute existing before the actual non-existence. This attribute requires a substrate. The substrate cannot be the body itself, because then the possibility of non-existence would inhere in the body's existence, requiring the body to exist at the moment of non-existence, which is contradictory.

Key Terms
صحة العدم (possibility of non-existence)
محل (substrate)
ذات الجسم (essence of the body)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility of non-existence is merely a mental concept and does not require a real substrate.
Correction: The author treats possibility as an existent attribute (صفة موجودة) that must inhere in a real substrate, not just a mental consideration.

Checkpoint
Why can the possibility of a body's non-existence not subsist in the body itself?
A) Because the body is not a substrate for attributes.
B) Because it would require the body to exist while it is non-existent.
C) Because possibility is not an attribute.
D) Because the body is composed of atoms.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of non-existence is an existent attribute that must have a distinct substrate, and cannot be the body's own existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Refuting the Hyle as Substrate for Possibility
Refutation of hyle as substrate for possibility via two reductio arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why postulating a hyle as the substrate for a body's possibility of existence and non-existence leads to logical absurdities such as infinite regress or impossible inherence.

Core Idea
A hyle cannot be the substrate for possibility without logical contradiction.

In this passage, the author continues a proof by considering an alternative: that the substrate for a body's possibility of existence and non-existence is its hyle. He shows this leads to two impossible consequences, thus closing off this option. The argument is a classic example of reductio ad absurdum in Islamic philosophical theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Recognize that this passage is part of a larger proof. The author is refuting the idea that a hyle (underlying matter) could serve as the substrate for a body's possibility.
The second proof is examining this as a possible alternative.
2
Identify the two objections
Read the two reasons why affirming a hyle for the body is impossible. The first reason has two subcases: if the hyle is extended, it leads to infinite regress; if not extended, it leads to an impossible inherence. The second reason alludes to established proofs against the hyle.
The text cuts off before completing the second reason.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider how these objections prevent infinite regress and maintain the argument for the existence of a Necessary Being. The hyle cannot be a neutral substrate without generating contradictions.
This reinforces the need for a non-physical cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني أيضاً باطل لأن الشيء الذي يقوم به صحة وجود الشيء ، وصحة عدم ذلك الشيء : هو هيولاه ، فلو حصل للجسم شيء يقوم به صحة وجوده ، وصحة عدمه، لزم إثبات هيولى للجسم ، وذلك باطل من وجهين : الأول : إن هيولى الجسم ، إن كان متحيزاً لزم التسلسل ، وإن لم يكن متحيزاً ، لزم حلول المتحيز في محل ، لا حصول له في ذلك المكان ، وفي تلك الجهة . وذلك محال . الثاني : إن بتقدير أن يكون للجسم هيولى ، فقد ثبت بالدلائل
The second is also false. Because that thing which subsists in the possibility of a thing's existence and the possibility of that thing's non-existence is its hyle. So if there were for the body something in which subsist the possibility of its existence and the possibility of its non-existence, it would necessitate the affirmation of a hyle for the body, and that is false on two counts: First: If the hyle of the body is extended, it leads to infinite regress; and if it is not extended, it necessitates the inherence of an extended thing in a substrate that has no location in that place and direction, which is impossible. Second: On the supposition that the body has a hyle, it has been established by proofs
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage continues the second proof by examining the alternative: that the substrate of the possibility is something other than the body itself. That something would be the hyle (matter) of the body. But affirming a hyle for the body is impossible for two reasons. First, if the hyle is itself extended (spatially located), then it would require yet another hyle, leading to infinite regress; if it is not extended, then an extended body would inhere in a non-extended substrate, which is absurd. Second, even if we suppose a hyle for the body, proofs have established its impossibility (the text cuts off).

Key Terms
هيولى (hyle/matter)
متجيز (extended)
تسلسل (infinite regress)
حلول (inherence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think of hyle as a neutral substrate for possibility, but the author shows it leads to logical contradictions.
Correction: The hyle cannot be a substrate because assuming it leads to either infinite regress or impossible inherence.

Checkpoint
According to the text, what problem arises if the hyle of a body is itself extended?
A) It would require another hyle, leading to infinite regress.
B) It would cause circularity.
C) It would make the hyle identical to the body.
D) It would mean the hyle is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Postulating a hyle as the substrate for a body's possibility leads to either infinite regress or impossible inherence, thus it cannot be the substrate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever treat your own habits or circumstances as a 'neutral substrate' that limits what Allah can do in your life? Where might you be assuming a fixed foundation that actually leads to logical or spiritual contradictions? Will you examine and surrender that assumption today?
?
The author shows that postulating a hyle leads to absurdity. In your relationship with Allah, are there any beliefs or practices you hold that, if examined closely, require an infinite regress of justifications or an impossible inherence of the divine in the physical? Are you willing to let go of them to avoid self-contradiction?
?
If something extended cannot inhere in something non-extended, what does that imply about how you can seek closeness to Allah? Is there any part of your worship that you try to anchor in something other than Allah Himself, expecting it to hold? How can you redirect your reliance to the One who needs no substrate?

The Impossibility of Annihilation of Bodies
Concludes the first proof that the world cannot perish after existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the annihilation of bodies is impossible according to the threefold division of possible causes of non-existence.

Core Idea
Bodies cannot perish because all possible means of perishing are impossible.

This lesson concludes an intricate proof that the world, having come into existence, cannot subsequently be annihilated. The author reduces all conceivable ways a body could cease to exist to three categories, then shows each is impossible. Understanding this argument is key to the Islamic theological position on the permanence of the world after its creation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three ways for non-existence
Read the passage and find the three possible causes for bodies to become non-existent as listed by the author.
Look for the enumeration: إما أن يكون بإعدام القادر، أو بطريان الضد، أو بزوال الشرط.
2
Understand the elimination reasoning
Although the full reasoning is not repeated here, the passage states that each of the three divisions has been proven false in the preceding discussion. Consider why each might be impossible: a powerful agent cannot annihilate a substance, contraries cannot affect incorporeal substances, and conditions for existence are not removable.
The commentary explains the logical elimination.
3
Grasp the conclusion
The author concludes that since all three possible ways are false, the claim that the world perishes after existence is false. This upholds the doctrine that the created world endures permanently.
The conclusion is: فكان القول بفناء العالم بعد وجوده باطلاً.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
محال . وأما إن كان شيئاً مبايناً عن الجوهر ، فذلك الشيء إن كان باقياً ، عاد التقسيم في كيفية عدمه، وإن كان غير باق ، كان ذلك باطلا . لما بينا أن كل ما كان قابلاً للوجود ، كان قابلاً للبقاء ، فيثبت بهذه البيانات : أن أجسام العالم لو عدمت ، لكان عدمها ، إما أن يكون بإعدام القادر ، أو بطريان الضد ، أو بزوال الشرط . [ وثبت أن ] الأقسام الثلاثة باطلة ، فكان القول بفناء العالم بعد وجوده باطلاً [ والله أعلم ]
Impossible. And if it is something distinct from the substance, then that thing: if it is lasting, the division [of how it becomes non-existent] returns; and if it is not lasting, that is false. Because we have shown that everything that is receptive to existence is receptive to permanence. So it is established by these evidences: that if the bodies of the world became non-existent, their non-existence would either be by the removal of the powerful, or by the occurrence of a contrary, or by the removal of a condition. [And it is established that] the three divisions are false, so the statement that the world perishes after its existence is false. [And God knows best.]
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage concludes an earlier argument by eliminating all possible ways a body could cease to exist. The author first considers a hypothetical object distinct from the substance (jawhar) of the body. If that object is lasting, the question of how it becomes non-existent reverts to the same division; if it is not lasting, that is false because anything receptive to existence is receptive to permanence. Therefore, the only possible ways for bodies to perish are: (1) by the removing action of a powerful agent (iʿdām al-qādir), (2) by the occurrence of a contrary (ṭuryān al-ḍidd), or (3) by the removal of a condition (zawāl al-sharṭ). All three are shown to be invalid in the preceding discussion, so the claim that the world perishes after existing is false.

Key Terms
جوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that the world is eternal and never had a beginning.
Correction: The argument only proves that bodies cannot perish after existence; it does not address whether they had a beginning. The world is created ex nihilo but endures permanently.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what are the three possible ways for the non-existence of bodies?
A) By the removal of the powerful, by the occurrence of a contrary, or by the removal of a condition.
B) By annihilation, by corruption, or by transformation.
C) By the will of God, by natural decay, or by accident.
D) By the passage of time, by the loss of form, or by the separation of parts.

Key Takeaway
The argument concludes that the world, having been created, cannot subsequently be annihilated because all possible means of perishing are ruled out.

The Substrate Cannot Be the Body's Existence
Sanusi eliminates one possible substrate for possibility of non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the substrate of the possibility of non-existence cannot be the body's own existence, as this would entail a logical contradiction.

Core Idea
Non-existence cannot be possible because its substrate would require existence to persist during non-existence.

In this proof, Sanusi begins by showing that if non-existence were possible for bodies, the possibility itself would be an attribute needing a substrate. He then eliminates the first candidate—the body's own existence—because it leads to an absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the initial premise and the conclusion that the substrate cannot be the body's existence.
2
Identify the key terms
Find the terms 'صحة العدم' and 'محل'. Understand that 'ṣiḥḥat al-ʿadam' is an attribute requiring a substrate.
The term 'محل' (substrate) is crucial for the argument.
3
Understand the contradiction
Ask yourself why the substrate cannot be the body's existence. Trace the logical impossibility: the existence would have to remain during non-existence.
This is a simple but powerful reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية على أن عدم الأجسام بعد وجودها : محال . هي أن نقول : لو صح العدم على ذوات الأجسام، لكانت صحة عدمها ، حاصلة قبل حصول عدمها . وتلك الصحة صفة موجودة ، ولا بد لها من محل . فنقول : محلها إما أن يكون هو ذات الجسم ، أو شيء آخر ، والأول محال . لان محل صحة الشيء ، هو الذي يمكن اتصافه بذلك الشيء ، فلو كان محل إمكان عدم الجسم ، هو وجود ذلك الجسم ، لزم أن يبقى وجوده حاصلا حال طريان عدمه، وذلك محال . لأن وجود الشيء يمتنع أن يبقى حال عدمه ، والثاني أيضاً باطل لأن الشيء الذي يقوم به صحة وجود الشيء ، وصحة عدم ذلك الشيء : هو هيولاه ، فلو حصل للجسم شيء يقوم به صحة وجوده ، وصحة عدمه. لزم إثبات هيولى للجسم ، وذلك باطل من وجهين : الأول : إن هيولى الجسم ، إن كان متحيزاً لزم التسلسل ، وإن لم يكن متحيزاً ، لزم حلول المتحيز في محل ، لا حصول له في ذلك المكان ، وفي تلك الجهة . وذلك محال . الثاني : إن بتقدير أن يكون للجسم هيولى ، فقد ثبت بالدلائل الظاهرة : أن هيولى الجسم ، يمتنع خلوها عن الجسمية إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : إن تلك الهيولى ، إن كانت قابلة للعدم ، افتقرت إلى هيولى أخرى ، ولزم التسلسل . وإن لم تكن قابلة للعدم ، وثبت أنه يمتنع خلوها عن الصورة الجسمية ، فحينئذ يلزم أن الجسم لا يقبل العدم.
The second proof that the non-existence of bodies after their existence is impossible is that we say: If non-existence were valid for the essences of bodies, then the validity of their non-existence would be existent before the occurrence of their non-existence. And that validity is an existing attribute, and it must have a substrate. So we say: its substrate is either the essence of the body or something else. The first is impossible because the substrate of the validity of something is that which can be described by that thing. So if the substrate of the possibility of non-existence of the body were the existence of that body, it would necessitate that its existence remains during the occurrence of its non-existence, and that is impossible because the existence of a thing cannot remain during its non-existence. The second is also false because the thing that sustains the validity of existence of a thing and the validity of non-existence of that thing is its matter (hayula). So if there occurred for the body a thing that sustains the validity of its existence and the validity of its non-existence, it would necessitate affirming matter for the body, and that is false from two aspects: First: if the matter of the body is extended (mutahayyiz), then an infinite regress would follow; and if it is not extended, then it would necessitate the indwelling of an extended thing in a substrate that has no location in that place and that direction, and that is impossible. Second: If we suppose that the body has matter, it has been established by clear proofs that the matter of the body cannot be free from corporeality. If this is established, then we say: that matter: if it is receptive to non-existence, it would need another matter, and an infinite regress would follow; and if it is not receptive to non-existence, and it is established that it cannot be free from the corporeal form, then at that point it necessitates that the body does not admit non-existence.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed proof against the possibility of bodies ceasing to exist. It begins by stating the second proof (al-ḥujja al-thāniya) and then constructs the argument: If non-existence could apply to bodies, then before non-existence actually happens there must be a 'validity' or possibility of non-existence (ṣiḥḥat al-ʿadam) that is an existing attribute requiring a substrate (maḥall). The substrate cannot be the body's own existence, because that would require the body's existence to persist simultaneously with its non-existence—a contradiction. The only other candidate is matter (hayūlā), the substrate that sustains both the possibility of existence and non-existence. But positing matter for a body leads to two absurdities: (1) If matter is extended (mutaḥayyiz), an infinite regress of matters follows; if it is not extended, an extended thing would inhere in a non-extended thing, which is impossible. (2) Even if matter existed, it cannot be free from corporeality (jismiyya). If that matter itself could become non-existent, it would require another matter, leading to infinite regress. If it cannot become non-existent and is inseparable from corporeal form, then the body cannot admit non-existence at all. Therefore, bodies cannot perish.

Key Terms
صحة العدم
محل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why cannot the substrate of the possibility of non-existence be the body's own existence?
A) Because the body's existence is not an attribute.
B) Because that would require the body to exist and not exist simultaneously.
C) Because the body's existence is not receptive to non-existence.
D) Because the body's existence is the same as its substance.

Key Takeaway
The possession of non-existence cannot be a property of the body's own existence because that would entail existence and non-existence coexisting, which is absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your body cannot logically cease to exist, what does that mean for how you should treat your physical self? Are you living as if your body is a temporary shell or as if it has an enduring reality?
?
The proof shows that existence is built into the very essence of bodies. How does that challenge your view of death and the afterlife? Does it make you more certain that your existence will continue after death, or does it raise new questions?
?
Sanusi argues that non-existence cannot be an attribute of the body itself. If your existence is inseparable from you, what responsibility does that place on you to seek the truth about your ultimate purpose?

The Matter Hypothesis Leads to Absurdities
Sanusi refutes the matter (hayula) hypothesis with two objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why positing matter (hayula) as the substrate for the possibility of non-existence leads to logical absurdities, thereby proving that bodies cannot cease to exist.

Core Idea
Matter cannot be the substrate for possibility either—it leads to infinite regress or impossible inherence.

Having ruled out the body's essence, Sanusi turns to the alternative: matter (hayula). He shows that affirming matter for a body leads to two impossible consequences, ultimately closing the door on the possibility of non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the two arguments against matter.
2
Analyze the first argument
Why is matter either causing infinite regress or impossible inherence? Understand the dilemma.
The key terms are 'متحيز' and 'تسلسل'.
3
Analyze the second argument
Even if matter existed, it cannot be free from corporeality, leading to another dilemma: infinite regress or non-receptivity to non-existence.
This completes the proof that bodies cannot admit non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The second proof that the non-existence of bodies after their existence is impossible is that we say: If non-existence were valid for the essences of bodies, then the validity of their non-existence would be existent before the occurrence of their non-existence. And that validity is an existing attribute, and it must have a substrate. So we say: its substrate is either the essence of the body or something else. The first is impossible because the substrate of the validity of something is that which can be described by that thing. So if the substrate of the possibility of non-existence of the body were the existence of that body, it would necessitate that its existence remains during the occurrence of its non-existence, and that is impossible because the existence of a thing cannot remain during its non-existence. The second is also false because the thing that sustains the validity of existence of a thing and the validity of non-existence of that thing is its matter (hayula). So if there occurred for the body a thing that sustains the validity of its existence and the validity of its non-existence, it would necessitate affirming matter for the body, and that is false from two aspects: First: if the matter of the body is extended (mutahayyiz), then an infinite regress would follow; and if it is not extended, then it would necessitate the indwelling of an extended thing in a substrate that has no location in that place and that direction, and that is impossible. Second: If we suppose that the body has matter, it has been established by clear proofs that the matter of the body cannot be free from corporeality. If this is established, then we say: that matter: if it is receptive to non-existence, it would need another matter, and an infinite regress would follow; and if it is not receptive to non-existence, and it is established that it cannot be free from the corporeal form, then at that point it necessitates that the body does not admit non-existence.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed proof against the possibility of bodies ceasing to exist. It begins by stating the second proof (al-ḥujja al-thāniya) and then constructs the argument: If non-existence could apply to bodies, then before non-existence actually happens there must be a 'validity' or possibility of non-existence (ṣiḥḥat al-ʿadam) that is an existing attribute requiring a substrate (maḥall). The substrate cannot be the body's own existence, because that would require the body's existence to persist simultaneously with its non-existence—a contradiction. The only other candidate is matter (hayūlā), the substrate that sustains both the possibility of existence and non-existence. But positing matter for a body leads to two absurdities: (1) If matter is extended (mutaḥayyiz), an infinite regress of matters follows; if it is not extended, an extended thing would inhere in a non-extended thing, which is impossible. (2) Even if matter existed, it cannot be free from corporeality (jismiyya). If that matter itself could become non-existent, it would require another matter, leading to infinite regress. If it cannot become non-existent and is inseparable from corporeal form, then the body cannot admit non-existence at all. Therefore, bodies cannot perish.

The Dimension Distinction Argument
Proves that bodies must exist because dimensions are indispensable for spatial distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the possibility of distinguishing spatial directions like above and below proves that dimensions (and therefore bodies) must exist.

Core Idea
Spatial distinction proves dimensions exist; dimensions prove bodies exist.

This short proof uses a thought experiment: imagine the three dimensions (length, width, depth) do not exist. Could we still tell above from below, front from back, right from left? The answer, by self-evident reason, shows that dimensions are indispensable for such distinctions. This leads to the necessary existence of bodies, as argued earlier.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the literal translation. Notice the conditional structure: if...then...
The proof is very compressed; each step is crucial.
2
Understand the assumption
The proof begins by supposing the non-existence of the three dimensions. Consider what this state would entail.
Dimensions here are the three spatial extensions.
3
Analyze the two possibilities
The author then asks: would spatial distinctions (above/below, etc.) still be possible? Identify the two outcomes and their consequences.
The impossibility of distinction is rejected as false to self-evidence.
4
Connect to bodies
The proof concludes that if dimensions exist, then bodies exist. Recall the earlier discussion linking dimensions to physical bodies.
This step relies on the previous definition of body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إنا إذا فرضنا عدم الأبعاد الثلاثة ، فبعد ذلك ، هل يتميز جانب الفوق ، عن جانب التحت ، والقدام عن الوراء ، واليمين عن الشمال ؟ فإن لم يحصل هذا الامتياز ، فذلك باطل في بديهة العقل ، كما قررناه . وإن حصل هذا الامتياز ، فالأبعاد موجودة . وإذا كانت الأبعاد موجودة ، كانت الأجسام موجودة. على ما قررناه في المقالة المتقدمة
The third proof: When we suppose the non-existence of the three dimensions, then after that, is the side of above distinguished from the side of below, and the front from the back, and the right from the left? If this distinction does not occur, then that is false according to the self-evidence of the intellect, as we have established. And if this distinction occurs, then the dimensions are existent. And if the dimensions are existent, then the bodies are existent, according to what we have established in the preceding discourse.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This short proof relies on the self-evident nature of spatial differentiation. The author posits: assume the three dimensions (abʿād) are non-existent. Then ask whether we can still distinguish directions like above/below, front/back, right/left. If such distinctions are impossible, that contradicts clear self-evidence (badīhat al-ʿaql). If they are possible, then the dimensions must exist, because distinction implies something real. And since dimensions exist, bodies (ajsām) also exist, as established earlier. The argument thus shows that the non-existence of dimensions is inconceivable, so bodies cannot cease to exist.

Key Terms

امتياز
بديهة العقل
فوق

وراء
يمين
شمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Spatial distinctions could exist without any real dimensions, such as in a void.
Correction: The proof argues that if we can distinguish above from below, there must be some real basis—namely dimensions—making the distinction possible. A void without dimensions would not allow such differentiation.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if spatial distinctions (like above and below) still exist after supposing the non-existence of dimensions?
A) The dimensions must be non-existent.
B) The dimensions must be existent.
C) The intellect is deceived.
D) The world is not eternal.

Key Takeaway
Spatial distinctions are self-evident and prove that dimensions, and thus bodies, necessarily exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

In what specific action can you respond to that truth this week?
?
The author calls the impossibility of spatial distinction 'false by self-evidence.' Where in your own life have you been ignoring or denying something that is self-evident about Allah's creation? What would it cost you to accept it fully today?

Proof That Body Does Not Accept Non-Existence via Matter
The author argues that matter cannot be stripped of form, so body cannot cease.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the body cannot undergo annihilation because its matter must always be united with corporeal form, and any alternative leads to infinite regress.

Core Idea
Matter cannot be stripped of form, so the body is indestructible.

This lesson presents a key kalam argument for the impossibility of the annihilation of bodies. The argument hinges on the nature of matter (hayula) and its necessary conjunction with corporeal form. By examining the logical consequences of matter being receptive to non-existence, the author demonstrates that the body must be eternal. Let's read the passage carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own wording introduce the dilemma.
Notice the compact style of the argument.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The argument splits into two possibilities: matter can accept non-existence, or it cannot. Trace each branch to its logical outcome.
The first branch leads to infinite regress; the second to the conclusion.
3
Connect the conclusion
Understand how the second branch leads directly to the statement that the body does not accept non-existence. Ask yourself why this proof matters for the broader discussion of bodily annihilation.
This argument is a common proof for the eternal nature of bodies in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الظاهرة : أن هيولى الجسم ، يمتنع خلوها عن الجسمية إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : إن تلك الهيولى ، إن كانت قابلة للعدم ، افتقرت إلى هيولى أخرى ، ولزم التسلسل . وإن لم تكن قابلة للعدم ، وثبت أنه يمتنع خلوها عن الصورة الجسمية ، فحينئذ يلزم أن الجسم لا يقبل العدم.
The evident: that the matter (hayula) of a body, it is impossible for it to be devoid of corporeality (jismiyya). If this is established, we say: that matter, if it is receptive to non-existence, it would need another matter, and infinite regress would follow. And if it is not receptive to non-existence, and it is established that it is impossible for it to be devoid of the corporeal form, then at that point it follows that the body does not accept non-existence.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the impossibility of the annihilation of the body based on its matter. The author states that it is evident that matter cannot exist without corporeal form. Then he presents a dilemma: either matter is capable of ceasing to exist or not. If it is capable, then its non-existence would require another matter to receive its form, leading to an infinite regress. If it is not capable of non-existence, and it must always have a form, then the body composed of that matter cannot be destroyed. Thus the body is eternal.

Key Terms
هيولى
جسم
عدم
تسلسل
صورة جسمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes the actual existence of prime matter (hayula) as a real entity.
Correction: The argument does not require the real existence of prime matter; it is a hypothetical proof based on logical implications.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion if matter is receptive to non-existence?
A) It leads to infinite regress
B) It proves matter is eternal
C) It shows matter cannot accept form
D) It proves the body is destructible

Key Takeaway
The body cannot be annihilated because its matter must always be united with corporeal form; otherwise, an infinite regress would result.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument suggests that your body, as a composite of matter and form, cannot be annihilated. If your body cannot cease to exist, then what is the significance of death in your faith? How does this perspective change the way you view your physical existence and its purpose?
?
This proof depends on the premise that matter cannot be stripped of form. Consider something in your life that you thought could be removed or changed without affecting its essence. What does this passage teach you about the inseparable nature of essential qualities? How might you apply that to your relationship with Allah, who is the Creator of forms?
?
The author uses a logical dilemma to reach a conclusion. Reflect on a personal dilemma you have faced where both options seemed impossible. How did you resolve it? Does the method of this argument—eliminating the absurd option—help you think more clearly about difficult questions in your faith? What step are you willing to take today to trust that Allah's creation is logically coherent?

The Good Design Argument for the World's Eternity
Argument for world's eternity via good design, with objections and responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the world's perfect design implies its eternity, and the responses to objections about changing divine wisdom and the transience of individuals.

Core Idea
A perfectly made world cannot be destroyed by a good God.

This passage presents a classic theological argument: the world is so well-made that only an evil being would destroy it. Since God is all-good, He will not destroy it, and no one else can. But critics raise objections: what if God destroys it with a different wisdom? And what about the constant change we see in individuals? The author responds by clarifying the nature of divine action and the cause of sublunary change.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: argument, objection, answer.
The argument is concise; pay attention to the logical connectors.
2
Identify the main argument
Find the core claim: the world is good, only an evil person would destroy it, God is not evil, so He won't destroy it, and no one else can.
This is a simple syllogism.
3
Analyze the first objection and answer
The objector says God could destroy it with a different wisdom. The author distinguishes between creatures whose actions depend on conditions and God who is the source of all events.
For God, benefit and harm do not shift because He is not subject to external conditions.
4
Analyze the second objection and answer
The objector points to the transience of individuals. The author explains that individual changes are caused by celestial motions, but these motions cannot themselves be caused by other motions ad infinitum.
This avoids infinite regress, so the world as a whole remains eternal.
5
Reflect on the overall logic
Consider how the answers protect the main argument. The world's design necessitates eternity, not just temporary existence.
The argument assumes God's goodness is constant and His power is unique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة في بيان أن العالم يجب أن يكون أبدياً : يقال : العالم حسن ، جيد الصنعة . وما كان لا يبطله ولا ينقضه إلا شرير ، وإله العالم ليس بشرير ، فلا ينقضه . وظاهر أن غيره لا يقدر على نقضه ، فوجب أن لا يبطل هذا العالم ، ولا ينتقض أبداً. فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه تعالى : صنعه الحكمة مخصوصة ، ثم إنه ينقضه ويبطله الحكمة أخرى . وأيضاً : فهذا ينتقض بأشخاص العالم . وأجاب عن الأول : بأن ما كان مصلحة ، قد ينقلب مفسدة في حق من كانت أفعاله موقوفة على حدوث شرائط ، لا يكون حدوثها من جهته. أما الباري تعالى لما كان حدوث كل الحوادث منه، وبإيجاده. فهذا التفاوت في حقه : محال . وأجاب عن السؤال الثاني : بأن حدوث الأشخاص في هذا العالم ، إنما كان بسبب أن حدوث هذه الحوادث السفلية ، مشروط بحدوث التغيرات الحاصلة ، بسبب اختلاف الحركات الفلكية . فلو قلنا مثل هذا المعنى في حدوث الحركات الفلكية ، لزم افتقار حدوث تلك الحوادث الفلكية إلى أفلاك أخرى ، ولزم الذهاب إلى ما لا نهاية له . وهو محال .
The fourth argument in demonstrating that the world must be eternal: It is said: The world is good, well-made. And that which none but an evil person would annul and destroy it, and the God of the world is not evil, so He does not destroy it. And it is apparent that no other is able to destroy it, so it is necessary that this world not be invalidated and not be destroyed ever. If they say: why is it not permissible to say that He, exalted, made it with a particular wisdom, then He destroys it and invalidates it with another wisdom? Also, this is contradicted by the individuals of the world. And he answered the first: that a thing which is beneficial may become harmful in the case of one whose actions are dependent on the occurrence of conditions which do not occur from him. As for the Creator, exalted, since the occurrence of all events is from Him and by His bringing into existence, this disparity is impossible with respect to Him. And he answered the second question: that the occurrence of individuals in this world is because the occurrence of these sublunary events is conditioned by the occurrence of changes that happen due to the variation of celestial motions. If we say a similar meaning for the occurrence of celestial motions, then the occurrence of those celestial events would require other celestial spheres, and that would lead to an infinite regress, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage presents a theistic argument for the eternity of the world. The author states that the world is well-made and only an evil being would destroy it. Since God is not evil, He will not destroy it, and no one else can. Then an objection is raised: God may have created it with one wisdom and destroy it with another, and objection from the transient individuals (people) in the world. The first answer: for creatures, benefit may turn to harm due to changing conditions outside their control, but for God, all events come from Him, so such disparity is impossible. The second answer: the coming to be of individuals is due to celestial motions, which if also required other motions, would lead to infinite regress. Thus the world as a whole must be eternal.

Misconception: God's wisdom might change, so He could destroy the world.
Correction: The author argues that for God, benefit does not turn into harm because all events originate from Him, so there is no external condition to cause a change in wisdom.

The Wisdom-of-Creation Dilemma for Eternity
Dilemma argument for the eternity of the world

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a dilemma based on the goodness and wisdom of creation forces the conclusion that the world must be eternal.

Core Idea
If creation was wise, its destruction would be evil; otherwise change leads to absurdity.

This passage presents a sophisticated theological argument that uses the very fact of creation's goodness to argue for its permanence. The author constructs a dilemma: either the world's creation was good and wise or not. If it was not good, that would contradict God's attributes. If it was good, then that goodness must persist—otherwise we face either uncaused change or an infinite regress. Let's trace the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Notice the tight logical structure: a question, a division, then two main branches with sub-branches.
The author uses the term 'تقسيم' (division) explicitly.
2
Identify the initial division
The first split: was creation correct/wise/good or not? The author dismisses 'not' immediately, forcing the premise that creation was good.
This is a common kalam move to force an opponent into a corner.
3
Follow the main dilemma
Given creation was good, has that goodness remained or not? If it remained, destroying the world would be evil. If it did not remain, then change occurred—either by itself or by the agent.
Focus on the options under 'did not remain': self-change or agent-change.
4
Analyze the self-change branch
If good turned into evil by itself, that allows events to occur without any cause, which destroys causality and opens the door to all events happening without an effect.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: the opponent cannot accept uncaused events.
5
Consider the agent-change branch
If the agent (God) changed it, then the same dilemma applies to that change: was that change good or not? This leads to an infinite regress or forces the original goodness to remain.
The argument closes the loop: the only consistent option is that the goodness remains, so the world must be eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : قالوا : خلق هذا العالم . هل كان صواباً وحكمة ، وخيراً ، أم لا ؟ والقسم الثاني باطل، وإلا لكان يجب على الخير الرحيم الحكيم أن لا يفعل ، لكنه قد فعله فيبقى الأول ، فنقول : والوجه الذي بإعتبارائه كان صواباً وحكمة وخيراً ، هل بقي أو لم يبق ؟ فإن بقي كان نقض هذا العالم وإبطاله شراً . وكان فاعله شريراً . وإن لم يبق ، فذلك التغير حصل لذاته أو بذلك الفاعل ؟ فإن حصل لذاته ، فقد جوزتم أن ينقلب الخير شراً لذاته من غير فاعل . وإذا جوزتم ذلك في بعض الحوادث، فجوزوا مثله في البقية . وذلك يفتح عليكم باب تجويز حدوث الحوادث ، لا لمؤثر أصلا . وإن كان ذلك لأجل أن ذلك الفاعل قلبه من كونه خيراً ، إلى كونه شريراً ، عاد التقسيم الأول فيه .
The fifth argument: They said: The creation of this world: was it correct, wise, and good, or not? The second division is false, otherwise the good, merciful, wise would have to not do it, but He has done it, so the first remains. We say: and the aspect by which it was correct, wise, and good: has it remained or not? If it remained, then destroying and invalidating this world would be evil, and its doer would be evil. And if it did not remain, then that change occurred by itself or by that agent? If it occurred by itself, then you have allowed that good turns into evil by itself without an agent. And if you allow that in some events, then allow the like in the rest. And that opens for you the door of allowing the occurrence of events without any effect at all. And if that was because that agent changed it from being good to being evil, then the first division returns upon it.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage presents a dilemma argument for the eternity of the world. The author asks whether the creation of the world was good and wise. If not, God (who is good and wise) would not have created it, but He did, so it must have been good and wise. Then the question: has that aspect of goodness and wisdom remained or not? If it remained, then destroying the world would be evil, implying an evil destroyer. If it did not remain, then the change occurred either by itself or by the agent (God). If by itself, then good can turn into evil without any cause, which opens the door to all events happening without an effect, destroying causality. If by God, then the same dilemma applies to that change, leading to an infinite regress or absurdity. Thus the world must remain as it is, i.e., eternal.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument only works if we assume creation was good, which is arbitrary.
Correction: The argument forces the opponent to accept creation was good by appealing to God's attributes: if it were not good, God would not have done it. So the premise is not arbitrary but derived from the opponent's own assumptions.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The wisdom-of-creation dilemma shows that denying the eternity of the world leads to either evil destruction, uncaused events, or an infinite regress—all unacceptable, so the world must be eternal.

?

?
The argument ends with the world being eternal—meaning it cannot be destroyed. How does that confront your fear of loss or your attachment to this life? Are you living as if this world will last forever, or are you preparing for what is truly eternal?

Sixth Argument: The World as Beneficence Must Continue
The author presents the sixth argument linking beneficence to divine perfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth argument that the world is a benefaction and why God must continue it.

Core Idea
God's beneficence cannot be cut off by incapability, ignorance, or stinginess.

This lesson presents a concise argument from divine beneficence. The author contends that since the world was created as a benefaction for needy creatures, stopping it would imply a defect in God. As we read, consider how this argument relies on three possible flaws that are impossible for God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical structure: premise, conditional, conclusion.
2
Identify the three reasons for cutting off beneficence
The passage lists three possible reasons. Find each one and understand how they relate to human behavior, then consider why they cannot apply to God.
3
Reflect on why each reason is impossible for God
Think about divine attributes: God is all-powerful (not incapable), all-knowing (not ignorant), and all-generous (not stingy). The commentary will clarify this.
This step connects the three reasons to God's perfection.
4
Consider the conclusion
The argument concludes that cutting off beneficence is impossible for God, so the world must continue. Ask yourself: does this argument presuppose that the world is still beneficial? How would you respond?
The common misunderstanding addresses this.

Therefore the world must remain eternally.

Seventh Argument: Time Cannot Cease Without Contradiction
A logical proof that time cannot cease without contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical contradiction that arises if time were to cease.

Core Idea
If time stops, its 'after' requires time to exist — a contradiction.

This passage presents a concise logical refutation of the possibility that time could ever cease. The author argues that any attempt to think of time stopping leads to an unavoidable contradiction. Let's follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the English translation. Notice the compact logical structure.
2
Identify the three logical parts
The argument has a premise, an implication, and a conclusion. Find each one in the text.
Look for 'if... then...' structure.
3
Understand the contradiction
Why does the 'after' relation require time? How does that lead to time being both existent and non-existent?
Posteriority is a temporal concept.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Why does this argument imply that time is eternal? Think about what would be needed for time to cease.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : إن الزمان لو انقطع ، لكان عدمه بعد وجوده وتلك البعدية بالزمان. فيلزمكم : كون الزمان موجوداً ، حال كونه معدوماً . وذلك محال .
The seventh argument: If time were to cease, then its non-existence would be after its existence, and that posteriority is by time. So it would be necessary for you: time existing while it is non-existent. And that is impossible.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage presents a logical refutation of the possibility of time ceasing. If time were to stop, its non-existence would occur after its existence. But the 'after' relation itself requires time to exist. Thus time would have to exist at the moment it is non-existent, a contradiction. Therefore time cannot cease, and by extension the world (which exists in time) is eternal.

Correction: The argument shows that the very idea of 'after cessation' implicitly requires time, thus time cannot cease.

B) Time would exist while non-existent
C) Time would be empty
D) Time would have a beginning

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of time ceasing demonstrates that time is eternal, and any change in time requires time itself to persist.

Galen's Empirical Argument from Celestial Stability
Galen's empirical argument against celestial decay

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Galen's empirical argument that the heavens show no signs of decay, and how this is used to argue for their indestructibility.

Core Idea
If the heavens were decaying, we would see the signs—but we don't.

Galen, the famous physician and philosopher, offered an argument based on observation: if the celestial spheres and stars were subject to decay, we should see gradual signs of deterioration. Since no such signs have ever been observed, and historical records confirm their constancy, the heavens must be indestructible. This passage presents that reasoning directly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical structure: conditional premise (if...then), empirical observation, historical testimony, conclusion.
The passage is a complete argument from Galen.
2
Identify the conditional premise
Galen states: 'If the celestial spheres and planets were to be annihilated and destroyed, then it would be necessary that signs of ruin and destruction appear in them little by little.' This is the if-then statement.
3
Consider the empirical evidence
Galen then says we have not sensed any such signs, nor has any historical record reported a change from perfection to deficiency. This is the evidence against the consequent.
This is a modus tollens argument: if P then Q, not Q, therefore not P.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Galen concludes that the claim (that the heavens can be destroyed) is invalid. Understand why this argument was influential in debates about the eternity of the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : ما ذكره جالينوس ، فقال : « لو كانت الأفلاك والكواكب تبطل وتنهدم ، لوجب أن يظهر فيها آثار الخراب والانهدام قليلا قليلاً ، لكنا لم نحس بذلك البتة، ولم يصل إلينا خبر من التواريخ الماضية ، بأن أحوال الأفلاك والكواكب ، قد تغيرت من الكمال إلى النقصان . فيبطل القول بذلك
The eighth argument: What Galen mentioned, for he said: If the celestial spheres and planets were to be annihilated and destroyed, then it would be necessary that signs of ruin and destruction appear in them little by little, but we have not sensed that at all, nor has any news reached us from past histories that the states of the celestial spheres and planets have changed from perfection to deficiency. Therefore the claim regarding that is invalidated.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage presents an empirical argument from Galen for the eternity of the heavens. Galen argued that if the celestial spheres and stars were decaying, we would gradually observe signs of deterioration over time. However, we do not observe any such signs, and historical records never report any change from perfection to deficiency. Therefore, the claim that the heavens can be destroyed is false. This argument relies on empirical observation and historical testimony.

Checkpoint

A) They would disappear suddenly
B) Signs of ruin would appear gradually
C) They would stop moving
D) They would fall to earth

Key Takeaway
Galen's argument uses the absence of observed decay and historical constancy to conclude that the heavens are indestructible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Fifth Argument: Morality of Creation and Annihilation
Argues that if creation was good, its destruction would be evil, leading to contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth kalam argument against the temporal annihilation of the world, which highlights a moral contradiction.

Core Idea
If creation is good, its destruction would be evil—this forces a logical dilemma.

Sanusi presents the fifth proof, which examines the moral status of the world's creation. The argument challenges the idea that the world could be annihilated while remaining consistent with God's goodness and wisdom. This passage uses a series of conditional questions to expose an inevitable contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial dilemma
The argument begins by asking whether the world's creation was good. If not, a merciful God would not have created it. So creation must be good.
This sets up the premise that creation is morally positive.
2
Follow the branching logic
The argument then examines whether the goodness remains or disappears. Each branch leads to a problem: if it remains, destruction is evil; if it disappears, the change must be explained.
Pay attention to the two options: essence change or agent change.
3
See the final absurdity
If goodness changes by itself, then events can occur without cause, which is absurd. If the agent changes it, the same dilemma repeats. Thus, annihilation is impossible.
This concludes the proof by contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : قالوا : خلق هذا العالم . هل كان صواباً وحكمة ، وخيراً ، أم لا ؟ والقسم الثاني باطل، وإلا لكان يجب على الخير الرحيم الحكيم أن لا يفعل ، لكنه قد فعله فيبقى الأول ، فنقول : والوجه الذي بإعتباره كان صواباً وحكمة وخيراً ، هل بقي أو لم يبق ؟ فإن بقي كان نقض هذا العالم وإبطاله شراً . وكان فاعله شريراً . وإن لم يبق ، فذلك التغير حصل لذاته أو بذلك الفاعل ؟ فإن حصل لذاته ، فقد جوزتم أن ينقلب الخير شراً لذاته من غير فاعل . وإذا جوزتم ذلك في بعض الحوادث، فجوزوا مثله في البقية . وذلك يفتح عليكم باب تجويز حدوث الحوادث ، لا لمؤثر أصلا . وإن كان ذلك لأجل أن ذلك الفاعل قلبه من كونه خيراً ، إلى كونه شريراً ، عاد التقسيم الأول فيه .
The fifth proof: They said: The creation of this world: was it correct, wise, and good, or not? The second division is false, otherwise the Good, Merciful, Wise would have had to not do it, but He did it, so the first remains. So we say: The aspect by which it was correct, wise, and good—did it remain or not? If it remained, then annulling this world and abolishing it would be evil, and its doer would be evil. If it did not remain, did that change occur in its essence or by that agent? If it occurred in its essence, then you have allowed that good can turn into evil by itself without an agent. And if you allow that in some events, then allow the like in the rest. And that opens for you the door of allowing the occurrence of events with no source at all. If that was because that agent changed it from being good to being evil, then the first division returns to it.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument (al-hujja al-khamisa) regarding the moral consistency of creation and destruction. It begins by asking whether the creation of the world was good, wise, and correct. The opponent cannot deny that, for if it were not good, a benevolent God would have refrained, but He did create. So the creation is good. Then the argument examines whether the quality of goodness remains or disappears. If it remains, destroying the world would be evil and its agent evil. If the goodness disappears, the change must be either in the essence of the world or caused by the agent. If by essence, then goodness can turn into evil without cause, implying events can occur without any source, which is absurd. If the agent changes it from good to evil, the original question recurs—was that change good or evil? Thus the argument forces a contradiction.

Key Terms
صواب
حكمة
خير
نقض
إبطال
شر
فاعل
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies God's power to annihilate the world.
Correction: It does not deny power; it shows that annihilation would contradict God's wisdom and goodness, making it impossible in a moral sense.

Checkpoint
What does the fifth argument conclude about the possibility of the world's annihilation?
A) It is impossible because God is good.
B) It leads to a contradiction about the goodness of the world.
C) It is possible if the world changes its essence.
D) It is necessary for the afterlife.

Key Takeaway
The fifth argument shows that admitting the world's annihilation leads to a contradiction regarding its inherent goodness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If goodness is an objective attribute that cannot arbitrarily change, how does that affect the way you evaluate your own actions? Are there areas where you treat good as if it could become evil without consequence—like excusing minor sins because 'it doesn't matter'?
?
The argument forces you to see that destroying goodness is evil. When you have the opportunity to tear down something good—a relationship, a reputation, a truth—do you pause to consider that you might be acting against God's wisdom? What would it take for you to submit to the permanence of His goodness today?

The Seventh Argument: Time Cannot Cease
Argues that time ceasing leads to a logical contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the seventh argument proves that time cannot cease to exist on pain of logical contradiction.

Core Idea
Time cannot cease because its end would require time to exist.

This passage presents a crisp logical argument from the Islamic kalam tradition. The author argues that the very concept of time ending is self-contradictory. Let's unpack the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the compact structure: a conditional, a consequence, and a conclusion.
2
Identify the key claim
The central claim is that time (zaman) cannot cease (yanqati'). Find the premise and the conclusion.
3
Trace the logical steps
Break the argument: 1) Suppose time ceases. 2) Then its nonexistence is after its existence. 3) 'Afterness' (ba'diyya) is a temporal relation, requiring time. 4) Therefore time would exist and not exist simultaneously.
4
Understand the contradiction
The contradiction is that time would be both existent (to provide the 'after') and nonexistent (having ceased). That is impossible (muhal).
5
Reflect on the significance
This argument defends the eternity of time (in the sense of its necessary continuity) as a foundation for further theological proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : إن الزمان لو انقطع ، لكان عدمه بعد وجوده وتلك البعدية بالزمان. فيلزمكم : كون الزمان موجوداً ، حال كونه معدوماً . وذلك محال .
The seventh proof: If time were to cease, then its nonexistence would be after its existence, and that afterness is by time. So it follows for you: time being existent while being nonexistent. And that is impossible.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh argument (al-hujja al-sabi'a). It argues against the possibility of time ending. If time (zaman) were to stop, its nonexistence would occur after its existence. But the concept of 'after' (ba'diyya) is itself a temporal relation, requiring time to exist. Thus we would have a situation where time is both existent (to measure the afterness) and nonexistent (after ceasing), which is a contradiction. Therefore, time cannot cease.

Key Terms
زمان
انقطع
بعد
وجود
عدم
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time can cease without contradiction.
Correction: The argument shows that if time ceases, its afterness requires time to exist, creating a contradiction. Thus time cannot cease.

Checkpoint
What contradiction does the seventh argument point to if time were to cease?
A) Time would be both finite and infinite.
B) Time would exist and not exist simultaneously.
C) The world would not have a beginning.
D) God would not be eternal.

Key Takeaway
Time cannot cease because its end implies a temporal afterness, which makes time simultaneously existent and nonexistent—a logical impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Time cannot cease—it is a necessary continuum. If you truly believe this, then what is the ultimate consequence for your own mortality? Do you live as though your time is infinite or as though it will end? Which belief aligns with this proof, and how will you change your priorities today?
?
The argument forces you to see that even imagining the end of time implies time continues. How does this affect your trust in Allah's promise of an eternal afterlife? In what specific way will you prepare for that endless time starting now?
?
You exist in time right now, reading this. If time cannot cease, then every moment you spend is part of a never-ending continuum. What fleeting thing are you chasing that will not survive this continuum? Name one attachment you will release today because of this truth.
