Galen's Argument and Al-Razi's Objections
Galen's argument from no observed decay, with Al-Razi's two objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Galen's argument for the indestructibility of the heavens based on lack of observed decay, and Al-Razi's two objections to that argument.

Core Idea
Absence of observed decay does not prove impossibility of destruction.

Galen, the famous Greek physician, offered an empirical argument: if the heavens were perishable, we should see gradual signs of decay. Since none are observed, they must be eternal. But Muhammad ibn Zakariyya al-Razi, the Persian philosopher-physician, raised two incisive objections that challenge the logical force of Galen's observation. This passage presents the argument and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read Galen's argument
Read the first part of the Arabic passage where Galen states his argument from lack of observed decay. Notice the reasoning: if decay, then gradual signs; no signs; therefore no decay.
Galen relies on the principle that corruption is a gradual process.
2
Understand Al-Razi's first objection
Al-Razi points out that Galen's argument only holds if decay is gradual. If destruction occurs instantaneously, no gradual signs appear. Consider the logical point: the argument assumes a specific mode of decay.
This objection attacks the necessity of gradualness.
3
Understand Al-Razi's second objection
Even if decay is gradual, the celestial bodies may be so solid that the amount of decay over recorded history is imperceptible, like ruby. The great distance further reduces perception. This undermines the argument from silence.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author notes that these objections are well-founded. Reflect on how empirical arguments can be logically challenged without denying the possibility of the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : ما ذكره جالينوس ، فقال : « لو كانت الأفلاك والكواكب تبطل وتنهدم ، لوجب أن يظهر فيها آثار الخراب والانهدام قليلا قليلاً ، لكنا لم نحس بذلك البتة، ولم يصل إلينا خبر من التواريخ الماضية ، بأن أحوال الأفلاك والكواكب ، قد تغيرت من الكمال إلى النقصان . فيبطل القول بذلك . واعترض محمد بن زكريا الرازي على هذه الحجة من وجهين : الأول : وهو أن هذا إنما يلزم ، إذا كان عدم الأفلاك والكواكب عدماً ذبولياً ، على الإستمرار. أما بتقدير أن يكون عدمها واقعاً دفعة ، فإن هذا غير لازم . والثاني : إن بتقدير أن يكون عدمها عدماً ذبولياً ، إلا أنه يحتمل أن تكون تلك الأجرام صلبة. فالذبول الذي يحصل فيه، في المقدار الذي يصل إلينا تواريخها ، يكون قليلاً لا يمكن الإحساس به . ألا ترى أن الياقوت جرم صلب ، ولا بد وأن يتحلل منه شيء ، إلا أنه لما كان قوي الصلابة ، لم يكن مقدار ما يتحلل منه محسوساً في أعمارنا . فالأفلاك والكواكب أصلب من الياقوت، والبعد بيننا وبينها كثير جداً. فلا يمتنع أن يكون عدم الإحساس بما يحصل فيها من الذبول ، كان لهذا السبب . وهذان الاعتراضان على كلام جالينوس ، واقع حسن .
The eighth proof: What Galen mentioned, he said: 'If the celestial spheres and stars were to perish and collapse, then signs of decay and collapse would necessarily appear in them little by little, but we have never sensed that at all, nor has any news reached us from past histories that the states of the spheres and stars have changed from perfection to deficiency. So the claim of that is invalidated.' And Muhammad ibn Zakariyya al-Razi objected to this proof in two ways: The first: that this only follows if the nonexistence of the spheres and stars is a gradual decay continuously. But on the assumption that their nonexistence occurs all at once, then this does not follow. The second: Even assuming their nonexistence is a gradual decay, it is possible that those bodies are solid. The decay that occurs in them, in the amount that reaches us in their histories, would be too little to be perceived. Do you not see that ruby is a solid body, and something inevitably decomposes from it, but because it is strongly solid, the amount that decomposes from it is not perceptible in our lifetimes. The spheres and stars are harder than ruby, and the distance between us and them is very great. So it is not impossible that the lack of perception of the decay occurring in them is due to this reason. And these two objections to Galen's statement are well-founded.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth argument (al-hujja al-thamina) from Galen (Jalinus). Galen argues that if the heavens and stars were to perish, we should see gradual signs of decay, but no such signs are observed, nor do historical records show any change from perfection to deficiency. Thus, the claim of annihilation is false. Then Muhammad ibn Zakariyya al-Razi (the famous physician and philosopher) raises two objections. First: the argument only applies if decay is gradual (dhabulian 'ala al-istimrar); if destruction happens instantaneously (daf'a), no gradual signs appear. Second: even if decay is gradual, the celestial bodies may be so solid that the amount of decay over human history is imperceptible, just as ruby decays imperceptibly due to its hardness. Given the great distance and extreme solidity of the spheres, lack of observation is not evidence of impossibility. The author notes that these objections are well-founded (waqi' hasan).

Key Terms
جالينوس
أفلاك
كواكب
خراب
انهدام
ذبول
صلابة
ياقوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Al-Razi's objections prove the heavens are indeed perishable.
Correction: The passage only critiques the logical force of Galen's empirical argument; it does not establish the contrary view.

Checkpoint
What is Al-Razi's first objection to Galen's argument?
A) The heavens cannot decay because they are made of ether.
B) Decay might be instantaneous, not gradual.
C) The historical records are incomplete.
D) Galen was not a reliable observer.

Key Takeaway
The absence of observed decay in the heavens does not logically entail their indestructibility, as Al-Razi's objections show.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed something about your faith, character, or life was permanent simply because you haven't seen it change? What might be decaying imperceptibly while you remain unaware?
?
Galen relied on historical records to claim the heavens never changed. In what areas of your life have you trusted your limited experience or received narratives over the possibility of unseen realities?
?
Al-Razi shows that absence of evidence is not evidence of absence. What spiritual truth have you neglected because you haven't 'sensed' it? Will you now seek it with greater humility, knowing your perception is limited?

The Ninth Argument: Circular Nature Prevents Decay
Sanusi presents a two-part argument for celestial spheres' immunity to decay.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ninth argument's logical structure: why celestial spheres' natural circular motion precludes rectilinear inclination and thus prevents decay.

Core Idea
Circular nature blocks rectilinear motion, making decay impossible.

Sanusi now presents his ninth argument, which draws on the observed perpetuity of circular motion in the heavens. The argument proceeds in two steps: first establishing that the spheres' circular motion is natural, then showing that this natural circularity excludes any rectilinear inclination, thereby ruling out disintegration. This is a careful causal argument about inherent natures.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how the argument is structured with two 'Amma bayan' sections.
The passage is a complete logical unit.
2
Identify the two proofs
The passage first proves that celestial spheres are circular by nature (based on the perpetuity of circular motion). Then it proves that this natural circularity excludes rectilinear inclination, because two opposite motions cannot coexist in one body.
The second proof uses the principle of non-contradiction.
3
Connect to the conclusion
Understand that without rectilinear inclination, the spheres cannot undergo tearing, healing, separation, or fragmentation—i.e., they are naturally immune to decay.
This conclusion applies only to natural processes, not to divine power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : إن الأفلاك مستديرة بطباعها ، والمستدير بالطبع لا يقبل الميل المستقيم ، وإذا لم يكن قابلاً للميل المستقيم ، امتنع كونه قابلا للخرق والالتئام ، والتفرق والتمزق . أما بيان أنها مستديرة بطباعها : وذلك لأن الحركة المستديرة ، لو لم تكن لها طباعية ، لما كانت دائمة ولا أكثرية. وحيث كان الأمر كذلك ، علمنا أنها طباعية. وأما بيان أنها لما كانت مستديرة بطباعها ، لم يكن فيها ميل مستقيم : لأن الميل المستقيم يوجب الصعود والنزول . والميل المستدير يوجب الانحراف عن الصعود والنزول . فلو حصل في الشيء الواحد : ميل طبيعي إلى الحركة المستقيمة والمستديرة ، لزم اجتماع المتنافيين في الشيء الواحد وهو محال . فيثبت : أنه ليس في الأجرام الفلكية : ميل مستقيم . وإن كان كذلك ، لم تكن تلك الأجسام قابلة للتفرق والتمزق والبطلان .
The ninth proof: The celestial spheres are circular by their nature, and what is naturally circular does not accept rectilinear inclination. And if it is not receptive to rectilinear inclination, it is impossible for it to be receptive to tearing and healing, separation and fragmentation. As for the demonstration that they are circular by nature: that is because circular motion, if it were not natural, would not be perpetual nor predominant. And since it is that way, we know it is natural. As for the demonstration that because they are circular by nature, they have no rectilinear inclination: because rectilinear inclination entails ascent and descent. Circular inclination entails deviation from ascent and descent. So if there were in one thing a natural inclination to both rectilinear and circular motion, there would be a conjunction of two opposites in one thing, which is impossible. So it is established that there is no rectilinear inclination in celestial bodies. And if that is so, then those bodies are not receptive to separation, fragmentation, and annihilation.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth argument (al-hujja al-tasi'a). It has two main parts: first, proving that celestial spheres are circular by nature (tabi'a), and second, showing that their circular nature precludes rectilinear inclination, which in turn prevents them from being subject to decay. The proof that they are naturally circular: if circular motion were not natural, it would not be perpetual or dominant; since it is perpetual and dominant, it must be natural. The proof that circular nature excludes rectilinear inclination: rectilinear motion involves ascent and descent, while circular motion involves curvature away from these. Having both natural tendencies in the same body would require contradictory properties (social al-mutanafiyayn), which is impossible. Therefore celestial bodies have no rectilinear inclination, meaning they cannot be rent, healed, separated, or fragmented—i.e., they cannot undergo annihilation.

Key Terms
أفلاك
مستديرة
طبيعة
ميل مستقيم
حركة مستديرة
خرق
التئام
تفرق
تمزق
متنافيين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims celestial spheres are absolutely indestructible, even by God.
Correction: The argument only claims that their natural constitution makes decay impossible by natural processes; it does not deny God's power to destroy them.

Checkpoint
Why does the ninth argument claim that celestial spheres cannot disintegrate?
A) Because they are made of a fifth element.
B) Because they have no natural rectilinear motion.
C) Because God directly preserves them.
D) Because they are infinitely large.

Key Takeaway
The ninth argument shows that celestial spheres' natural circular motion excludes any rectilinear inclination, making natural decay impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The celestial spheres have a nature that prevents decay and contradiction. In your own life, are there contradictory inclinations that you allow to coexist—such as a desire for both worldly pleasure and spiritual elevation? Which one truly defines your nature?
?
Sanusi argues that two opposites cannot coexist in a single subject. Think of a time when you tried to serve two masters—Allah and something else. Did that tear you apart? What would it take for you to align your inclination wholly toward Allah?
?
Just as the spheres’ natural motion is constant and orderly, your deeds reveal your inner nature. If your actions are examined, do they point to a consistent direction toward your Creator, or are they scattered in many directions? What needs to change in your heart to make your life's motion circular and focused on Allah?

The Ninth Proof: Celestial Spheres Are Circular and Imperishable
He proves that celestial spheres are circular by nature and thus imperishable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the circular nature of celestial spheres implies their imperishability through the absence of straight inclination.

Core Idea
Circular motion by nature excludes straight inclination, so spheres cannot be broken.

This passage presents the ninth proof for the eternity of the world, focusing on the nature of celestial spheres. The proof argues that since the spheres are naturally circular, they cannot possess straight inclination, and thus cannot undergo processes like perforation or tearing. Let's examine the logical steps carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the full argument.
Notice the two-part structure: establishing circular nature, then deducing no straight inclination.
2
Understand the first sub-proof
Why does the author say circular motion is natural? Because it is perpetual and predominant.
Natural motions are those that occur persistently, not accidentally.
3
Understand the second sub-proof
How does circular nature exclude straight inclination? Because straight and circular inclinations are opposites and cannot coexist in one body.
This uses the principle of non-contradiction.
4
Connect to imperishability
Without straight inclination, the spheres cannot be perforated, torn, or separated—all of which require straight motion.
Thus they are incorruptible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : إن الأفلاك مستديرة بطباعها ، والمستدير بالطبع لا يقبل الميل المستقيم ، وإذا لم يكن قابلاً (٢) للميل المستقيم ، امتنع كونه قابلا للخرق والالتئام ، والتفرق والتمزق . أما بيان أنها مستديرة بطباعها : وذلك لأن الحركة المستديرة ، لو لم تكن لها طباعية ، لما كانت دائمة ولا أكثرية. وحيث كان الأمر كذلك ، علمنا أنها طباعية. وأما بيان أنها لما كانت مستديرة بطباعها ، لم يكن فيها ميل مستقيم : لأن الميل المستقيم يوجب الصعود والنزول . والميل المستدير يوجب الانحراف عن الصعود والنزول . فلو حصل في الشيء الواحد : ميل طبيعي إلى الحركة المستقيمة والمستديرة ، لزم اجتماع المتنافيين في الشيء الواحد وهو محال . فيثبت : أنه ليس في الأجرام الفلكية : ميل مستقيم . وإن كان كذلك ، لم تكن تلك الأجسام قابلة للتفرق والتمزق والبطلان .
The ninth proof: that the spheres are circular by their nature, and what is circular by nature does not accept straight inclination, and if it does not accept straight inclination, it is impossible for it to accept perforation and healing, and separation and tearing. As for the demonstration that they are circular by their nature: that is because circular motion, if it were not natural, would not be perpetual nor predominant. And since it is so, we know it is natural. And as for the demonstration that when they are circular by their nature, there is no straight inclination in them: because straight inclination necessitates ascent and descent. And circular inclination necessitates deviation from ascent and descent. So if there occurred in one thing both a natural inclination to straight motion and to circular motion, it would necessitate the conjunction of two opposites in one thing, which is impossible. So it is established that there is no straight inclination in celestial bodies. And if that is so, those bodies are not receptive to separation, tearing, and annihilation.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth proof for the eternity of the world, arguing that celestial spheres are imperishable because they are circular by nature. The proof has two parts. First, it demonstrates that celestial spheres are circular by nature: circular motion is perpetual and predominant, indicating it is natural rather than forced. Second, it shows that such naturally circular bodies cannot possess straight inclination. Straight inclination involves up and down; circular inclination deviates from that. A single body cannot have both natural inclinations because they are opposite. Thus, celestial bodies have no straight inclination, meaning they cannot be penetrated, healed, separated, or torn—all processes that involve straight motion. Therefore, they are imperishable.

مستدير (circular)
طبيعة (nature)
ميل مستقيم (straight inclination)
خرق (perforation)
التئام (healing)
تفرق (separation)
تمزق (tearing)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'circular by nature' means the spheres themselves are made of circles.
Correction: It means their motion and intrinsic tendency are circular, not that they are geometric circles in substance.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

?

The World's Eternity Does Not Imply Independence
Opening of the twelfth treatise stating the thesis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the central thesis of the twelfth treatise: that the world's eternity does not make it independent of its cause.

Core Idea
Eternity does not equal self-sufficiency.

This lesson introduces the twelfth treatise of al-Maṭālib al-ʿĀliyah, which tackles a key objection against the need for a creator. Some argue that if the world is eternal, it has no need of a cause. The author counters that eternity does not entail independence. We will examine this thesis and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the English translation. Notice the concise formulation of the thesis.
The passage is the opening of the treatise; it states the claim to be proven.
2
Identify the key terms
Identify the three key terms: al-ʿālam (the world), azalī (eternal), and al-muʾaththir (the effective cause). Reflect on their meanings.
3
Understand the logical claim
The passage asserts that eternity (azaliyya) does not logically imply independence (istighnāʾ) from the cause. Ask yourself: why might someone think otherwise?
This thesis sets up the proof that the world, even if eternal, is contingent upon a creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الثانية عشر
بيان أن كون العالم أزليا ، لا يقتضي استغناؤه عن المؤثر
The twelfth treatise: An explanation that the fact that the world is eternal does not necessitate its independence from the effective cause (al-muʾaththir).
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the twelfth treatise (al-maqālah al-thāniyah ʿashar). It presents the central thesis to be defended: the proposition that the world (al-ʿālam) being eternal (azaliyyan) does not imply (lā yaqtaḍī) that it is independent (istighnāʾ) of the effective cause (al-muʾaththir). The author is countering a common assumption that eternal things are self-sufficient. Instead, he asserts that even eternality requires a cause. The term 'al-muʾaththir' refers to the agent or cause that brings about existence. This sets the stage for a proof that the world, even if considered eternal, is still contingent upon a creator. The phrase 'lā yaqtaḍī' indicates that the logical consequence does not follow; eternity does not entail independence.

Key Terms
العالم (al-ʿālam)
أزلي (azali)
المؤثر (al-muʾaththir)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Eternal things do not need a cause because they have always existed.
Correction: This passage asserts that eternity does not imply independence from the cause; even an eternal entity requires a cause for its existence.

Checkpoint
What is the central claim of the twelfth treatise as stated in this passage?
A) The world is eternal and therefore has no cause.
B) The world's eternity does not imply its independence from the effective cause.
C) The world is not eternal and requires a cause.
D) The world's eternity proves the existence of a cause.

Key Takeaway
The world's eternity does not imply independence from Allah; even an eternal world requires a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the entire universe, even if eternal, is utterly dependent on Allah at every moment, what does that mean for your own sense of self-sufficiency? In what area of your life do you secretly believe you can manage without Him?
?
Imagine you truly internalized that nothing—not even your most stable habits, relationships, or achievements—is independent of Allah’s continuous creation. How would that change your morning routine today?
?
The world’s eternity does not make it independent. What ‘eternal’ pattern in your life—a long-held sin, a chronic attitude, a deep-seated assumption—have you treated as self-sustaining and therefore beyond the reach of Allah’s transformative power? What would it cost you to admit it needs His intervention right now?

The Argument that an Eternal World Would Be Independent of a Cause
The proponents of creation argue that an eternal world would be independent of a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that an eternal world would be independent of a creator, as presented by proponents of creation.

Core Idea
If the world were eternal, it would need no cause.

This passage presents a key argument from those who argue for the world's creation. They claim that an eternal world would be independent of any cause, because what is eternal exists forever and cannot be caused without redundancy. They support this with a logical proof and concrete examples. We will examine their reasoning and the examples they provide.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the full argument presented by the proponents of creation.
Notice the structure: claim, proof, examples.
2
Identify the main claim
The claim is that if the world were eternal (qadīm), it would be independent (ghani) of an effect (muʼaththir), leading to denial of the Creator.
3
Understand the logical proof
The proof defines the eternal as that which exists forever; if its existence were caused, that would be bringing into existence what already exists (ījād al-mawjūd), which is impossible.
This is the core logical argument.
4
Examine the examples
Three examples illustrate the point: a building needs a builder only at origin; henna dye persists after the cause; a stone's compulsive force continues after generation.
Each example shows that a thing can remain without the cause that originated it.
5
Consider the implication
This argument is used to support the claim that an eternal world would need no Creator, thus denying the Maker.
The passage ends with a summary statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان أن كون العالم أزليا لا يقتضي استغناء عن المؤثر
اعلم أن القائلين بالحدوث قالوا: لو كان العالم قديماً، لكان غنياً عن المؤثر، وذلك يوجب نفي الصانع بالكلية. واحتجوا على صحة قولهم: بأنه لو كان قديماً، لكان غنياً عن المؤثر. وأما الحجة: فهي أنهم قالوا: القديم هو الذي يكون موجوداً أبداً. فلو قلنا: إن وجوده حصل لسبب، لزم منه إيجاد الموجود وتحصيل الحاصل، وهو محال. وأما المثال: فهو أنهم قالوا: البناء يحتاج إلى الباني حال حدوثه، وأما حال بقائه فلا يحتاج إليه. وأيضاً: إن من خضب يده بالحناء، فإنه يحتاج في أول الأمر، إلى إلصاق الحناء باليد، ثم إن اللون يبقى بعد زوال ذلك السبب. وأيضاً: إن من رمى حجراً إلى فوق، فإن المذهب الصحيح أن ذلك القاسر يحدث في ذلك الجسم قوة قسرية، تحرك ذلك الجسم إلى فوق. فتلك القوة حال حدوثها تحتاج إلى المؤثر، وأما حال بقائها، فإنها غنية عن المؤثر. فهذا جملة كلام القوم.
Statement that the world's being eternal does not necessitate independence from the effect. Know that the proponents of creation said: If the world were eternal, it would be independent of the effect, and that necessitates the total denial of the Maker. And they argued for the correctness of their statement: that if it were eternal, it would be independent of the effect. As for the proof: it is that they said: The eternal is that which exists forever. If we say that its existence occurred due to a cause, then that would necessitate bringing into existence what already exists and obtaining what is already obtained, which is impossible. As for the example: it is that they said: The building needs the builder at the time of its origination, but at the time of its remaining it does not need it. Also: Whoever dyes his hand with henna needs at the first instance to attach the henna to the hand, then the color remains after that cause ceases. Also: Whoever throws a stone upward, the correct doctrine is that the compeller generates in that body a compulsive force that moves that body upward. That force at the time of its origination needs the effect, but at the time of its remaining it is independent of the effect. This is the summary of their statement.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage presents a rational argument from the proponents of the world's creation (al-ḥudūth). They argue that if the world were eternal (qadīm), it would be independent of an effect (muʼaththir), leading to the denial of the Creator. They support this claim with both a logical proof (ḥujja) and an example (mithāl). The proof defines the eternal as that which exists forever; if its existence were caused, that would be a case of bringing into existence what already exists (ījād al-mawjūd), which is impossible. The example illustrates that a building requires a builder only at its origin, not during its continuation; likewise, henna dye needs initial application but persists after the cause is gone; similarly, a stone thrown upward receives a compulsive force only at the start, but the force continues independently. Thus, an eternal world would need no cause, negating the Maker. The passage ends with 'This is the summary of their statement.'

Key Terms

حادث (ḥādith)
مؤثر (muʼaththir)
غنى (ghinā)
حجة (ḥujja)
مثال (mithāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument conclusively proves that an eternal world would need no cause.
Correction: The argument relies on a specific definition of 'eternal' as self-sufficient; the definition itself is not proven and is contested.

Checkpoint
What is the logical proof used by the proponents of creation to show that an eternal world would be independent of an effect?
A) The eternal is defined as that which exists forever; if it were caused, that would imply bringing into existence what already exists.
B) The eternal is defined as that which is created; if it were caused, it would be contingent.
C) The eternal must have a beginning; otherwise it cannot exist.
D) The eternal world would need a cause at every moment, not just at origin.

Key Takeaway
The proponents of creation argue that an eternal world would be causally independent because, by definition, the eternal exists forever and cannot be caused without redundancy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if God is only the 'builder' who started the universe and now leaves it to run on its own? What would change in your life today if you truly believed that without God's constant support you would cease to exist?
?
In your daily life, do you act as if you are independent of God, relying on your own strength and plans? What practical step can you take today to acknowledge your absolute dependence on Him?
?
The proof says that causing what already exists is impossible. Yet how often do you try to 'add' to what God has given you as if you could create your own success? Identify one area where you are acting as your own source, and commit to attributing every good thing to God alone.

Two Kinds of Cause and the Agreement Between Philosophers and Theologians
Razi divides causes and notes theological agreement on eternal effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between an agent by choice and a necessitating cause, and how this distinction underlies the disagreement between philosophers and theologians on the eternity of the world.

Core Idea
The type of cause determines whether an eternal effect is possible.

This passage from Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a foundational distinction in Islamic philosophy and theology: the division of efficient causes into two types. Razi then shows how both philosophers and theologians agree on certain principles but diverge in their conclusions because of their different conceptions of God's nature. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping the classical debate on the eternity of the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the technical terms.
2
Identify the two types of cause
Note the definitions of 'agent by choice' and 'necessitating cause' as given by Razi.
3
Understand the points of agreement
Both groups agree on the impossibility of an eternal effect from a free agent, but possibility from a necessitating cause.
4
See how beliefs about God lead to disagreement
Philosophers see God as necessitating by essence, theologians see Him as a free agent, leading to opposite conclusions.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi calls this the rational discourse; appreciate how a simple classification leads to major theological positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن المؤثر على قسمين: أحدهما: الفاعل المختار، والثاني: العلة الموجبة. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول: إن الفلاسفة والمتكلمين اتفقوا على أن إسناد الأثر القديم إلى الفاعل المختار محال، واتفقوا على أن إسناد الأثر القديم إلى العلة القديمة غير ممتنع. إلا أن الفلاسفة لما اعتقدوا أن إله العالم موجب بالذات، لا جرم قالوا: لا يمتنع إسناد العالم القديم إليه. وأما المتكلمون لما اعتقدوا أن إله العالم فاعل مختار، لا جرم اتفقوا على أنه يمتنع إسناد الأثر القديم إليه. هذا هو الكلام المعقول في هذا الباب.
And know that the efficient cause is of two types: one: the agent by choice (al-fa'il al-mukhtar), and the second: the necessitating cause (al-illah al-mujibah). If this is established, then we say: The philosophers and the theologians agreed that attributing an eternal effect to the agent by choice is impossible, and they agreed that attributing an eternal effect to the eternal cause is not impossible. However, because the philosophers believed that the God of the world is necessitating by essence (mujib bi-dhatihi), they necessarily said: it is not impossible to attribute the eternal world to Him. And as for the theologians, because they believed that the God of the world is an agent by choice, they necessarily agreed that it is impossible to attribute an eternal effect to Him. This is the rational discourse in this chapter.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage classifies efficient causes (al-mu'aththir) into two kinds: the agent by choice (al-fa'il al-mukhtar), who acts freely and voluntarily, and the necessitating cause (al-illah al-mujibah), which necessitates its effect necessarily and eternally. After establishing this division, Razi notes a point of agreement between the philosophers (falasifa) and the theologians (mutakallimun): both agree that attributing an eternal effect to a free agent is impossible (muhal), because a free agent's act is temporally originated. They also agree that attributing an eternal effect to an eternal necessitating cause is not impossible (ghayr mumtani'). However, they differ in their conclusions because of their distinct conceptions of God. The philosophers believe that God is 'necessitating by essence' (mujib bi-dhatihi), meaning that His nature necessarily results in the world, so they affirm the possibility of an eternal world (al-'alam al-qadim) being from Him. The theologians, on the other hand, believe that God is an agent by choice (fa'il mukhtar), who acts freely, and therefore they affirm that attributing an eternal effect to Him is impossible. Razi concludes that this is the rational discourse (al-kalam al-ma'qul) on the topic.

Key Terms
مؤثر
فاعل مختار
علة موجبة
موجب بالذات
فلاسفة
متكلمون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All eternal effects are impossible regardless of the type of cause.
Correction: The text shows that eternal effects are impossible only from an agent by choice, but possible from a necessitating cause.

Checkpoint
What do philosophers and theologians agree on regarding the eternal effect?
A) It can be attributed to both an agent by choice and a necessitating cause.
B) It cannot be attributed to an agent by choice but can be attributed to a necessitating cause.
C) It can only be attributed to an agent by choice.
D) It cannot be attributed to any cause at all.

Key Takeaway
The classification of causes into agent by choice and necessitating cause determines the possibility of an eternal effect, and this underlies the philosophical vs theological view of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own actions, do you see yourself as a 'free agent' (فاعل مختار) or more like a 'necessitating cause' driven by impulses? How does that affect how you understand God's actions towards you?
?
The philosophers and theologians agreed on the principle but differed on God's nature. Where in your own belief system have you held onto a principle but failed to apply it correctly to God? What would it take to align your application with revelation?
?
If God were a necessitating cause, would you feel less personally accountable? Since He is a free agent, how does that change your sense of relationship and responsibility before Him today?

First Proof: The Possible Always Needs a Cause
Razi presents the first proof that a persistent possible still requires a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that an eternal effect can be caused: namely, that a possible entity remains possible during its persistence and therefore continues to need a cause.

Core Idea
Even persistence does not remove need for a cause; the possible always requires one.

In this lesson, we examine Razi's first proof that an eternal effect does not pose a logical problem. He argues that if something is possible in itself, it remains possible as long as it exists, so it never outgrows its need for a cause. This directly challenges the objection that persistence eliminates causal need.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: Razi states there are multiple proofs, then presents the first proof with its premises and conclusion. The passage cuts off as he begins to justify one premise.
The text is incomplete; see if you can infer the missing part.
2
Identify the core claim
The key claim: The possible, even while persisting, remains possible. Therefore it still needs a cause. This counters the idea that existence removes causal need.
3
Consider the incomplete demonstration
Razi starts to explain why the possible remains possible but stops mid-sentence. Try to think how you would complete the argument. What is the nature of possibility that persists?
This shows the importance of understanding the nature of attributes like possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول: الذي يدل على أنه لا يمتنع إسناد الأثر القديم إلى المؤثر القديم. وجوه: الحجة الأولى: أن نقول: الممكن حال بقائه بقي ممكناً. وكل ممكن فلا بد له من سبب. فالممكن حال بقائه مفتقر إلى السبب. وذلك يدل على أن كونه باقياً لا يمنع من افتقاره إلى السبب. أما بيان أن الممكن حال بقائه بقي ممكناً: فلان الإمكان. إما أن
Then we say: That which indicates that it is not impossible to attribute an eternal effect to an eternal cause are aspects. The first proof: that we say: The possible, in its state of persistence, remains possible. And every possible necessarily has a cause. So the possible, in its state of persistence, is in need of the cause. And that indicates that its being persistent does not prevent its need for the cause. As for the demonstration that the possible, in its state of persistence, remains possible: it is because possibility. Either...
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage introduces the first proof (al-hujjah al-ula) that it is not impossible to attribute an eternal effect to an eternal cause. Razi begins by stating that there are multiple arguments (wujuh). The first proof argues: a possible entity (al-mumkin) in its state of remaining (hal baqa'ihi) continues to be possible. Since every possible necessarily requires a cause (sabab), the possible in its state of remaining is still in need of a cause. Therefore, its persistence (kawnuhu baqiyan) does not negate its need for a cause. This reasoning directly counters the opponents' examples (like the building or henna) which suggested that once something exists, it no longer needs a cause for its persistence. Razi then attempts to demonstrate that the possible remains possible during its persistence, but the text cuts off mid-sentence at 'because possibility. Either...' (fa-lianna al-imkan. imma an). The argument is incomplete in this window.

بقاء
سبب
إمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Once something exists, it no longer needs a cause for its continuation.
Correction: Razi argues that the possible remains possible during persistence, so it continues to need a cause.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what is true of the possible in its state of persistence?
A) It becomes necessary and no longer needs a cause.
B) It remains possible and still needs a cause.
C) It becomes impossible and therefore needs no cause.
D) It becomes independent of any cause.

Key Takeaway
The first proof establishes that a possible entity never ceases to need a cause, even when it persists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If possibility never leaves you, then you never outgrow your need for Allah. Does this make you feel humbled or burdened? How can you actively cultivate a sense of utter dependence in your daily life, rather than just knowing it intellectually?

Classification of the Cause: Free Agent and Necessitating Cause
The author classifies the cause into free agent and necessitating cause and states the agreement on eternal effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of causes in Islamic theology and how the nature of God determines whether an eternal effect can be attributed to Him.

Core Idea
God's nature as free agent or necessitating cause decides the possibility of an eternal creation.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah introduces a key classification in causality: the cause is either a free agent or a necessitating cause. The author then shows how both philosophers and theologians agree on certain rules about eternal effects, but diverge on whether God can have an eternal creation based on His nature. This sets the stage for the debate on the world's eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification of cause
Read the Arabic passage carefully and identify the two types of cause mentioned: the free agent and the necessitating cause.
Notice that the author uses 'fa'il mukhtar' and 'illa mujiba'.
2
Understand the agreement points
The passage states that both philosophers and theologians agree on two points regarding eternal effects. Find these agreements.
One is about free agent, the other about ancient cause.
3
See how each group applies the rule to God
Philosophers and theologians differ on God's nature, leading to different conclusions about the world's eternity. Trace their reasoning.
Philosophers believe God is necessitating by essence; theologians believe He is free agent.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this classification is foundational for the debate on the world's eternity. How does it set up the problem?
This is the rational discourse on this topic, as the author says.

And know that the cause is of two divisions: one: the free agent, and the second: the necessitating cause. If this is established, we say: The philosophers and the theologians agreed that ascribing the eternal effect to the free agent is impossible. And they agreed that ascribing the eternal effect to the ancient cause is not impossible. Commentary
This passage classifies the cause into two types: the free agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar) and the necessitating cause (al-'illah al-mujibah). The author then notes a point of agreement between philosophers (falasifa) and theologians (mutakallimun): both agree that ascribing an eternal effect (al-athar al-qadim) to a free agent is impossible. Both also agree that ascribing an eternal effect to an ancient cause (al-'illah al-qadimah) is not impossible. The difference arises because philosophers believe God is a necessitating cause (mujib bi al-dhat), so they allow the world to be eternal. Theologians believe God is a free agent (fa'il mukhtar), so they hold that an eternal effect from Him is impossible. This sets up the problem: the theologians must explain why an eternal effect from a free agent is impossible, which is the subject of the following arguments.

Correction: In fact, this impossibility is agreed only by those who hold that God is a free agent. Philosophers, who believe God is a necessitating cause, do not see an eternal effect as impossible.

Key Takeaway

?

?

The theologian insists God is a free agent to preserve His sovereignty and will. The Contingent Remains Contingent in Persistence
The author demonstrates the first premise: the contingent remains contingent during persistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author proves that the contingent remains contingent even while persisting, either because possibility is essential to its essence or because positing it as accidental leads to an infinite regress.

Core Idea
Possibility is either essential or leads to an infinite regress — either way, contingency persists.

Before we can prove that the contingent always needs a cause, we must establish that it remains contingent in the first place. The author presents a crucial distinction: possibility (imkan) is either a necessary property of the essence or a separate accident. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the first premise
Read the Arabic passage beginning from 'أما بيان أن الممكن حال بقائه' up to 'وهو المطلوب'. Follow the translation to grasp the structure.
Notice the binary split: either essential or accidental.
2
Analyze the two options for possibility
If possibility is a necessary concomitant of the essence, it is always present. If it is an accidental attribute, then its possibility precedes it, leading to an infinite regress.
The author rejects the regress, concluding that possibility must be essential.
3
See the conclusion of the first premise
The argument ends with the conclusion that possibility is a necessary concomitant, so the contingent remains contingent at all times.
This sets up the need for a cause in the next step.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : الذي يدل على أنه لا يمتنع إسناد الأثر القديم إلى المؤثر القديم . وجوه :
الحجة الأولى : أن نقول : الممكن حال بقائه بقي ممكناً . وكل ممكن فلا بد له من سبب . فالممكن حال بقائه مفتقر إلى السبب . وذلك يدل على أن كونه باقياً ، لا يمنع من افتقاره إلى السبب
أما بيان أن الممكن حال بقائه ، بقي ممكناً : فلان الإمكان . إما أن يكون من لوازم الماهية وإما أن لا يكون فإن كان الأول ، وجب حصول الإمكان في جميع الأوقات ، وإن كان الثاني كان الإمكان عرضاً مفارقاً للماهية وكل ما كان كذلك ، كان إمكان حصوله سابقاً على حصوله ، وحينئذ ينقل الكلام إلى إمكان الإمكان، ويلزم الذهاب إلى ما لا نهاية له ، وهو محال . أو الانتهاء إلى إمكان لازم للماهية ، وهو المطلوب
·
وأما بيان أن كل ممكن فهو محتاج إلى السبب . فالعلم به بديهي . فثبت : أن كل ممكن ، فإنه حال بقائه ممكن ، وثبت أن كل ممكن فهو محتاج إلى المؤثر ينتج أن كل ممكن ، فإنه حال بقائه محتاج إلى المؤثر ، وذلك يدل على أن كونه باقياً ، لا يمنع من كونه محتاجاً إلى المؤثر. فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن الشيء حال بقائه ، يكون الوجود به أولى ، فلاجل حصول هذه الأولوية يستغنى عن السبب . فنقول : حصول هذه الأولوية ، إما أن يكون من هذه الماهية ، أو لا يكون . فإن كان الأول ، وجب أن يقال : إنه كان حاصلا حال حدوثه ، فوجب أن يستغنى عن المؤثر ، حال حدوثه ، بل مطلقاً . وهو محال . وإن كان الثاني ، فهذه الأولوية ما كانت حاصلة حال الحدوث ، ثم حصلت حال البقاء . فالشيء حال بقائه ، إنما بقي لحصول هذه الأولوية . وهذا الاعتراف بأن الشيء حال بقائه : محتاج إلى السبب . إلا أنكم سميتم ذلك السبب بالأولوية . وهذا تسليم في المعنى ، ونزاع في العبارة .
Then we say: That which indicates that it is not impossible to ascribe the eternal effect to the eternal cause are [several] aspects: The first proof: We say: The contingent, while persisting, remains contingent. And every contingent must have a cause. So the contingent, while persisting, is in need of a cause. And that indicates that its being persisting does not prevent its being in need of the cause. As for the demonstration that the contingent, while persisting, remains contingent: because possibility (imkan) is either a necessary concomitant of the essence or not. If the first, then possibility must obtain in all times. If the second, then possibility is an accidental attribute separate from the essence, and everything that is like that, the possibility of its occurrence is prior to its occurrence, and then the discourse is transferred to the possibility of possibility, and an infinite regress is entailed — which is impossible — or ending at a possibility that is a necessary concomitant of the essence, which is what is sought. As for the demonstration that every contingent is in need of a cause: knowledge of it is self-evident. So it is established that every contingent, while persisting, is contingent, and it is established that every contingent is in need of the effecting cause, resulting that every contingent, while persisting, is in need of the effecting cause. And that indicates that its being persisting does not prevent its being in need of the effecting cause. If they say: Why may it not be said that the thing, while persisting, has existence more suitable to it, and due to the obtaining of this suitability it dispenses with the cause? We say: This suitability's obtaining is either from this essence or not. If the first, then it must be said that it was obtaining at the time of its origination, so it should dispense with the effecting cause at its origination, indeed absolutely — and that is impossible. If the second, then this suitability was not obtaining at origination, then it obtained during persistence. So the thing, while persisting, only persists due to the obtaining of this suitability. And this is an admission that the thing, while persisting, is in need of the cause, except that you named that cause 'suitability'. And this is a concession in meaning and a dispute in wording.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents the first of several proofs that it is not impossible for an eternal effect to be attributed to an eternal cause. The proof proceeds as follows: The contingent (mumkin) while persisting (hal baqa'ihi) remains contingent. Every contingent requires a cause. Therefore, the contingent while persisting requires a cause, showing that persistence does not remove the need for a cause. The author then demonstrates both premises. First, that the contingent remains contingent: He argues that possibility (imkan) is either an essential property (lazim li al-mahiyyah) or an accident ('arid). If it is essential, it must always be present. If it is accidental, then the possibility of its occurrence precedes its occurrence, leading to a regress of possibilities or ending with an essential possibility — which is what is sought. Second, that every contingent needs a cause is self-evident (badihi). Thus, the conclusion follows. The author then addresses an objection: someone might claim that the thing, while persisting, has a suitability (awlawiyyah) that makes it independent of a cause. The author responds by analyzing the source of this suitability: if it is from the essence, it should have been present at origination, eliminating the need for a cause at that time, which is absurd. If it is from something else, then the suitability arises at persistence, meaning the thing persists because of this suitability, implying it still needs a cause — just that the cause is called 'suitability'. Thus, the objection concedes the point and only argues about terminology.

Key Terms
الممكن
الإمكان
اللوازم
العرض المفارق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that contingency (imkan) is an accidental property that can change over time, so an existing contingent might become necessary.
Correction: The author shows that possibility is either essential (permanent) or leads to absurdity, so the contingent always remains contingent.

Checkpoint
If possibility (imkan) were an accidental attribute, what does the author argue would result?
A) It would be present at all times.
B) It would lead to an infinite regress of possibilities.
C) The contingent would become necessary.
D) The proof would be invalid.

Key Takeaway
The contingent remains contingent because possibility is a necessary concomitant of its essence, not an accidental property.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that possibility is a necessary concomitant of your essence — you are always contingent on Allah. In what ways do you still act as if you are self-sufficient? Will you today make a conscious effort to feel your need for Allah in every moment?
?
If possibility were accidental, an infinite regress would result, which is impossible. How does this logical truth deepen your conviction that Allah alone is the Necessary Being? Does your heart rely on that certainty when doubts arise?
?
The proof shows that the contingent never becomes necessary. How does this reality shape your dua and reliance on Allah? Are you willing to let go of any feeling of independence and submit fully to His power over your persistence?

The Self-Evident Need and the Objection of Suitability
The author presents the second premise, the conclusion of the proof, and responds to an objection about suitability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the need of the contingent for a cause is self-evident and how the author refutes the objection that a thing might be self-sufficient due to suitability (awlawiyyah).

Core Idea
Need for a cause is self-evident, and 'suitability' merely renames the cause.

After establishing that the contingent remains contingent, the author asserts that every contingent needs a cause as a self-evident truth. Then he addresses a potential objection: perhaps the contingent, while persisting, gains a suitability (awlawiyyah) that makes it independent of an external cause. This lesson unpacks the objection and the author's incisive response.

Guided Path
1
Read the second premise and conclusion
Read from 'وأما بيان أن كل ممكن فهو محتاج إلى السبب' up to 'لا يمنع من كونه محتاجاً إلى المؤثر'. Notice that the author treats the need for a cause as self-evident (badihi).
This is a foundational principle in kalam.
2
Understand the conclusion
The two premises (contingent remains contingent + every contingent needs a cause) yield that the contingent needs a cause even while persisting.
Persistence does not remove the need.
3
Read the objection
Read the objection: 'فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن الشيء حال بقائه ، يكون الوجود به أولى ، فلاجل حصول هذه الأولوية يستغنى عن السبب'.
The objector claims that the thing itself gains a suitability for existence that eliminates the need for a cause.
4
Analyze the two possibilities for suitability
The author splits the source of suitability into either from the essence or not. If from the essence, it should have been present at origination, eliminating the need then. If from something else, it is itself a cause.
This parallels the earlier dichotomous argument.
5
See the final response
The author concludes that the objector merely renames the cause as 'suitability', conceding the need for a cause while disputing terminology.
This is a powerful refutation that shows the objection is verbal.

ثم نقول : الذي يدل على أنه لا يمتنع إسناد الأثر القديم إلى المؤثر القديم . وجوه :
الحجة الأولى : أن نقول : الممكن حال بقائه بقي ممكناً . وكل ممكن فلا بد له من سبب . فالممكن حال بقائه مفتقر إلى السبب . وذلك يدل على أن كونه باقياً ، لا يمنع من افتقاره إلى السبب
أما بيان أن الممكن حال بقائه ، بقي ممكناً : فلان الإمكان . إما أن يكون من لوازم الماهية وإما أن لا يكون فإن كان الأول ، وجب حصول الإمكان في جميع الأوقات ، وإن كان الثاني كان الإمكان عرضاً مفارقاً للماهية وكل ما كان كذلك ، كان إمكان حصوله سابقاً على حصوله ، وحينئذ ينقل الكلام إلى إمكان الإمكان، ويلزم الذهاب إلى ما لا نهاية له ، وهو محال . أو الانتهاء إلى إمكان لازم للماهية ، وهو المطلوب

Then we say: That which indicates that it is not impossible to ascribe the eternal effect to the eternal cause are [several] aspects: The first proof: We say: The contingent, while persisting, remains contingent. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

When I rely on my own plans, skills, or health, am I not effectively claiming a false independence that this lesson refutes? Today, I will consciously remind myself that at this very moment, I am contingent and sustained only by His will.
?
The objection of 'suitability' is dismissed as a mere word game. In my own heart, do I ever use comforting words like 'it just works out' or 'things happen naturally' to avoid acknowledging that Allah is the direct cause of every persistent blessing? Will I strip away these euphemisms and see His hand in every continuing favor, including my life itself?
?
The author shows that disagreement over wording masks a deeper concession: the objector admits the need for a cause but renames it. Am I guilty of similar verbal evasion in my faith? For instance, do I attribute goodness to amulets, habits, or people while the true cause is Allah alone? Search my heart for any such hidden ascription of causal power and repent from it today.

5 min
Why Occurrence Cannot Be the Cause of Need
The author argues that temporal origination cannot be the cause of need for a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why temporal origination (huduth) cannot be the cause of a thing's need for a cause, based on the logical priority of existence and its properties.

Core Idea
Occurrence is a property of existence, and properties are too far downstream to cause the need for a cause.

This lesson presents the second proof from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah that challenges a common assumption. If a thing's temporal origination were the reason it needs a cause, then every event would require a cause. But the author argues that occurrence is a property of existence that comes after existence in logical rank, making it impossible to be the cause of need.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the translation to get the flow of the argument.
2
Understand the definition of occurrence
The author defines occurrence as existence preceded by non-existence. This definition is key.
3
Trace the chain of posteriority
Note the ordering: occurrence is posterior to existence, existence posterior to agent's influence, influence posterior to need, need posterior to cause. Therefore occurrence cannot be the cause of need.
The argument is incomplete in the text, but the logical chain is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : إنه يمتنع أن تكون علة الحاجة إلى المؤثر : هي الحدوث . وذلك لأن الحدوث عبارة عن كون وجوده مسبوقاً بالعدم . وهذه المسبوقية صفة لذلك الوجود ، وصفة الشيء متأخرة عنه بالرتبة ، فحدوثه متأخر عن وجوده ، المتأخر عن تأثير الفاعل فيه، المتأخر عن احتياجه إلى الفاعل ، المتأخر عن علة
The second proof: It is impossible that the cause of the need for the effecting cause is occurrence. That is because occurrence is [defined as] its existence being preceded by non-existence. And this precedence is a property of that existence, and the property of a thing is posterior to it in rank. So its occurrence is posterior to its existence, which is posterior to the influence of the agent upon it, which is posterior to its need for the agent, which is posterior to the cause [of need?].
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof that it is not impossible to attribute an eternal effect to an eternal cause. The proof aims to refute the claim that the need for a cause is due to temporal origination (huduth). The author argues that occurrence is defined as existence preceded by non-existence. This precedence is a property of that existence. Since a property is posterior in rank to the thing itself, the occurrence is posterior to the existence. Now, the existence is itself posterior to the agent's influence, which is posterior to the thing's need for the agent. Therefore, the occurrence is too far downstream in the order to be the cause of the need. The argument is cut off here, but the intended point is that the need for a cause is more fundamental than occurrence, so occurrence cannot be the cause of need. This supports the possibility of an eternal effect needing a cause without temporal origination.

Key Terms
علة الحاجة
الحدوث
المسبوقية بالعدم
المتأخر بالرتبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the need for a cause is due to temporal origination (huduth).
Correction: Occurrence is a property posterior to existence, so it cannot be the cause of the more fundamental need.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, why cannot occurrence (huduth) be the cause of the need for a cause?
A) Because occurrence is prior to existence.
B) Because occurrence is a property posterior to existence and thus far downstream.
C) Because existence is eternal.
D) Because the agent is not free.

Key Takeaway
Occurrence is not the cause of need because it is a property posterior to existence and thus too far downstream in the causal order.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your need for Allah is constant, not just at the moment you were created? When you think about a habit or a relationship in your life that feels self-sustaining, does your heart acknowledge that even now it depends entirely on Allah's continuous power? Commit today to whisper 'Ya Hayyu Ya Qayyum' and feel that dependence for one minute.
?
You often treat your own attributes—your intelligence, your patience, your health—as if they are the cause of your success. But this lesson shows that properties are downstream from existence. When you achieved something last week, did you thank Allah for the attribute itself or for the existence He sustains? Be honest: is there an area where you attribute your 'need-meeting' to your own qualities rather than to Allah? Repent and reframe.
?
Occurrence cannot be the cause of need—need is deeper than a temporal beginning. So, when do you act as if your obligation to Allah only started when you 'became Muslim' or when you 'committed a sin'? Your very existence demands gratitude right now. What is one thing you will do in the next hour to live out the truth that you need Allah at this very moment, not just for your past or future, but for the breath you are about to take?

Causality and Created Things
advanced
8 min
The Continuous Need for a Cause
Argument that contingent being always needs a cause, with refutation of priority objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why contingent beings need a cause not only at their origination but also throughout their existence, and how the objection based on 'priority' is refuted.

Core Idea
Permanence does not remove contingency; the need for a cause is continuous.

This passage presents a key argument in Islamic theology about causality. The author argues that contingent beings require a cause not just when they first come into being, but for as long as they continue to exist. He then addresses an objection that might claim the thing gains a 'priority' for existence that makes it independent. Let us follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the nature of possibility
The passage begins with a discussion of whether possibility (imkan) is a necessary concomitant of the essence or an accidental attribute. This distinction is crucial for the argument.
Necessary concomitant means it is intrinsic and always present.
2
Grasp the core argument
Sanusi then states that it is self-evident that every possible being needs a cause, and since the contingent remains possible, it continues to need a cause.
3
Examine the objection about priority
The opponent claims that during continuance, the thing has a 'priority' (awlawiyya) for existence that obviates the need for a cause.
4
Follow the refutation
Sanusi shows that this priority either comes from the essence (which would have made it independent from the start) or from outside (which is just another name for the cause). The objection collapses into a verbal dispute.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that the need for a cause is continuous, not just at origination. This has implications for the argument for God's existence as the sustaining cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون من لوازم الماهية وإما أن لا يكون فإن كان الأول وجب حصول الإمكان في جميع الأوقات وإن كان الثاني كان الإمكان عرضاً مفارقاً للماهية وكل ما كان كذلك كان إمكان حصوله سابقاً على حصوله وحينئذ ينقل الكلام إلى إمكان الإمكان ويلزم الذهاب إلى ما لا نهاية له وهو محال أو الانتهاء إلى إمكان لازم للماهية وهو المطلوب. وأما بيان أن كل ممكن فهو محتاج إلى السبب فالعلم به بديهي. فثبت أن كل ممكن فإنه حال بقائه ممكن وثبت أن كل ممكن فهو محتاج إلى المؤثر ينتج أن كل ممكن فإنه حال بقائه محتاج إلى المؤثر وذلك يدل على أن كونه باقياً لا يمنع من كونه محتاجاً إلى المؤثر. فإن قالوا لم لا يجوز أن يقال إن الشيء حال بقائه يكون الوجود به أولى فلأجل حصول هذه الأولوية يستغنى عن السبب فنقول حصول هذه الأولوية إما أن يكون من هذه الماهية أو لا يكون فإن كان الأول وجب أن يقال إنه كان حاصلاً حال حدوثه فوجب أن يستغنى عن المؤثر حال حدوثه بل مطلقاً وهو محال وإن كان الثاني فهذه الأولوية ما كانت حاصلة حال الحدوث ثم حصلت حال البقاء فالشيء حال بقائه إنما بقي لحصول هذه الأولوية وهذا الاعتراف بأن الشيء حال بقائه محتاج إلى السبب إلا أنكم سميتم ذلك السبب بالأولوية وهذا تسليم في المعنى ونزاع في العبارة.
It is either from the necessary concomitants of the essence or it is not. If it is the first, then possibility must be present at all times. If it is the second, then possibility is an accidental attribute separate from the essence, and everything that is like that, the possibility of its occurrence precedes its occurrence, and then the discussion shifts to the possibility of possibility, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible, or ending at a possibility that is a necessary concomitant of the essence, which is what is sought. As for the demonstration that every possible being is in need of the cause, knowledge of it is self-evident. So it is established that every possible being, while it continues to exist, is possible, and it is established that every possible being is in need of the effecter. It follows that every possible being, while it continues to exist, is in need of the effecter, and that indicates that its being permanent does not prevent it from being in need of the effecter. If they say: why is it not permissible to say that the thing, while it continues to exist, existence is more appropriate for it, and because of this priority it becomes independent of the cause? We say: this priority either comes from this essence or it does not. If it is the first, it must be said that it was present at the time of its origination, so it should have been independent of the effecter at the time of its origination, indeed absolutely, and that is impossible. If it is the second, then this priority was not present at the time of origination, then it occurred during continuance, so the thing continues to exist only because of this priority. This is an admission that the thing, while it continues to exist, is in need of the cause, except that you have called that cause 'priority'. This is an agreement in meaning and a dispute in expression.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument for the claim that contingent beings (mumkin) need a cause (mu'aththir) not only at the moment of origination (huduth) but also during their continuance (baqa'). The argument begins with a discussion of possibility (imkan): either possibility is a necessary concomitant of the essence or it is an accidental attribute. If it is necessary, then possibility is always present; if accidental, it leads to an infinite regress. Therefore, possibility must be a necessary concomitant. Then it asserts that it is self-evident (badihi) that every possible needs a cause. From this, it concludes that every possible, while it continues to exist, is possible and therefore needs the cause. Thus, permanence (baqa') does not remove the need. Then it addresses an objection: perhaps during continuance, the thing has a priority (awlawiyya) for existence that makes it independent of the cause. The refutation considers two cases: if the priority comes from the essence, it should have been present at origination, making the thing independent from the start, which is false; if it comes from elsewhere, then it arose after origination, meaning the thing continues to exist because of this priority, which is itself a cause. Thus, the objector is admitting the need for a cause but merely renaming it 'priority'. The argument ends by stating that this is a mere verbal dispute, not a substantive one.

Key Terms
لوازم الماهية (lazim al-mahiyya)
الإمكان (imkan)
السبب (sabab)
المؤثر (mu'aththir)
الحدوث (huduth)
البقاء (baqa')
الأولوية (awlawiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The need for a cause only applies at the moment of creation and not during continued existence.
Correction: The argument demonstrates that contingent beings remain possible while they exist, and therefore they continuously need a cause. The objection based on 'priority' is shown to be either false or merely a renaming of the cause.

Checkpoint
What does the argument conclude about the contingent being's need for a cause during its continuance?
A) It becomes independent of the cause due to priority.
B) It still needs a cause because it remains possible.
C) It only needed a cause at origination.
D) The need for a cause is replaced by the need for a sustaining factor.

Key Takeaway
A contingent being remains in need of its cause for as long as it exists, because its possibility is a permanent feature, not a temporary one.

After reading that your very existence at every moment requires a cause, when do you most feel independent from God? What does that feeling reveal about your state of belief, and how will you combat that illusion today?
?
The objector renamed the cause 'priority' to avoid admitting dependence. Where in your own life do you use different words to avoid acknowledging your absolute need for Allah? Be specific about one area and commit to changing that language this week.
?
If possibility is a permanent feature of your soul, then every breath you take is contingent. How does this truth reshape your next prayer? Write a single intention you will bring to your next salah based on this lesson.

The Second Argument: Huduth as a Candidate Cause
Definition of huduth and initial claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of origination (huduth) and the initial claim that it cannot be the cause of the need for a cause.

Core Idea
Origination is not the cause of need; it is a property posterior to existence.

In this lesson, we examine the second proof from the author. He begins by defining what he means by 'origination' (huduth) and then asserts that it is impossible for origination to be the cause of the need for an effecter. We will explore the reasoning behind this claim, focusing on the nature of properties and their temporal priority.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note key terms
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the key terms: al-hujja al-thaniya, huduth, illat al-haja, mu'aththir, mashuqiyya, sifa, muta'akhkhir bi al-rutba.
These terms are central to the argument.
2
Grasp the definition of huduth
Huduth is defined as 'existence preceded by non-existence'. This being-preceded (mashuqiyya) is a property (sifa) of existence.
Properties are ontologically posterior to their subjects.
3
Trace the regress of posteriority
The passage lists a chain: huduth is posterior to existence, which is posterior to the agent's effect, which is posterior to the need, etc. This sets up the argument that if huduth were the cause, it would be posterior to the effect, leading to absurdity.
Notice the cumulative list of things that huduth is posterior to.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : إنه يمتنع أن تكون علة الحاجة إلى المؤثر : هي الحدوث . وذلك لأن الحدوث عبارة عن كون وجوده مسبوقاً بالعدم . وهذه المسبوقية صفة لذلك الوجود ، وصفة الشيء متأخرة عنه بالرتبة ، فحدوثه متأخر عن وجوده ، المتأخر عن تأثير الفاعل فيه، المتأخر عن احتياجه إلى الفاعل ، المتأخر عن علة احتياجه إلى الفاعل، وعن جزء تلك العلة، وعن شرط تلك العلة . فهذا يدل على أن الحدوث ليس علة للحاجة ، ولا جزءاً من تلك العلة ، ولا شرطاً لتلك العلة . وإلا لزم تأخر الشيء عن نفسه بمراتب ، وذلك محال . وإذا ثبت أن الحدوث غير معتبر في حصول الحاجة أصلاً ، ثبت أنه سواء حصل الحدوث أو لم يحصل ، فإنه لا عبرة به في تحقق الحاجة إلى المؤثر .
The second proof: It is impossible for the cause of the need for the effecter to be origination. This is because origination is the fact that its existence is preceded by non-existence. This being-preceded is a property of that existence, and the property of a thing is posterior to it in rank. So its origination is posterior to its existence, which is posterior to the effect of the agent upon it, which is posterior to its need for the agent, which is posterior to the cause of its need for the agent, and to a part of that cause, and to a condition of that cause. This indicates that origination is not the cause of the need, nor a part of that cause, nor a condition of that cause. Otherwise, it would entail the thing being posterior to itself by multiple degrees, which is impossible. And if it is established that origination has no bearing at all on the occurrence of the need, it is established that whether origination occurs or not, it is irrelevant to the realization of the need for the effecter.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof (al-hujja al-thaniya) that the need for a cause is not due to origination (huduth). Huduth is defined as existence preceded by non-existence. This being-preceded (mashuqiyya) is a property (sifa) of existence. Since the property of something is ontologically posterior (muta'akhkhir bi al-rutba) to the thing itself, huduth is posterior to existence. Existence is posterior to the agent's effect (ta'thir al-fa'il), which in turn is posterior to the thing's need for the agent, and so on. Thus, if huduth were the cause of the need, it would be posterior to the need itself, leading to an absurdity where a thing is posterior to itself by multiple degrees. Therefore, huduth cannot be the cause, nor a part, nor a condition of the need. Consequently, huduth is irrelevant to the need for the cause; the need exists regardless of whether the thing is originated or not.

Key Terms
الحجة الثانية (al-hujja al-thaniya)
الحدوث (huduth)
علة الحاجة (illat al-haja)
المؤثر (mu'aththir)
المسبوقية (mashuqiyya)
صفة (sifa)
متأخر بالرتبة (muta'akhkhir bi al-rutba)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Origination is the reason why a contingent being needs a cause.
Correction: The argument shows that origination is a property posterior to existence and cannot be the cause, as the need is ontologically prior.

Checkpoint
What is the definition of huduth according to this passage?
A) Existence preceded by non-existence
B) The need for a cause
C) A property of the agent
D) The effect of the agent

Key Takeaway
The need for a cause cannot be due to origination because origination is a property that comes after existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often say 'I am dependent on Allah because He created me.' But this lesson says your need for Allah is actually prior to your creation. If that is true, then your need isn't caused by your createdness — it is more fundamental. Does this change how you feel about your relationship with Allah? Will you begin to see your need as something eternal about you, not just a result of coming into existence?
?
Consider your own attributes: your knowledge, strength, or piety. Do you ever think these qualities are why Allah favors you? This lesson shows that properties are posterior to existence and cannot be the cause of your need. How would your humility deepen if you truly believed that your very existence — not your attributes — is what makes you utterly dependent on Him?
?
When you look at other people, do you judge them based on their actions or their 'creatureliness'? This lesson suggests that the real cause of need is something deeper than any attribute. How might your view of others change if you saw every person as fundamentally needy, not because of what they do, but because of what they are — a being that requires a cause?

The Regress and Its Implication
Conclusion and absurdity of the regress

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the regress argument proves that origination cannot be the cause, a part, or a condition of the need for a cause.

Core Idea
If huduth were the cause, it would lead to a thing being posterior to itself.

In this continuation, we see the conclusion of the second proof. The author draws out the implications of the regress: that origination cannot be the cause, part, or condition of the need. Otherwise, an absurdity would follow. Finally, he concludes that origination is irrelevant to the need.

Guided Path
1
Read the conclusion passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. This passage is the conclusion of the second argument.
Notice the emphatic language: 'fa-hādhā yadullu ʿalā anna...'
2
Understand the absurdity of self-posteriority
The author argues that if huduth were the cause, it would entail the thing being posterior to itself (ta'akhkhur al-shay' ʿan nafsihi) by multiple degrees, which is impossible.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Grasp the final implication
Since huduth has no bearing on the need, it is irrelevant whether the thing is originated or not; the need for a cause remains.
This reinforces the independence of the need from temporal origination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : إنه يمتنع أن تكون علة الحاجة إلى المؤثر : هي الحدوث . وذلك لأن الحدوث عبارة عن كون وجوده مسبوقاً بالعدم . وهذه المسبوقية صفة لذلك الوجود ، وصفة الشيء متأخرة عنه بالرتبة ، فحدوثه متأخر عن وجوده ، المتأخر عن تأثير الفاعل فيه، المتأخر عن احتياجه إلى الفاعل ، المتأخر عن علة احتياجه إلى الفاعل، وعن جزء تلك العلة، وعن شرط تلك العلة . فهذا يدل على أن الحدوث ليس علة للحاجة ، ولا جزءاً من تلك العلة ، ولا شرطاً لتلك العلة . وإلا لزم تأخر الشيء عن نفسه بمراتب ، وذلك محال . وإذا ثبت أن الحدوث غير معتبر في حصول الحاجة أصلاً ، ثبت أنه سواء حصل الحدوث أو لم يحصل ، فإنه لا عبرة به في تحقق الحاجة إلى المؤثر .
The second proof: It is impossible for the cause of the need for the effecter to be origination. This is because origination is the fact that its existence is preceded by non-existence. This being-preceded is a property of that existence, and the property of a thing is posterior to it in rank. So its origination is posterior to its existence, which is posterior to the effect of the agent upon it, which is posterior to its need for the agent, which is posterior to the cause of its need for the agent, and to a part of that cause, and to a condition of that cause. This indicates that origination is not the cause of the need, nor a part of that cause, nor a condition of that cause. Otherwise, it would entail the thing being posterior to itself by multiple degrees, which is impossible. And if it is established that origination has no bearing at all on the occurrence of the need, it is established that whether origination occurs or not, it is irrelevant to the realization of the need for the effecter.

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof (al-hujja al-thaniya) that the need for a cause is not due to origination (huduth). Huduth is defined as existence preceded by non-existence. This being-preceded (mashuqiyya) is a property (sifa) of existence. Since the property of something is ontologically posterior (muta'akhkhir bi al-rutba) to the thing itself, huduth is posterior to existence. Existence is posterior to the agent's effect (ta'thir al-fa'il), which in turn is posterior to the thing's need for the agent, and so on.
