6 min
The Need is for Existence as Possible
Analysis of three aspects to isolate the cause of need

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof that the need for an effecter arises from the possibility of existence, not from its non-existence or origination.

Core Idea
Need for a cause arises from possibility, not from origination.

In this passage, Sanusi presents a precise analysis of an originated thing to identify what truly requires a cause. By examining three aspects—prior non-existence, subsequent existence, and the fact that existence is preceded by non-existence—he eliminates two common misconceptions and establishes that the real needy entity is existence itself, but only insofar as it is possible. This argument shifts the focus from temporal origination to ontological contingency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three aspects
Read the first part of the passage and note the three matters of the originated thing: prior non-existence, subsequent existence, and being preceded by non-existence.
These are the three aspects to be analyzed.
2
Understand why non-existence is not needy
Non-existence is pure negation; it cannot be attributed to an effecter. Therefore, it cannot be the thing that needs a cause.
This eliminates the first candidate.
3
Understand why precedence is not the cause of need
The fact that existence is preceded by non-existence is a necessary concomitant of this existence; it is not a separate thing that could be in need. If the existence were necessary in itself, it would not need a cause.
This eliminates the second candidate.
4
See that existence itself is needy
Since the first two are invalid, existence itself must be the entity in need of the effecter.
But this needs further refinement.
5
Refine to existence as possible
Existence cannot need a cause simply for being existence, because that would require even the Necessary Being to have a cause. So the need must be due to existence being possible. This possibility is the true cause of need, and it persists whether the thing is originated or eternal.
This is the conclusion of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : الحادث له أمور ثلاثة : أحدها : العدم السابق . والثاني : الوجود اللاحق . والثالث : كون هذا الوجود مسبوقاً بذلك العدم . والمحتاج إلى المؤثر ، ليس هو العدم السابق ، لأن العدم نفي محض فلا يمكن إسناده إلى المؤثر ، وليس هو أيضاً كون ذلك الوجود مسبوقاً بالعدم ، لأن مسبوقية هذا الوجود بالعدم لازم من لوازم هذا الوجود فإن هذا الوجود يمتنع حصوله وتقرره إلا إذا كان مسبوقاً بالعدم . وأما إذا كان واجب الثبوت لذاته امتنع افتقاره إلى المؤثر . ولما بطل هذان القسمان ثبت أن المحتاج إلى المؤثر والمفتقر إليه هو الوجود . ثم لا يخلو إما أن يكون ذلك الوجود محتاجاً إلى المؤثر ومفتقراً إليه لكونه وجوداً فقط أو لكونه وجوداً ممكناً . والأول باطل وإلا لافتقر كل موجود إلى المؤثر وهو محال . فبقي الثاني وهو أنه إنما افتقر إلى المؤثر لكونه موجوداً ممكناً . وإذا ثبت أن علة الحاجة ليست إلا هذا المعنى فمتى كان هذا المعنى حاصلاً كانت الحاجة إلى المؤثر حاصلة سواء حصل الحدوث أو لم يحصل .
The third proof: The originated thing has three matters: one of them is the prior non-existence; the second is the subsequent existence; the third is that this existence is preceded by that non-existence. That which is in need of the effecter is not the prior non-existence, because non-existence is pure negation and cannot be attributed to the effecter; nor is it the fact that this existence is preceded by non-existence, because the being-preceded of this existence by non-existence is a necessary concomitant of this existence, for this existence cannot come to be and be established unless it is preceded by non-existence. But if it were necessary of existence in itself, its being in need of the effecter would be impossible. Since these two divisions have been invalidated, it is established that that which is in need of the effecter and is dependent on it is existence. Then it is inevitable either that this existence is in need of the effecter and dependent on it for being existence only, or for being possible existence. The first is false, otherwise every existent would be in need of the effecter, which is impossible. So the second remains, namely that it is dependent on the effecter for being possible existence. And if it is established that the cause of the need is only this meaning, then whenever this meaning is present, the need for the effecter is present, whether origination occurs or not.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof (al-hujja al-thalitha). It begins by analyzing the originated thing (al-hadith) into three aspects: (1) the prior non-existence (al-'adam al-sabiq), (2) the subsequent existence (al-wujud al-lahiq), and (3) the fact that this existence is preceded by non-existence (kawn hadha al-wujud masbuqan bi dhalika al-'adam). It then argues that the needy entity (al-muhtaj) is not the prior non-existence because non-existence is pure negation (nafy mahd) and cannot be attributed to the effecter. Nor is it the fact of precedence, because this precedence is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of this existence—it cannot be separated from it. However, if the existence were necessary in itself (wajib al-thubut li dhatihi), it would not be in need of a cause. Therefore, the needy entity must be existence itself. Then it considers whether existence needs the cause because it is existence simply (wujud faqat) or because it is possible existence (wujud mumkin). The first is false because it would entail that every existent needs a cause, including the Necessary Being, which is absurd. So the need is due to the existence being possible (mumkin). Thus, the cause of need is possibility (imkan), which is present whenever the being is possible, regardless of whether it is originated or existing eternally. Therefore, the need for the cause persists as long as the possibility remains.

Key Terms
الحجة الثالثة (al-hujja al-thalitha)
العدم السابق (al-'adam al-sabiq)
الوجود اللاحق (al-wujud al-lahiq)
مسبوقية (masbuqiyya)
لازم (lazim)
واجب الثبوت (wajib al-thubut)
ممكن (mumkin)
وجود (wujud)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that the need for a cause arises from the fact that a thing is originated or from its non-existence.
Correction: The third proof shows that non-existence and the fact of being preceded by non-existence are not the cause of need; rather, the need is due to the possibility of existence itself.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, what exactly is in need of the effecter?
A) The prior non-existence.
B) The fact that existence is preceded by non-existence.
C) Existence itself, but only insofar as it is possible existence.
D) Existence itself, whether possible or necessary.

Key Takeaway
The need for a cause is due to the possibility (contingency) of existence, not its origination or non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you believe your need for God is only when you face a crisis (origination), what does this passage reveal about your constant state of need? How will you change your daily awareness of God because of this?
?
The passage argues that your need for a cause is due to your inherent possibility, not just when you came into existence or when things go wrong. In what ways do you still live as if you are self-sufficient between problems? What practical step can you take today to acknowledge your moment-by-moment contingency?
?
If you truly internalized that your existence is possible at every moment, how would that transform your reliance on Allah in your next decision? Will you pause and recognize your need before acting, or will you act as if you are independent?

intermediate
6 min
The True Object of Need: Possibility, Not Origination
Al-Razi argues that the need for an effect is due to possibility, not temporal origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the need for a cause in contingent things is due to their possibility (imkān) rather than their temporal origination (ḥudūth).

Core Idea
Need for a cause is rooted in possibility, not in being created in time.

In this third argument, Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī carefully dissects a contingent being into its constituent aspects to locate the precise reason it requires an external cause. He eliminates prior non-existence and the property of being preceded by non-existence as candidates, then shows that need is a function of existence itself, but only insofar as that existence is possible. This insight shifts the discussion from temporal origination to the more fundamental modal property of possibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how al-Razi enumerates three aspects of a contingent thing.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three aspects
The passage lists: prior non-existence, subsequent existence, and the fact that the existence is preceded by non-existence. Understand each.
These are the three things a contingent has.
3
Follow the elimination process
Al-Razi eliminates prior non-existence (pure negation) and being-preceded (a concomitant) and then pure existence (would imply all existents need a cause). See why each fails.
Note the logical reasoning at each step.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The only remaining possibility is that need is due to existence being possible (mumkin). This is the true cause of need, independent of temporal origination.
This is the key insight of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : الحادث له أمور ثلاثة : أحدها : العدم السابق . والثاني : الوجود اللاحق . والثالث : كون هذا الوجود مسبوقاً بذلك العدم . والمحتاج إلى المؤثر ، ليس هو العدم السابق ، لأن العدم نفي محض فلا يمكن إسناده إلى المؤثر ، وليس هو أيضاً كون ذلك الوجود مسبوقاً بالعدم ، لأن مسبوقية هذا الوجود بالعدم لازم من لوازم هذا الوجود فإن هذا الوجود يمتنع حصوله وتقرره ، إلا إذا كان مسبوقاً بالعدم . وأما إذا كان واجب الثبوت لذاته ، امتنع افتقاره إلى المؤثر . ولما بطل هذان القسمان ، ثبت : أن المحتاج إلى المؤثر ، والمفتقر إليه ، هو الوجود . ثم لا يخلو إما أن يكون ذلك الوجود محتاجاً إلى المؤثر ومفتقراً إليه ، لكونه وجوداً فقط ، أو لكونه وجوداً ممكناً . والأول باطل . وإلا لافتقر كل موجود إلى المؤثر ، وهو محال . فبقي الثاني وهو أنه إنما افتقر إلى المؤثر ، لكونه موجوداً ممكناً . وإذا ثبت أن علة الحاجة ليست إلا هذا المعنى ، فمتى كان هذا المعنى حاصلاً ، كانت الحاجة إلى المؤثر حاصلة . سواء حصل الحدوث ، أو لم يحصل.
The third argument: The contingent has three things: one of them: prior non-existence. The second: subsequent existence. The third: that this existence is preceded by that non-existence. That which is in need of the effect is not the prior non-existence, because non-existence is pure negation so it cannot be attributed to the effect; nor is it also that that existence is preceded by non-existence, because the being-preceded of this existence by non-existence is a necessary concomitant of this existence—for this existence cannot be obtained and realized unless it is preceded by non-existence. But if it is necessary of existence in itself, its being in need of the effect is impossible. And when these two divisions have been invalidated, it is established that that which is in need of the effect and dependent upon it is existence. Then it is not free from either being that existence in need of the effect and dependent upon it because it is existence only, or because it is possible existence. The first is false; otherwise every existent would be in need of the effect, which is impossible. So the second remains: that it is only dependent upon the effect because it is possible existence. And when it is established that the cause of need is nothing but this meaning, then whenever this meaning is present, the need for the effect is present, whether temporal origination occurs or does not occur.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument in a series of proofs. The author breaks down a contingent thing (al-ḥādith) into three aspects: prior non-existence, subsequent existence, and the fact that the existence is preceded by non-existence. He then examines which of these is the true object in need of an effect (muʼaththir). He rules out prior non-existence because it is a pure negation and cannot be attributed to an effect. He also rules out the aspect of being preceded by non-existence because that is merely a necessary concomitant of contingent existence—it is not the thing itself that is in need. If the existence were necessary in itself, it would not need an effect. Therefore, the real subject of need is existence itself (al-wujūd). But existence considered purely as existence cannot be the cause of need, because that would entail that every existent (including the Necessary Being) would need an effect, which is absurd. So the need must be due to existence being possible (mumkin). Thus the cause of need is possibility (imkān), not temporal origination (ḥudūth). Therefore, the need for an effect is present whenever possibility is present, regardless of whether origination occurs.

Key Terms
الحادث
العدم السابق
الوجود اللاحق
مسبوقية
المؤثر
واجب الثبوت
وجود ممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The need for a cause is due to temporal origination (being created in time).
Correction: Al-Razi argues that the true cause of need is possibility, not temporal origination. A thing's need for a cause is rooted in its possibility, and this need exists regardless of whether the thing has a temporal beginning.

Checkpoint
According to the third argument, what is the true object in need of an effect (muʼaththir)?
A) Prior non-existence
B) The fact that existence is preceded by non-existence
C) Existence itself
D) Possible existence

Key Takeaway
The cause of a thing's need for a creator is its possibility, not its temporal beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as though your very existence hangs on God's will every moment, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient? What would change if you felt the weight of your own possibility—that you could have been nothing and still nothing without Him?
?
This lesson shows that your need for God does not begin when you were born; it is rooted in the very nature of your existence. Have you ever thanked God not just for what He gave you, but for the fact that He keeps you from slipping into non-existence at every instant?
?
If the cause of need is possibility, not time, then you are dependent on God right now, not just at your creation. When you feel self-reliant or proud of your achievements, does this truth humble you? Commit to a specific way you will remind yourself today of your constant need for Him.

Why Prior Non-Existence Cannot Be a Condition
Al-Razi argues that prior non-existence cannot be a condition for the effect due to contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's logical proof that prior non-existence cannot be a condition for the effect because it contradicts both the existence of the effect and the effect's being effective.

Core Idea
Prior non-existence contradicts existence; a condition cannot contradict what it conditions.

Al-Razi presents a crisp logical argument: prior non-existence (al-ʿadam al-sābiq) cannot be a condition (sharṭ) for the effect because it contradicts both the existence of the effect and the effect's being effective. He then defends against a temporal objection. This lesson unpacks his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim and its significance
Read the opening statement: prior non-existence cannot be a condition because it contradicts the effect. Why does this matter? It challenges a common assumption about prerequisites.
The claim is a key part of al-Razi's broader argument about causality.
2
Examine the two demonstrations
Al-Razi gives two separate proofs: (1) prior non-existence contradicts existence itself; (2) prior non-existence contradicts the effect's being effective. Notice how each proof is structured.
The first is obvious; the second uses the idea of a necessary concomitant.
3
Grasp the general rule
Al-Razi states the logical principle: what contradicts a thing cannot be its condition. Reflect on why this is necessarily true based on definitions.
Condition requires coexistence; contradiction requires non-coexistence.
4
Analyze the objection and response
The objection tries to separate 'contradictory' and 'condition' into different times. Al-Razi responds that the condition must be present at the time of the conditioned thing.
This refutation preserves the logical force of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : العدم السابق على وجود الأثر ، ينافي وجود الأثر ، وينافي كون الأثر مؤثراً في وجوده . وما كان منافياً للشيء ، امتنع كونه شرطاً له . أما بيان أن عدمه السابق عليه ينافي وجوده ، فظاهر : الشيء ينافي وجوده . وأما بيان أن عدمه السابق ينافي كون المؤثر مؤثراً في وجوده . فذلك لأن كون المؤثر مؤثراً في وجوده ، لا يحصل إلا عند حصول الأثر ، وحصول الأثر ينافي عدمه ، والمنافي للازم الشيء ، مناف له . فيثبت : أن العدم السابق على الشيء ، مناف لوجوده ، ومناف لكون المؤثر مؤثراً في وجوده . وأما أن المنافي للشيء يمتنع كونه شرطاً لحصوله : فالعلم ضروري . لأن المنافي هو الذي لا يتقرر الشيء إلا مع عدمه ، وشرط الشيء هو الذي لا يتقرر الشيء إلا مع وجوده ، والجمع بينهما متناقض . فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه مناف ، بمعنى أنه لا يكون مقارناً له وشرط بمعنى أنه يجب تقدمه عليه . وإذا صرفنا كل واحد من هذين الاعتبارين إلى زمانين مختلفين ، زال التناقض . والجواب : أن المحتاج إليه ويجب حصوله حال حصول المحتاج . فإذا امتنع حصول المقارنة امتنع حصول هذه الحاجة.
The fourth argument: Prior non-existence to the existence of the effect contradicts the existence of the effect, and contradicts the effect's being effective in its existence. And whatever contradicts a thing, it is impossible for it to be a condition for it. As for the demonstration that its prior non-existence contradicts its existence, it is apparent: the thing contradicts its existence. As for the demonstration that its prior non-existence contradicts the effect's being effective in its existence, that is because the effect's being effective in its existence does not occur except when the effect occurs, and the occurrence of the effect contradicts its non-existence, and what contradicts a necessary concomitant of a thing contradicts the thing itself. So it is established: that prior non-existence to a thing contradicts its existence, and contradicts the effect's being effective in its existence. As for the fact that what contradicts a thing cannot be a condition for its occurrence, that is known necessarily. Because the contradictory is that without which the thing is not realized except with its absence, while the condition of a thing is that without which the thing is not realized except with its presence, and combining them is contradictory. If they say: Why is it not permissible to say that it is contradictory in the sense that it does not accompany it, and a condition in the sense that it must precede it? And if we refer each of these two considerations to two different times, the contradiction disappears. The answer: That which is needed and whose occurrence is necessary is at the time of the occurrence of the needy thing. So if the accompaniment is impossible, then this need is impossible.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument. The author argues that prior non-existence (al-ʿadam al-sābiq) cannot be a condition (sharṭ) for the effect's existence or for the effect's being effective, because it contradicts both. The proof is: prior non-existence obviously contradicts existence; and it also contradicts the effect's being effective in its existence because the effect's being effective occurs only when the effect exists, and existence contradicts non-existence. Since what contradicts a thing cannot be its condition (as condition requires conjunction, while contradiction requires separation), prior non-existence cannot be a condition. The author then considers an objection: perhaps 'contradicts' means it does not coexist at the same time, and 'condition' means it must precede; if we separate the times, there is no contradiction. The response is that what is needed (the condition) must be present at the time of the needy thing; if accompaniment is impossible (i.e., they cannot coexist), then the need cannot be fulfilled. Prior non-existence cannot be present at the time of existence, so it cannot serve as a condition.

Key Terms
العدم السابق
مناف
شرط
المؤثر
مقارنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prior non-existence is a necessary prerequisite for the effect because it must come before it.
Correction: While temporal priority is necessary for some conditions, prior non-existence contradicts the very existence of the effect, and a condition must be compatible, not contradictory.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth argument, why can't prior non-existence be a condition for the effect?
A) Because it is purely hypothetical
B) Because it contradicts the existence of the effect and the effect's being effective
C) Because it is an attribute of the effect
D) Because the effect is eternal

Key Takeaway
Prior non-existence is logically incompatible with being a condition because it contradicts the effect's existence and efficacy, and a condition must be non-contradictory and present at the time of the conditioned thing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life do you unconsciously assume that something must be absent before it can be present? How might that assumption actually contradict the reality of what you seek—like peace, faith, or closeness to Allah? What would it look like to drop that 'prior non-existence' condition?
?
Have you ever set a condition for your own spiritual growth that logically conflicts with the growth itself—for example, 'I will pray more once I feel more spiritual,' even though prayer itself cultivates spirituality? How does al-Razi's argument expose the contradiction in such thinking?
?
Consider a belief you hold about yourself that you treat as a 'condition' for Allah's mercy or guidance—like 'I must stop sinning completely first.' Is that condition actually compatible with receiving mercy, or does it contradict the very nature of mercy, which meets us in our weakness? What would it mean for you to let go of that condition today?

The Fifth Argument: Does Possibility Have a Beginning?
Al-Razi presents a dilemma on the beginning of possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's dilemma concerning whether the possibility of the world's existence has a beginning, and how it supports the possibility of God's eternal causation.

Core Idea
If possibility has no beginning, God's eternal act is not impossible.

This lesson examines Al-Razi's fifth argument for the eternity of the world. He presents a logical dilemma: either the possibility of the world's existence has a beginning or it does not. The argument challenges a potential objection that God's power cannot produce an eternal effect, showing instead that eternal causation is coherent. By working through the dilemma, we see how necessity of infinite regress forces acceptance of eternal possibility.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma's form
Al-Razi sets up a dichotomy: either the possibility (imkān) and validity (ṣiḥḥa) of the world's existence have a beginning or they do not. Watch for the two alternatives as you read.
The Arabic uses 'إما أن يقال... أو يقال'.
2
Trace the first alternative: no beginning
If there is no beginning to the possibility, then God's being effective and the world's occurrence in pre-eternity are not impossible. This means an eternal effect from an eternal cause is logically acceptable.
This is the desired conclusion.
3
Trace the second alternative: a beginning
If the possibility has a beginning, then that beginning is a contingent event requiring another cause, leading to an infinite regress. Since infinite regress is impossible, this alternative is rejected.
This forces acceptance of the first alternative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : أن نقول : وجود العالم ، ووجود تأثير الله في وجود العالم. إما أن يقال : إنه لا أول لإمكان كل واحد منهما ولصحته ، أو يقال : لإمكانها وصحتهما : أول : فإن كان الأول ، فحينئذ لم يكن كون البارىء تعالى مؤثراً في وجود العالم في الأزل : ممتنعاً . ولم يكن وقوع العالم بتأثير قدرة الله تعالى في الأزل : ممتنعا . وعلى هذا التقدير لا يكون إسناد الأثر القديم ، إلى المؤثر القديم : ممتنعاً . وإن كان الثاني ، فحينئذ يلزم أن يحصل لإمكان وجود العالم ولصحة وقوعه أول ، وذلك أول هو أمر حادث لا بد له من مؤثر . ويسلسل إلى ما لا نهاية له . وهو محال . فتعين الأول .
The fifth argument: That we say: The existence of the world, and the existence of God's effect in the existence of the world. Either it is said that there is no beginning for the possibility of each of them and their validity, or it is said that for their possibility and validity there is a beginning. If the first, then the Creator's being effective in the existence of the world in pre-eternity would not be impossible, and the occurrence of the world by the effect of God's power in pre-eternity would not be impossible. On this supposition, attributing the eternal effect to the eternal cause would not be impossible. If the second, then it would necessitate that the possibility of the world's existence and the validity of its occurrence have a beginning, and that beginning is a contingent thing that must have an effect, and it would regress infinitely, which is impossible. So the first is determined.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument in a dilemma form. The argument concerns the possibility (imkān) of the world's existence and the validity (ṣiḥḥa) of its occurrence. The question is whether this possibility has a beginning or not. If it has no beginning, then God's acting from eternity is possible, and an eternal effect from an eternal cause is not impossible. If it has a beginning, then that beginning itself is a contingent event requiring another cause, leading to infinite regress. Since infinite regress is impossible, the first alternative (no beginning) is established. This argument supports the possibility of the world's eternity and that God's power can act from eternity.

Key Terms
وجود العالم
تأثير الله
إمكان
صحة
الأزل
الأثر القديم
المؤثر القديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Temporal beginning is necessary for creation; an eternal effect from God is impossible.
Correction: This argument shows that if possibility has no beginning, then God's eternal causation is not impossible, and an eternal effect does not contradict God's power.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth argument, what is the consequence if the possibility of the world's existence has no beginning?
A) The world must be eternal
B) God's causing the world in pre-eternity is not impossible
C) Infinite regress follows
D) The world cannot exist

Key Takeaway
The possibility of the world's existence not having a beginning allows for God's eternal causation without contradiction or infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider the possibility that something you think of as 'impossible' may only seem so because you assume a beginning. Is there a situation in your life where you have limited God's power by your own notion of 'too late' or 'too early'? How can you surrender that assumption today?
?
If the world's possibility has no beginning, then God's relationship to creation is not about 'when' but about dependency. Do you live as if your existence is dependent on God every moment, not just at a starting point? What would change if you truly felt that right now?

Why Prior Non-Existence Cannot Be a Condition
Refutation of prior non-existence as a condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why prior non-existence is incompatible with being a condition for existence, and how the author refutes a key objection.

Core Idea
Prior non-existence contradicts existence itself, so it cannot be a condition.

This passage tackles a subtle point about causality: can something's prior non-existence serve as a condition for its existence? The author argues no, because non-existence is inherently incompatible with existence. He then anticipates an objection about temporal separation and provides a crisp refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the three-part structure: establishing incompatibility, the objection, and the answer.
The argument is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the key claim
The author asserts that prior non-existence is incompatible with existence and with the cause's efficacy. Understand why he says this is 'apparent'.
3
Understand the objection
The objector suggests that 'incompatible' and 'condition' can refer to different times, avoiding contradiction. Grasp the move: temporal separation.
The objector is trying to rescue prior non-existence as a condition.
4
Analyze the answer
The author responds that the condition must be present at the moment of need. If incompatibility means it cannot coexist, then the condition fails.
Focus on the logic: need implies simultaneous presence.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this argument defends the principle that only something compatible can be a condition. Connect to broader kalam discussions on causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيان كون الأثر مؤثراً في وجوده، وما كان منافياً للشيء، امتنع كونه شرطاً له. أما بيان أن عدمه السابق عليه ينافي وجوده، فظاهر: الشيء ينافي وجوده. وأما بيان أن عدمه السابق ينافي كون المؤثر مؤثراً في وجوده. فذلك لأن كون المؤثر مؤثراً في وجوده، لا يحصل إلا عند حصول الأثر، وحصول الأثر ينافي عدمه، والمنافي للازم الشيء، مناف له. فيثبت: أن العدم السابق على الشيء، مناف لوجوده، ومناف لكون الأثر مؤثراً في وجوده. وأما المنافي للشيء يمتنع كونه شرطاً لحصوله: فالعلم ضروري. لأن المنافي هو الذي لا يتقرر الشيء إلا مع عدمه، وشرط الشيء هو الذي لا يتقرر الشيء إلا مع وجوده، والجمع بينهما متناقض. فإن قالوا: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إنه مناف بمعنى أنه لا يكون مقارناً له، وشرط بمعنى أنه يجب تقدمه عليه. وإذا صرفنا كل واحد من هذين الاعتبارين إلى زمانين مختلفين، زال التناقض. والجواب: أن المحتاج إليه ويجب حصوله حال حصول المحتاج. فإذا امتنع حصول المفارقة امتنع حصول هذه الحاجة.
And the explanation that the effect is effective in its existence, and whatever is incompatible with a thing is prevented from being a condition for it. As for explaining that its prior non-existence contradicts its existence, it is apparent: the thing contradicts its existence. And as for explaining that its prior non-existence contradicts the effectiveness of the effective cause in its existence: that is because the effectiveness of the effective cause in its existence only occurs when the effect occurs, and the occurrence of the effect contradicts its non-existence, and whatever contradicts the concomitant of a thing contradicts the thing itself. So it is established: that prior non-existence of a thing contradicts its existence, and contradicts the effectiveness of the effect in its existence. And as for the contradictory of a thing being prevented from being a condition for its occurrence: that is known necessarily. Because the contradictory is that without which the thing is not established except with its non-existence, and the condition of a thing is that without which the thing is not established except with its existence, and combining them is contradictory. Then if they say: Why is it not permissible to say that it is contradictory in the sense that it is not concomitant with it, and a condition in the sense that it must precede it? And if we assign each of these two considerations to two different times, the contradiction is removed. The answer: That which is needed and must occur at the time of the need. So if the occurrence of the separation (non-concomitance) is prevented, the occurrence of this need is prevented.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The author first establishes that prior non-existence is incompatible with a thing's existence and with the efficacy of its cause, so it cannot be a condition. He then presents an objection: perhaps 'incompatible' means not concomitant with it at the same time, while 'condition' means it must precede it in time; if we assign these to different times, there is no contradiction. The author answers that the needed thing must be present at the moment of need; if separation (i.e., non-concomitance) is impossible, then the need is impossible. Thus prior non-existence cannot fulfill the conditional role.

شرط
مناف
مؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prior non-existence can be a condition because it temporally precedes existence.
Correction: Prior non-existence is incompatible with existence itself and with the cause's efficacy; being prior does not make it a valid condition, as a condition must be present when the conditioned occurs.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that prior non-existence cannot be a condition?
A) Because it is prior in time
B) Because it is incompatible with existence and with the cause's efficacy
C) Because existence does not need a condition
D) Because the objection is invalid

Key Takeaway
Prior non-existence is fundamentally incompatible with existence, so it cannot serve as a condition—regardless of temporal order.

The Fifth Proof: The Dilemma of Possibility's Beginning
Al-Matalib al-Aliyah presents the fifth proof using a dilemma: either possibility is beginningless or it has a beginning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth proof from the dilemma of the beginning of possibility, and why it shows that an eternal effect from an eternal cause is rationally possible.

Core Idea
If possibility had a beginning, impossibility would precede it – an absurdity.

This proof tackles a key objection to the eternity of the world. Some argue that if the world is eternal, it would be an 'eternal effect' – a concept some deem impossible. Al-Matalib al-Aliyah counters by analyzing the possibility of the world and God's influence. The argument shows that the only alternative to a beginningless possibility leads to a logical contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma
The proof presents two options: either the possibility of the world and God's influence has no beginning, or it has a beginning. Identify these two horns.
Notice that the dilemma is exhaustive – one of these must be true.
2
Examine the first horn
If possibility has no beginning, then attributing an eternal effect to an eternal cause is not impossible. This horn is accepted as logically sound.
This horn avoids any temporal beginning and preserves the eternal nature of God's act.
3
Examine the second horn
If possibility has a beginning, then before that beginning possibility was absent, meaning there was intrinsic impossibility, which then changed to possibility – an absurdity.
This horn leads to a violation of the principle that essential properties do not change.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since the second horn is absurd, the first must be true. Thus, it is rationally established that an eternal effect from an eternal cause is possible.
This conclusion supports the possibility of an eternal world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة: أن نقول: وجود العالم، ووجود تأثير الله في وجود العالم. إما أن يقال: إنه لا أول لإمكان كل واحد منهما ولصحته، أو يقال: لإمكانها وصحتهما: أول: فإن كان الأول، فحينئذ لم يكن كون البارىء تعالى مؤثراً في وجود العالم في الأزل: ممتنعاً. ولم يكن وقوع العالم بتأثير قدرة الله تعالى في الأزل: ممتنعا. وعلى هذا التقدير لا يكون إسناد الأثر القديم، إلى المؤثر القديم: ممتنعاً. وإن كان الثاني، فحينئذ يلزم أن يحصل لإمكان وجود العالم: أول يحصل لإمكان تأثير قدرة الله تعالى في وجود العالم: أول فيلزم أن يقال: إن قبل ذلك الأول، ما كان الإمكان الذاتي حاصلا. فيلزم أن يقال: كان الحاصل قبل ذلك المبدأ هو الامتناع الذاتي، ثم انقلب من الامتناع الذاتي إلى الإمكان الذاتي. وأنه محال. فثبت: أن إسناد الأثر القديم، إلى المؤثر القديم: غير ممتنع في العقول.
The fifth proof: We say: the existence of the world and the existence of God's influence on the existence of the world. Either it is said that there is no beginning to the possibility and soundness of each of them, or it is said that their possibility and soundness have a beginning. If the first, then the Exalted Creator's being effective in the existence of the world in eternity is not impossible, and the occurrence of the world by the influence of God's power in eternity is not impossible. On this assumption, attributing the eternal effect to the eternal cause is not impossible. If the second, then it would necessitate that the possibility of the existence of the world has a beginning, and the possibility of the influence of God's power on the existence of the world has a beginning. Then it would necessitate saying that before that beginning, intrinsic possibility was not present. Then it would necessitate saying that what was present before that beginning was intrinsic impossibility, then it turned from intrinsic impossibility to intrinsic possibility. And that is absurd. So it is established that attributing the eternal effect to the eternal cause is not impossible in the intellects.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof, a dilemma argument. The author considers the possibility of the world and the possibility of God's influence on it. Either these possibilities have no beginning, or they have a beginning. If they have no beginning, then the world and God's influence can be eternal, making an eternal effect from an eternal cause possible. If they have a beginning, then that beginning would mark a transition from intrinsic impossibility to intrinsic possibility, which is absurd. Therefore, the eternal effect from the eternal cause is not impossible.

Key Terms
إمكان
امتناع
أثر قديم
مؤثر قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An eternal effect (athar qadim) is impossible because every effect must have a temporal beginning.
Correction: The proof shows that if the possibility of the effect is beginningless, there is no need for a temporal beginning. The impossibility only arises if possibility itself has a beginning, which leads to an absurd change.

Checkpoint
What is the absurd consequence if the possibility of the world had a beginning?
A) The effect would be eternal
B) Intrinsic possibility would be absent before that beginning, implying a change from impossibility
C) God's power would be insufficient
D) The world would not exist

Key Takeaway
The fifth proof establishes that it is rationally possible for an eternal effect to proceed from an eternal cause, because the alternative leads to an absurd change from impossibility to possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's power is not bound by time, or do you subconsciously imagine that God's action must have a starting point? What would change in your worship if you internalized that God's causality is eternal?
?
If intrinsic impossibility cannot become possibility, what does that say about the nature of sin or disbelief—can a heart that is truly impossible for faith ever change, or is that absurd? How does this lesson challenge the way you view your own spiritual state?
?
You have just read that an eternal effect from an eternal cause is rationally permissible. Does this threat to the idea that everything has a temporal beginning make you uncomfortable? Why? What does your reaction reveal about your assumptions regarding creation?

The Sixth Proof: Analogy of Fever and Eternal Attributes
The sixth proof uses the analogy of fever and attributes to show an eternal effect from an eternal cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the analogy of fever and the theologians' own statements about attributes demonstrate that an eternal effect can be caused by an eternal cause.

Core Idea
An eternal effect from an eternal cause is not impossible; even theologians accept causation among eternal attributes.

This lesson presents the sixth proof against those who deny that the world could be eternal and caused. Using a vivid analogy from medicine and a reference to the theologians' own positions, the argument shows that causation does not require temporal priority. The passage challenges a key objection to the eternity of the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy of fever
Read the first part of the passage about rottenness causing fever. Notice how the argument distinguishes between origination and continuance of the effect.
The key point is that the effect depends on the cause for both its beginning and its remaining.
2
Understand the principle
The analogy establishes that a cause can sustain its effect continuously. If rottenness ceases, the fever ends. So an eternal cause could sustain an eternal effect.
3
Examine the theological precedent
Read the second part where theologians attribute eternal attributes to eternal causes. They accept that eternal knowledge is caused by eternal knowledge itself, etc. This shows they already allow eternal causation.
This is a powerful argument from their own framework.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes by asking why they reject an eternal effect from an eternal cause when they accept it in the case of attributes.
The inconsistency is highlighted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة: إن عفونة الخلط، توجب حصول الحمى، وكما يفتقر حدوث هذه الحمى إلى حدوث هذا السبب، فكذلك بقاء هذه الحمى، مفتقر إلى بقاء هذا السبب. فإن عند زوال هذه العفونة، يمتنع بقاء الحمى العفونية. فيثبت: أن إسناد الممكن إلى المؤثر حال بقائه: غير ممتنع. وأيضاً: فالمتكلمون يقولون: العالمية الأزلية، معللة بالعلم الأزلي. والقادرية الأزلية معللة بالقدرة الأزلية. والعلم الأزلي، مشروط بالحياة الأزلية. فقد صرحوا بأن الأزلي ممكن أن يكون معللاً بالعلة المؤثرة. فكيف استبعدوا تعليل الأثر القديم، بالمؤثر القديم؟
The sixth proof: Indeed, rottenness of the humors causes the occurrence of fever, and just as the origination of this fever depends on the origination of this cause, so too the continuance of this fever depends on the continuance of this cause. For if this rottenness ceases, the continuance of the fever caused by rottenness is impossible. So it is established that attributing a possible thing to the effective cause in its state of continuance is not impossible. Also, the theologians say: the eternal attribute of knowledge is caused by eternal knowledge, and the eternal attribute of power is caused by eternal power, and eternal knowledge is conditional upon eternal life. Thus they have explicitly stated that the eternal can be caused by an effective cause. So how did they deem it far-fetched to attribute an eternal effect to an eternal cause?
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof, which uses two arguments. First, an analogy: rottenness causes fever, and just as the origination of the fever depends on the origination of the cause, so its continuance depends on the continuance of the cause. Thus a possible effect can be sustained by a persisting cause, so an eternal effect from an eternal cause is not impossible. Second, the theologians themselves affirm that eternal attributes (knowledge, power) are caused by eternal principles (knowledge, power, life). They allow the eternal to be caused, so they should not reject an eternal effect from an eternal cause.

حمى
علة
عالمية أزلية
قادرية أزلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A cause must temporally precede its effect.
Correction: The fever analogy shows that a cause can sustain its effect simultaneously; causation does not require temporal priority.

Checkpoint
What do the theologians' statements about attributes demonstrate according to this proof?
A) That attributes are not caused
B) That an eternal can be caused by an effective cause
C) That attributes are temporal
D) That the world is eternal

Key Takeaway
The sixth proof establishes that an eternal effect can be caused by an eternal cause, using an analogy from fever and a reference to theologians' acceptance of eternal causation among attributes.

?

The Total Cause Argument for Eternity
Sanusi presents the seventh proof and its two supporting arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the total cause argument for the eternity of the world and why the effect cannot be delayed if the total cause is eternal.

Core Idea
The total cause's eternity necessitates the eternity of its effect.

This seventh proof from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah presents a subtle causal argument: if the total cause of God's creative act existed from eternity, then the effect—the world—must also be eternal. The proof rests on two key principles: the impossibility of infinite regress and the impossibility of preponderance without a preponderator. In this lesson, you'll examine each step of the argument and see how it leads to the conclusion that the eternal is caused by the eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Let the author's own phrasing set the rhythm.
Note the key terms: مجموع ما لأجله (total cause) and تخلف الأثر (delay of effect).
2
Identify the proof's structure
The passage first states the proof, then gives two sub-proofs (بيان الأول and بيان الثاني). Identify where each part begins.
3
Understand the first sub-proof
The first sub-proof argues that if the total cause were not eternal, its occurrence would be originated, leading to an infinite regress, which is absurd. Grasp why this supports the eternity of the total cause.
This is a standard kalam argument against infinite regress.
4
Understand the second sub-proof
The second sub-proof argues that if the total cause is present and the effect does not occur, that would be a case of preponderance without a preponderator, which is absurd. This shows the effect must immediately follow.
The principle that a possible cannot be preponderated without a cause is fundamental.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة: مجموع ما لأجله كان البارىء، مؤثراً في وجود العالم كان حاصلا في الأزل. ومتى كان ذلك المجموع حاصلا، امتنع تخلف الأثر عنه. ومتى كان الأمر كذلك، فقد لزم القطع بكون الأزلي معللاً بالأزلي. أما بيان الأول: فلانه لو لم يكن ذلك المجموع حاصلاً، لكان حصوله حادثاً. فافتقر إلى مؤثر آخر، ويعود الكلام الأول فيه. وهو محال. وأما بيان الثاني: فهو أن عند حصول مجموع ما لا بد منه في المؤثرية. لولم يحصل الأثر، لزم رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر، لا لمرجح، وهو محال.
The seventh proof: The totality of that for which the Creator is effective in the existence of the world was present in eternity. And whenever that totality is present, the effect's delay is impossible. And whenever the matter is so, then it necessitates the certainty that the eternal is caused by the eternal. As for explaining the first: because if that totality were not present, its occurrence would be originated, so it would need another effect, and the same argument recurs regarding it, and that is absurd. As for explaining the second: that when the totality of what is necessary for efficacy is present, if the effect does not occur, it would necessitate the preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible without a preponderator, and that is absurd.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof, which argues that the total cause (the set of conditions for God's efficacy) existed from eternity. If the total cause is present, the effect cannot be delayed. To explain: if the total cause were not eternal, its occurrence would be originated, leading to an infinite regress, which is absurd. Moreover, if the total cause is present and the effect does not follow, that would be a case of a possible thing being preponderated without a preponderator, which is also absurd. Therefore, the eternal effect from the eternal cause is necessary.

Key Terms
مجموع ما لأجله
تخلف الأثر
مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the cause's efficacy depends on a temporal condition, so the effect could be delayed.
Correction: The proof holds that the total cause is eternal and includes all necessary conditions, so no delay is possible.

Checkpoint
Why is it impossible for the effect to be delayed when the total cause is present?
A) Because cause and effect are the same
B) Because it would require a preponderator without which the possible would not be preponderated
C) Because God is not bound by causality
D) Because the effect is eternal by definition

Key Takeaway
The seventh proof argues that because the total cause of God's creative act is eternal, the world must also be eternal, otherwise either infinite regress or preponderance without cause would occur.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the total cause of God's creative act is eternal, then your existence is tied to an eternal necessity. Does this make you feel that your life is predetermined and lacking freedom? Or does it give you a sense of ultimate purpose because you are part of an eternal plan? Examine your heart: do you truly accept that God's will has no temporal delay, or do you wish for a more 'modern' view where God intervenes in time? What is your immediate reaction to the idea that the world has always existed?
?
The argument states that any delay in the effect requires a preponderator. In your own life, do you sometimes delay responding to God's call, thinking you have time? How does this argument challenge your perception of urgency in matters of faith? If God's action is immediate and eternal, what does that say about your procrastination in spiritual duties?
?
This proof uses a principle that 'one side of a possible cannot be preponderated without a preponderator'. When you make choices, do you often act without a clear preponderator? How does this relate to your trust in God's wisdom? Are you content with the idea that everything in existence has a sufficient reason in God's eternal decree, or do you sometimes feel that events happen random? Let this question drive you to surrender your intellect to divine wisdom.

The Eighth Proof: Need Is from Contingency, Not Origination
Author proves need for cause is due to contingency, not temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why contingency, not temporality, is the true reason a thing needs an efficient cause.

Core Idea
Contingency, not temporality, creates the need for a cause.

This proof tackles a key debate: Is a thing's need for a cause due to its being created in time (temporal) or due to its inherent possibility? The author argues that only contingency (imkan) creates dependence, using a clever thought experiment that contrasts two ways of considering an originated thing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once for its flow, then the English translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
The passage is dense; read slowly to follow the two scenarios.
2
Analyze the first scenario: necessary occurrence
The author first considers an originated thing that necessarily occurs (e.g., by divine decree). Under this aspect, it seems self-sufficient and independent of a cause.
Attributing it to another leads to a contradiction (necessary by self and by another).
3
Analyze the second scenario: equal possibility
The author then considers the thing's essence (mahiyya) where existence and non-existence are equal. Reason demands a preponderator to tip the scale.
This judgment does not depend on whether the thing is originated or not.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The conclusion follows: the need for a cause arises from contingency, not from temporality. Temporality is irrelevant.
This refutes the view that 'originated in time' is the reason for needing a cause.
5
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Many think being created in time (huduth) makes a thing dependent. This proof shows that even a non-temporal contingent thing would need a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة: إنا لو فرضنا حادثاً وتصورنا أنه حدث، مع وجوب أن يحدث. فإنا نحكم بامتناع استناده إلى السبب المنفصل، لأنا لما اعتقدنا فيه أنه حدث مع وجوب أن يحدث، كان راجح الوجود لنفسه. فلو أسندناه إلى غيره، فحينئذ يلزم أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته، واجب الوجود لغيره معاً، لزم الجمع بين النقيضين، وهو محال. فيثبت: أنا لو فرضنا حادثاً وتصورنا أنه حدث، مع وجوب أن يحدث. فإنا نحكم عليه بالاستغناء عن المؤثر. وأما إذا تصورنا ماهيته، وتصورنا أن نسبة الوجود والعدم إليها على الإستواء، فههنا يحكم العقل بأنه لولا المرجح، لامتنع حصول الرجحان. ولا يفتقر في هذا الحكم إلى اعتقاد أنه حادث أم لا؟ فيثبت بما ذكرنا: أن مجرد كونه ممكناً يحوجه إلى المؤثر، وأن كونه حادثاً لا يحوجه إلى المؤثر. فيثبت: أن علة الحاجة هي الإمكان، لا الحدوث.
The eighth proof: If we suppose an originated thing and we conceive that it occurs, with the necessity that it occur, then we judge that it is impossible to attribute it to a separate cause, because when we believe concerning it that it occurs with the necessity that it occur, it is preponderant in existence by itself. So if we attribute it to another, then it would necessitate that it be necessary of existence by itself and necessary of existence by another simultaneously, which necessitates the combination of two contradictories, and that is absurd. Thus it is established that if we suppose an originated thing and we conceive that it occurs, with the necessity that it occur, then we judge it to be independent of the efficient cause. But when we conceive its quiddity, and we conceive that the relation of existence and non-existence to it is equal, then the intellect judges that without the preponderator, the occurrence of preponderance is impossible. And this judgment does not depend on believing whether it is originated or not. So it is established by what we have mentioned that merely its being contingent necessitates it to need an efficient cause, and that its being originated does not necessitate it to need an efficient cause. Thus it is established that the cause of need is contingency, not origination.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth proof, which distinguishes two ways of considering a thing. First, if we imagine an originated thing that necessarily occurs (e.g., by divine decree), it appears to preponderate itself and thus seems independent of a cause. Attributing it to another would make it both necessary by itself and by another, a contradiction. Hence, if we consider only its necessary occurrence, we judge it independent. Second, if we consider the essence (mahiyya) of the same thing without regard to its occurrence, we see that existence and non-existence are equally possible for it. Then reason judges that a preponderator (murajjih) is needed to tip the scales. This judgment does not depend on whether the thing is originated or not; it depends solely on its contingency (imkan). The conclusion is that the need for an efficient cause is due to contingency, not temporal origination. This refutes the Ash'arite claim that need is due to temporality.

Key Terms
حادث
وجوب أن يحدث
راجح الوجود
واجب الوجود
ماهية
إمكان
استواء
مرجح
رجحان
علة الحاجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think being temporal (originated in time) is the reason a thing needs a cause.
Correction: This proof shows that the need for a cause is due to contingency (equal possibility of existence and non-existence), not temporality. Even a non-temporal contingent thing would need a cause.

Checkpoint
According to the eighth proof, what is the true cause of a thing's need for an efficient cause?
A) Its temporality (huduth)
B) Its contingency (imkan)
C) Its necessity
D) Its non-existence

Key Takeaway
A thing's need for a cause arises from its contingency (imkan), not from its being originated (huduth).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan your future actions, do you assume you have independent control, or do you recognize your deep contingency before Allah? What would change if you truly internalized that at every moment you need His preponderator?
?
Think of a blessing in your life that you consider 'necessary' or 'guaranteed'—like health or provision. This proof shows that even such 'necessary' occurrences are contingent in essence. Are you living in gratitude for that moment-to-moment dependence?
?
If the need for a cause is due to contingency, not temporality, then your very existence at this moment requires Allah's constant preponderating. How would your day change if you reminded yourself of this dependency with every breath?

The Coexistence of Cause and Effect in Eternity
The author refutes the impossibility of eternal causation by noting the coexistence of effect and cause in eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the coexistence of effect with cause in eternity refutes the impossibility of eternal causation.

Core Idea
The effect's presence with the cause in eternity proves eternal causation is possible.

In this brief but pivotal passage, the author addresses a key objection against eternal causation. He argues that since the effect exists simultaneously with the cause in eternity, the claim that eternal causation is impossible collapses. This lesson unpacks this concise argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's wording.
Pay attention to the key terms: cause (مؤثر), effect (أثر), eternity (أزل).
2
Understand the key terms
Define each term: المؤثر (the cause that produces an effect), الأثر (the effect produced), الأزل (eternity without beginning).
These terms are foundational for the argument.
3
Grasp the logical move
The author argues that because the effect exists together with the cause in eternity, the statement that eternal causation is impossible is invalid. Coexistence implies possibility, so the objection falls away.
This argument relies on the prior exposition referenced by the author.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بيان الثالث : فهو أنه لما كان الأثر حاصلاً مع المؤثر في الأزل ، فقد سقط القول : بأن التأثير الأزلي محال . وقد تقدم تقرير هذا الدليل
As for the explanation of the third: it is that since the effect exists together with the cause in eternity, the statement that eternal causation is impossible has fallen away. And the exposition of this proof has preceded.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage briefly explains the third point in a series of arguments. It states that because the effect (al-athar) is concomitant with the cause (al-mu'aththir) in eternity (al-azal), the objection that eternal causation is impossible becomes invalid. The author notes that this proof has already been fully expounded earlier.

Key Terms
الأثر
المؤثر
التأثير الأزلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'eternal causation' means the effect is as old as the cause in the sense of being uncaused or independent.
Correction: The passage simply affirms the coexistence of effect and cause in eternity, not that the effect is uncaused. The cause still produces the effect, but both exist without beginning.

Checkpoint

A) It is proven true.
B) It falls away because the effect coexists with the cause in eternity.
C) It is possible only for some effects.
D) It is based on a false premise.

Key Takeaway
The coexistence of effect with cause in eternity refutes the claim that eternal causation is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Eighth Proof: Need for a Cause Is Due to Possibility
Argument on the cause of need for a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the need for an effect-causing agent arises from possibility (imkan) and not from origination (huduth).

Core Idea
Possibility, not origination, explains why things need a cause.

This lesson presents the eighth proof from al-Matalib al-Aliyah. The author argues that if an originated thing occurs necessarily, it would be self-sufficient; thus, need for a cause arises from possibility, not from being originated. The argument sharpens our understanding of causality in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Note that the passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two scenarios: (1) an originated thing that occurs necessarily, and (2) a quiddity with equal possibility. Notice how the author treats them differently.
3
Follow the reasoning for each scenario
For the first scenario, the author shows that a necessarily occurring originated thing cannot depend on a cause, otherwise it would combine necessity by self and by another. For the second, he shows that equal possibility demands a preponderating factor.
The key is the contradiction in the first scenario.
4
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that need arises from possibility, not from being originated. Reflect on how this refines the concept of causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : إنا لو فرضنا حادثاً وتصورنا أنه حدث ، مع وجوب أن يحدث. فإنا نحكم بامتناع استناده إلى السبب المنفصل، لأنا لما اعتقدنا فيه أنه حدث مع وجوب أن يحدث ، كان راجح الوجود لنفسه . فلو أسندناه إلى غيره ، فحينئذ يلزم أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته، وهو محال . لأن كونه واجب الوجود بذاته ، يقتضي أن لا يلزم من فرض عدم غيره : عدمه. فلو كان شيء واحد واجب الوجود لذاته ، ولغيره معاً ، لزم الجمع بين النقيضين ، وهو محال . فيثبت : أنا لو فرضنا حادثاً وتصورنا أنه حدث ، مع وجوب أن يحدث . فإنا نحكم عليه بالاستغناء عن المؤثر . وأما إذا تصورنا ماهيته، وتصورنا أن نسبة الوجود والعدم إليها على السوية فههنا يحكم العقل بأنه لولا المرجح ، لامتنع حصول الرجحان . ولا يفتقر في هذا الحكم إلى اعتقاد أنه حادث أم لا ؟ فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن مجرد كونه ممكناً يحوجه إلى المؤثر ، وأن كونه حادثاً لا يحوجه إلى المؤثر . فيثبت : أن علة الحاجة هي الإمكان ، لا الحدوث .
The eighth proof: If we suppose an originated thing and conceive that it occurred with necessity of its occurring, then we judge that it is impossible for it to be dependent on a separate cause. For when we believe that it occurred with the necessity of its occurring, it is preponderant in existence by itself. So if we ascribe it to another, then it would follow that it is necessary of existence by itself, which is impossible. Because its being necessary of existence by itself entails that it is not necessary from the assumption of the non-existence of another: its non-existence. So if one thing were necessary of existence by itself and by another together, that would require combining two contradictories, which is impossible. Thus it is established that if we suppose an originated thing and conceive that it occurred with the necessity of its occurring, then we judge it to be independent of the effect-causing agent. But if we conceive its quiddity and conceive that the relation of existence and non-existence to it is equal, then here the intellect judges that without a preponderating factor, preponderance would be impossible. And this judgment does not depend on believing whether it is originated or not. So it is established by what we have mentioned that merely its being possible necessitates it to need the effect-causing agent, and that its being originated does not necessitate it to need the effect-causing agent. Thus it is established that the cause of need is possibility, not origination.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth argument to demonstrate that the true cause of need for an external cause (al-mu'aththir) is possibility (al-imkan), not origination (al-huduth). The argument proceeds by considering a hypothetical originated thing (hadith) that occurs necessarily (wajib an yahduth). If such a thing were dependent on a separate cause, it would be both necessary by itself (since it must occur) and necessary by another, which is contradictory. Hence, it must be self-sufficient. In contrast, when we consider the quiddity (mahiyya) alone, existence and non-existence are equally possible, and the intellect judges that a preponderating factor (murajjih) is needed. Thus, need arises from possibility, not from being originated.

واجب الوجود
النقيضين
المرجح
الإمكان

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that originated things always need a cause because they are originated.
Correction: This proof shows that need is due to possibility, not origination. An originated thing that occurs necessarily would be self-sufficient.

Checkpoint

C) The act of the cause
D) The preponderance of existence

Key Takeaway
The cause of need for a cause is possibility, not origination.

?

?
The proof shows that being 'new' (originated) does not inherently demand a cause; rather, it is your possibility that demands a Preponderator every moment. How would your life look different if you truly lived each second acknowledging that you are constantly being brought from non-existence to existence by Allah's will?

The Ninth Proof: Only Possibility Necessitates Need
The author eliminates two states to prove that possibility alone necessitates need.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why only possibility (al-imkan) necessitates need for an effect-causing agent, and why existence or non-existence do not.

Core Idea
Need arises not from what a thing is, but from its inherent possibility of being or not.

This proof examines the true source of a thing's need for a cause. The author systematically rules out two obvious candidates—the thing when it exists or when it doesn't—and shows that need must stem from the thing's neutral capacity for either state. That capacity is what we call possibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note the author's threefold division of the quiddity's states.
The passage is concise; focus on the logical elimination.
2
Identify the three scenarios
List the three states: quiddity as existent, quiddity as non-existent, quiddity stripped of both. Understand why the first two are invalid.
Each state is examined based on its implications for need.
3
Understand why existence and non-existence fail
The existent quiddity is necessary so it doesn't need a cause; the non-existent is impossible so it also doesn't need a cause. Both are self-sufficient in their respective modes.
4
Grasp the remaining scenario and its conclusion
The only remaining possibility is the quiddity considered without existence or non-existence. This neutral capacity—called possibility—is what necessitates need for a preponderating cause.
This establishes the core principle: need stems from possibility alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : إن منشأ الحاجة. إما الماهية حال كونها موجودة ، أو الماهية حال كونها معدومة ، أو الماهية مع حذف هذين القيدين . والأول باطل . لأن الماهية حال كونها موجودة ، واجبة الوجود ، ووجوب الوجود يغني عن المؤثر ولا يحوج إليه. والثاني باطل . لأن الماهية حال كونها معدومة ممتنعة الوجود . وامتناع الوجود يغني عن المؤثر. فلم يبق إلا أن يقال : المحوج إلى المؤثر هو الماهية مع حذف هذين القيدين . فنقول : ما لم تكن الماهية قابلة للوجود والعدم، امتنع أن تفتقر إلى المؤثر في رجحان أحد الطرفين على الآخر. وهذه القابلية نفس الإمكان. فيثبت : أن المحوج إلى المؤثر هو الإمكان فقط .
The ninth proof: The source of need is either the quiddity in the state of being existent, or the quiddity in the state of being non-existent, or the quiddity with both these conditions removed. The first is invalid, because the quiddity in the state of being existent is necessary of existence, and necessity of existence suffices from the effect-causing agent and does not necessitate need to it. The second is invalid, because the quiddity in the state of being non-existent is impossible of existence, and impossibility of existence suffices from the effect-causing agent. So nothing remains except to say that what necessitates need to the effect-causing agent is the quiddity with both these conditions removed. Then we say: As long as the quiddity is not receptive to existence and non-existence, it is impossible that it be in need of the effect-causing agent for the preponderance of one side over the other. And this receptivity is itself possibility. Thus it is established that what necessitates need to the effect-causing agent is only possibility.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth proof by analyzing three possible states of the quiddity (mahiyya) to determine what necessitates need for an external cause. The three states are: (1) the quiddity as existent, (2) as non-existent, and (3) stripped of both conditions. The first state is invalid because existence makes the quiddity necessary, which precludes need. The second state is invalid because non-existence makes it impossible, also precluding need. Thus, only the third state remains: the quiddity considered without existence or non-existence. This quiddity has the capacity (qabiliyya) for both existence and non-existence, and that capacity is precisely possibility (al-imkan). Therefore, it is possibility alone that necessitates need for a preponderating factor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Need arises from a thing's existence or non-existence.
Correction: Need arises from the quiddity's neutrality—its possibility—not from its actual existence or non-existence.

Checkpoint

A) Existence of the quiddity
B) Non-existence of the quiddity
C) The quiddity stripped of both existence and non-existence
D) The combination of existence and non-existence

Key Takeaway
The source of need is possibility alone, not existence or non-existence.

It needed Allah to tip the scale. Does this make you feel fragile or grateful? Will you thank Allah today for every breath as if it were the first—because it is?

The proof eliminates existence and non-existence as sources of need. So your need for Allah is not from your existent state or your non-existent state, but from your essential possibility. The Tenth Proof: Need and Origination
The author argues against the necessity of need being tied to origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument against the necessity of need being tied to origination, and why the first originated thing would not be needy.

Core Idea
If need required origination, the first thing would need no cause.

This lesson presents the tenth proof from Al-Matalibul Aliyah, which challenges the assumption that every needy thing must be originated. The author constructs a conditional argument to show that if that assumption were true, it would lead to a contradiction: the first originated thing would not be needy. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Let the author's own reasoning come through.
This passage is a dense logical argument; read slowly.
2
Identify the conditional structure
The proof is a conditional: if the needy must be originated, then the originated would not necessarily be needy. Recognize that this is a modus tollens argument.
The author later shows the consequent is false, so the antecedent must be false.
3
Follow the explanation of the first part
The author explains why the antecedent leads to the consequent: there must be a first originated thing that occurs without any cause, so it would not be needy.
This step reveals the core logical move.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since the consequent (that the originated would not be needy) is false, the antecedent (that the needy must be originated) is also false. Thus, need is not caused by origination.
This refutes the idea that all originated things are needy because they are originated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة : لو وجب في المحتاج أن يكون محدثاً ، لما وجب في المحدث أن يكون محتاجاً . والتالي باطل . فالمقدم مثله . بيان الأول : هو أنه لو وجب في المحتاج أن يكون محدثاً ، لوجب انتهاء الحوادث إلى حدوث هو أول الحوادث ، ولا يكون مسبوقاً بحادث آخر . ولو كان الأمر كذلك ، لكان حدوث ذلك الحادث، الذي هو أول الحوادث في ذلك الوقت ، دون ما قبله وما بعده : واقعاً لا لسبب أصلا . فحينئذ يكون ذلك الإختصاص حادثاً واقعاً ، لا عن سبب . فحينئذ لا يكون ذلك المحدث محتاجا . وذلك يقتضي أن لا يكون الحدوث علة للحاجة . فيثبت : أنه لو وجب في المحتاج أن يكون محدثاً لما وجب في المحدث أن يكون محتاجاً . والثاني باطل ، فالمقدم مثله
The tenth proof: If it were necessary for the needy to be originated, then it would not be necessary for the originated to be needy. The consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise. Explanation of the first: If it were necessary for the needy to be originated, then the originated things would have to end at an origination that is the first of the originated things, and it would not be preceded by another originated thing. And if it were so, then the occurrence of that originated thing which is the first of the originated things at that time, not before or after, would be actual without any cause at all. Thus that specification would be originated occurring without a cause. Then that originated thing would not be needy. And that entails that origination is not the cause of need. Thus it is established that if it were necessary for the needy to be originated, then it would not be necessary for the originated to be needy. The second is false, so the antecedent is likewise.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth proof, which is a conditional argument. The author assumes the claim that 'what is needy must be originated' (al-muhtaj la budda an yakuna muhdathan). From this, he derives that the originated would not necessarily be needy. Since the consequent is false (we know originated things do need causes), the antecedent must also be false. The reasoning is: if the needy must be originated, then there must be a first originated thing (the first of all originated things) that comes to be at a specific time without any prior cause. But such a first thing, lacking a cause, would not be needy. Thus, origination does not entail need, contradicting the original claim. Therefore, need is not due to origination.

Key Terms
المحتاج
المحدث
أول الحوادث
العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All originated things are needy because they are originated.
Correction: The tenth proof shows that if need were caused by origination, the first originated thing would have no cause and thus not be needy. Hence need is not due to origination itself.

Checkpoint
What does the tenth proof conclude about the relationship between need and origination?
A) Need is caused by origination.
B) Origination is not the cause of need.
C) The first originated thing needs a cause.
D) All originated things are needy.

Key Takeaway
Origination is not the cause of need; the first originated thing would lack a cause and thus not be needy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe you are independent, yet every moment of your existence depends on causes outside your control. If the first cause of all things needed no cause, what does that say about your own constant need? How does recognizing this shift your sense of self-sufficiency?
?
The proof shows that if need required origination, the first originated thing would have no cause—and thus no need. Are you willing to trace your own needs back to their ultimate source? Or do you stop at secondary causes because facing the first cause demands a change in how you live?
?
You know that everything originated is needy, but this proof says origination is not the cause of need. What then is the true cause of your need? And if you cannot answer that, are you content to live without knowing why you are so dependent? What are you actually relying on?
