The Eleventh Proof: Effecthood Does Not Require Origination
Proof that effecthood is not conditional on origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that effecthood (ma'luliyya) is not conditional on origination (huduth), based on the eternal non-existence of the effect.

Core Idea
Effecthood can be eternal if the cause is eternal.

This lesson presents the eleventh proof from al-Matalib al-Aliyah. The author argues that the relationship of cause and effect does not require the effect to have a beginning in time. By examining the non-existence of the effect, we see that effecthood can be predicated eternally.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the concise style of the proof.
The passage is a single sentence with three parts.
2
Analyze the logical premises
Identify the two premises: (1) The non-existence of the effect is due to the non-existence of the cause. (2) This non-existence has no beginning.
These premises establish an eternal state.
3
Connect to the conclusion
Understand why the conclusion follows: Since effecthood can be attributed in a beginningless state, it is not tied to origination (huduth).
This refutes the idea that effects must originate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الحادية عشر : إن عدم المعلول لعدم العلة ، ولا أول لعدم المعلول . فالمعلولية غير مشروطة بالحدوث
The eleventh proof: The non-existence of the effect is due to the non-existence of the cause, and there is no beginning for the non-existence of the effect. Thus effecthood is not conditioned by origination.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This brief passage presents the eleventh proof. It states that the non-existence of an effect ('adam al-ma'lul) is caused by the non-existence of the cause ('adam al-'illa). This non-existence of the effect has no beginning (la awwal lahu), meaning it is eternal. Since effecthood (ma'luliyya) can be predicated in such an eternal state, it is not conditional on origination (huduth). In other words, the relationship of cause and effect does not require the effect to be temporally originated; it can be an eternal effect as well.

Key Terms

عدم العلة
المعلولية
الحدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an effect must always come into existence after its cause.
Correction: This proof shows that effecthood can be eternal if the cause is eternal, so origination is not a necessary condition for effecthood.

Checkpoint

B) It is not conditioned by origination.
C) It requires the effect to have a beginning.
D) It is impossible without origination.

Key Takeaway
Effecthood is an eternal relationship and does not require the effect to have originated in time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If effecthood does not require origination, then your entire existence and every moment of your life are directly linked to the Eternal Cause without any gap of time. How does that immediacy affect the way you turn to God in supplication and trust?

The Tenth Proof: Need is Not Caused by Origination
The author presents the tenth proof showing that need for a cause is not due to origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that need for a cause is not conditional on origination.

Core Idea
Origination does not automatically imply need for a cause.

This lesson presents the tenth proof, which refutes the claim that need for a cause is conditional on origination. The author uses a conditional argument (if A then B) and a reductio ad absurdum to show that the premise leads to a contradiction. By following the reasoning, you will see why need and origination are not necessarily linked.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the conditional structure and the logical progression.
The proof is presented in a compact form; take your time.
2
Identify the conditional premise
The proof starts with 'If it were necessary for the needy to be originated, then the originated would not necessarily be needy.' This is the key conditional.
This is the first line of the passage.
3
Follow the reductio argument
The author explains why the consequent is false: if the needy were always originated, the first originated thing would occur without a cause, meaning it would not be needy. This contradicts the premise.
Think about the chain of origination and the necessity of a cause.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since the consequent is false, the antecedent (need implies origination) is also false. Thus, need for a cause is not due to origination.
The proof ends by restating the conditional and its negation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة : لو وجب في المحتاج أن يكون محدثاً ، لما وجب في المحدث أن يكون محتاجاً . والتالي باطل . فالمقدم مثله . بيان الأول : هو أنه لو وجب في المحتاج أن يكون محدثاً ، لوجب انتهاء الحوادث إلى حدوث هو أول الحوادث ، ولا يكون مسبوقاً بحادث آخر . ولو كان الأمر كذلك ، لكان حدوث ذلك الحادث، الذي هو أول الحوادث في ذلك الوقت ، دون ما قبله وما بعده : واقعاً لا لسبب أصلا . فحينئذ يكون ذلك الإختصاص حادثاً واقعاً ، لا عن سبب . فحينئذ لا يكون ذلك المحدث محتاجا . وذلك يقتضي أن لا يكون الحدوث علة للحاجة . فيثبت : أنه لو وجب في المحتاج أن يكون محدثاً [ لما ] وجب في المحدث أن يكون محتاجاً . والثاني باطل ، فالمقدم مثله.
The tenth proof: If it were necessary for the needy to be originated, then the originated would not necessarily be needy. And the consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise false. Explanation of the first: If it were necessary for the needy to be originated, it would be necessary for the series of originated things to end at an origination which is the first of the originated things, not preceded by another originated thing. If that were the case, then the happening of that originated thing, which is the first originated thing at that time, to the exclusion of what comes before it and after it, would occur without any cause at all. Then that particularization would be an originated occurrence without a cause. Then that originated thing would not be needy. And that would imply that origination is not the cause of need. So it is established: If it were necessary for the needy to be originated, then the originated would not necessarily be needy. And the second is false, so the antecedent is likewise false.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth proof in a series of arguments against the claim that need for a cause is conditional on origination (huduth). The author states the premise: if being needy (muhtaj) necessarily implies being originated (muhdath), then the converse would not hold—an originated thing would not necessarily be needy. The consequent is false, so the antecedent is false. He then explains: if every needy thing must be originated, the chain of originated things would lead to a first originated event not preceded by another. That first event's occurrence at a specific time without a cause would mean it is not needy, contradicting the idea that origination causes need. Thus, need is not tied to origination.

Key Terms
حاجة (need)
حدوث (origination)
محتاج (needy)
محدث (originated)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a thing is originated, it must have a cause (need), so need is the same as being originated.
Correction: This proof shows that origination does not automatically entail need. In fact, the first originated thing would not need a cause if need were tied to origination, which shows that need is a separate attribute.

Checkpoint
What does the tenth proof conclude about the relationship between need and origination?
A) Need requires origination.
B) Origination requires need.
C) Need is not necessarily tied to origination.
D) The first originated event is not needy.

Key Takeaway
Need for a cause does not come from being originated; it is a separate attribute.

How does that make you feel about your daily actions?

The Eleventh Proof: Effecthood Does Not Require Origination
A concise proof that effecthood does not require temporal origination.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
An effect's non-existence has no beginning, so being an effect doesn't require having a temporal start.

This lesson presents a concise proof from causality to argue that effecthood does not depend on huduth (temporal origination). The proof hinges on the eternal non-existence of the effect before its cause exists. Understanding this helps clarify the relationship between cause and effect in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two premises and the conclusion.
The proof is very concise; every word matters.
2
Unpack the two premises
The first premise says non-existence of effect is due to non-existence of cause. The second says that non-existence has no beginning.
The second premise is crucial: the effect's non-existence is beginningless.
3
Grasp the conclusion
The conclusion is that effecthood is not conditional on huduth; rather, it is simply dependence on the cause.
This does not mean the effect itself is eternal, only its non-existence is beginningless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الحادية عشر : إن عدم المعلول لعدم العلة ، ولا أول لعدم المعلول . فالمعلولية غير مشروطة بالحدوث.
The eleventh proof: The non-existence of the effect is due to the non-existence of the cause, and there is no beginning to the non-existence of the effect. Thus, effecthood is not conditional on origination.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This is a very concise proof. The author argues that the non-existence (adam) of an effect is caused by the non-existence of its cause. This non-existence of the effect has no beginning—it is eternal in the sense that it was always non-existent until the cause exists. Therefore, effecthood (ma'luliyya) does not require temporal origination (huduth); it is simply based on dependence on the cause, which may be eternal or originated. The point is that the effect's non-existence is beginningless, so its existence when the cause exists is not necessarily a temporal beginning.

Key Terms
عدم
معلول
علة
معلولية
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof implies the effect itself is eternal.
Correction: No, it only states that the effect's non-existence is beginningless. The effect itself comes into existence when its cause exists, but that existence has a beginning only if the cause has a beginning.

Checkpoint
According to the eleventh proof, what is effecthood conditional on?
A) Origination.
B) Non-existence of the cause.
C) Dependence on the cause, not origination.
D) The beginning of non-existence.

Key Takeaway
Effecthood is defined by causal dependence, not by temporal origination.

?
The non-existence of everything besides Allah has no beginning. That means before you were born, your non-existence was not a 'time' without you—it was just the absence of you. How does that shape your understanding of your own significance? If your existence is entirely dependent on Allah, why do you cling to created causes as if they are necessary? Commit to one specific way you will shift your reliance from creatures to the Creator.

The Twelfth Proof: The Chain of Effects Leads to an Eternal Effect
Reductio proof against requiring origination for agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that refutes the necessity of origination for causality, demonstrating that an infinite regress forces the existence of a permanent agency and an eternal effect.

Core Idea
Origination is not required for causality; otherwise, infinite regress would compel an eternal effect.

This proof is a classic reductio ad absurdum in Islamic theology. It challenges the claim that a cause must itself have come into existence before it can produce an effect. By showing that such a requirement leads to an endless chain of causes, the proof establishes that there must be a permanent, unoriginated cause whose effect is eternal. This argument is foundational for understanding the relationship between the eternal Creator and the created world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'
Pay attention to the logical connectors like 'otherwise' and 'so'.
2
Identify the condition being challenged
The condition is that for an agent to produce an effect, the agent must have been preceded by non-existence. Find where this is stated.
3
Follow the logical chain
Trace the argument: if every agency requires origination, then each originated agency needs another agency, leading to infinite regress. The conclusion is that an infinite regress is impossible, so there must be a permanent agency.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Understand the conclusion
The proof concludes that influence is not conditional on origination. An eternal cause can have an eternal effect.
This refutes the view that causality necessarily involves temporal priority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية عشر : إنه لو وجب في كون المؤثر مؤثراً في الأثر أن يكون مسبوقاً بالعدم، لوجب في كون المؤثر مؤثراً في الأثر ، أن يكون مسبوقاً بالعدم لأن المؤثرية لا تحصل إلا عند حصول الأثر ، لكن لا يجب في كل مؤثرية، أن تكون حادثة ، وإلا لافتقرت إلى مؤثرية أخرى ، ويلزم التسلسل . فالمؤثريا ت لابد وأن تنتهي بالآخرة ، إلى مؤثرية دائمة ، فيكون ذلك الأثر دائماً . وذلك يبطل القول بأن تأثير الشيء في الشيء : مشروط بالحدوث .
The twelfth proof: If it were necessary for the agent to be an agent in the effect that it be preceded by non-existence, then it would be necessary for the agent to be an agent in the effect that it be preceded by non-existence, because agential action does not occur except when the effect occurs. But it is not necessary for every agential action to be originated; otherwise it would need another agential action, and infinite regress would follow. So agential actions must ultimately end at a permanent agential action, and that effect would be perpetual. And that invalidates the statement that the influence of one thing upon another is conditional on origination.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This proof argues against the claim that an agent's effect must be preceded by the agent's own non-existence. The author states: if being an agent (mu'aththir) in producing an effect required being preceded by non-existence, then that precedence would be necessary because agency occurs only with the effect. However, not every instance of agency needs to be originated; if it did, it would require another agency, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul). Thus, agency must ultimately reach a permanent (da'ima) agency, and its effect would be eternal (da'iman). This refutes the view that causality is conditional on origination. In other words, an eternal cause can have an eternal effect; origination is not necessary for the causal relation.

Key Terms
مؤثر (agent/effective cause)
أثر (effect)
مؤثرية (agency)
حدوث (origination)
دوام (permanence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the proof claims all effects are eternal.
Correction: The proof only shows that if origination were a necessary condition, an infinite regress would force an eternal effect; thus origination is not a necessary condition. It does not assert that every effect is eternal.

Checkpoint
What does the twelfth proof conclude about the condition of influence?
A) Influence must be preceded by non-existence.
B) Influence is conditional on origination.
C) Influence is not conditional on origination.
D) All agency is originated.

Key Takeaway
The twelfth proof refutes the necessity of origination for causality by demonstrating that requiring it leads to an infinite regress, thus establishing the existence of a permanent, unoriginated cause and its eternal effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you find yourself more inclined to trust in visible, temporal causes or in the unseen, eternal Cause? What would change if you truly internalized that the chain of agency must end in a permanent Agent?
?
If every effect requires an originated cause, then who originated the first cause? This proof cuts off that regress. When you face uncertainty or lack of apparent causes, do you turn to the Eternal Cause, or do you anxiously search for temporal explanations?
?
This proof demands that you reconsider what you think is necessary for causality. Is there a belief you hold that 'this must have a beginning in time' that this argument challenges? Will you allow this truth to reshape your reliance on Allah as the Permanent Agent?

The Thirteenth Proof: Need is Not Due to Origination Alone
A reductio proof that need is not solely from origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a powerful reductio argument that shows need cannot be caused solely by origination, because enduring qualities still depend on their sustaining causes.

Core Idea
If origination caused need, continuing things would not need their causes—but they do.

In previous proofs, the author established that contingent beings need a cause. Now he refines the argument: is that need due to the fact that they are originated? This proof demonstrates that need is not merely a consequence of origination; it is intrinsic to contingency itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the overall argument. Note the conditional 'if...then' structure.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Identify the assumption and consequences
The author assumes that need is caused by origination. From this, he derives that enduring states (motion, knowledge) would not need their sustaining factors during duration. Identify these two examples.
3
Understand the refutation
The author notes that theologians reject the independence of motion from its source and of knowledge from life. This shows the assumption is false. Ask: why does this refute the idea that need is only from origination?
The continuity of need proves it is not purely a function of coming into existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة عشر : إن علة الحاجة لو كانت هي الحدوث ، لاستغنت المتحركية عن الحركة حال دوامها ، ولاستغنت العالمية عن العلم حال دوامها والمتكلمون : لا يقولون به. ولا يستغني العلم عن الحياة حال دوامه.
The thirteenth proof: If the cause of need were origination, then the state of being moving would be independent of motion during its duration, and the state of being knowing would be independent of knowledge during its duration. But the theologians do not say that. And knowledge is not independent of life during its duration.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This proof is a reductio. The author supposes that need (haja) is caused by origination (huduth). If that were so, then once something exists and continues, it would no longer need its sustaining cause. For example, a moving object (mutaharrik) would not need motion (haraka) while it continues moving, and a knower would not need knowledge while continuing to know. But this is false; theologians (mutakallimun) do not accept that motion can persist without a source of motion, nor that knowledge persists without life. Since these needs continue, origination cannot be the sole cause of need; rather, need is intrinsic to the contingent being itself.

Key Terms
حاجة (need)
حدوث (origination)
متحركية (state of being moving)
حركة (motion)
عالمية (state of being knowing)
علم (knowledge)
حياة (life)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof denies that origination involves any need at all.
Correction: The proof only denies that origination is the exclusive cause of need. Need also attaches to enduring states, so it is intrinsic to contingent existence.

Checkpoint
According to the thirteenth proof, if need were caused by origination, what would follow?
A) Motion would not need motion during duration.
B) Knowledge would need life during duration.
C) Theologians would accept this.
D) Need would be permanent.

Key Takeaway
Need is not solely from origination; it is an essential property of contingent existence itself.

?

The Fourteenth Proof: External Concomitants Are Effects of Essences
The author provides a detailed proof using the example of three's oddness to show need is not conditional on origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the example of three's oddness demonstrates that an effect's dependence on its cause is based on contingency (imkan) rather than temporal origination (huduth).

Core Idea
Need for a cause depends on contingency, not origination.

This lesson presents a sophisticated proof from the Mutakallimun that the dependence of an effect on its cause is rooted in the inherent possibility of the essence, not in whether it is created in time. Using the simple example of oddness being a necessary concomitant of three, the author shows that the relation of effect to cause can be eternal and does not require temporal beginning. The proof is decisive against those who argue that only originated things need a cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the literal translation. Let the logical flow of the proof become clear.
The passage is dense but proceeds step by step.
2
Identify the example and its logical structure
Notice that the author uses the concrete example of three being odd and four being even. This example illustrates how a concomitant (oddness) follows from an essence (threeness) without temporal beginning.
Oddness is an external concomitant — it is not part of the essence but necessarily flows from it.
3
Trace the argument for dependence
Follow the reasoning: when three units combine, the essence of three is complete. Then oddness necessarily arises. Oddness depends on the essence. Everything dependent is possible. What is possible needs an agent. The only agent here is the essence itself (or whatever sustains it).
The argument shows that oddness is an effect (ma'lul) of the essence.
4
Grasp the universal conclusion
The proof concludes that the dependence of an effect on its cause is not conditional on the effect being originated (muhdath). The oddness of three is eternal yet dependent. Thus, need for a cause is due to contingency (imkan), not origination.
This refutes the view that only temporal things need a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة عشر : إن اللوازم الخارجية معلولات الماهيات . مثلاً : كون الثلاثة فرداً ، والأربعة زوجاً : أوصاف خارجة عن الماهية ، لازمة لها . والدليل عليه : أنه إذا حصل ثلاثة وحدات ، فقد حصلت الثلاثة . فالمقوم الماهية الثلاثة : اجتماع الوحدات الثلاثة . ثم إذا تمت هذه الماهية ، ترتب عليها كونها فرداً. فالفردية صفة من صفاتها ، ولاحق من لواحقها . وهذا اللاحق لا يكون موجوداً قائماً بنفسه ، بل لابد له من ماهية يكون هو ، لاحقاً لها ، وصفة من صفاتها. وكل ما افتقر إلى غيره ، فهو ممكن لذاته . وكل ممكن لذاته ، فلا بد له من مؤثر . ولا مؤثر في حصول هذا اللاحق، إلا هذه الماهية. فيثبت : أن كون الثلاثة : ثلاثة ، يوجب الفردية. وكون الأربعة : أربعة يوجب الزوجية . ثم إنه لا يعقل خلو الثلاثة عن الفردية البتة ، ولا خلو الأربعة عن الزوجية البتة . وهذا برهان قاطع على أن افتقار المعلول إلى العلة ، غير مشروط بكون المعلول محدثاً . وههنا آخر الكلام في مباحث القائلين بالقدم . والله أعلم .
The fourteenth proof: External concomitants are effects of essences. For example: three being odd, and four being even are attributes external to the essence, necessarily concomitant to it. The proof of that is: when three units are obtained, three is obtained. The constitutive of the essence of three is the combination of three units. Then when this essence is complete, its being odd follows from it. For oddness is one of its attributes and one of its concomitants. This concomitant does not exist subsisting by itself; rather, it must have an essence to which it is consequent and an attribute of its attributes. Everything that depends on another is possible in itself. Everything possible in itself must have an agent. And there is no agent in the attainment of this concomitant except this essence. So it is established: that three being three necessitates oddness, and four being four necessitates evenness. Moreover, it is inconceivable that three be free from oddness at all, nor four free from evenness at all. And this is a decisive proof that the dependence of the effect upon the cause is not conditional on the effect being originated. And here ends the discussion on the investigations of those who affirm eternity. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This lengthy proof demonstrates that need for a cause is based on contingency (imkan), not origination (huduth). The author uses the example of external concomitants (lawazim kharijiyya) such as oddness for three and evenness for four. These are attributes that follow from the essence (mahiyya) without temporal origination. The argument: when three units combine, the essence of three is complete; then oddness necessarily follows. This oddness depends on the essence of three, which is possible in itself (mumkin li-dhatihi). Since it is possible, it needs an agent (mu'aththir). The only agent that brings about this oddness is the essence itself (or what sustains it). Thus, the effect (oddness) depends on its cause (the essence) eternally, not temporally. This shows that effecthood does not require the effect to be originated (muhdath). The proof concludes that need is due to the very nature of contingency.

Key Terms
لازم خارجي (external concomitant)
ماهية (essence)
معلول (effect)
علة (cause)
إمكان (contingency)
حدوث (origination)
فرد (odd)
زوج (even)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof claims that the oddness of three is created by three itself.
Correction: The proof does not attribute creative power to the essence; rather, it shows that the relation of dependence can be eternal. The oddness is an effect that follows from the essence, but the ultimate cause is the agent that sustains the essence.

Checkpoint
What does the example of three's oddness prove according to the fourteenth proof?
A) Oddness is an accident that begins in time.
B) The effect's dependence on its cause is not conditional on origination.
C) Three causes oddness through its own power.
D) Oddness is independent of the essence.

Key Takeaway
The dependence of an effect on its cause is rooted in the inherent contingency of the essence, not in temporal origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your existence at this very moment depends entirely on Allah, just as oddness depends on the essence of three. Do you live with the awareness that you are contingent in every breath, or do you take your being for granted? What would change in your daily actions if you genuinely felt that dependence?
?
The proof shows that need for a cause does not require temporal origination—you need Allah now, not just at your birth. When you sin, do you act as if you are independent, forgetting that your very ability to sin is sustained by Allah? How can you cultivate a constant sense of reliance on Him?
?
Consider that no ‘natural’ cause can operate without Allah’s continuous creation. Does your heart attribute power to created causes, or does it see them as mere occasions for Allah’s action? Ask yourself: In a moment of crisis, do you instinctively turn to Allah or to worldly means?

Infinite Regress Argument Against Conditional Causation
Argument showing conditional causation leads to infinite regress and permanent effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that causation is conditional on origination leads to an infinite regress and is therefore invalid.

Core Idea
Conditional causation leads to infinite regress, requiring a permanent cause and effect.

This passage presents a classic reductio argument in Islamic theology. The opponent claims that a cause's influence depends on its effect being originated. The author refutes this by showing it leads to an infinite chain of causes, which is impossible. Instead, the chain must end in a permanent cause, making the effect permanent as well.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the argument's structure become clear.
Notice the conditional 'otherwise' which signals the reductio.
2
Identify the reductio structure
The passage begins with a premise, then shows it leads to infinite regress (absurd), then proposes a necessary resolution, and finally draws a conclusion that contradicts the original premise.
This is a standard method in kalam: assume the opponent's position, then derive a contradiction.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that the statement 'influence is conditional upon origination' is invalid. Ask yourself: why does the author think that? The answer lies in the permanent effect that results from the permanent cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مؤثرية، أن تكون حادثة، وإلا لافتقرت إلى مؤثرية أخرى، ويلزم التسلسل. فالمؤثريات لابد وأن تنتهي بالآخرة، إلى مؤثرية دائمة، فيكون ذلك الأثر دائماً. وذلك يبطل القول بأن تأثير الشيء في الشيء: مشروط بالحدوث.
That an effective cause be originated, otherwise it would need another effective cause, leading to infinite regress. The effective causes must eventually end at a permanent effective cause, so that effect becomes permanent. And that invalidates the statement that the influence of one thing on another is conditional upon origination.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage presents a reductio ad absurdum argument. The opponent claims that a cause's influence is conditional upon the effect's being originated (hadith). The author argues that if that were true, then the cause itself, being originated, would need another cause, leading to an infinite regress. To avoid regress, the series must end in a permanent cause (mu’aththir dā’im). But then the effect of that permanent cause would also be permanent, contradicting the conditional claim. Therefore, the claim that causation is conditional on origination is false.

Key Terms
مؤثرية
تسلسل
مؤثرية دائمة
أثر دائم
مشروط بالحدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinite regress is possible or acceptable in causal series.
Correction: In Islamic kalam, infinite regress (tasalsul) in actual causal chains is impossible because it leads to logical absurdities and contradicts the necessity of a first cause.

Checkpoint
What does the argument conclude about the claim that influence is conditional upon origination?
A) It is proven true.
B) It is invalidated because it leads to an infinite regress and a permanent effect.
C) It is valid only for originated beings.
D) It is unrelated to infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
The claim that causation is conditional on origination is false because it leads to infinite regress and requires a permanent cause and effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Need for Cause Is Not Due to Origination Alone
Sanusi argues that if need for cause were due to origination, enduring states would need no cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's argument that the need for a cause cannot be due to origination alone, and why this refutes a common theological position.

Core Idea
Need for a cause persists as long as the effect lasts — not just at its origin.

This passage presents a concise but powerful argument that challenges a common view about causation. Sanusi argues that if the need for a cause were merely due to an entity's beginning (origination), then once it exists, it would no longer need its sustaining cause. Yet we know that continuing states like motion and knowledge still depend on their causes. This refutes the premise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the logical structure of the argument.
Notice the conditional 'if...then' structure.
2
Break down the 'if-then' structure
Identify the premise (need due to origination) and the consequences (independence during continuance).
3
Identify the examples used
See how movability (متحركية) and knowing (عالمية) are used to illustrate the point.
These are states that continue over time.
4
Understand the refutation
The mutakallimūn reject the consequence, so the premise is false.
The additional example of knowledge not being independent of life reinforces the argument.
5
Reflect on the broader significance
This argument strengthens the doctrine that effects continuously depend on their cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة عشر : إن علة الحاجة لو كانت هي الحدوث، لاستغنت المتحركية عن الحركة حال دوامها، ولاستغنت العالمية عن العلم حال دوامها والمتكلمون : لا يقولون به. ولا يستغني العلم عن الحياة حال دوامه.
The thirteenth proof: If the cause of need were origination, then movability would be independent of motion during its continuance, and the state of being knowing would be independent of knowledge during its continuance. The theologians do not say that. Nor is knowledge independent of life during its continuance.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage presents the thirteenth proof against the conditionality of causation upon origination. The author argues: if the need for a cause were due to origination, then once a thing is originated and continues to exist, it would no longer need the cause for its continuance. For example, the state of being moving (mutaharrikiyya) would not need motion (the sustaining cause) during its continuance. Similarly, the state of being knowing (al-‘ālamiyya) would not need knowledge during its continuance. But the mutakallimūn do not hold that; they affirm that an effect continues to need its cause as long as it exists. Furthermore, knowledge is not independent of life (the condition of the knower) during its continuance. Thus, the premise that need is due to origination is false.

Key Terms
علة الحاجة
الحدوث
المتحركية
العالمية
المتكلمون
دوام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument is about motion needing a cause only at origination; but the author uses the opposite implication to refute that view.
Correction: The argument assumes the premise to show that if it were true, continuing states would need no cause, which is false, so the premise is false.

Checkpoint

A) The effect would need no cause after origination.
B) Motion would still need a cause during its continuance.
C) Knowledge would need life only at origination.
D) The theologians agree with this premise.

Key Takeaway
The need for a cause is not limited to origination; it persists for the duration of the effect's existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that your very existence, moment by moment, requires a continuous cause. Do you live as if God sustains you every second, or do you forget Him once you feel 'settled'? What would change if you truly internalized that you need Him now as much as at your first breath?
?
The argument uses knowledge's dependence on life. How does this apply to your own religious knowledge? Do you assume that once you learn something about Islam it stays with you, or do you actively renew it through study, reflection, and practice? What is one way you can 'sustain' your faith this week?
?
If you believed that every good quality you have (like patience, gratitude, or understanding) is constantly being sustained by Allah, how would that change your attitude in moments of weakness? Write down one concrete situation where you need to remember that you are never independent of His help.

The Fourteenth Proof: Necessary Concomitants and Causality
Sanusi proves that necessary concomitants are effects of essences, showing causality is not tied to origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how necessary concomitants demonstrate that causality is not conditional upon origination.

Core Idea
Necessary concomitants prove that causality does not require origination.

This lesson presents Sanusi's fourteenth proof, which uses the example of numerical properties—oddness for three and evenness for four—to show that an effect can depend on a cause without being temporally originated. The proof is decisive for the discussion on pre-eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: example, evidence, reasoning, conclusion.
The footnote marker (1) after 'الماهيات' refers to a textual variant.
2
Identify the example and its significance
The example is three being odd and four being even. Understand why these are external necessary concomitants (lawāzim khārijiyya).
They are not part of the essence but follow necessarily from it.
3
Follow the logical argument step by step
Trace how Sanusi argues: when three units combine, the quiddity is formed; oddness necessarily follows; oddness depends on the quiddity; dependency implies possibility; possibility requires a cause; the only cause is the quiddity.
4
Grasp the broader conclusion
Understand that this causal relation is eternal—oddness never leaves three. Thus, causality does not require the effect to be originated (muḥdath).
This refutes those who say only originated things have causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة عشر : إن اللوازم الخارجية معلولات الماهيات (١). مثلاً : كون الثلاثة فرداً، والأربعة زوجاً : أوصاف خارجة عن الماهية، لازمة لها . والدليل عليه : أنه إذا حصل ثلاثة وحدات، فقد حصلت الثلاثة . فالمقـوم الماهية الثلاثة : اجتماع الوحدات الثلاثة . ثم إذا تمت هذه الماهية، ترتب عليها كونها فرداً. فالفردية صفة من صفاتها، ولا حق من لواحقها . وهذا اللاحق لا يكون موجوداً قائماً بنفسه، بل لابد له من ماهية يكون هو، لاحقاً لها، وصفة من صفاتها. وكل ما افتقر إلى غيره، فهو ممكن لذاته . وكل ممكن لذاته، فلا بد له من مؤثر . ولا مؤثر في حصول هذا اللاحق، إلا هذه الماهية. فيثبت : أن كون الثلاثة : ثلاثة، يوجب الفردية. وكون الأربعة : أربعة يوجب الزوجية . ثم إنه لا يعقل خلو الثلاثة عن الفردية البتة، ولا خلو الأربعة عن الزوجية البتة
وهذا برهان قاطع على أن افتقار المعلول إلى العلة، غير مشروط بكون المعلول محدثاً
وههنا آخر الكلام في مباحث القائلين بالقدم . والله أعلم .
The fourteenth proof: External necessary concomitants are effects of quiddities (1). For example: three being odd, and four being even are attributes external to the quiddity, necessary for it. The evidence for this is that when three units are obtained, the number three is obtained. The constitutive of the quiddity of three is the aggregation of the three units. Then when this quiddity is completed, its being odd follows. So oddness is an attribute of its attributes, and a consequent of its consequents. This consequent does not exist subsisting by itself, but must have a quiddity to which it is a consequent and an attribute of its attributes. And everything that depends on another is possible in itself. And everything possible in itself must have an effecting cause. And there is no effecting cause in the obtaining of this consequent except this quiddity. Thus it is established that three being three necessitates oddness, and four being four necessitates evenness. Then it is not conceivable that three is devoid of oddness at all, nor four devoid of evenness at all. And this is a decisive proof that the dependence of the effect on the cause is not conditional upon the effect being originated. And here ends the discussion of the proponents of pre-eternity. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage presents the fourteenth proof, which uses the example of necessary concomitants (lawāzim) to show that causality is not conditional on origination. The author argues that properties such as oddness for three and evenness for four are external necessary attributes that follow from the quiddity (māhiyya). The proof proceeds as follows: 1) When three units are combined, the quiddity of three is formed. 2) Once this quiddity is complete, oddness necessarily follows. 3) Oddness is not self-subsistent; it depends on the quiddity of three. 4) Anything dependent on another is possible in itself (mumkin li-dhātihī) and therefore requires a cause. 5) The only possible cause for oddness is the quiddity itself. 6) Therefore, the quiddity of three is the cause of its oddness. 7) This causal relation is eternal, not temporal, because oddness is never absent when the quiddity exists. 8) Thus, causality does not require the effect to be originated (muḥdath). The passage concludes the discussion of the proponents of pre-eternity (al-qā’ilūn bi-l-qidam).

Key Terms
لوازم خارجية
ماهيات
فرد
زوج
لاحق
ممكن لذاته
مؤثر
برهان قاطع
قدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that necessary concomitants like oddness are part of the essence of the number.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that oddness is an external attribute that follows from the essence but is not part of it. It depends on the quiddity as its cause.

Checkpoint
What does the fourteenth proof conclude about the dependence of the effect on the cause?
A) It is conditional upon the effect being originated.
B) It is not conditional upon the effect being originated.
C) It only applies to mental entities.
D) It is irrelevant to the discussion of pre-eternity.

Key Takeaway
The dependence of an effect on its cause does not require the effect to be temporally originated; necessary concomitants demonstrate eternal causality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If causality does not require temporal precedence, then your very existence as a contingent being is constantly dependent on the Creator every moment. Do you live with that constant awareness, or do you feel independent? What would change if you truly felt that dependence?
?
The proof shows that even the most obvious facts (like three being odd) have a cause. Where in your life have you assumed something 'just is' without a cause, and how does this lesson challenge that assumption?
?
The proof refutes those who claim that only originated things need a cause. Is there an area in your belief where you unconsciously limit God's power to temporal causation? How can you correct that?

The Fourth Proof: Daily Increments Show the Past Is Finite
The author presents the fourth proof that the past is finite by showing daily increments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth proof from daily increments that the past cannot be infinite.

Core Idea
If the past were infinite, adding a day would not increase the total.

The fourth proof is elegantly simple: each new day adds one unit to the total number of past days. That means the total before today was smaller than the total after today. This change — an increase — is something that happens perpetually. But if the past were infinite, such an increase would be impossible, because infinity cannot be added to. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author describes the process of increase and decrease.
The passage is short but dense — take your time.
2
Identify the core claim
What is the author trying to prove? That the past is finite. How? By showing that the total of past events changes when a new day occurs.
3
Understand the logical structure
The argument has two premises: (1) Whenever a day occurs, the past sum increases by one. (2) Therefore, the sum before today is less than the sum after today. The implicit conclusion: such increases could not happen if the past were infinite.
This is a simple comparative argument.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Some may object that infinite sets can be added to (e.g., Hilbert's Hotel). But the author's notion of 'sum' here is a cumulative total that must be finite to allow increase.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إنه كلما حدث (۱) يوم ، فإنه تزاد الجملة الماضية بيوم واحد ، فكان المجموع الحاصل [ قبل هذا اليوم ، أنقص من المجموع الحاصل(۲) ] بعد حصول هذا اليوم. وهذه الزيادات والنقصانات حاصلة أبداً .
The fourth [argument]: Whenever a day occurs, the accumulated past [sum] is increased by one day, so the total sum [obtained] before this day is less than the total sum [obtained] after the occurrence of this day. And these increases and decreases occur perpetually.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof that the past must be finite. It is a simple argument from addition: each new day adds one unit to the total of past events. Therefore, the total before today is less than the total after today. This demonstrates that the sum of past events is subject to increase and decrease, which implies finitude (as will be argued later). The author uses the phrase 'الجملة الماضية' (the past sum) to refer to the cumulative count of past days or events. The argument relies on the intuitive notion that adding one unit changes the total, so the total cannot be infinite because an infinite quantity cannot be increased (as a later premise will clarify). The proof is straightforward: because the sum changes, it must be finite.

Key Terms
الجملة الماضية
زيادة
نقصان
أبد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Adding a day to an infinite past would still result in an infinite past, so the argument fails.
Correction: The argument assumes a conception of 'sum' as a countable total that can be increased. If the past were actually infinite, adding one would not change its cardinality, but the author is working with a notion of temporal succession where each day adds a new unit, implying the total is finite.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth proof, what happens to the total of past events when a new day occurs?
A) It stays the same.
B) It increases by one day.
C) It decreases by one day.
D) It becomes infinite.

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof shows that because the sum of past events increases with each new day, the past cannot be infinite but must be finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that each new day increases the total sum of your past. You cannot stop the addition. Does this not make you feel the weight of each day? If the past is finite, then your personal timeline will also come to an end. Will you live today as if it is your last chance to add a meaningful day?
?
The author says increases and decreases occur perpetually. Think about your own life: are your deeds increasing in quality? Or are you experiencing a net loss spiritually as days pass? How will you ensure that tomorrow's sum is greater in righteousness than today's?

The Fifth Proof: Comparing Two Time Intervals
Sanusi presents the fifth proof by comparing two infinite sums of past events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth proof that the past must be finite by comparing two sums of past events.

Core Idea
Two infinite sums cannot differ; the past cannot be infinite.

This lesson presents the fifth proof from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah. The argument compares two intervals of past events to show that an infinite past leads to contradiction. We will examine how the author juxtaposes two sums of events and derives the necessity of a finite past.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument.
Notice the two sums: from today to eternity and from the Flood to eternity.
2
Identify the two sums
Identify sum A (present day to eternity) and sum B (Flood to eternity). Note that sum A is larger by the time between Flood and present.
3
Analyze the juxtaposition
The author aligns the two sums from the side nearest to us. Consider the two possibilities: either they extend equally without difference, or a difference appears.
This is the crux of the proof.
4
Examine the absurdity
If no difference, the larger sum equals the smaller, which is absurd. If a difference appears, one sum is less than the other, meaning it is finite.
Thus the past cannot be infinite.
5
Draw the conclusion
Conclude that the past must be finite. Reflect on how this argument fits into the broader discussion of divine attributes and causality.
This is a standard kalam cosmological argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : إنا إذا أخذنا الحوادث الماضية من هذا اليوم الحاضر إلى الأزل ، جملة واحدة. وأخذناها من أول وقت الطوفان إلى الأزل جملة أخرى . فلا شك أن الجملة الأولى أزيد من الجملة الثانية ، بالمقدار الذي حصل من زمان الطوفان إلى هذا اليوم . فإذا أطبقنا هاتين الجملتين من الجانب الذي يلينا ، فإما أن يمتد (۳) من الجانب الأول أبداً من غير حصول التفاوت ، أو لابد وأن يظهر التفاوت. والأول يقتضي أن يكون الزائد مساوياً للناقص . وهو محال . والثاني يقتضي [ أن تكون (٤) ] إحدى الجملتين ناقصة عن الأخرى .
The fifth [argument]: If we take the past events from this present day to eternity [past] as one sum. And we take them from the first time of the Flood to eternity [past] as another sum. There is no doubt that the first sum is greater than the second sum by the amount that occurred from the time of the Flood to this day. Then if we juxtapose these two sums from the side nearest to us, either [the side] extends from the first side perpetually without any difference occurring, or the difference must appear. The first [alternative] entails that the greater is equal to the lesser, which is absurd. And the second [alternative] entails that one of the two sums is less than the other.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof. It compares two sums of past events: (1) from the present day back to eternity (i.e., all past events) and (2) from the time of the Flood back to eternity (i.e., past events before the Flood). Obviously, the first sum is larger by the amount of time between the Flood and today. If both sums are infinite, then when we align them starting from the present (i.e., the nearer side), either they both extend infinitely without any difference, or a difference must appear. If they both extend infinitely without difference, then the greater (the sum including post-Flood events) would be equal to the lesser (the sum before the Flood), which is absurd. If a difference appears, then one sum is less than the other, meaning it is finite. Thus, the past must be finite.

Key Terms
الحوادث الماضية
الأزل
الطوفان
تفاوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Comparing two infinite sets by aligning their endpoints is always valid.
Correction: This argument assumes a specific ordering (from the present end) which is appropriate for past events, but it does not prove all comparisons of infinite sets lead to contradiction.

Checkpoint
What does the fifth proof conclude about the two sums of past events?
A) Both are infinite.
B) One must be less than the other.
C) They are equal.
D) The sum from the Flood is larger.

Key Takeaway
The fifth proof demonstrates that an infinite past leads to contradiction, so the past must be finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the past is finite and creation has a beginning, then your life is not an accident of an endless chain. You were intentionally brought into existence. How does that truth expose the excuses you make for ignoring your Creator's commands? Will you live today as if your existence matters or as if it is meaningless?
?
The proof shows that infinity in the past leads to logical absurdity. But you often live as if your time is infinite — procrastinating repentance, delaying good deeds. If the past is finite, then the future is also uncertain. What is one specific sin you have been postponing leaving? How will you cut it off today, not assuming tomorrow belongs to you?

The Sixth Proof: Infinite Cycles and Accumulating Souls
A proof against infinite past using accumulative souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the example of immortal souls from infinite past cycles leads to an absurdity that disproves an eternal past.

Core Idea
An infinite past would produce an infinite that can increase, which is impossible.

This proof uses a clever hypothetical: if something imperishable arises in each cycle of an infinite past, the sum today would be infinite. Yet we know that number can increase (e.g., more souls after Noah's flood than before). An infinite cannot increase, so the cycles cannot be infinite. The argument is particularly effective because it borrows the premise of immortal souls from the philosophers themselves.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow: a general supposition, then an example from the philosophers, then the contradiction.
The passage begins with 'السادس' indicating this is the sixth proof.
2
Understand the supposition
The author asks us to suppose that in every past cycle something comes into being and never perishes. If the cycles are infinite, the collection of those things today would be infinite in number.
This is a conditional statement: if infinite cycles and eternal persistence, then infinite sum.
3
Examine the example of rational souls
The philosophers claim that rational souls are created in each cycle and do not admit of non-existence. Applying the supposition, the total souls existing today would be infinite.
The author uses the philosophers' own doctrine to build the argument (ad hominem).
4
Identify the contradiction
The text states that this infinite sum is 'قابل للزيادة والنقصان' (susceptible to increase and decrease). It gives the example that the number of souls at the time of Noah's flood was less than today, proving increase is possible.
An infinite number cannot increase or decrease; therefore the original assumption of infinite past cycles is false.
5
Reflect on the argument's force
Even if one does not accept the immortality of souls, the argument shows that if an eternal past existed with any imperishable things, an absurdity follows. This strengthens the case for a finite past.
The argument relies on the impossibility of an actual infinite in the real world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : إنا لو قدرنا أنه حدث في كل دورة من الدورات (٥) الماضية شيء ، وبقي بحيث لا يغنى البتة . فعلى هذا التقدير لو كانت الدورات الماضية غير متناهية ، لكان مجموع تلك الأشياء المجتمعة في زماننا عدداً غير متناهي ومثاله : أن عند الفلاسفة حدث في كل دورة من الأدوار الماضية أعداد من النفوس الناطقة . ثم قالوا : * النفوس الناطقة لا تقبل العدم، فعلى هذا التقدير ، يلزم أن يقال : إن مجموع النفوس الناطقة الموجودة في هذا اليوم : عدد غير متناه . ثم لا شك أن هذا المجموع قابل للزيادة والنقصان . لأن من المعلوم بالضرورة : أن العدد الذي كان حاصلا في زمان الطوفان ، أقل من العدد الذي حصل الآن
The sixth [argument]: If we suppose that in every cycle of the past cycles something occurred and remained such that it never perishes at all. Then on this supposition, if the past cycles were infinite, the sum of those things gathered in our time would be an infinite number. An example: according to the philosophers, there occurred in every cycle of the past cycles numbers of rational souls. Then they said: 'The rational souls do not admit of non-existence.' So on this supposition, it necessarily follows that the sum of rational souls existing today is an infinite number. Then there is no doubt that this sum is susceptible to increase and decrease. Because it is known necessarily that the number that existed at the time of the Flood is less than the number that exists now.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof, which uses an example from the philosophers regarding rational souls. If past cycles were infinite, and in each cycle something imperishable (like a rational soul) came into being and survived, then the total number of such things existing today would be infinite. But we know that the number of souls at the time of the Flood was less than the number today, so the total can increase and decrease. An infinite number cannot increase or decrease, so the cycles must be finite.

Key Terms
دورات
نفوس ناطقة
عدم
زيادة
نقصان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes souls are actually immortal as a fact.
Correction: The author is not asserting that souls are immortal; rather, he borrows the premise from the philosophers to show that even on their own assumptions, an infinite past leads to absurdity.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth proof, what does the assumption of infinite past cycles and persisting beings lead to?
A) The number of beings today would be finite.
B) The number would be infinite but cannot increase.
C) The number would be infinite and susceptible to increase.
D) The number would be zero.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of an infinite past is demonstrated by showing that it would entail an actual infinite that can increase, which is logically absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument uses the increase of souls from the Flood to now to show a contradiction. Reflect: Is your own soul being added to that count? If you knew your soul would exist forever, how would you change the way you live this finite life?
?
The proof shows that an infinite past leads to logical absurdity. Do you ever act as if your time is infinite, putting off accountability? What would it mean for you to truly believe that the past is finite and your present moment is unique?
?
The philosophers believed souls are immortal, yet their own belief leads to contradiction. Is there any belief you hold that, if followed to its logical conclusion, would contradict something you know to be true about Allah's creation? Are you willing to examine it?

The Seventh Proof: Infinite Cycles Produce Infinite Multiples of Souls
Seventh proof: reductio showing infinite past cycles yield absurd multiples of souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh proof's reductio ad absurdum: that if past cycles were infinite, the number of souls today would be unacceptably infinite multiple times over, implying cycles must be finite.

Core Idea
Infinite past cycles produce absurdly many souls.

The seventh proof continues the attack on the philosophers' claim of infinite past cycles. Here, the author uses a minimal assumption—just one soul per cycle—to show that even that would lead to an infinite number of souls today. Since the philosophers actually claim many souls per cycle, the absurdity multiplies.

Guided Path
1
Understand the minimal assumption
The argument begins by supposing that in each of the infinite past cycles, only one soul occurred and remained. This is a deliberately minimal assumption to show the absurdity even in the best case for the philosophers.
Note that 'cycle' here refers to a period of cosmic history in the philosophers' theory.
2
Trace the first consequence
If one soul per infinite cycle, then there would be an infinite number of souls existing now. This itself seems problematic because it implies an actual infinite of souls.
The author does not elaborate on why this is absurd; it's taken as self-evident that an infinite number of souls is unacceptable.
3
See the escalation with many souls per cycle
But the philosophers actually claim that each cycle produces many souls, not just one. So instead of one soul per cycle, we have many. The number of souls now then becomes infinite multiplied many times over—an even greater absurdity.
The phrase 'أضعافاً غير متناهية مراراً كثيرة' emphasizes the infinite repetition.
4
Grasp the conclusion about cycles
From this, the author deduces that the number of past cycles must be less than the number of rational souls. Since each cycle produces a finite number of souls, the cycles cannot be infinite; otherwise, the number of souls would be at least as large as the number of cycles, and the comparison 'less than' would be meaningless.
This conclusion points to the finitude of past cycles, a key claim of the mutakallimun.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : لو قدرنا أنه حصل في كل دورة من الدورات الماضية ، الغير المتناهية : نفس واحدة . وبقيت . لكان قد حصل الآن أعداد لا نهاية لها من النفوس . فإذا علمنا : أن الحاصل في كل واحد من الأدوار الماضية أعداد كثيرة من النفوس ، فحينئذ يلزم أن يكون العدد الموجود الآن من النفوس أضعافاً غير متناهية مراراً كثيرة. يدل على أن عدد الأدوار الماضية قابل للزيادة والنقصان . فإنا بينا أن عدد الأدوار الماضية، أقل من عدد النفوس الناطقة
The seventh [argument]: If we suppose that in every cycle of the past infinite cycles, one soul occurred and remained, then there would have occurred now numbers of souls with no end. So if we know that what occurred in each one of the past cycles are many numbers of souls, then it necessarily follows that the number of souls existing now is infinite multiples many times over. This indicates that the number of past cycles is susceptible to increase and decrease. For we have shown that the number of past cycles is less than the number of rational souls.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This seventh proof builds on the previous one. It starts with a minimal assumption: one soul per infinite past cycle. That alone would yield an infinite number of souls today. But in reality, the philosophers claim that each cycle produces many souls, so the current number of souls would be multiples of infinity—an even more absurd consequence. Moreover, the number of cycles is less than the number of souls (since each cycle has many souls), so the number of cycles is subject to increase and decrease, implying it is finite.

Key Terms
دورات
نفوس
أضعاف لا متناهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that infinite past cycles and infinite souls can coexist without contradiction, because both are infinite.
Correction: The argument uses a naive notion of 'greater than' for infinities: if each cycle has many souls, then the number of souls is a multiple of the number of cycles, so if cycles are infinite, souls would be an infinite multiple of infinity, which is considered absurd.

Checkpoint
What does the seventh proof conclude about the number of past cycles?
A) It is infinite.
B) It is finite.
C) It is equal to the number of souls.
D) It is less than the number of souls.

Key Takeaway
The seventh proof shows that if past cycles were infinite, the number of souls today would be absurdly infinite multiples, implying cycles are finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If past cycles were infinite, your soul would be just one among an endless crowd. But the proof shows cycles are finite — you were created at a specific time by Allah. How does this uniqueness change the way you view your purpose and the time you have left?
?
The philosophers believed in eternal return; the proof shows that leads to absurdity. In your daily actions, do you ever act as if you have infinite tomorrows? What would it mean for you to truly live as if your time is finite and your soul is a one-time gift?
?
The argument turns on the idea that infinite cycles produce infinite souls, which is impossible. If you really believed that your existence is not a random result of endless repetitions but a deliberate, singular creation, what would you need to change in your life right now to reflect that conviction?

The Eighth Proof: Parity and Deficiency in Number
The author argues parity shows past cycles are finite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the parity (even/odd) of past cycles proves they are finite.

Core Idea
The number of past cycles is either even or odd, proving it is limited.

This lesson presents a concise number-theoretic argument. The number of past cycles must be either even or odd; in either case, it can be increased by one. This shows the cycles are not infinite, but finite. The passage is short but logically tight.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic once, then the translation. Notice how the argument moves from classification to conclusion.
2
Understand the parity classification
The argument states that the number of past cycles is either even (شفع) or odd (وتر). This covers all possibilities.
3
See the deficiency in each case
If even, it is one less than the odd above it. If odd, it is one less than the even above it. In both cases, the number can be increased by one.
4
Conclude finitude
Because the number can be increased, it is susceptible to increase and decrease. An infinite number cannot be increased, so past cycles must be finite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن : وهو أن عدد الأدوار الماضية. إما أن يكون شفعاً أو وتراً . فإن كان شفعاً فهو أنقص من الوتر ، الذي فوقه بواحد . وإن كان وتراً ، فهو أنقص من الشفع الذي فوقه بواحد . وعلى جميع الأحوال ، فالأمور الماضية قابلة للزيادة والنقصان
The eighth [argument]: It is that the number of past cycles is either even or odd. If it is even, it is less than the odd [number] above it by one. If it is odd, it is less than the even [number] above it by one. In all cases, the past affairs are susceptible to increase and decrease.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This eighth proof is a simple number-theoretic argument. The number of past cycles must be either even or odd. If it is even, then it is one less than the next odd number; if odd, it is one less than the next even number. In either case, the number can be increased by one, so it is not infinite. Thus, the past cycles are susceptible to increase and decrease, proving they are finite.

Key Terms
شفع
وتر
زيادة
نقصان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinite numbers can be classified as even or odd.
Correction: In standard transfinite arithmetic, infinite cardinal numbers are not considered even or odd; parity applies only to finite natural numbers.

Checkpoint
According to the eighth proof, what does the fact that the number of past cycles can be even or odd imply?
A) It is infinite.
B) It is finite.
C) It is zero.
D) It is unknown.

Key Takeaway
Because the number of past cycles is always either even or odd and can be increased by one, it must be finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the number of past cycles is finite, that means your every thought and deed is countable and bounded. How does this truth confront the way you spend your fleeting moments? Will you live today as if it is one more countable unit leading to an appointment, or as if you have infinite tomorrows?
?
The proof shows that what can be increased is limited. Your lifespan is also subject to increase and decrease. Knowing that every day you live increases your record, what will you add to your balance today that you would not be ashamed to present before your Creator?
?
You believe that Allah alone is infinite and that everything else is finite. But do your attachments reflect that? Are you clinging to something finite as if it were infinite, relying on created things rather than the Creator who alone has no end?

The Ninth Proof: Half of a Finite Number is Finite
A proof that the number of past cycles is finite using the concept of half.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that the number of past cycles must be finite because its half is finite.

Core Idea
If half of something is finite, then the whole must be finite.

This lesson examines the ninth proof, which uses a simple arithmetic principle. The author argues that since the number of past cycles has a half, and that half is finite, the whole number of cycles must also be finite. We'll follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Note the concise style.
The passage is very short and dense.
2
Understand the premise
The argument assumes that the series of past cycles has a number (count). That number has a half. Identify the half as a proper part.
3
Follow the logical chain
The half is finite because it is less than the whole. Then the double of that finite half is finite, so the whole number is finite.
The key step is that doubling a finite number yields a finite number.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسع : إن الأدوار الماضية لها عدد فنصف ذلك العدد : أقل من كله فنصفه متناه، فضعفه : ضعف للمتناهي. وضعف المتناهي : متناهي.
The ninth [argument]: The past cycles have a number. So half of that number is less than the whole, so its half is finite. So its double is a double of the finite. And the double of the finite is finite.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This ninth proof uses a simple arithmetic principle. The past cycles have a number (i.e., a count). That number has a half. The half is less than the whole, so it is finite (since an infinite cannot have a proper part that is less? Actually, the argument assumes that if a number has a finite half, then the whole is finite). Then the double of that finite half is also finite. Therefore, the original number (the whole) is finite. The reasoning is that if something is finite, its double is finite; since the number of cycles is the double of its half (which is finite), the number itself must be finite.

Key Terms
نصف
ضعف
متناه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that since half of an infinite number is also infinite, the argument is flawed.
Correction: The argument assumes a classical notion where a proper part is smaller and hence finite; this works within the intended framework, though modern mathematics treats infinite sets differently.

Checkpoint
According to the ninth proof, what can we conclude about the number of past cycles?
A) It is infinite.
B) It is finite.
C) It is half-infinite.
D) It is zero.

Key Takeaway
The number of past cycles must be finite because its half is finite and doubling a finite yields a finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The proof uses the simple logic that half of a finite is finite. Have you ever evaded logical conclusions about your own existence because they felt unsettling? Will you submit to the truth even when it demands a change in your worldview?

Since the cycles of time are finite, your life is a limited opportunity. What have you done today that acknowledges the finitude of your own existence and the urgency of preparing for what comes after?

The Tenth Proof: Comparing Celestial Cycles
The author compares cycles of different celestial spheres to show the past is finite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how comparing the total cycles of multiple celestial spheres with the cycles of individual spheres demonstrates that the past must be finite.

Core Idea
Infinite cycles cannot be compared as greater or lesser—so they must be finite.

The tenth proof uses a simple mathematical intuition: if each celestial sphere has its own set of cycles, and if those cycles were infinite, then the total number across all spheres would be greater than the cycles of any single sphere. But one infinite quantity cannot be greater than another. This contradiction forces us to conclude that the cycles—and thus past states—are finite.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage once silently. Then read the English translation. Notice the logical structure: premise, assumption, comparison, conclusion.
The author lists three spheres as examples: the Greatest Sphere, the Sphere of Fixed Stars, and Saturn.
2
Identify the key spheres
Find the names of the spheres mentioned: 'الفلك الأعظم' (Greatest Sphere), 'فلك الثوابت' (Fixed Stars), and 'زحل' (Saturn). Why did the author pick these?
These represent different sizes and speeds, but the argument works for any distinct spheres.
3
Understand the comparison
The argument says: if each sphere has infinite cycles, then the total cycles (sum) is greater than the cycles of the Greatest Sphere alone. Since 'greater than' cannot apply to infinities, this is impossible.
This relies on the principle that a proper subset cannot equal the whole when both are infinite in number.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The author concludes that the number of past states is 'susceptible to increase and decrease'—a property of finite quantities. Therefore, the past is finite.
This conclusion supports the broader argument for a beginning of creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشر : لا شك أنه حصل لكل واحد من الأفلاك : أدوار مخصوصة على حدة . فلو كانت أدوار الفلك الأعظم غير متناهية ، وكذلك أدوار فلك ه الثوابت ، غير متناهية ، وكذلك أدوار فلك : زحل » غير متناهية . وكذا القول في البواقي . فنقول : لا شك أن عدد مجموع هذه الأدوار : أكثر من عدد أدوار الفلك و الأعظم ، وحده . ومن عدد أدوار فلك : الثوابت ، وحده . وهذا يدل على أن عدد الأحوال الماضية يقبل الزيادة والنقصان
The tenth [argument]: There is no doubt that for each of the celestial spheres there are specific cycles individually. So if the cycles of the Greatest Sphere are infinite, and likewise the cycles of the Sphere of the Fixed Stars are infinite, and likewise the cycles of the Sphere of Saturn are infinite, and so on for the rest. Then we say: There is no doubt that the total number of these cycles is greater than the number of cycles of the Greatest Sphere alone, and greater than the number of cycles of the Sphere of the Fixed Stars alone. And this indicates that the number of past states is susceptible to increase and decrease.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This tenth proof uses the multiplicity of celestial spheres. Each sphere has its own cycles. If each sphere's cycles were infinite, then the total number of cycles across all spheres would be greater than any single sphere's cycles (because it includes at least another set of cycles). Since one infinite cannot be greater than another infinite, this shows that the cycles are not infinite. Again, the susceptibility to increase and decrease is taken as a sign of finitude.

Key Terms
أفلاك
أدوار
فلك الأعظم
فلك الثوابت
زحل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that infinite sets can be compared by 'greater than' in the same way as finite sets.
Correction: In set theory, an infinite set can be put into one-to-one correspondence with a proper subset, so 'greater than' does not apply straightforwardly. The argument relies on the intuitive rejection that an infinite total can exceed an infinite part.

Checkpoint
What does the tenth proof compare to show the cycles are finite?
A) Cycles of different planets.
B) Cycles of the same sphere.
C) Cycles of the Greatest Sphere only.
D) Cycles of the Fixed Stars only.

Key Takeaway
The tenth proof uses the multiplicity of celestial spheres to show that cycles cannot be infinite, because the total would exceed the part—an impossibility for infinities—thus establishing that the past is finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that the total cycles would be greater than the cycles of one sphere only if both are finite. In your own life, do you act as if your time is infinite, putting off repentance and good deeds? How does the logical certainty of finitude move you to act today?
?
If the greatest celestial spheres have a finite number of cycles, then even the most magnificent parts of creation are limited. What does that tell you about your own ambitions and legacy? Are you building something that will endure beyond the finite?
?
The contradiction in the proof forces us to accept that the past had a beginning. What does that beginning imply about the One who caused it? Are you living in conscious gratitude to your Creator, or are you distracted by the illusion of self-sufficiency?
