The Second Premise: Increase and Decrease Imply Finitude
The author states the second premise and its logical exposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second premise used in the kalām argument for the temporal origination of the world: that anything subject to increase or decrease must be finite in number.

Core Idea
If something can increase or decrease, it must have a limit.

This lesson presents the second premise in the cosmological argument for the world's beginning. Before proving that the past must be finite, the author establishes a key logical rule: anything that admits increase or decrease cannot be infinite. The passage explains why a lesser amount cannot be infinite, and why a greater amount built on it must also be finite. Though the text is cut off, the core principle is clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the premise statement
Read the Arabic sentence that states the premise: كل ما يقبل الزيادة والنقصان فله عدد متناه. Note how it is introduced as a 'second premise'.
2
Follow the reasoning for 'less than'
The author explains that for something to be less than another, it must have reached its end while the other still has something left. Anything that reaches its end is finite.
This uses the concept of 'end' in number.
3
Draw the conclusion for deficient numbers
The text concludes that every deficient number (i.e., a number that is less than another) is finite. Then it begins to argue that the greater number added to a finite number remains finite, but the text cuts off.
4
Reflect on the premise's role
Think about how this premise is used: to show that the past, which can be increased by adding future events, must be finite. This counters the idea of an infinite past.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ وأما المقدمة الثانية : وهي قولنا : أن كل ما يقبل الزيادة والنقصان(۱) ] فله عدد متناه . فتقريره : أن الشيء إنما يكون أنقص من غيره ، لو انتهى ذلك الشيء إلى حيث لا يبقى منه شيء. مع أنه بقي من ذلك الزائد شيء ، وكل ما انتهى عدده إلى حيث لا يبقى منه شيء ، كان متناهياً ، وينتج : أن كل . عدد ناقص فهو متناه . ثم إن الزائد إنما زاد على ذلك الناقص المتناهي : بعدد
And as for the second premise: it is our statement that everything that admits increase and decrease has a finite number. Its exposition: A thing is only less than another if that thing has reached the point where nothing remains of it, while something remains of the greater. And everything whose number has reached the point where nothing remains of it is finite. And it follows that every deficient number is finite. Then the greater only increased over that finite deficient by a number ...
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage introduces the second premise of the overall argument. The premise states: anything that can increase or decrease must be finite in number. The explanation: for something to be less than something else, it must have reached its end (i.e., its total is exhausted) while the other still has some remaining. Anything that has an end is finite. Therefore, any number that is deficient (i.e., less than another) is finite. The greater number then adds to that finite number, resulting in another finite number. The text is cut off, but the conclusion is clear: the past must be finite because it is subject to increase and decrease.

Key Terms

متناه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an infinite set can have a subset that is less, so increase/decrease does not prove finiteness.
Correction: The premise relies on the idea of reaching an end in number. An infinite number cannot be exhausted; thus anything that can be less (exhausted) must be finite. The author is not discussing set theory but the nature of numbers of past events.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The second premise establishes that anything capable of increase or decrease must be finite in number, a key step in proving the temporal origination of the world.

The argument shows that anything admitting increase and decrease cannot be infinite. Reflect: What do you seek to increase—wealth, status, pleasure? Objection: Celestial Motion Has No Parts or Number
Objection that celestial motion is continuous, not composed of parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand an objection against the premise that celestial motion has countable parts, and how the opponent argues that motion is continuous and not composed of indivisible parts.

Core Idea
Celestial motion is a single continuous whole, free from parts and number.

In this passage, the author presents an objection to the argument that celestial motion has numbers and parts. The objector argues that motion from start to end is one continuous reality, not divisible into moments or parts. He distinguishes worldly motion, which has beginning and end due to rest, from eternal celestial motion, which has no such rest and therefore no parts or number. This objection challenges the basis for counting cycles and deriving conclusions from them.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection's premise
Read the opening statement: the objector denies that celestial motion has numbers and parts. Note that this is the target of the objection.
The objection is responding to an earlier argument that relied on counting cycles.
2
Grasp the argument for continuity
The objector establishes a premise: motion from the beginning to the end of a distance is one motion in itself, not composed of contiguous moments or successive parts. This is because if it were composed of parts, the distance would be made of indivisible atoms, which is false.
The argument references proofs against the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard).
3
Recognize the distinction
Worldly motion has a first and last because it begins after rest and ends in rest. If there were no rest at the boundaries, the motion would have no first or last.
This distinction is crucial for the objection.
4
See the consequence for celestial motion
Celestial motion has no beginning or end (according to those who hold it is eternal). Therefore, it is one continuous motion without parts or number. All reasoning about increase/decrease of cycles based on numbers is invalid.
The passage ends with 'سلمنا' (we concede), indicating a counter-response that is cut off.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : مدار هذا الدليل على أن الحركة الفلكية لها أعداد وأجزاء وذلك ممنوع . وهذا السؤال لا يتم إلا بتقرير مقدمة : وهي أنا نقول : الجسم إذا تحرك من أول المسافة إلى آخرها ، فتلك الحركة: حركة واحدة في نفسها ، ولا يمكن أن يقال : إنها مركبة من أنات متلاصقة ، وأجزاء متعاقبة. إذ لو كان الأمر كذلك ، لكانت تلك المسافة مركبة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ ، وذلك باطل بالدلائل الدالة على نفي الجوهر الفرد . فيثبت : أن الحركة من أول المسافة إلى آخرها : حركة واحدة في الحقيقة ، وليست مركبة من أقسام وأجزاء. إذا عرفت هذا ، فنقول : إنما حصل الأول والآخر لهذه الحركات التي نشاهدها في عالمنا هذا : لأن ذلك المتحرك ابتدأ بالحركة بعد أن كان ساكناً ، وانتهى في آخر الأمر إلى السكون . ولو فرضنا عدم هذا السكون في أول هذه الحركة وآخرها ، لم يحصل لهذه الحركة أول ولا آخر [ بل كانت حركة واحدة في ذاتها ، مبرأة عن الكثرة والعدد . وإذا عرفت هذا ، فنقول : الحركة الفلكية ليس لها أول وآخر ] على مذهب القائلين بأنه لا أول لها ، فكانت في نفسها حركة واحدة متصلة أزلاً وأبداً . ولا يكون لها شيء من الأجزاء والأعداد . وجميع ما ذكرتم في كون تلك الدورات قابلة للزيادة والنقصان بحسب الأعداد، ومبني على ثبوت العدد ، فلما بطل ذلك سقط جميع ما ذكرتم . سلمنا : حصول الأعداد والدورات، لكنا نقول : المحكوم عليه بقبول
If it is said: This argument is based on the fact that celestial motion has numbers and parts, and that is denied. This question is not complete except by establishing a premise: that we say: If a body moves from the beginning of the distance to its end, that motion is one motion in itself, and it cannot be said that it is composed of contiguous moments and successive parts. For if that were the case, that distance would be composed of indivisible parts, and that is false based on proofs that negate the indivisible atom. Thus it is established that motion from the beginning of the distance to its end is one motion in reality, not composed of sections and parts. If you understand this, we say: The first and last occur for these motions that we observe in this world of ours because that moving body began motion after having been at rest, and ended at the end with rest. And if we suppose the absence of this rest at the beginning and end of this motion, there would be no first or last for this motion [rather it would be one motion in itself, free from multiplicity and number. And if you understand this, we say: Celestial motion has no first or last] according to the doctrine of those who say it has no beginning, so it is in itself one continuous motion eternally and everlastingly. And it has no parts or numbers. And all that you mentioned concerning those cycles being subject to increase and decrease according to numbers is based on the establishment of number, and since that is invalid, all that you mentioned falls. We concede the occurrence of numbers and cycles, but we say: that which is judged to accept...
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the previous arguments. The objector claims that the argument depends on celestial motion having countable parts, which he denies. He argues that motion from one point to another is one continuous whole, not composed of indivisible instants or parts. In our world, motion has a beginning and end due to preceding and following rest. But for celestial motion, which is eternal, there is no prior or subsequent rest, so it is one continuous motion without parts or number. Hence, the counting of cycles and the reasoning about increase and decrease are invalid. The response begins with 'سلمنا' (we concede), but the text cuts off, likely arguing that even if we grant numbers, the conclusion remains. The passage is incomplete, but it highlights the debate on the nature of motion and time.

Key Terms
الحركة الفلكية
أجزاء لا تتجزأ
الجوهر الفرد
سكون
عدد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All motion is composed of discrete indivisible instants.
Correction: According to this objection, motion from one point to another is one continuous whole, not composed of moments or indivisible parts, as that would require the path to be composed of indivisible atoms, which is false.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The objector argues that celestial motion is one continuous motion without parts or number because it lacks the rest that gives worldly motion its beginning and end.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

In your daily life, you experience 'rests'—pauses between tasks, distractions, even death. ?

The Eighth Refutation: No First Moment for Possibility of Events
The eighth refutation argues that the validity of contingent events cannot have a beginning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the possibility of contingent events cannot have a first moment, and the two arguments that prove this impossibility.

Core Idea
Possibility is eternal; it cannot begin.

This lesson examines the eighth refutation from our text, which argues that the possibility of contingent events cannot have a first moment. The argument uses two reasons: one logical and one temporal. By engaging with this passage, you will see why Muslim theologians maintain that the possibility of events is co-eternal with God's attribute of power.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Note how the argument is structured: a main claim followed by two reasons.
Pay attention to the terms 'ممتنع لعينه' and 'ممكن لعينه'.
2
Identify the main claim and the two reasons
The passage first states that the validity of events from eternity to the Flood is less than to today, so it would have a first moment. Identify the two reasons given why this is impossible.
3
Understand the first reason
The first reason argues that if there is a first moment, before it events would be impossible in themselves, then become possible—an absurd change. Reflect on why this is impossible.
4
Understand the second reason
The second reason shows that any assumed first moment already had possibility before it, because if an event occurred just before that first, it wouldn't become eternal. So the assumed first is not truly a beginning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقض الثامن : إن صحة حدوث الحوادث من الأزل إلى وقت الطوفان ، أقل من امتداده من الأزل إلى هذا اليوم. فيلزم : إثبات أول لهذه الصحة . وذلك محال لوجهين :
الأول : إنه يلزم أن يقال : إنه كان قبل ذلك المبدأ ممتنعاً لعينه ، ثم
انقلب بعده ممكناً [ لعينه (‫٢‬) ] وإنه محال .
والثاني : وهو أن كل وقت يفرض كونه أولاً ، لإمكان حدوث الحوادث . فإن ذلك الإمكان كان حاصلا قبل ذلك الأول ، فإنه لو حصل ذلك الحادث قبل ذلك لأول بيوم أو يومين، فإنه بسبب هذا القدر لا يصير أزلياً فيثبت بهذا : أن فرض أول وبداية لصحة حدوث الحوادث : محال .
The eighth refutation: The validity of the occurrence of contingent events from eternity to the time of the Flood is less than its extension from eternity to this day. So it necessitates affirming a first for this validity. But that is impossible for two reasons:
First: It would necessitate saying that before that beginning it was impossible in itself, then afterwards it became possible in itself, which is impossible.
Second: Every time that is assumed to be a first for the possibility of the occurrence of contingent events—that possibility existed before that first; for if that event occurred before that first by a day or two, it does not become eternal due to this amount. So it is established that assuming a first and beginning for the validity of the occurrence of contingent events is impossible.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth refutation. It argues that the temporal extent of the possibility of contingent events (صحة حدوث الحوادث) from eternity to the Flood is shorter than from eternity to today. If so, then this validity would have a beginning (أول), but that is impossible for two reasons. The first reason: if there is a first moment when possibility begins, then before that moment, the events were impossible in themselves, and then later became possible in themselves, which is an absurd change in the nature of possibility. The second reason: no matter what time we posit as the first moment for possibility, that possibility must have existed before that time; otherwise, if an event had occurred just before that first moment, it would not become eternal, so the assumed first is not truly a beginning. Therefore, the very notion of a first moment for the possibility of contingent events is impossible.

أول
ممتنع لعينه
ممكن لعينه
أزلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility can begin in time.
Correction: Possibility is eternal; it cannot have a first moment because that would imply an absurd change or a contradictory 'before'.

Checkpoint
According to the eighth refutation, why is it impossible for the validity of contingent events to have a first moment?
A) Because events are eternal
B) Because before that first moment, possibility would have to be impossible, which is absurd
C) Because the Flood marks the beginning
D) Because possibility is not real

Key Takeaway
The possibility of contingent events cannot have a beginning because that would imply an absurd change in the nature of possibility and because any assumed first would not be truly first.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The argument shows that a first moment for possibility is impossible because it would require a change in the essence of events. In your life, do you ever assume that you can 'start' something new by your own power? How does this lesson challenge you to see every beginning as arising from Allah's eternal will, not your own initiative?
?
You have just read that any assumed first for possibility is not truly first because possibility existed before it. This means that your very capacity to choose or act has no temporal starting point—it is always from Allah. Does this realization make you more accountable for your choices? How will you respond to the fact that your possibilities are not self-made but eternally granted?

Asymmetry Between Eternity Past and Future
The ninth refutation compares possibilities from past and future eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why comparing the possibility of events from eternity past and eternity future reveals a contradiction in the opponent's view.

Core Idea
Comparing past and future infinities reveals an impossible asymmetry.

The ninth refutation targets the opponent's assumption that past and future infinities are symmetric. Imam al-Razi argues that the possibility of events from the Flood to the end of future eternity is greater than from today onwards. This difference forces a choice between two absurd outcomes: either the greater equals the lesser, or the future has an end. Both are impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully.
Read the Arabic text once, focusing on the key terms: الأزل, الأبد, إمكان, التفاوت. Notice the two conditional scenarios introduced by 'فإن'.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the comparison being made.
The passage compares two time spans: from the Flood (a past point) to future eternity, and from today to future eternity. The first is longer.
This 'length' difference is key.
3
Analyze the first case: no difference.
If we align the ends from the past side and both extensions are infinite without difference, the greater would equal the lesser—an impossibility.
This shows infinite past leads to contradiction.
4
Analyze the second case: difference appears.
If a difference appears, it implies the future side has an end, which is agreed to be false.
Thus the only consistent view is that the past itself must have a beginning.
5
Reflect on the conclusion.
The refutation shows that the opponent's claim of an eternal past is untenable because it leads to absurdity in either scenario.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقض التاسع : إنه يعارض جانب الأزل بجانب الأبد . فنقول : لا شك أن إمكان حدوث الحوادث من يوم الطوفان ، إلى آخر الأبد [ أكثر من صحتها من هذا اليوم إلى آخر الأبد (‫٣‬) ] فإن أطبقنا طرف هذين الامتدادين من هذا الجانب ، فإن امتدا ، أبداً من الجانب الآخر ، من غير أن يظهر التفاوت ، بلزم : أن يكون الزائد مساوياً للناقص . وهو محال . وإن ظهر التفاوت من ذلك الجانب لزم انتهاء صحة حدوث الحوادث من جانب الأبد، إلى آخر ونهاية . وذلك باطل بالاتفاق .
The ninth refutation: The side of eternity past is compared to the side of eternity future. So we say: There is no doubt that the possibility of the occurrence of contingent events from the Day of the Flood to the end of eternity is greater than its validity from this day to the end of eternity. For if we match the ends of these two extensions from this side, then they extend infinitely from the other side without difference appearing, then it would necessitate that the greater be equal to the lesser, which is impossible. And if difference appears from that side, then it would necessitate that the validity of the occurrence of contingent events from the side of eternity ends at a final point, which is agreed to be false.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth refutation, which compares the possibility of contingent events from the perspective of eternity past (الأزل) and eternity future (الأبد). The argument: the possibility from the Flood to the end of future eternity is greater than that from today to the end of future eternity. If we try to align the endpoints from the past side (perhaps assuming both extend infinitely), then if both sides are infinite without any difference, the greater would equal the lesser, which is impossible. If we instead posit a difference (i.e., the past side ends at a beginning), then the future side would also have to end, which is false by consensus. This refutes the opponent's attempt to posit a beginning for possibility.

Key Terms
الأزل
الأبد
إمكان حدوث الحوادث
التفاوت
باطل بالاتفاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Past and future infinities are symmetric and can be compared without contradiction.
Correction: This refutation shows that comparing them leads to either greater = lesser or a finite future, both impossible.

Checkpoint
What does the ninth refutation conclude from comparing the possibility from the Flood to future eternity versus from today to future eternity?
A) Both possibilities are equal
B) The longer possibility proves that the past must have a beginning
C) If no difference appears, the greater equals the lesser, which is impossible
D) The future is longer than the past

Key Takeaway
Comparing past and future possibility spans reveals an asymmetry that forces the past to have a beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the past is not infinite, then your life, too, has a definite beginning. Does your daily routine reflect the weight of that starting point—or do you drift as if you've always been here?

The refutation shows that infinite past leads to absurdities. How often do you act as if you have an infinite future, postponing repentance and good deeds? What will you do today to acknowledge the reality that your time has an end?
?
This lesson forces you to see that some of your assumptions about time may be false. What deeply held belief about your own timeline—past or future—needs to be reexamined in light of this truth? Will you examine it now?

The Eighth Refutation: No First Beginning for Possibility
This passage presents the eighth refutation with two reasons against a first beginning for the possibility of events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the possibility of events occurring from pre-eternity cannot logically have a first beginning, based on two compelling arguments.

Core Idea
Possibility itself cannot have a first moment—it always already was.

This refutation tackles a key objection: if events can occur from pre-eternity, mustn't their very possibility have a starting point? The author argues that this leads to absurd consequences. By examining two reasons, you'll see that 'possibility' is a timeless logical state, not a temporal one. This passage sharpens your understanding of how kalam deals with eternity and change.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Get a feel for the argument's flow before diving into details.
Notice the structure: a claim followed by two reasons.
2
Identify the main thesis
The initial statement sets up a comparison of durations to show that a first beginning for the validity of events is required—then states that this is impossible. Understand the opponent's implied position.
The key term is 'ṣiḥḥat ḥudūth' (validity of occurrence).
3
Analyze the first reason
The first reason: if there were a first beginning for possibility, then before that beginning possibility would be impossible in itself, and after it would become possible—an absurd change in intrinsic nature.
This targets the idea that possibility could change from impossible to possible by itself.
4
Analyze the second reason
The second reason: any supposed first moment for possibility already had that possibility existing before it; otherwise, an event occurring slightly earlier would not become eternal. Thus no moment can be the first.
This shows the infinite regress problem.
5
Synthesize the conclusion
Combine the two reasons to see why the very concept of a first beginning for possibility is logically impossible. Reflect on how this refutes the opponent's position.
The conclusion strengthens the kalām argument for a temporal origin of the universe.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقض الثامن : إن صحة حدوث الحوادث من الأزل إلى وقت الطوفان ، أقل من امتداده من الأزل إلى هذا اليوم. فيلزم : إثبات أول لهذه الصحة . وذلك محال لوجهين :
الأول : إنه يلزم أن يقال : إنه كان قبل ذلك المبدأ ممتنعاً لعينه ، ثم انقلب بعده ممكناً لعينه وإنه محال .
الثاني : وهو أن كل وقت يفرض كونه أولاً ، لإمكان حدوث الحوادث . فإن ذلك الإمكان كان حاصلا قبل ذلك الأول ، فإنه لو حصل ذلك الحادث قبل ذلك لأول بيوم أو يومين، فإنه بسبب هذا القدر لا يصير أزلياً فيثبت بهذا : أن فرض أول وبداية لصحة حدوث الحوادث : محال .
The eighth refutation: The validity of the occurrence of events from pre-eternity to the time of the flood is less than its extension from pre-eternity to this day. So it follows that a first beginning must be established for this validity. And that is impossible for two reasons: First, it would necessitate that before that beginning it was impossible in itself, then after it became possible in itself, and that is impossible. Second, every time that is supposed to be a first for the possibility of the occurrence of events: that possibility existed before that first, because if that event had occurred before that first by a day or two, it would not become eternal due to this amount. Thus it is established that supposing a first and beginning for the validity of the occurrence of events is impossible.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth refutation. It begins by stating that the soundness (or validity) of events occurring from pre-eternity to the flood is less than that from pre-eternity to today. Therefore, if the opponent's reasoning requires a first beginning for past states, it would also require a first beginning for this 'soundness' of possibility. The author then gives two reasons why that is impossible. The first reason: if there were a first beginning for the possibility, then before that moment the possibility would have been impossible in itself, and then after it became possible in itself—that would be an absurd change in the intrinsic nature of possibility. The second reason: whenever we suppose a certain moment as the first for the possibility of events, that possibility must have already existed before that moment, because if an event had occurred a day or two before that supposed first moment, it would not become eternal merely due to that small difference. Hence, the very concept of a first beginning for the possibility of occurrence is impossible.

Key Terms
صحة حدوث (ṣiḥḥat ḥudūth)
أزل (azal)
امتناع (imtinā‘)
إمكان (imkān)
أول (awwal)
محال (muḥāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The refutation is about physical time and the start of the universe.
Correction: It is not about physical time but about the logical status of possibility itself—whether the very possibility of events can have a beginning.

Checkpoint
What is the second reason given in the eighth refutation against a first beginning for the possibility of events?
A) It would make the possibility eternal.
B) The possibility already existed before any supposed first moment.
C) The event would become necessary after the first moment.
D) The first moment cannot be defined.

Key Takeaway
Possibility itself is a timeless logical state; therefore, it cannot have a first beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If possibility has no first beginning, then your own ability to change or grow spiritually was never limited by a 'starting point'. What does this mean for the way you view your past failures?
?
The passage argues that before any supposed first moment of possibility, that possibility already existed. Have you ever assumed something is impossible for you (like repentance or improvement) because it hasn't happened yet? How does this lesson challenge that assumption?
?
If 'impossible in itself' cannot become 'possible in itself' without absurdity, then what does that say about your own excuses of 'I am not capable'? Are those claims about your essence or just about your current state?

The Ninth Refutation: Comparing Pre-Eternity with Post-Eternity
Sanusi presents the ninth refutation comparing pre-eternity and post-eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the ninth refutation argues against the opponent by comparing the pre-eternal and post-eternal aspects of possibility, showing that the opponent's view leads to a contradiction.

Core Idea
Comparing pre-eternity and post-eternity reveals a contradiction in the opponent's position.

Sanusi now presents the ninth refutation, which employs a clever comparison between the pre-eternal (azal) and post-eternal (abad) aspects of possibility. By examining the spans of possible events, he shows that the opponent's position leads to an absurdity. This passage demonstrates the power of logical argumentation in theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two extensions
Identify that the passage compares two spans: from the flood to eternity, and from today to eternity. Notice that the first is longer because it includes a longer past.
The flood is used as a fixed point in time.
2
Follow the conditional reasoning
The passage presents two possibilities: either the extensions are both infinite from the other side (leading to equality of greater and lesser), or a difference appears (leading to an end of possibility).
Both outcomes are problematic for the opponent.
3
Grasp the contradiction
If no difference appears, then the greater equals the lesser, which is impossible. If a difference appears, then the possibility of future events ends, which is agreed to be false. Thus the opponent's position is refuted.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقض التاسع : إنه يعارض جانب الأزل بجانب الأبد . فنقول : لا شك أن إمكان حدوث الحوادث من يوم الطوفان ، إلى آخر الأبد أكثر من صحتها من هذا اليوم إلى آخر الأبد . فإن أطبقنا طرف هذين الامتدادين من هذا الجانب ، فإن امتدا ، أبداً من الجانب الآخر ، من غير أن يظهر التفاوت ، بلزم : أن يكون الزائد مساوياً للناقص . وهو محال . وإن ظهر التفاوت من ذلك الجانب لزم انتهاء صحة حدوث الحوادث من جانب الأبد، إلى آخر ونهاية . وذلك باطل بالاتفاق .
The ninth refutation: He compares the pre-eternal aspect with the post-eternal aspect. We say: There is no doubt that the possibility of occurrence of events from the day of the flood to the end of eternity is more than its validity from this day to the end of eternity. If we join the ends of these two extensions from this side, then they extend infinitely from the other side without any difference appearing, then it would follow that the greater is equal to the lesser, which is impossible. And if difference appears from that side, then it would necessitate that the validity of occurrence of events from the post-eternal side has an end and a termination, and that is false by agreement.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage is the ninth refutation. It sets up a comparison between the pre-eternal (al-azal) and post-eternal (al-abad) aspects of the possibility of events. The author points out that the possibility of events from the day of the flood to the end of eternity is greater than that from today to the end of eternity. If we consider the two spans from the same starting point (today) and they both go on infinitely without any difference, then the greater (the flood-to-abad span) would equal the lesser (today-to-abad span), which is absurd. If instead a difference appears—meaning the post-eternal side has an end—then that would contradict the agreed-upon fact that the possibility of events in the future has no end. Hence, the opponent's position leads to a contradiction.

Key Terms
أزل (al-azal)
أبد (al-abad)
امتداد (imtidād)
زائد (zā’id)
ناقص (nāqiṣ)
محال (muḥāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage is about actual events occurring in time.
Correction: It is about the possibility of events, not actual occurrence.

Checkpoint
According to the ninth refutation, what would happen if the two extensions (from flood to eternity and from today to eternity) are considered from the same starting point and both extend infinitely?
A) The greater would equal the lesser, which is impossible.
B) The possibility would have an end.
C) The flood would be eternal.
D) The pre-eternal side would dominate.

Key Takeaway
The ninth refutation shows that comparing the pre-eternal and post-eternal spans of possibility leads to a contradiction, thereby undermining the opponent's objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the possibility of events in your own life, do you tend to think of them as limited or unlimited? How does this lesson's logical argument challenge you to think about the eternal nature of God's power?
?
The argument shows that contradictions arise when we compare two infinite spans. In your own beliefs, are there any contradictions you are comfortable with? How does this lesson call you to be intellectually consistent?
?
If the possibility of events in the future has no end, then every moment of your life is part of an infinite chain. How does that affect your sense of urgency in seeking Allah's pleasure?

The Imkān of Imkān: Infinite Regress in Possibility
First sub-argument of the tenth refutation on infinite regress of possibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how treating possibility as an attribute of possible things leads to an infinite regress of possibilities.

Core Idea
Possibility as attribute forces an endless chain of possibilities.

This passage presents a sharp critique of the view that possibility is an attribute inhering in possible things. By showing that this attribute itself is contingent, it forces an infinite regress of possibilities, revealing a logical flaw in the opponent's framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage states that possibility is an established attribute (ṣifa) of the possible thing (mumkin). Note this premise.
3
Trace the reasoning
The attribute is not necessary in itself, so it too must be possible. This implies a second-order possibility (imkān al-imkān).
Ask yourself: why does the attribute's contingency necessitate further possibility?
4
Understand the conclusion
The argument concludes that infinite regress (tasalsul) follows from the opponent's position. Recognize why this is considered absurd in kalam.
Infinite regress is rejected because it implies no ultimate grounding.
5
Reflect on the refutation's purpose
This is the first sub-argument of the tenth refutation, aiming to show that the opponent's view leads to an unacceptable consequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقض العاشر : فالأول : إن الإمكان صفة ثابتة للمكن ، وتلك الصفة الثابتة ليست واجبة الوجود لذاتها ، فهي ممكنة فيكون إمكان الإمكان زائداً عليه ، ولزم التسلسل
The tenth refutation: The first: Indeed, possibility (imkān) is an established attribute of the possible thing (al-mumkin), and that established attribute is not necessary in existence by itself, so it is possible. Thus the possibility of possibility (imkān al-imkān) is additional to it, and infinite regress (tasalsul) follows.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage is the first of several sub-arguments within the tenth refutation, aiming to show that the opponent's position leads to unacceptable infinite regress (tasalsul). Here, the argument focuses on the concept of possibility (imkān). The author states that possibility is an attribute (ṣifa) that inheres in a possible thing (al-mumkin). This attribute itself is not necessary in essence—it is contingent. Therefore, it too must have a possibility, which is the possibility of its own possibility (imkān al-imkān). That second-order possibility is additional to the first, and by the same reasoning, it would require yet another possibility, and so on ad infinitum. Thus, the opponent's view leads to an infinite chain of possibilities, which is absurd.

Key Terms
إمكان (imkān)
مكن (mumkin)
صفة (ṣifa)
وجود (wujūd)
واجب بالذات (wājib bi-dhāt)
ممكن بالذات (mumkin bi-dhāt)
تسلسل (tasalsul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility is an abstract concept that does not require further possibility because it is not a real attribute.
Correction: The argument treats possibility as a real attribute (ṣifa) inherent in the possible thing, which must itself be contingent, thus requiring its own possibility.

Checkpoint
In the first sub-argument of the tenth refutation, why does the attribute of possibility itself require a further possibility?
A) Because possibility is an accident that inheres in a substance.
B) Because possibility is not necessary in itself, so it must be possible.
C) Because possibility cannot exist without a subject.
D) Because possibility is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The first sub-argument of the tenth refutation demonstrates that treating possibility as an inherent attribute leads to an infinite regress of possibilities, exposing a fatal flaw in the opponent's view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that possibility as an attribute leads to an infinite regress, proving that only the Necessary Being can ground existence. Look at your own life: what are you relying on as the foundation for your existence—your health, wealth, or relationships? Are these not merely possible things? How does this truth expose the fragility of what you depend on, and will you today transfer your trust to the only Necessary Being?
?
The argument shows that if possibility were an attribute, it would require an endless chain. But you know that you are a possible being (mumkin). If you consider your own existence, what is your ultimate source of necessity? Have you ever truly internalized that without the Necessary Being, you would be nothing? Reflect: In the next hour, will you act as if your existence is self-grounded, or will you consciously acknowledge your contingency?

The Fourth Sub-Argument on Causal Agency and Infinite Regress
The fourth argument in the tenth refutation on causal agency and infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth sub-argument of the tenth refutation, which shows that the conceptual distinction between an agent's influence and its essence leads to an infinite regress of influences.

Core Idea
Influence is an attribute distinct from the agent, so it needs another influence — ad infinitum.

This passage presents the fourth sub-argument in a series of refutations against a certain conception of causal agency. The argument challenges the idea that an agent's influence is a distinct attribute by showing that it leads to an infinite regress. We will unpack the logical steps and see why this is problematic for the opponent.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the key terms: mu’aththir (influential), athar (effect), and mukhālafa (distinctness).
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the premise
The argument begins with the premise that the concept of being influential is distinct from both the agent's essence and the effect's essence. This is supported by the evidence that we can conceive of the agent and effect without considering the influence relation.
This distinction is key to the argument.
3
Follow the reasoning
From this distinction, the argument deduces that 'being influential' is an attribute of the agent. Attributes are dependent on their subject, so this attribute is contingent (possible in itself). The contingent requires an agent to bring it about.
The attribute 'being influential' now needs its own influence.
4
Grasp the regress
Thus, the influence of the agent (mu’aththiriyya) itself requires another influence (mu’aththiriyyat al-mu’aththiriyya) to obtain, and this leads to an infinite regress (tasalsul) of influences.
This is the conclusion that refutes the opponent's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقض العاشر : الرابع: إن المفهوم من كون الشيء مؤثراً في الأثر، مغايراً لذات المؤثر، ولذات الأثر. بدليل : أنه يصح تعقل ذاتيهما مع الذهول عن كون هذا مؤثراً في ذلك ، وكون ذلك أثراً لهذا . والمعلوم : مغاير لغير المعلوم ، فكون هذا مؤثراً في ذاك ، مغاير لذات هذا ، ولذات ذاك . ثم ذلك التغاير صفة لذات ذلك المؤثر ، وصفة الشيء مفتقر إلى ذلك الموصوف ، والمفتقر إلى الغير ممكن لذاته، والممكن لذاته لا بد له من مؤثر ، فمؤثرية المؤثرية في حصول تلك المؤثرية : زائدة عليها ، فيلزم التسلسل
The tenth refutation: The fourth: Indeed, the concept of something being influential (mu’aththir) in the effect is different from the essence of the agent and from the essence of the effect. Evidence: it is valid to conceive of both essences while being heedless of the fact that this is influential in that and that that is an effect of this. And what is known is different from what is not known. So the fact that this is influential in that is different from the essence of this and from the essence of that. Then that difference (al-taghāyur) is an attribute of the essence of that agent, and an attribute of a thing is dependent on that described thing, and what is dependent on another is possible in itself, and what is possible in itself must have an agent. So the agent-causality of the agent-causality (mu’aththiriyyat al-mu’aththiriyya) in obtaining that agent-causality is additional to it, and infinite regress follows.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This is the fourth sub-argument of the tenth refutation, focusing on causal agency (ta’thīr). The argument proceeds as follows: The fact that an agent (mu’aththir) has an influence on an effect is conceptually distinct from both the agent's essence and the effect's essence. This is proven by the fact that we can conceive of the agent and the effect without thinking about the influence relation. Therefore, the relation of influence is an additional attribute. This attribute is itself dependent on the agent, and anything dependent on another is contingent (mumkin bi-dhāt). Contingent things require an agent to bring them about. Hence, the influence itself requires a further influence to bring it about, leading to an infinite regress of influences (mu’aththiriyyat al-mu’aththiriyya). This shows that the opponent's way of analyzing causal relations leads to an unacceptable chain.

Key Terms
مؤثر (mu’aththir)
أثر (athar)
مغايرة (mukhālafa)
صفة (ṣifa)
مفتقر (muftaqir)
ممكن بالذات (mumkin bi-dhāt)
مؤثرية المؤثرية (mu’aththiriyyat al-mu’aththiriyya)
تسلسل (tasalsul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies that God acts as an agent or that causes produce effects.
Correction: The argument is not denying causal efficacy per se, but exposing a flawed metaphysical analysis that treats the influence-relation as a distinct attribute, leading to a regress. The refutation is directed at a specific philosophical position, not at the reality of causation.

Checkpoint

B) Because it is an attribute dependent on the agent and thus contingent, requiring an agent.
C) Because the agent is not truly influential.
D) Because the effect is not distinct from the agent.

Key Takeaway
The fourth sub-argument demonstrates that treating the influence of an agent as a distinct attribute leads to an infinite regress of influences, undermining the opponent's causal analysis.

The Fourth Regress: Efficacy Requires an External Agent
Fourth regress on the attribute of being an effective cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that the attribute of being an agent (efficacy) is distinct from the essence, and why this leads to an infinite regress of agents.

Core Idea
Efficacy is an additional attribute distinct from both agent and effect.

This lesson explores a subtle argument about causality. If an agent’s efficacy were intrinsic to its essence, it would not need an external cause. But the fourth regress shows that efficacy is an additional attribute, which itself requires an agent—leading to an infinite chain. We’ll break down the proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument. Notice the logical flow from claim to proof to regress.
2
Understand the core claim
Identify the claim: efficacy is different from the agent's essence and the effect's essence. This is the starting point.
3
Follow the proof via conceivability
The proof uses the conceivability argument: we can think of agent and effect without thinking of their causal relation, so the relation must be additional.
This is a key epistemological argument.
4
Trace the regress argument
If efficacy is an attribute, it depends on the agent, making it contingent. Contingent things need a cause. That cause's efficacy also depends, leading to infinite regress.
The regress is a reductio ad absurdum against the opponent's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: إن المفهوم من كون الشيء مؤثراً في الأثر، مغايراً لذات المؤثر، ولذات الأثر. بدليل : أنه يصح تعقل ذاتيهما مع الذهول عن كون هذا مؤثراً في ذلك ، وكون ذلك أثراً لهذا . والمعلوم : مغاير لغير المعلوم ، فكون هذا مؤثراً في ذاك ، مغاير لذات هذا ، ولذات ذاك . ثم ذلك التغاير صفة لذات ذلك المؤثر ، وصفة الشيء مفتقر إلى ذلك الموصوف ، والمفتقر إلى الغير ممكن لذاته، والممكن لذاته لا بد له من مؤثر ، فمؤثرية المؤثرية في حصول تلك المؤثرية : زائدة عليها ، فيلزم التسلسل
The fourth: that the concept of a thing's being effective (mu’aththir) in the effect is different from the essence of the agent and the essence of the effect. The proof: it is possible to conceive the two essences while being heedless of the fact that this is effective in that, and that that is an effect of this. And the known is different from the unknown. So this thing's being effective in that is different from the essence of this and the essence of that. Then that difference (al-taghāyur) is an attribute of the essence of that agent, and an attribute of a thing depends on that qualified thing. That which depends on another is possible in itself, and that which is possible in itself must have an agent. So the efficacy of efficacy (mu’aththiriyyat al-mu’aththiriyya) in obtaining that efficacy is additional to it, so infinite regress follows.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth regress, focusing on the relationship of efficacy between agent and effect. The argument: the attribute 'being an agent' is conceptually distinct from both the agent's essence and the effect's essence. This is proven by the fact that we can conceive of both essences without thinking about their causal relation. Since the attribute of efficacy is additional, it becomes an attribute of the agent. Any attribute depends on the qualified thing, and that dependency makes it possible in itself, thus it needs another agent to bring it about. That would require a further efficacy, leading to an infinite regress of efficacies.

Key Takeaway
The attribute of efficacy is distinct from the agent's essence, and if it were additional it would require another agent, leading to infinite regress.

Are Relations Merely Mental? An Objection Refuted
An objection that relations are mental constructs, and the author's response using causal effectiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that all relations are mental constructs and the author's refutation by showing that causal effectiveness must be real, which preserves the infinite regress argument.

Core Idea
If causal effectiveness were merely mental, causation itself would be unreal—so at least some relations are real.

The author anticipates an objection that might undermine his argument: perhaps all the relations he has been discussing (like attributes and causal connections) are nothing more than mental constructs with no external reality. If that were true, the problem of infinite regress would vanish. In this passage, he offers a sharp refutation, focusing on the concrete case of causality to show that such a dismissal is untenable.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the opening lines where the hypothetical objector claims relations (الإضافات) have no existence in external entities, only in the mind. Notice the conclusion: the problem of regress disappears.
The objector is trying to undercut the entire regress argument by denying the reality of relations.
2
Understand the author's response
The author dismisses this as extremely weak. Then he zeroes in on a critical question: is the cause's effectiveness (المؤثرية) in producing its effect real or merely mental?
3
Analyze the dilemma
The author presents two possibilities: either the effectiveness is real (then regress follows) or it is mental (then causality itself becomes unreal, which is absurd). Work through each horn to see why the objection fails.
This shows that at least one relation—causal efficacy—must be real.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : جملة هذه الصور التي ذكرتموها ، أمور إضافية نسبية والإضافات والنسب لا وجود لها في الأعيان. وإنما هي أمور يعتبرها العقل ، ويفرضها الذهن فقط . وعلى هذا التقدير فالسؤال زائل . لأنا نقول : هذا الكلام في غاية الضعف ، وذلك لأنا نقول : ذات المؤثر ، هل هي مؤثرة في الأثر في نفس الأمر ، أو ليس كذلك ، بل هذه المؤثرية غير حاصلة في نفس الأمر ، وإنما يفرضها العقل ، ويعتبرها الذهن فقط ؟ فإن كان الأول فهذه المؤثرية حاصلة في نفس الأمر ، وحينئذ يلزم التسلسل على ما قررناه
It should not be said: All these forms that you have mentioned are relational matters, and relations have no existence in external entities. They are only things that the mind considers and the intellect assumes. On this estimation, the question is removed. For we say: This speech is extremely weak. That is because we say: The essence of the effect (al-muʼaththir), is it effective in the effect (al-athar) in actual fact, or not? Rather, is this effectiveness not attained in actual fact, and the mind only assumes it and the intellect only considers it? If the first, then this effectiveness is attained in actual fact, and then an infinite regress follows as we have established.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and the author's response. The objection claims that all the examples given (like attributes, relations) are merely mental considerations (أمور اعتبارية) and have no external reality. If so, then the problem of infinite regress disappears because regress only arises if these are real. The author responds forcefully that this position is extremely weak. He drives the point home by asking about causality: Is the cause's effectiveness in producing the effect real or merely mental? If it is real (as everyone must admit), then that effectiveness is a relation that is real, and the same regress argument applies. If the effectiveness is merely mental, then causation itself becomes unreal, which is absurd. Therefore, one cannot dismiss relations as purely mental; at least some relations (like causal efficacy) are real, and they lead to regress. Thus, the objection fails.

Can Relations Be Merely Mental? The Rebuttal
Objection and rebuttal about relational matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the objective reality of causal relations against the objection that they are merely mental constructs, and how he forces a choice between infinite regress and sophistry.

Core Idea
Relations cannot be dismissed as merely mental without leading to absurdity.

The objection tries to dissolve the infinite regress problem by claiming that all causal relations are merely mental constructs. The author responds with a sharp dilemma: either the agent's efficacy is real or it is not. If real, infinite regress stands; if not, all distinctions vanish into sophistry. This passage shows why relations cannot be reduced to mental fictions.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the objector's claim: that all examples of causal efficacy are merely relational and have no external existence. They are only mental considerations.
Notice the objector's goal: to avoid the infinite regress by denying external reality to relations.
2
Identify the author's dilemma
The author poses a dilemma about the affectingness (mu'aththiriyya) of the agent: is it real in external reality or merely mental?
This is the core of the rebuttal.
3
Follow the first horn
If the affectingness is real, then infinite regress follows as previously established. The objection fails.
4
Follow the second horn
If the affectingness is merely mental, then in external reality the agent is not an agent, attributes vanish, and all distinctions collapse into sophistry.
This is unacceptable.
5
Consider the conclusion
The only consistent paths are either accepting infinite regress or pure sophistry. The author forces the objector to choose.
This rebuttal defends the objective reality of relations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : جملة هذه الصور التي ذكرتموها ، أمور إضافية نسبية والإضافات والنسب لا وجود لها في الأعيان. وإنما هي أمور يعتبرها العقل ، ويفرضها الذهن فقط . وعلى هذا التقدير فالسؤال زائل . لانا نقول : هذا الكلام في غاية الضعف ، وذلك لأنا نقول : ذات المؤثر ، هل هي مؤثرة في الأثر في نفس الأمر ، أو ليس كذلك ، بل هذه المؤثرية غير حاصلة في نفس الأمر ، وإنما يفرضها العقل ، ويعتبرها الذهن فقط ؟ فإن كان الأول فهذه المؤثرية حاصلة في نفس الأمر ، وحينئذ يلزم التسلسل على ما قررناه . وإن كان الثاني ، كان حاصل هذا الكلام : أن في نفس الأمر ، ليس المؤثر مؤثراً في الأثر ، وليس الذات موصوفة بالصفة ، وليس مثلا للسواد ، ولا ضداً للبياض ، ولا مخالفاً للحركة ، وذلك دخول في السفسطة وفي الجهالة فعلمنا : أن الحق إما التزام محض السفسطة ، وإما التزام التسلسلات ، والاعتراف بدخول ما لا نهاية له في الوجود ، وذلك هو المطلوب فهذا هو تقرير وجه السؤال على الدليل المذكور .
It should not be said: All these forms that you have mentioned are relational and relative matters, and relations and relative things have no existence in external entities; rather they are things that the intellect considers and the mind hypothesizes only. On this estimation, the question is invalid. Because we say: This speech is extremely weak, because we say: The essence of the agent, is it affecting the effect in reality, or not? Rather, this affectingness is not obtained in reality, but rather the intellect hypothesizes it and the mind considers it only? If it is the first, then this affectingness is obtained in reality, and then infinite regress follows as we have established. And if it is the second, then the result of this speech is that in reality, the agent is not affecting the effect, and the essence is not described by the attribute, and it is not similar to blackness, nor opposite to whiteness, nor different from motion, and that is entry into sophistry and ignorance. So we know that the truth is either pure sophistry or committing to infinite regresses and admission of what has no end into existence, and that is what is sought. So this is the exposition of the question on the mentioned proof.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objector attempts to avoid infinite regress by claiming that all the examples discussed (like causal efficacy) are merely relational (iḍāfī) and mental—they have no external reality. The author rebuts with a dilemma: either the affectingness (mu'aththiriyya) of the agent is real or it is merely mental. If it is real, the same infinite regress applies. If it is mental, then in external reality the agent is not an agent, essences lack attributes, and all distinctions vanish—this is pure sophistry. Therefore, the only consistent paths are either admitting real infinite regress or accepting sophistry. This rebuttal defends the objective reality of relations and attributes against a nominalist objection.

The Even-Odd Argument's Circularity
Critiques the even-odd and half-less-than-whole arguments for circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why arguments that use even-odd or half-less properties to prove finiteness are circular and therefore invalid.

Core Idea
If a property only applies to finite numbers, using it to prove finiteness is circular.

In kalam, arguments for the finiteness of bodies often rely on numerical properties. But as Sanusi shows, some of these arguments inadvertently assume what they try to prove. This passage exposes circular reasoning in two related proofs: the even-odd argument and the half-less-than-whole argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage Carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note that the author is critiquing two separate arguments in quick succession.
The two critiques share the same circularity structure.
2
Identify the First Critique
The author first targets the even-odd argument. Notice that evenness and oddness only apply to finite numbers. The opponent uses these properties to prove finiteness, which assumes finiteness already.
Circularity arises because the proof relies on a property that presupposes the conclusion.
3
Identify the Second Critique
The second critique concerns the claim that every number has a half less than itself. Again, having a half only applies to finite numbers. Using this to prove finiteness is equally circular.
The structure is identical: property presupposes finiteness.
4
Reflect on the Circularity
Ask yourself: Why is it invalid to use even-odd or half to prove finiteness? Because you are already assuming the number is finite when you even call it even or odd.
This shows the importance of avoiding circular reasoning in kalam arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نختم الكلام في هذه السؤلات بكلام آخر : وهو أنا نقول : دليل الشفع والوتر في غاية الخساسة . وذلك لأن العدد الذي يوصف بكونه شفعاً أو وترأ ، هو العدد المتناهي. فأما بتقدير كون العدد غير متناه ، فإنه يمتنع وصفه بكونه شفعاً أو وتراً . وعلى هذا لا يمكن وصف العدد بكونه شفعاً أو وتراً ، إلا إذا كان متناهياً . وأنتم بينتم كونه متناهياً، على كونه شفعاً أو وتراً ، وذلك يوجب الدور . وهذا أيضاً هو الاعتراض على قوله : إن كل عدد ، فإن نصفه أقل من كله وذلك لأن العدد الذي له نصف، هو العدد المتناهي، فإذا لم يثبت كون العدد متناهيا ، امتنع أن يفرض له نصف . وأنتم تثبتون كـونه متناهياً ، بالبناء على إثبات النصف له وذلك يوجب الدور ، فهذا تمام الكلام في تقرير السؤال . والله أعلم
Then we conclude the discussion on these questions with another statement: And we say: The argument of the even and the odd is extremely weak. That is because the number that is described as even or odd is the finite number. In the case of the number being infinite, it is impossible to describe it as even or odd. And on this basis, it is not possible to describe a number as even or odd unless it is finite. And you have proven its finiteness by its being even or odd, and that necessitates circularity. And this also is the objection to his statement: 'Every number, its half is less than it' — because the number that has a half is the finite number. So if the finiteness of the number is not established, it is impossible to assume a half for it. And you prove its finiteness by building on the establishment of a half for it, and that necessitates circularity. So this completes the exposition of the question. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage is a critique of a specific proof—the argument from even and odd (shafʿ wa watar). The author points out that the property of being even or odd only applies to finite numbers. Therefore, using evenness or oddness to prove finiteness is circular, because it already assumes finiteness. Similarly, the argument that every number has a half that is less than it also presupposes a finite number, and using the existence of a half to prove finiteness is equally circular. The author is exposing a logical fallacy in the opponent's reasoning, showing that the proof does not establish finiteness independently.

Key Terms
شفع
وتر
عدد متناه
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the author rejects all arguments for finiteness, but he only rejects these two circular ones.
Correction: The author only critiques specific proofs; he does not deny that numbers can be finite, only that these proofs are valid.

Checkpoint

A) Because it is based on scripture
B) Because it only applies to infinite numbers
C) Because it is circular
D) Because it is contradictory

Key Takeaway
The even-odd and half-less arguments fail because they presuppose the very finiteness they seek to prove.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author does not reject the conclusion that numbers are finite, only the flawed proof. Are there beliefs you hold that you defend with weak arguments? Are you willing to abandon those arguments and seek stronger ones, even if it feels unsettling?
?
When you encounter a flaw in a reasoning you once admired, does it shake your confidence or purify your understanding? How can you guard against being attached to flawed proofs while still holding onto the truth they try to prove?

Three Arguments for Multiplicity in Motion
Three proofs that motion entails multiplicity and change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three rational proofs that motion entails multiplicity and change, as presented in Islamic theological discourse.

Core Idea
Motion is not a single state; it necessarily involves change and multiplicity.

In this passage, the author presents three distinct arguments to demonstrate that motion inherently involves multiplicity and change. These arguments are foundational for understanding the Islamic theological perspective on the nature of motion and its implications for causality and divine action. As you read, notice how each argument approaches the concept from a different angle: the first deals with the future and parts, the second with the essence of motion, and the third with observable planetary motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to understand the overall meaning.
The first argument is not explicitly labeled 'الأول' but is implied.
2
Identify the three arguments
Look for the three separate reasons given for multiplicity. Note how each is introduced: the first through future and parts, the second through the essence of motion, the third through planetary cycles.
3
Analyze each argument's structure
For each argument, identify the premise and conclusion. Consider why the author considers these convincing proofs.
The second argument uses the definition of motion itself.
4
Connect to broader theology
Reflect on why establishing multiplicity in motion matters for Islamic theology, particularly regarding causality and divine action.
This relates to the concept of a contingent world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المستقبل هو الذي لم يوجد، وسيصير موجوداً، وإذا اختلفت خواص الأجزاء المفترضة في تلك الحركة اختلافاً على سبيل الوجوب، وجب الاعتراف بحصول الأجزاء والأبعاض. الثاني: إن الحركة ماهيتها التغير من حال إلى حال، ولا شك أن الحالية المتنقل عنها، مغايرة للحالة المتنقل إليها. وذلك يوجب التغير والتعدد. الثالث: إن في كل دورة، حصول للكوكب طلوع من المشرق، ووصول إلى نصف النهار، وغروب في المغرب. ولا شك أن هذه الأحوال متغايرة متعددة. وحينئذ يحصل الغرض والله أعلم.
The future is that which does not exist yet and will come to exist. And if the properties of the supposed parts in that motion differ in a necessary manner, then acknowledgment of the occurrence of parts and portions is obligatory. Second: The essence of motion is change from one state to another, and there is no doubt that the state being moved from is different from the state being moved to. That necessitates change and multiplicity. Third: In every revolution, there occurs for the planet a rising from the east, arrival at midday, and setting in the west. There is no doubt that these states are diverse and multiple. Then the purpose is achieved, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments supporting the occurrence of parts and multiplicity in motion. The first argument (implied as first) defines the future as non-existent but about to exist, and argues that if the properties of hypothetical parts in motion differ necessarily, then the existence of parts and sections must be admitted. The second argument states that the essence of motion is change from one state to another, and the starting state is undeniably different from the ending state, which entails change and multiplicity. The third argument uses the example of a planet's daily cycle—rising, culmination, setting—which are clearly distinct states, thus demonstrating multiplicity. The conclusion is that the intended purpose is achieved.

الحركة (al-haraka)
التغير (al-taghayyur)
الأجزاء (al-ajza')
الأبعاض (al-ab'aḍ)
الدورة (al-dawra)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion is a single, undifferentiated event.
Correction: The passage demonstrates that motion necessarily involves change between states and multiplicity of parts.

Checkpoint

A) Existence of parts
B) Change from one state to another
C) Rising and setting of planets
D) Division into future and past

Key Takeaway
The passage provides three rational arguments to establish that motion entails multiplicity and change, supporting the Islamic theological view of a contingent world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The General vs. Specific Existence of Past Events
First refutation: general vs. specific existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the sum of past events can be judged to have existed even if it never existed collectively.

Core Idea
Falsity of the specific does not falsify the general.

The opponent argues that since the sum of past events never exists as a whole, it cannot be judged. The author counters by distinguishing between general existence and collective existence. Using the analogy of seeing all townspeople, he shows that the general claim remains true even if the specific collective existence is false. This lesson unpacks that critical distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the first answer
Read the Arabic passage that begins with 'لنا في الجواب عنه وجوه: الأول:' and ends before 'والوجه الثاني'. Then read the translation.
2
Understand the analogy of the townspeople
The author uses an analogy: seeing the whole town's people is general, while seeing them collectively is specific. Ask yourself how this applies to the sum of past events.
3
Trace the logical conclusion
The author then argues that the sum exists in a state of separation, and since the compound (existence with separation) is true, its parts (simple existence) must also be true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الثاني: وهو قوله: (مجموع الحوادث لا وجود له البتة، وما لا وجود له، يمتنع الحكم عليه بقبول الزيادة والنقصان..) فنقول: لنا في الجواب عنه وجوه: الأول: إن قولنا: مجموع الحوادث قد وجد، أعم من قولنا: مجموع الحوادث قد وجد بصفة الاجتماع. والدليل عليه: أن القائل إذا قال: رأيت جملة أهل البلد، فإنه يصح أن يقال له: أرأيت جملتهم بصفة الاجتماع أو لا بصفة الاجتماع؟ وذلك يدل على أن المفهوم من رؤيتهم بجملتهم، أعم من المفهوم من رؤية جملتهم بصفة الاجتماع. فكذلك قولنا: جملة الحوادث الماضية قد وجدت، أعم من قولنا: إن جملة الحوادث الماضية قد وجدت بصفة الاجتماع. وأنتم إنما أبطلتم هذا المفهوم الخاص. وظاهر: أنه لا يلزم من كذب الخاص، كذب العام. فثبت: أنه لا يلزم من كذب قولنا: جملة الحوادث الماضية وجدت بصفة الاجتماع: كذب قولنا: جملة الحوادث قد وجدت. بل نقول: الدليل على أن جملة الحوادث قد وجدت: أنه يصدق قولنا: إن جملة الحوادث قد وجدت بصفة الافتراق والتعاقب. وقولنا: جملة الحوادث قد وجدت، جزء من قولنا: جملة الحوادث قد وجدت بصفة الافتراق. ومتى صدق المركب، فقد صدقت أجزاؤه لا محالة. فوجب أن يصدق قولنا: إن جملة الحوادث قد وجدت. فالحاصل: أنهم أقاموا الدليل على بطلان الخاص، ثم استدلوا به على بطلان العام. وذلك باطل. وأما نحن فقد أقمنا الدلالة على صدق الخاص، ثم استدللنا به على صدق العام. وذلك حق. والوجه الثاني: إن قولهم: إن جملة الحوادث الماضية لا يصح الحكم عليها البتة: كلام متناقض من وجوه: الأول: إنهم حكموا بامتناع الحكم عليها. فهذا حكم على تلك الجملة بهذا الامتناع. فيلزم من هذا الاعتبار. والثاني: إنا إذا قلنا: الأحوال الماضية لا نهاية لها. فهذه قضية. ولكل قضية موضوع ومحمول. فالموضوع في هذه القضية، إما كل واحد من تلك الأحوال الماضية، وهو ظاهر الفساد. لأن كل واحد منها لا يصدق عليه أنه أمور متناهية. وإما كل تلك الأمور وجملتها. وحينئذ يكون هذا اعتراف بأنه يمكن الحكم على ذلك الحكم وعلى تلك الجملة، بأحكام معلومة. وذلك يبطل قولهم: إنه لا يمكن الحكم عليه البتة. والثالث: إنهم يقولون: الأحوال الماضية دائمة مستمرة من الأزل إلى الأبد. فالمحكوم عليه بالاستمرار من الأزل إلى الأبد: ليس هو كل واحد من تلك الحوادث بل مجموعها. وذلك يناقض قولهم: إن ذلك المجموع لا يمكن الحكم عليه.
As for the second question: his statement: 'The sum of past events has no existence at all, and what has no existence cannot be judged as accepting increase or decrease.' We say: We have several aspects in answering it. First: Our statement 'the sum of past events has existed' is more general than our statement 'the sum of past events has existed collectively.' The proof of that is that when someone says 'I saw the whole town's people,' it is valid to say to him: 'Did you see them collectively or not collectively?' That indicates that the concept of seeing them as a whole is more general than the concept of seeing them collectively. Similarly, our statement 'the sum of past events has existed' is more general than our statement 'the sum of past events has existed collectively.' You have only refuted this specific concept. It is apparent that the falsity of the specific does not entail the falsity of the general. So it is established that the falsity of our statement 'the sum of past events existed collectively' does not entail the falsity of our statement 'the sum of past events has existed.' Rather, we say: The evidence that the sum of past events has existed is that our statement 'the sum of past events has existed in a state of separation and succession' is true. And our statement 'the sum of past events has existed' is part of our statement 'the sum of past events has existed in a state of separation.' Whenever the compound is true, its parts are necessarily true. So it is necessary that our statement 'the sum of past events has existed' is true. The conclusion: They presented evidence for the falsity of the specific, then used it to argue for the falsity of the general. That is false. As for us, we have presented evidence for the truth of the specific, then used it to argue for the truth of the general. That is correct. Second aspect: Their statement 'the sum of past events cannot be judged at all' is self-contradictory in several ways: First: They judged that judging it is impossible. That is a judgment on that sum of this impossibility. So it follows from this consideration. Second: When we say 'past states are infinite,' this is a proposition. Every proposition has a subject and a predicate. The subject in this proposition is either each individual past state—which is clearly false, because each individual state cannot be described as being infinite—or all these things and their totality. In that case, this is an acknowledgment that it is possible to make known judgments about that judgment and about that totality. That refutes their statement that it cannot be judged at all. Third: They say 'past states are continuous from eternity to everlasting.' What is being described as continuing from eternity to everlasting is not each individual event but their totality. That contradicts their statement that this totality cannot be judged.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection claiming that the sum of past events has no existence and therefore cannot be subject to judgments like increase or decrease. The author provides two main refutations. The first refutation distinguishes between the general statement 'the sum has existed' and the specific statement 'the sum has existed collectively.' Using an analogy of seeing all the townspeople—where one can ask whether they were seen collectively or not—the author shows that the opponent's argument only disproves the specific collective existence, not the general existence. Since the general does not depend on the specific, the opponent's inference is invalid. Moreover, the author argues that the general statement is true because the sum exists in a state of separation and succession, and the compound statement (existence in separation) implies the truth of its parts (simple existence). The second refutation demonstrates that the opponent's own claims are self-contradictory. First, by stating that the sum cannot be judged, they themselves make a judgment about it. Second, when discussing infinite past states, the subject of that proposition must be the totality, implying it can be judged. Third, their assertion that past states are continuous from eternity applies to the totality, not individuals, again contradicting their claim that the totality cannot be judged.

Key Terms
مجموع الحوادث (majmu' al-hawadith)
الخاص (al-khas)
العام (al-'am)
الافتراق (al-iftiraq)
التعاقب (al-ta'aqub)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if the sum of past events never exists collectively, then it has no existence at all and cannot be judged.
Correction: The sum can exist in a non-collective manner (i.e., in separation and succession), so it does have existence and can be judged.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the author use to illustrate the distinction between general and specific?
A) Seeing a planet rise and set
B) Seeing the whole town's people
C) The essence of motion
D) Infinite past states

Key Takeaway
The sum of past events has existed because its existence in a state of separation implies general existence, even if collective existence is false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed your entire effort in worship because you couldn't maintain a single, perfect act, forgetting that the sum of your deeds exists in scattered moments? What one small action today can you affirm as truly existing, even if it feels isolated?
?
The opponent argued that because the sum of past events has no collective existence, it has no existence at all. Is there an area of your life—like your repentance, your knowledge, or your faith—where you believe that because you haven't seen a complete, flawless moment, your entire struggle is worthless? How does this lesson challenge that belief?
?
If the truth of the compound (the sum existing in separation) proves the truth of the part (the sum exists), then what does the scattered existence of your good deeds today say about the reality of your faith? Will you commit to recognizing one good deed tonight as genuinely yours, even if it wasn't part of a grand collective act?
